Jerusalem Talmud Gittin
תלמוד ירושלמי גיטין
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Gittin



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who brings a bill of divorce from overseas<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">“Overseas” stands for all places who do not have a rabbinic authority ordained by the Patriarchate in the Land of Israel or the two Academies acting on the authority of the Prince of the Diaspora in Babylonia. If one cannot be sure that all fine points of the rules of bills of divorce were followed, the court must have the possibility of examining the messenger about the details of the writing and signing of the document.</i> must be able to say: “It was written and signed before me.” Rabban Gamliel says, even somebody who brings from Reqam<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Two places “Reqam” are mentioned as border towns in Transjordan in the description of the borders of the Land of Israel; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1, Notes 47, 51.</i> and Ḥeger<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This place has not been identified; it is conjectured to be on the Southern border of the Land of Israel.</i>; Rebbi Eliezer says, even from Kefar Ludim to Lydda<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Which were close to one another but in different court districts.</i>; but the Sages say that the only one who has to be able to say that it was written and signed before him is one who brings from overseas or to overseas. One who brings from one province<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While in Biblical Hebrew מדינה means “province”, in Talmudic usage the word often has the late hieroglyphic, and Arabic, meaning of “capital”.</i> to another overseas has to say that it was written and signed before him; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even from one district to the next.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who brings a bill of divorce from overseas,” etc. This is difficult. If somebody brings a gift document from overseas,would you require him to say, it was written and signed<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The form נחתם is that of most Mishnah mss. and of the Babli. The form נתחתם in the Mishnah is that of several Palestinian Mishnah mss.</i> before me? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, there is a difference because they are not conversant with the fine points of bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 2" href="/Gittin.2">Babli, 2a/2b</a>, it is pointed out that a bill of divorce has to be written for the woman to be divorced since the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>): “He shall write <i>for her</i>”. While the text of the bill must mention the names of husband and wife, it cannot be ascertained from the text whether the scribe was instructed to write the text specifically for that woman; there must be a live witness available who can be examined about this point. A second opinion notes that the signatures of the witnesses themselves would need confirmation.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, that is a leniency lest she sit abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 3a" href="/Gittin.3a">Babli, 3a</a>, this is an anonymous (unanimous) opinion. As explained in the sequel, if the husband could come later and claim the the bill of divorce was fake, no woman could ever remarry on a bill of divorce written far away for fear that her children from a second husband could retroactively be declared to be bastards; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 10:1</a>.</i>. Is that a leniency? It is only a restriction, for if he does not say to her, it was written and signed before me, you do not permit her to remarry. Rebbi Yose said, the difficulty which you impose upon her at the beginning, that he is required to say, it was written and signed before me, makes it easy for her at the end. For if [the husband] would come and protest, his protest would be void. Rebbi Mana wanted to say, a protest other than the text of the bill<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., if the husband claims that he had attached a condition to the bill not provided for in the bill and that the condition was not satisfied. Since the condition was not presented to the court which oversaw the delivery of the document, it cannot be considered.</i>. But is a protest about the text of the bill a protest which has no validity<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He claims that the entire bill is fake or that there is an intrinsic defect which makes the entire document invalid.</i>? Even for a protest which has validity, said Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, since you say that the reason for the difficulty which you impose upon her at the beginning, that he is required to say, it was signed before me, is to make it easy for her at the end, that if [the husband] would come and protest, his protest would be void; this means that there is no difference whether it is a protest not about the text of the bill or about the body of the bill, a protest which has no validity, or a protest which does have validity<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since there was a witness who was cross-examined about the validity of the bill, the husband cannot be admitted to testify since when he claims to be still the husband of his divorcee he declares himself a relative by marriage who is barred from acting as a witness for or against his wife.</i>! But should you worry that maybe be gave the bill to disqualified witnesses to sign<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The messenger who delivers the bill might not know that the witnesses either were relatives of one of the parties or convicted felons; their signatures would invalidate the document.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, he is not suspect to damage her before Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For if both the local court and the wife act in good faith, Heaven will absolve her but he has sinned. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.4.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 15:4, Notes 92–93</a>.</i>. Before the court he is suspect to damage her. But since he knows that if he comes and protests his protest is void, he will choose qualified witnesses to sign.
If [the bill of divorce] contained documentation of a gift and [the messenger] said, it was written and signed before me, is [the husband’s] protest about the gift void since it is void about the divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The question presupposes that one follows R. Joḥanan’s opinion in the preceding paragraph. The messenger who delivers the bill of divorce is a single witness who cannot validate a monetary claim. But since by tradition he has the power to validate a bill of divorce and the delivery of the bill implies authorization of the divorcee to collect her <i>ketubah</i>, it follows that in matters of bills of divorce the single messenger has the power to validate money claims as long as they are included in or dependent upon the bill of divorce.</i>? Or, since his protest is void [only] about the divorce, is it valid about the gift<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since annulment of the special gift from the husband to his divorcee will not hinder her from remarrying, there seems to be no special reason why a single witness should make a money claim definitive.</i>? We have stated later: “Bills of divorce for wives and documents of manumission are equal in the rules of carrying and bringing.” If [the document of manumission] contained documentation of a gift and [the messenger] said, it was written and signed before me, is [the master’s] protest about the gift void since it is void about the manumission? Or since his protest is void [only] about the manumission, is it valid about the gift<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If the document of manumission was accepted by the local court at the point of delivery, the slave upon immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> became a full Jew. A Jew cannot be enslaved by a fellow Jew (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:42" href="/Leviticus.25.42"><i>Lev</i>. 25:42</a>). The reason why the master’s protest is void has nothing to do with monetary claims; there seems to be no reason why the master cannot disclaim the gift.</i>? If somebody signed all his property over to his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to have been a common occurrence in Roman society.</i>, you say that the gift is the document of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of freedom is that a person is master of himself; the gift document transfers mastership of the slave to himself.</i>. How do you treat this? Is it a document of manumission and his protest is void or is it a gift and his protest is valid? This can be compared to the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The following is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 11:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.11.5.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 11:5</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.3.2-3">Notes 97–102</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 1:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.1.7.3"><i>Makkot</i> 1:16</a>. The variant readings are given in <i>Ketubot</i>. The messenger who delivers the document of manumission, which in this case is a document about money matters, is believed as if he were a group of two in regard of the manumission but is disqualified for the money part. All the questions asked up to this point can be answered if one decides whether to follow R. Joḥanan or R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> If somebody signed all his property over to two persons in one document and the testimony of the witnesses was valid for one but invalid for the other. Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot</i>: R. Yasa. Since rabbis Yasa (Assi) and Immi (Ammi) form a permanent pair in the Babli, there is no real difference in the attributions.</i>: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagreed; one said, since it is invalid for one it is invalid for the other, but the other said, it is valid for one and invalid for the other. Rebbi Mana did not specify; Rebbi Abin specified: Rebbi Joḥanan said, since it is invalid for one it is invalid for the other; but Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is valid for one and invalid for the other. Rebbi Eleazar said, a Mishnah supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “Since testimony of two [witnesses] is invalid if one of them turns out to be related or disqualified, so also of three [witnesses] it is invalid if one of them turns out to be related or disqualified”. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Ḥanina the colleague of the rabbis and the rabbis disagree. One says, the argument of Rebbi Eleazar is correct, but the other says, Rebbi Eleazar is not correct. For him who says, the argument of Rebbi Eleazar is correct, it is a testimony about one person; as testimony it is totally invalid if it is partially invalid. For him who says, the argument of Rebbi Eleazar is not correct, it is as if two groups of witnesses came, valid for one and disqualified for the other.
Only if he says, it was written before me during the day and signed during the day<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">“The day” means the day written as date in the document. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.2.1">Mishnah 2:2</a> notes that a pre-dated bill of divorce is invalid like all pre-dated documents. The bill may have been written in the night and signed the following day since the rabbinic day is counted from sundown to sundown; it cannot have been written during daytime and signed the following night since that would make it pre-dated.</i>; only if he says, it was written before me especially for her and signed expressly for her<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The messenger must have heard the husband tell the scribe to write a bill of divorce for his wife, mentioning her by name. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 3a" href="/Gittin.3a">Babli agrees, 3a</a>.</i>. It was asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: Does he have to know the names of the witnesses at the moment of their signing? He said to them, did the Gentiles Luciani sign it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If the names sound Roman, it does not mean anything. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 11b" href="/Gittin.11b">Babli, 11b</a>, R. Joḥanan notes that if a Gentile name is known to be used by Jews, the matter does not have to be investigated. Examples given in the Babli are Λύκος “wolf”, a frequent substitute for Benjamin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:27" href="/Genesis.49.27"><i>Gen</i>. 49:27</a>), and Λίς “lion” (in epic poetry, cf. Semitic ליש), a substitute for Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:9" href="/Genesis.49.9"><i>Gen</i>. 49:9</a>). Cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Jewish Family Names and their Origins: An Etymological Dictionary</i> (Ktav, 1992; German edition <i>Etymologisches Wörterbuch der jüdischen Familiennamen</i>, Saur 1996), Introduction: Sobriquets, Animal Names.</i>? And you say so? A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “All bills of divorce coming from overseas are valid even if their names sound Gentile since Jews outside the Land of Israel bear Gentile names.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 11b" href="/Gittin.11b">Babli 11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta 6:4</a>.</i>” He said only, outside the Land; therefore not in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It should not be assumed that Jewish people with Gentile names cannot sign a bill of divorce in the Land of Israel; there are a number of rabbis with Gentile names such as Eudaimon, Pappos, Antigonos. But in case such a name appears, an inquiry has to be made about the identity of the witness, wheras in documents coming from outside the Land, all names are accepted without inquiry.</i>. What about this? Rebbi Bevai in the name of Rebbi Issi: unless he writes, at a Jewish place<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Greek Ὶουδαϊκός, -ή, -όν “Jewish”. If the names of the witnesses are Gentile, some indication of this being a purely Jewish document is needed since all documents executed in a Gentile court are valid except bills of divorce.</i>. If there is no Jewish place, in the synagogue. If there is no synagogue, he assembles there ten people<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">All of whom have to sign as witnesses even if most of them are related to the couple, if only two of the witnesses are acceptable in law; it is to be assumed that not all of their names will be characteristically Gentile. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta 7:11</a>; M. A. Friedman, <i>Jewish Marriage in Palestine</i>, Vol. 1, p. 489.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, it would be better to certify the signatures<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">After the document was duly signed, the local court could append a certification that the witnesses were known to the court and in good standing. Such a certification would have to be accepted by every rabbinic court anywhere (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.3.1">Mishnah 1:3</a>).</i> than to assemble there ten people. What about this? Even in a Jewish store<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The document must mention the place where it was written. One might add that it was written in a Jewish store to emphasize the Jewish character of the document, implicitly validating the Gentile-sounding signatures.</i>.
If he did not say: “it was written and signed before me”? Should not an Amora have asked this question? He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear to whom this refers; the text seems defective.</i>, that is what Rabin ben Rav said: If he brought a bill of divorce, gave it to her, but did not tell her: “It was written and signed before me”<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Is the bill of divorce invalid in this case? In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 5b" href="/Gittin.5b">Babli, 5b</a>, Rabin bar Rav Ḥisda brought a bill of divorce before R. Joḥanan, who instructed him to deliver the bill in the presence of two witnesses and to make the required declaration. The question here can be interpreted to mean that Rabin asked whether the formality was really necessary in view of the fact, reported in the next paragraph, that R. Joshua ben Levi, of the generation of R. Joḥanan’s teachers, did not require the declaration. In the Babli, which is edited according to strict historical principles, R. Joshua ben Levi is mentioned before R. Joḥanan.</i>? There came a case before Rebbi Joḥanan, who said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The messenger who had delivered the bill of divorce without making the required declaration.</i>, take it from her and say in front of two [witnesses]: “It was written and signed before me.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood that the Mishnah requires the declaration to be made either in the presence of two witnesses or at the local court. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a> permits the declaration to be made “even after three years” if the bill of divorce can be returned to the messenger.</i>” Does Rebbi Joḥanan follow Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar, since Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>; a different version <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78a" href="/Gittin.78a">Babli 78a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84b" href="/Gittin.84b">84b</a>. The statement refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.1">Mishnah 8:2</a>: “If he said to her, take this bond, or she took it out from his belt, and when she read it she realized that it was her bill of divorce, it is not a bill of divorce unless he says to her: This is your bill of divorce.” The reason is that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires that in a divorce the husband deliver the bill into the wife’s hand. The question arises whether the required declaration is valid after the bill is already in the wife’s hand. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78a" href="/Gittin.78a">Babli 78a</a>, Rebbi is quoted as holding that the bill is invalid unless given as a bill of divorce; R. Simeon ben Eleazar permits the husband to reclaim the bill from his wife and deliver it a second time accompanied by the required declaration. In the Tosephta, Rebbi is reported not to require the declaration, R. Simeon ben Eleazar requires that the declaration accompany the delivery. The language of the Tosephta is that of the Yerushalmi; it is not clear whether R. Simeon ben Eleazar permits taking the document back.</i>, it is no bill of divorce unless he tells her at the moment of delivery that it is her bill of divorce? Can the position of Rebbi Joḥanan be derived from Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar? Would Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar agree that if he told her at the moment of delivery that it was her bill of divorce it would not be a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The answer to this rhetorical question clearly is no; the only condition imposed by R. Simeon ben Eleazar is that the wife be informed of the nature of the document at the moment of delivery.</i>? But here, even if he tells her at the moment of delivery that it is her bill of divorce, it is no bill of divorce unless he tells her in front of two [witnesses]: “It was written and signed before me.” But Rebbi Joḥanan might hold that a woman cannot be believed if she says, I received a bill of divorce from my husband’s agents<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the delivery of the document characterized as a bill of divorce must be made before two witnesses in good standing. It seems that in his case, the document was delivered in private; the main reason of R. Joḥanan was not the recitation of the formula but the delivery in front of witnesses.</i>. But we did state<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.6.1">Mishnah 2:7</a>. If the husband delivers the document to her on condition that it should not become effective until she presents it to the court which has to preside over the payment of her <i>ketubah</i>, she can bring it herself and declare before the court that “it was written and signed before me.”</i>: “The wife herself can bring her bill of divorce.” Would you not have to be afraid that she might have received the bill from a representative of the husband<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Why should she be believed if she brings the document from abroad when she is not believed if the document is delivered to her in private? It is implied here that the act which makes the document valid is its <i>signing</i> by two reliable witnesses, not its <i>delivery</i>.</i>? How is it? The reason of Rebbi Joḥanan is to make her known as divorcee in front of two [witnesses]<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the requirement of the declaration mentioned in the Mishnah is prescriptive but its omission does not invalidate the divorce.</i>.
A case came before Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 5b" href="/Gittin.5b">Babli, 5b</a>, it was R. Simeon bar Abba, a recognized scholar, who brought the document.</i>. He said to him, it is unnecessary. The opinion of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi seems inverted. There, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, there is a difference because they are not conversant with the fine points of bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 2" href="/Gittin.2">Babli, 2a/2b</a>, it is pointed out that a bill of divorce has to be written for the woman to be divorced since the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>): “He shall write <i>for her</i>”. While the text of the bill must mention the names of husband and wife, it cannot be ascertained from the text whether the scribe was instructed to write the text specifically for that woman; there must be a live witness available who can be examined about this point. A second opinion notes that the signatures of the witnesses themselves would need confirmation.</i>, but here, he says so? The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: What you said was in earlier times when no Fellows<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Here, “Fellow” does not have the technical meaning of “person who observes the rules of food purity” but means “person learned in the law.”</i> were found outside the Land, but now that there are Fellows found outside the Land, they are competent<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is R. Joshua ben Levi’s explanation given to R. Simeon bar Abba the Babylonian.</i>. But did we not state, “and one who brings there<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, the duty to declare that the document was written in the presence of the messenger is extended to bills of divorce written in the Land and delivered abroad.</i>”? And even if you say that no Fellows are found outside the Land, are we not found in the Land of Israel? In order not to make a distinction in the bills of divorce from outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">There otherwise would be no reason to require a declaration for bills originating in the Land.</i>. But then one who brings from overseas should not have to say: “It was written before me and signed before me.” Not to make a distinction in the bills of divorce in the Land of Israel. What about it? One is restrictive in a simple case because of the complicated one and one is not permissive in the complicated because of the easy one<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It is preferable to require a declaration for documents originating in Palestine (the easy case) rather than to eliminate the declaration for documents originating outside the Land (the complicated case).</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Abba: This<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that the declaration was unnecessary.</i> of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, if it was done. But from the start even Rebbi Joshua ben Levi agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In that, R. Joshua ben Levi agrees with R. Joḥanan. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 5b" href="/Gittin.5b">Babli, 5b</a>, disagrees since in its version R. Simeon bar Abba asked R. Joshua ben Levi whether he had to make the declaration when delivering the bill of divorce.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav, Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Abba bar Ḥana: Two who brought a bill of divorce do not have to declare: It was written before us and signed before us<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 5a" href="/Gittin.5a">Babli, 5a</a>, this opinion is attributed to Rav Huna, Rav’s student.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They have to<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 16" href="/Gittin.16">Babli, 16a/b</a>, he is quoted in the opposite sense.</i>. He himself who brought the bill of divorce does not have to deliver the bill in the presence of two [witnesses]<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, a fundamental question is whether a bill of divorce is validated by the signatories of the bill or the witnesses to the delivery. The first opinion is attributed in the Babli to R. Meïr; it is generally accepted in the Yerushalmi. The second opinion is R. Eleazar’s in both Talmudim (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.3.1">Mishnah 9:4</a>). The Babli considers R. Eleazar the dominant author in matters of divorce; the Yerushalmi considers his a minority opinion. Accordingly, the Yerushalmi permits private delivery from husband to wife. Nevertheless, since marriage is a public affair, the divorcee cannot remarry unless her divorce is made public. The husband therefore has to publicly declare her a divorcee after his private delivery.</i> in order to declare her a divorcee in the presence of two. “If he gave her the bill<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the presence of witnesses.</i>, took it from her, and threw it into the sea or into a river<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Where it cannot be retrieved.</i>. If later he says to her that it was an empty papyrus or a paid bond, he cannot be believed to disqualify her.”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.2">Tosephta 6:2</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 19b" href="/Gittin.19b">Babli 19b</a>.</i> Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: If <i>he</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since the preceding sentence deals with the husband, the question is whether it extends to everybody else.</i> cannot disqualify her, who can? He said to him, since she was declared a divorcee in the presence of two [witnesses], he cannot be believed to disqualify her<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are two witnesses to the delivery, the divorce could be put in doubt only by a proof that the witnesses are false. In the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 55a" href="/Gittin.55a">Babli, 55a</a>, the divorce is valid if the witnesses knew that the document was a bill of divorce, but the wife did not. It is possible to read the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.2-6">Yerushalmi 5:5</a> in the same sense.</i>. If he gave her a bill of divorce which was found to be invalid, they<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The court supervising the divorce. A forced divorce is valid if forced and executed by a rabbinic court, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah 9:10</a>. However, the power of a court to force divorces is severely circumscribed, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4-9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4-9.3">Halakhah 9:10</a>. In a case of forced divorce, it has to be ascertained whether the court remained within the limits of his authority. It seems clear that the court has the authority to force a divorce if the giver of the invalid bill was a Cohen since even an invalid divorce forbids the wife to return to her priestly husband; but without a valid divorce she cannot marry any other man or collect the sums due her.</i> forced him to give her a replacement. This case came before the rabbis and they declared it valid. Did we not say, he cannot be believed to disqualify her? There, the flaw was not proved; here the flaw was proved<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot invalidate a bill of divorce which is not available for inspection; the court can invalidate a document in its possession.</i>. As the following: Ḥinena, the son of Rebbi Asi, was a medical doctor and threw a bill of divorce to his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Under certain circumstances, a bill of divorce can be delivered by depositing it in the wife’s domain, Chapter 8.</i>. She cried. Her neighbor came, grabbed it from her, and gave her an empty papyrus. The case came before the rabbis and they took it into account<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">She was “divorced and not divorced.” Since the document is not available for inspection, it cannot be declared invalid. Since the delivery was irregular, it cannot be declared valid. The husband has to be forced to deliver a second bill.</i>. But did not Rebbi Yasa say in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: If the designation was checked and found to be based on [the testimony of] women or children, the designation is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4-11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4-11.3">Halakhah 9:11</a>. The Mishnah states that if a woman is generally said to have received a bill of divorce, one can accept the divorce as a fact. R. Joḥanan notes that if an investigation reveals that nobody who might appear as a formal witness in criminal cases is the source of the opinion, one has to disregard the “general knowledge” of the population. If it is known that she received a document from her husband, it does not necessarily mean that it was a bill of divorce.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 89a" href="/Gittin.89a">Babli, 89a</a>, it is a matter of dispute between Babylonian authorities whether a “general opinion” must be traced to a person who might appear as witness in good standing before a criminal court.</i>? There, the designation was not mentioned. But here the name “bill of divorce” was mentioned. And some want to say, if he asserted that it was a valid bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The husband himself is the source of the rumor. If he is a person of good standing, Mishnah 9:11 requires that his information be trusted. According to this interpretation, the wife is divorced and does not need a second bill in order to collect her <i>ketubah</i> and remarry.</i>, but she cried, her neighbor came, grabbed it from her, and gave her an empty papyrus.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he wrote it in the Land of Israel, signed it outside the Land, went to deliver it to her outside the Land but did not find her outside the Land, returned and found her in the Land of Israel. He must say, “it was written and signed before me,” because he wrote it in the Land of Israel and signed it outside the Land But if it was written and signed in the Land of Israel, he went to deliver it to her outside the Land but did not find her outside the Land, he need not say, “it was written and signed before me.”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to refer to a tannaïtic statement similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>: “If he wrote it in the Land and had it signed outside the Land, one has to say ‘it was written and signed before me.’ If it was written outside the Land but signed in the Land, one does not have to say ‘it was written and signed before me.’ ” Since a document must be signed on the date indicated in it, writing and signing must take place at a border point.</i> If he gave her bill of divorce to her when she said, my courtyard at Acco<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The border of the Land of Israel passes through Acco, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:2:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.2.2-8">Halakhah 2</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.8"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1, Note 30</a>. The bill of divorce was presented to her in the Israeli part of Acco, but she desired it to be deposited in her courtyard which was outside the Land. A bill of divorce can be legally delivered by being deposited in a house or walled courtyard which is the woman’s property (even if it was part of her dowry, which becomes the husband’s property but reverts to her at the moment of divorce.)</i> shall acquire it for me, Rebbi Ḥinena said, it is as if she had a long arm. If she had a long arm, she stretched it out and took it from there, does he not have to say “it was written and signed before me”<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Because the delivery took place outside the Land.</i>? Rebbi Abba said, who would deny it<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥinena’s statement is trivial; the nontrivial part is that a delivery in a courtyard is valid even if the wife is far away at the moment of delivery; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77b" href="/Gittin.77b">Rashba, <i>Novellae to Giṭṭin</i> 77b</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77b" href="/Gittin.77b">Babli, 77b</a>, delivery from a distance is a matter of contention and prohibited by Ulla; there nobody accepts the notion of an “extended arm.”</i>?
Rebbi Ezra asked before Rebbi Mana: If a man and his wife held on to a bill of divorce; she says, I acquired it, but he says, you did not acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He demands some money from her before he is willing to give her a divorce.</i>. Does this refer to the disagreement between Rebbi and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel? Since they disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 1:1, Babli <i>Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 7a.</i>: If creditor and borrower both hold on to the bond; the creditor says it is mine; I had lost it. The borrower says, it is mine because I paid it off. The document should be verified by its signatories, the words of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Babli, a private bond is valid only if its signatures have been notarized in court. Rebbi permits late notarizing, so the creditor can claim half.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, they should split<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Following Symmachos that “money in doubt shall be split,” Mishnah <i>Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 1:1; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 2, Note 9, 10, Note 62</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, everything depends on who actually holds the signatures of the witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the signatures validate the document.</i>. Where do they disagree? If it was written lengthwise<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In computer printers this is called “landscape format”. The writing parallels the long side of the paper; it is possible for both parties to hold on to part of the lines used for signatures.</i> and both hold part of the signatures of the witnesses. He said to him, it is everybody’s opinion that it is not a bill of divorce if all is in her hand but a thread is in his hand, for it is written: “He shall deliver it into her hand,” not unless all be in her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78b" href="/Gittin.78b">Babli, 78b</a> Rav Ḥisda formulates: If the bill is in her hands and a thread is in his hand: if he can draw it back to himself, she is not divorced, otherwise she is divorced. The Babli bases its ruling on the meaning of the word כריתות “divorce” which requires complete physical separation.</i>.
Rebbi Eliezer objected to the rabbis: Just as you hold that one who brings a bill of divorce from one province to another overseas has to say, “it was written and signed before me,” so I hold that one who brings a bill of divorce from one province to another in the Land of Israel has to say, “it was written and signed before me.” Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi said, it happened that one brought a bill of divorce from the harbor of Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He delivered it in the city of <i>Caesarea maritima</i>, formerly called Straton’s Tower, which was not part of the Land of Israel; cf. <i>Ševiʻit</i> 6:1, Note 32.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Abbahu who said yes, he has to say that it was written and signed before him. But is the harbor of Caesarea not part of Caesarea? Rebbi Abin said, it was from a departing ship, and it was stated thus: One who brings a bill of divorce from a ship is like one who brings from outside the Land and has to say that it was written and signed before him<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 7b" href="/Gittin.7b">Babli, 7b</a>, there are <i>baraitot</i> quoted either that the declaration is needed or not needed, but there the reference is to a person who brings a bill of divorce <i>in</i> a ship connecting places in the Land, rather than <i>from</i> a ship sailing overseas.</i>.
If there was one district which was subdivided into two, one does not have to say it was written and signed before me. Similarly if two were combined to form one.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, from Reqam<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Two places “Reqam” are mentioned as border towns in Transjordan in the description of the borders of the Land of Israel; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1, Notes 47, 51.</i> to the East and Reqam belongs to the East, from Ascalon to the South and Ascalon belongs to the South, from Acco to the North and Acco belongs to the North<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The list in this Mishnah mentions border towns from where the messenger has to affirm that the document was written and signed in his presence. The basic reference is the Baraita on the borders of the Land of Israel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.9">Sevi'it 6:1, Notes 32-51.</a></i>. Rebbi Meïr says, Acco belongs to the Land of Israel in matters of bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees that in matters of ritual purity and Sabbatical laws, most of Acco is outside the Land.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Jehudah says, from Reqam to the East,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan told the people of Sepphoris: You say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This name is obviously incorrect; it is impossible to determine what was in its place or whether the name should simply be deleted.</i>, even a person who brings a bill of divorce from Babylonia does not have to say “it was written and signed before me.” But I am saying that it is necessary, for there is a Mishnah: “Rebbi Jehudah says, from Reqam to the East and Reqam belongs to the East.” And even if you say that they disagree with Rebbi Jehudah and say that Reqam does not belong to the East, what about from Babylonia to here<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 6a" href="/Gittin.6a">Babli, 6a</a>, the argument is attributed to R. Jeremiah.</i>? Rav said, we consider ourselves as in the Land of Israel in the matter of bills of divorce. But Samuel said, even from one city quarter to another<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 6a" href="/Gittin.6a">Babli, 6a</a>, Rav is quoted as he is here; Samuel says that Babylonia is like any other place outside the Land, Rav Sheshet requires confirmation from one city quarter to another and Rav Ḥisda requires an attestation in all cases in which the witnesses are unknown to the court in whose district the bill is delivered.</i>. He asked before him to change his opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel convinced Rav that he should change his opinion.</i>, when Cahana told him, what would we do with the earlier cases of women who remarried<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Cahana pointed out that if Rav changed his practice, he would attach a presumption of illegitimacy to any children of women who had remarried according to his previous standard.</i>? He said to him, what do you have in your hand? For he was of Rebbi’s opinion as it was stated: If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas which was neither written nor signed in his presence, she has to leave under the thirteen penalties<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">All the disabilities enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.5.1">Mishnaiot 8:5</a> about a woman remarrying after an invalid divorce, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:1:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.1.2-12"><i>Yebamot</i> 10:1</a> regarding a woman who remarried thinking that her husband was dead when he was alive and later returned.</i>, the words of Rebbi. But the Sages did not agree in this matter<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the absence of the provision about writing and signing does not invalidate the bill, cf. Notes 32 ff.</i>.
Everybody agrees that if [the husband] comes and protests, his protest is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">9,</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 3a" href="/Gittin.3a">Babli, 3a</a>, this is an anonymous (unanimous) opinion. As explained in the sequel, if the husband could come later and claim the the bill of divorce was fake, no woman could ever remarry on a bill of divorce written far away for fear that her children from a second husband could retroactively be declared to be bastards; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 10:1</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since there was a witness who was cross-examined about the validity of the bill, the husband cannot be admitted to testify since when he claims to be still the husband of his divorcee he declares himself a relative by marriage who is barred from acting as a witness for or against his wife.</i>. That is, if he protested after she remarried. If he protested before she remarried and then she remarried<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Should one require that she not remarry before the matter has been decided by the local court?</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The same story is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 6b" href="/Gittin.6b">Babli, 6b</a>.</i>: It happened that somebody brought a bill of divorce before Rebbi Ismael. He asked him, from where are you? He answered, from Kefar Simai<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Probably Kafr Sumeïa on the road from Acco to Safed, in the border region described in <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta, 1:3</a>, the place is mentioned as ססי or סאסאי.</i> in the region of Acco. He said to him, then you have to say, “it was written and signed in my presence;” you do not need witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The signatures on the bill do not have to be notarized by the court, which would have been difficult in the court of R. Ismael in the far South on the border of Idumea.</i>. After [that person] left, Rebbi Ilai said to him, my teacher, is not Kefar Simai in the Land of Israel, closer to Sepphoris than to Acco? He answered, since the matter was resolved in a permissive way; it stands. What means “since the matter was resolved in a permissive way; it stands”? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, that if [the husband] came and protested, his protest would be invalid. But does a person who brings a bill of divorce from the Land of Israel for delivery in the Land of Israel have to say, “it was written and signed in my presence”? Therefore, there cannot be any difference whether he protested after she remarried or before she remarried and then she remarried<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">It does not depend on the declaration by the messenger; no bill of divorce delivered under the supervision of a court can be attacked.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Ada, Bar Athlay in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr in matters of bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira was looking at him<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Disapproving.</i>. He asked him, why do you look at me? Was it not said that practice follows the lenient opinion in rabbinic matters? They thought, that is a single opinion against a single opinion, but not a single opinion against the Sages, and here even a single opinion against the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that Acco is split in matters of divorce as it is split in matters of impurity, as explained later in the Halakhah.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya [bar] Abba said: If one sold a slave into Acco, the latter was set free<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 8a" href="/Gittin.8a">Babli, 8a</a>, he is quoted pointing out that R. Meïr declares all of Acco to be of the Land only for bills of divorce; therefore not for the laws of slaves. It is forbidden to sell slaves from the Land to places outside the land; any such sale frees the slave.</i>. Rebbi Ismael, the father of Rebbi Yudan, asked: Even from Acco to Acco? <sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is from <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.6">Notes 23–30</a>. Variant readings from there are noted <b>ש</b>. All indications are that the text in <i>Sevi‘it</i> is the original.</i> This follows what Rebbi Aḥa bar Jacob said in the name of Rebbi Immi: From two actions of Rebbi we learn that Acco is partly of the Land of Israel and partly outside the Land. Rebbi was in Acco when he saw them eating clean bread; he asked them, how did you knead [the dough]? They said to him, a student came here and instructed us that egg water does not prepare. We are boiling eggs and use their water to knead. They thought he was talking about cooking water of eggs when he spoke only of the eggs themselves. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said, at that moment they decreed that a student may not render decisions. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna: If a student gives instructions even according to practice, his instructions are no instructions.
It was stated: A student who gave instructions about practice in front of his teacher has committed a deadly sin. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: Nadab and Abihu died only because they determined practice in the presence of their teacher Moses. It happened that a student gave instructions about practice in front of his teacher Rebbi Eliezer. The latter said to his wife Imma Shalom: That one will not live out his week. The week was not completed when he died. His students said to him: Rebbi, you are a prophet. He said to them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 7:14" href="/Amos.7.14"><i>Amos</i> 7:14</a>) “I am neither a prophet nor the disciple of a prophet” but I have received a tradition that any student who gives instructions about practice in front of his teacher has committed a deadly sin. It was stated: A student is forbidden to give instructions about practice during the lifetime of his teacher unless he be at a distance of at least 12 <i>mil</i> from him, [the breadth of] the camp of Israel. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 33:49" href="/Numbers.33.49"><i>Num</i>. 33:49</a>) “They encamped along the Jordan from Bet Hayyešimot to Abel Haššiṭṭim in the plains of Moab”; how far is this? Twelve <i>mil</i>. Like this: Rebbi Tanḥum ben Jeremiah was in Ḥefer; they asked him and he gave instructions about practice. They said to him, did the Rabbi not teach us that a student may not give instructions about practice during the lifetime of his teacher unless he be at a distance of at least 12 <i>mil</i> from him, [the breadth of] the camp of Israel; and your teacher Rebbi Ḥana sits at Sepphoris! He said to them, so it should come over me that I did not know! From that moment on he did no longer give instructions.
Rebbi was in Acco when he saw a man on the rock. He said to him, “are you not the son of the Cohen X, was your father not a Cohen?” He said to him, “the eyes of my father were high, but he married a woman unfit for him and profaned this man.”
The villages in the territory of Sepphoris close to Acco, the villages in the territory of Acco close to Sepphoris, what do you do with them? Like Acco, like Sepphoris<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If practice follows R. Meïr, it does not make any difference in the rules of bills of divorce. Therefore, no answer is needed.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who brings a bill of divorce from the Land of Israel does not have to say: “It was written and signed before me.” If the document is contested, it should be confirmed by its signers<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The court at the place of origin can confirm that the signatures are genuine. A document bearing two certified signatures cannot be attacked. Since courts in the Land of Israel are usually competent, a bill of divorce which is not contested does not have to be certified.</i>. If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas and is unable to say: “it was written and signed before me,” it should be confirmed by its signers.<br>Bills of divorce and slaves’ bills of manumission are equal in [the requirement] about those who carry and bring<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">They have to be written and signed before the messenger when executed or delivered outside the Land, or have to be confirmed by a court before being delivered to a messenger.</i>. This is one of the aspects in which bills of divorce, have the same rules as bills of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This is an introduction to a series of Mishnaiot on the same subject. Since the slave of a Jew becomes a full member of the Jewish endogamous society upon his manumisson, strict rules are justified.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who brings a bill of divorce from the Land of Israel,” etc. Who protests? Rav Ḥisda said, the husband protests<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">It is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 9a" href="/Gittin.9a">Babli, 9a</a>, that in the absence of witnesses to fraud only the husband has standing to question the validity of a bill of divorce. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 9a:7:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.9a.7.2">Tosaphot 9a, <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. אלא</a>, read R. Yose’s statement in the argument of the Babli; this does not seem to be justified.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the buyers protest, lest she repossess from them<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband had real estate at the place of his wife which he sold and then sent a bill of divorce to his wife from far away without the payment due her upon divorce, the divorcee can reclaim any real estate sold after the date of her definitive marriage up to the value of the <i>ketubah</i> payment due her.</i>. Rebbi asked: So far a protest which does not concern the text of the bill<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., if the husband claims that he had attached a condition to the bill not provided for in the bill and that the condition was not satisfied. Since the condition was not presented to the court which oversaw the delivery of the document, it cannot be considered.</i>. A protest which concerns the text of the bill<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He claims that the entire bill is fake or that there is an intrinsic defect which makes the entire document invalid.</i> is in dispute between Rebbi Joḥanan and the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Their dispute is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.3.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 10:4 (Notes 97–102</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.2.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:2</a>. The Babylonian rabbis hold that a document whose signatures have been notarized as genuine by a court cannot be attacked.</i>. In the opinion of the rabbis there, there is no difference to a protest concerning or not concerning the body of the bill. <sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">A clause is missing here: “In the opinion of R. Joḥanan”.</i> A protest not concerning the text of the bill is void, a protest concerning the text of the bill is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A claim that a document is forged must always be investigated.</i>.
As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas which was not written and signed in his presence, he returns the bill to its place of origin, presents it to a court and has its signatures notarized. Then he does not have so say, ‘it was written and signed before me’, but he says, ‘I am deputized by the court.’ ”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Any document signed by a Samaritan is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since Samaritans, as a Sadducee sect, explain <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev</i>. 19:14</a> to mean that it is forbidden to put a stone in the path of a blind man, but not, as Pharisees do, as a prohibition to do damage to the inadvertent, there is no biblical prohibition for a Samaritan to sign false monetary documents as long as he does not swear or appears as witness in a court. The literal interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev</i>. 19:14</a> in Philo (<i>The Special Laws</i> IV, xxxviii, 198) is probably sufficient to characterize him as Alexandrian Sadducee. Bills of divorce or manumission become valid only by the signature of the witnesses (in contrast to a bond, where “the debtor’s signature is worth a hundred witnesses”); the Samaritan himself would be the guilty party in an adulterous remarriage by the wife served with a fake bill of divorce or the marriage of a slave with a free Jewish woman, forbidden to him as long as he is a slave. Since “those rules which they keep (including marriage taboos), the Samaritans keep more strictly than do the rabbinic Jews” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 10a" href="/Gittin.10a">Babli 10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:1:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.1.2-17">Yerushalmi <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:1</a>, 27b 1. 58), there is no reason to disqualify Samaritans from witnessing documents regarding marriage.</i>, except bills of divorce and bills of manumission. It happened that they brought before Rabban Gamliel at Kefar Othnay<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kafr ‘Uthnay</i>, a village near Megiddo on the Southern border of Jewish Galilee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:7</a>). While the Mishnah speaks only of <i>a Samaritan</i> witness, Rabban Gamliel accepted <i>two Samaritans</i>.</i> a bill of divorce whose witnesses were Samaritans and he declared it valid. All documents confirmed by a Gentile recorder’s office<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile court usually is ערכי, Greek τὰ ἀρχεῖα “public records, archives.” For ערכאות compare τὸ ἀρχεῖον “town hall”.</i> are valid, even if they are signed by Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">While Gentiles cannot be required to be honest, Gentile courts can be trusted to be jealous of their reputation.</i>, except bills of divorce and bills of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile court is unable to determine who can or cannot marry in Jewish law.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, all are valid<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">If executed by Jews and notarized by a Gentile court.</i>; they said it only when they were made by private persons.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Any document signed by a Samaritan is invalid,” etc. They are suspected about money; about money they were disqualified.They are not suspected about incest and adultery. And testimony in capital criminal cases has the same status as testimony about incest and adultery. Then even if both of them are Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Why does the Mishnah mention only a single Samaritan witness?</i>? There is a difference, for they are not conversant with the fine points of bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If both witnesses are Samaritans, one has to assume that the scribe also was Samaritan and that the rabbinic rules of writing a bill of divorce were not followed. A bill of divorce can be written only on the explicit demand of the husband; an infraction of this rule cannot be discovered by examination of the bill.</i>. Then even one Samaritan witness should be disqualified! Rebbi Abin said, explain it if a Jew signed last<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">He would not sign if the bill was invalid. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 10a" href="/Gittin.10a">Babli, 10a</a>, considers this to be R. Eleazar’s authoritative opinion (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.7">Note 51</a>).</i>. Rebbi Yose said, that is according to the opinion that the witnesses may sign separately, not in the presence of each other. But according to him who says that witnesses may sign only in the presence of each other<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 10b" href="/Gittin.10b">Babli, 10b</a>, for bills of divorce and manumission only.</i>, it is valid even if the Samaritan signed last.
If a bond was signed by four witnesses and it turned out that the first two were relatives or disqualified, it is valid and is confirmed by the remaining testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text (5 signatures, 3 of them invalid) is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta 7:11</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 162b" href="/Bava_Batra.162b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 162b</a>. In the text here, “disqualified” are people who cannot be admitted to be witnesses in money matters, such as Samaritans, gamblers, and fences. Relatives of either lender or borrower are disqualified for the particular document in question. Two of the signatures must belong to witnesses who can certify the document in court.</i>. Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Yasa: This follows him who says that witnesses sign only in the presence of each other. But following him who says that witnesses may sign without being in the presence of each other, even if the Jew signed last it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the problem discussed in the preceding paragraph about a bill signed by a Samaritan and a Jew.</i>. And would it not be remote testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7">Tosephta 7</a>:11 notes that a document whose signatures are so far removed from the text of the document that a sentence could have been added between text and signatures is invalid; it might be that there was a different text and the witnesses signed to that. In practice, this means that exactly one line must be empty between the text and the first signatures. If the qualified witnesses signed two lines below the text, it is admissible to have two unqualified witnesses sign on the line above to fill in the space between the document text and the signatures of the witnesses.</i>? That one cannot say since Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This means, R. Yasa in the name of Rav Jeremiah (who in the Yerushalmi frequently has the title of Rebbi, as a Babylonian authority predating the creation of the Title of “Rav”). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.7.2-4">Halakhah 9:8</a>: R. Abba in the name of Rav Jeremiah. Note the pure Babylonian Aramaic spelling דקאמר.</i> that disqualified witnesses do not constitute remote testimony since they came only to support the document’s validity<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 18b" href="/Gittin.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, invalid signatures do not diminish the validity of a document if the signatories were invited to sign for any reason other than being witnesses. This probably is understood here also. In particular, it was customary to have relatives sign on all marriage documents. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 162b" href="/Bava_Batra.162b"><i>Baba batra</i> 162b</a>, the statement of R. Yasa is quoted in the name of Ḥizqiah. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:10:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.10.2-6">Halakhot 8:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.7.2-4">9:8</a>.</i>.
Why are Samaritans disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Why does one not intermarry with Samaritans?</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, because they are lions’ proselytes<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The originally Gentile part of the population of Samaria adopted the worship “of the local god” because they were attacked by lions, <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 17:24-41" href="/II_Kings.17.24-41"><i>2K</i>. 17:24–41</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 75b" href="/Kiddushin.75b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 75b</a>, this is identified as the teaching of R. Ismael.</i>. But if somebody converted not for Heaven’s sake and then converted for Heaven’s sake, does one not accept him<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">That argument may have had validity in the first few generations after the destruction of Samaria, but in talmudic times the Samaritans had been monotheists for at least 700 years.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.6.2"><i>Jebamot</i> 7:6 (Note 129</a>), “R. Ismael”. In the Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., identified as teaching of R. Aqiba.</i>: Because the child is a bastard if a Gentile or a slave have intercourse with a Jewish woman<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that the deportees from Cutha and Media who were resettled in Samaria were mostly male. When they intermarried with local Jewish women, in this opinion the children were all bastards.</i>. But did not Rebbi Aqiba say, they are genuine proselytes<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the preceding opinion were generally accepted, which it is not, it would be irrelevant concerning Samaritans. Agreed to in the Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>.</i>? Because they require levirate marriage from the preliminarily married and free the definitively married<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6.2"><i>Jebamot</i> 1, Notes 192–196</a>. Pharisaic tradition frees the preliminarily married woman and obligates the definitively married one. The Samaritan ruling had its partisans among the rabbinic school of Shammai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6.2"><i>Jebamot</i> 1:6, Note 193</a>.)</i>. But do not the rabbis say, there is no bastard from a sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Even if a childless widow flouts the rules and marries an unrelated man without <i>ḥalîṣah</i>, the child is not a bastard and can marry in the congregation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:15:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.15.1">Mishnah <i>Jebamot</i> 4:15</a>). Therefore even a Samaritan who is the offspring of a rabbinically forbidden marriage should be an acceptable marriage partner.</i>? Because they are not conversant with the fine points in writing bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">There could be women divorced according to Samaritan rules who would not be considered divorced by rabbinic rules and, therefore, their children in a second marriage would be bastards not eligible for marriage with Jews.</i>. But does not Rabban Gamliel accept their bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kafr ‘Uthnay</i>, a village near Megiddo on the Southern border of Jewish Galilee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:7</a>). While the Mishnah speaks only of <i>a Samaritan</i> witness, Rabban Gamliel accepted <i>two Samaritans</i>.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because they intermingled with the priests of the High Places: “He chose priests from the borderline of the people,<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:31" href="/I_Kings.12.31"><i>1K</i>. 12:31</a>. The root קצה “to be distant, of the elite” of מקצת  is identified with קוץ, קצץ “to cut, to chop off”, one of whose derivatives is קוֹץ “thorn”. (The same explanation is in the Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., of R. Joḥanan following R. Ismael.) In any case, there is no reason to exclude marriages with Samaritans other than general convention; but cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.3"><i>Demay</i> 3:4, Note 98</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Ila said, from the “thorns” of the people, i. e., from the disqualified of the people.
Rebbi Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Later in the paragraph he is quoted as R. Jacob bar Aḥa. The latter attribution is correct since R. Jacob bar Aḥa was one of the teachers of R. Ze‘ira quoted later, while R. Aḥa lived after R. Ze‘ira.</i> said, the acts of a Gentile court are public knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If a document of indebtedness is not valid as a court document, it will be valid as being witnessed in public.</i>. Then even if both [witnesses] are Samaritan? Did one not say that they are not conversant with the fine points in writing bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">There could be women divorced according to Samaritan rules who would not be considered divorced by rabbinic rules and, therefore, their children in a second marriage would be bastards not eligible for marriage with Jews.</i>? But Rebbi Simeon validates their bills of divorce! Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: It turns out that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Eleazar. Just as Rebbi Eleazar said, even if no witnesses signed on it, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.3.1">Mishnah 9:4</a>. R. Eleazar (the Tanna, ben Shamua‘) holds that the essence of divorce is the delivery of the bill of divorce to the wife (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>). Therefore, he requires that the delivery be certified by two witnesses in good standing. They have to verify that the document contains the language which makes it a bill of divorce from the specified husband to the specified wife and that it was delivered into the hands of the wife either by the husband or by his duly appointed agent. Any signatures on the document are irrelevant. By contrast, R. Meïr holds that a bill of divorce not certified by two witnesses is invalid.</i>, so Rebbi Simeon said, even if no witnesses signed on it, it is valid. But then even if it was made by private persons<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Why does R. Simeon in the Mishnah invalidate bills of divorce signed by Gentiles not in a court of law?</i>? This shows that it was necessary that Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, the acts of a Gentile court are public knowledge. A bond<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A document of indebtedness, containing a mortgage clause.</i> was executed in Bet Shean<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile city in Mishnaic times.</i>, whose witnesses were Gentiles. Rebbi Yose says, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">ריש לקיש is the Babylonian version of ר׳ שמעון בן לקיש.</i> disagree, one said it is invalid, the other said it is valid. Rebbi Abbahu explained: Rebbi Joḥanan said it is invalid, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said it is valid. What is Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s reason? That a Jew should not lose money<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">That the creditor should be able to enforce the terms of the loan in a rabbinic court.</i>. But if that one would not lose money, the other would lose money<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Any gain of the lender is a loss for the borrower if both parties to the bond were Jewish.</i>! Rebbi Yudan said, it must be in order not to close the door before people, for tomorrow one would look to borrow but does not succeed<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">If mortgages could not be foreclosed, nobody would lend money and all economic activity would come to a standstill. Therefore, R. Joḥanan’s opinion cannot be considered even though he can point to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 144:8" href="/Psalms.144.8"><i>Ps</i>. 144:8</a>,11 invalidating Gentile witnesses.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one says, “give this bill of divorce to my wife or this document of manumission to my slave,” if he wants to change his mind in either case he can retract<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Before the delivery of the bill or the document. The husband can appoint an agent for delivery of the document; he cannot appoint the agent as receiver of the document on behalf of wife or slave, as explained in the Mishnah.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, only for women’s bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">It is held that any divorce other than one which the wife can enforce in court is to the wife’s detriment.</i> but not for slaves’ documents of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages hold that for a slave the benefits of freedom (and with it the status of a full Jew) always outweigh any possible material detriment attached to manumission.</i> because one may bestow benefit on a person in his absence but put a detriment on him only in his presence; for if one chooses not to sustain his slave, he has the right not to do so, but not to sustain his wife he has no right. He said to them, but he<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband or owner is a Cohen, all members of his household share in his sanctified food; unless the household members are born of priestly status they lose the access to sanctified food the moment they leave his household. In contrast to Roman law, a freedman is not part of his patron’s <i>familia</i>.</i> disqualifies his slave for heave the same way he disqualifies his wife! They answered him, because he is his property<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The slave’s ability to eat heave is not intrinsic; it is derivative from his servile status. The slave can eat heave even if his master, the Cohen, refuses to support him.</i>. If one says, “give this bill of divorce to my wife or this document of manumission to my slave” and dies, they shall not be delivered after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages who do not permit retraction of a document of manumission agree that both divorce and manumission become effective only at the moment of delivery of the document into the hands of the recipient by the agent representing his employer. But a dead person can neither divorce nor manumit. In Chapter 7 it is explained how a terminally ill childless person can divorce his wife to spare her (or prevent her from entering) a levirate marriage to his brother without divorcing her in case he recovers from his sickness.</i>; “give this <i>mina</i> to Mr. X” and dies, they shall deliver it after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">If the gift was given when the giver was aware of his impending death, the heirs are bound to respect the wishes of the deceased.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If one says, “give this bill of divorce to my wife,” etc. Elsewhere you say that “give” means “bring”, but here you say that “give” means “acquire”<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">If the messenger was appointed only as an agent for delivery, there would be no question that the sender has the right to retract his instructions. It does not seem reasonable to assume that a charge to deliver something lets the addressee acquire an interest in the document to be delivered at the moment it is given to the agent since “a person’s agent represents him in every respect” [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a> (62a 1.41), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 43a" href="/Gittin.43a">Babli 43a</a>]. Since a person can do what he wishes with a document still in his possession, why can he not do the same with the document in his agent’s hand?</i>! So is<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">It is not so, but must be interpreted in this way. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 11b" href="/Gittin.11b">Babli, 11b</a>, R. Jeremiah is reported to hold that “give to” really means “accept for”.</i> the Mishnah: “Acquire this bill of divorce for my wife; acquire this document of manumission for my slave.” The formulation<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">לשם is dialectal version of לשן, Aramaic equivalent of Hebrew לשון, cf. S. Lieberman, לשם לשן <i>Tarbiz</i> 6 (1935), p. 235; J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנסח המשנה<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>, Jerusalem-Tel Aviv 1964, p. 475.</i> of the Mishnah says so: “Because one may bestow benefit on a person in his absence<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">This argument would be futile if the owner had not bestowed freedom on his slave by handing the document to the messenger. Therefore, the rule of the Mishnah applies only if the owner had given some indication that the messenger also acted as the slave’s agent.</i> but put a detriment on a person only in his presence.” Rebbi Meïr says, it is a detriment for either of them, but the Sages say it is a benefit for the slave but a detriment for the wife. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Think of it, if he was the slave of a rich person, would it not be a detriment for the slave<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Who as a free man probably never would eat as well as he did as a slave.</i>?
