The colleagues said, Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Think of it, if she was the wife of a man suffering from boils<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">In which case the wife can force a divorce, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.8.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:10</a>.</i>, would it not be a benefit for the woman? You have only the following: If one sells his slave without the latter’s knowledge, is the sale of the slave not valid<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the slave can be manumitted without his consent and the owner can appoint the messenger as the slave’s agent without that latter’s knowledge.</i>? If one divorces his wife without her knowledge, is the divorce valid<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody can represent the wife without her consent; even if the husband would appoint a person to be his wife’s agent, the appointment would be invalid and the delivery of the bill of divorce by the agent to the wife would be illegal.</i> because she<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">That is the ms. version, copied in <i>editio princeps</i>. But it seems preferable to follow the commentators and read this clause as a quote from the Mishnah מִפּנֵי <b>שֶׁהוּא</b> קִנְייָנוֹ “because he (the slave) is his property”, asking what is the relevance of this fact to the problem in hand, i. e., whether manumission is a benefit or a detriment to the slave.</i> was acquired by him? Rebbi Abin said, he is his property but he disqualifies him for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Cohen owner can sell his slave to an Israel any time he wishes, the slave has no intrinsic right to heave that would be eliminated by the document of manumission. This explanation is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 13a" href="/Gittin.13a">Babli, 13a</a>, in the name of Rava (Rav Abba bar Rav Josef bar Ḥama).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> also appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:8:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.8.2-6"><i>Baba Batra</i> 8:9</a>, 16c.</i>“If somebody says, I freed my slave Ṭabi<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">For Ṭabi (m.), Ṭabitha (f.) as names of slaves cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.1.4.4"><i>Niddah</i> 1:5, Note 103</a>.</i>, I shall free him, he is free, he acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The slave acquired the right to be freed.</i>.” Rebbi Inaia<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba Batra:</i> R. Ḥiyya.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: But only by a document<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The slave cannot acquire freedom by the simple declaration of his owner; he only can acquire the right to a document of manumission.</i>. “He should be freed<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The owner said that the slave should be freed, but did not say it in the context of a last will and testament. The Sages hold that this is a not binding promise; Rebbi holds that this kind of promise is binding.</i>, Rebbi says, he acquired, but for the Sages he did not acquire. Give this document of manumission, and he died, Rebbi says, he did not acquire but the Sages say, he did acquire; one forces the heirs to fulfill the instructions of the deceased.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the owner who gave instructions to write a document for his slave.</i> if he did not specify. How do we hold? If he said, “free him”, Rebbi will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heirs are required to follow the wishes of the testator, they have to free the slave.</i>. If he said, “write and deliver,” the Sages will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case of the Mishnah; if the owner instructed to write the document and deliver it in his name, the instruction becomes void at his death.</i>. But we deal with the case that he said “give”. Rebbi says, one who says “give” is like one who said “write and deliver,” but the Sages say, one who says “give” is like one who said “free him”.
<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).8.1">Tosephta <i>Baba Batra</i> 8:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.7.2-4">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Batra</i> 8:8 16b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 138a" href="/Bava_Batra.138a">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 138a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 39b" href="/Chullin.39b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 39b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 24b" href="/Keritot.24b"><i>Keritut</i> 24b</a>.</i>“If somebody said that all his property should be given to X, a Cohen, and [the estate] contained slaves, even if [the Cohen] said ‘I cannot have them’ they eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Since the transfer of possession is automatic, the slaves belong to the Cohen until he disposes of them.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, since he said ‘I cannot have them’, the heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The legal heirs who would have inherited in the absence of a will.</i> acquired them.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they disagree if he<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The recipient.</i> did not specify. How do we hold? If he accepted at the first moment, Rabban Gamliel will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If the Cohen was owner of the slaves for one moment, he cannot dispose of them except by sale or manumission.</i>. If he did refuse at the first moment, the rabbis will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody is forced to accept a bequest against his will.</i>. But we deal with the case that he did not specify. Rebbi says, nobody says ‘it is impossible’ unless he had accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">And had time to inspect what he received.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, since he said ‘it is impossible’, the end is proof for the start<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was noncommittal at the start, his later rejection has retroactive power. The same explanation is given in the Babli in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>.
“If somebody says, give this <i>mina</i> to X because I owe it to him, bring this <i>mina</i> to X for the pledge which he has from me, if he wants to change his mind he cannot do so but he himself is responsible until X receives his due<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">While the agent can accept the payment for the creditor (which is to the creditor’s benefit), the debt is not paid until the creditor receives it; without the creditor’s instructions the responsibility for the money cannot be transferred to the creditor since that would be a detriment; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 14a" href="/Gittin.14a">Babli 14a</a>, Samuel disputes the ruling and holds that there is no acquisition without consent; the possibility of a detriment invalidates the benefit.</i>.” Rebbi Ila said, a gift is like a debt. <sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta 1:7</a>.</i>“Give this <i>mina</i> to X, bring this <i>mina</i> to X, give this gift document to X, if he wants to change his mind he can(not)<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">“Not” is in the ms. but not in the Tosephta. All Medieval authors who quote this Yerushalmi mention it in the version of the Tosephta: <i>Sefer Miṣwot Gadol</i> 2, 161d, #82; Rashba <i>ad</i> 14a, Tosaphot haRosh <i>ad</i> 13a, <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. והוא (col. 99 in the edition of H. B. Ravitz, Jerusalem 2004). Aviezri (Eliezer ben Joel, Ravia) is quoted in Mordechai <i>Giṭṭin</i> 532 as having seen in one Tosephta ms. the reading “cannot”; he rejects that since then the Tosephta could have been formulated together with the following one. This argument is accepted by S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah Giṭṭin</i> p. 793, but it does not seem conclusive since the treatment of the case that the recipient died is different in the two cases. Therefore, the reading לא should be provided with a question mark but cannot be rejected out of hand.</i> do so. If he went and found that X had died, he should return it to the sender<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Not deliver to the recipient’s heirs since it was not intended for them.</i>, and if that one had died he should give it to the latter’s heirs.” <sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>.</i>“Acquire this <i>mina</i> for X, accept this mina for X, acquire this gift document for X, accept this gift document for X, if he wants to change his mind he cannot do so. If he went and found that X had died, he should deliver it to his heirs. But if he acquired after [X’s] death<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">If it turned out that the recipient was already dead at the time the messenger was intended to become an agent for him, there was no transaction since nobody can become an agent for a dead person. The messenger was not appointed as agent for the deceased’s estate.</i> he should return it to the sender since nobody can acquire documents after his death.” <sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">If it turned out that the recipient was already dead at the time the messenger was intended to become an agent for him, there was no transaction since nobody can become an agent for a dead person. The messenger was not appointed as agent for the deceased’s estate.</i>“If somebody says, this <i>mina</i> is for X, take this <i>mina</i> for X, this <i>mina</i> shall be in your hand for X, if he wants to change his mind he cannot do so” because if somebody acquires for another person it cannot be changed.
<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel, in somewhat better shape, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.2-4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3</a>, 64b 1. 37. The readings of the Leiden ms. are given by <b>ק</b>, those of one half line in a Geniza fragment (<i>Qiddušin</i>) by <b>ג</b>,</i> Explanation: Rebbi Dositheos ben Rebbi Yannai and Rebbi Yose ben Kipper descended<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">“To descend” everywhere means: To travel from Galilee to Babylonia.</i> to collect there for a colleague<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel story in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 14" href="/Gittin.14">Babli, 14a/b</a>, R. Aḥai ben R. Joshia asked them to bring him a silver vessel which was owed to him in Nahardea. According to the story in <i>Qiddušin</i>, they went to collect money for the Galilean Academy.</i> when they were slandered<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">After they had collected the debt, a rumor was spread that they were dishonest.</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The debtors, afraid of losing their money, being responsible if it was stolen by the messengers.</i> came and wanted to take it back from them. They answered, we already acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are empowered by the creditor, the money became the creditor’s the moment it was given to us.</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The debtors, afraid of losing their money, being responsible if it was stolen by the messengers.</i> said, we wish for you to accept it in good faith<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">They wanted a declaration by the rabbis that they would be responsible for any loss in transit.</i>; they answered, we are unpaid trustees<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">An unpaid trustee is only responsible for losses incurred because of his negligence but not for losses through robbery or other events beyond his control (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.5.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 7:8</a>).</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The debtors, afraid of losing their money, being responsible if it was stolen by the messengers.</i> approached Rebbi Dositheos ben Rebbi Yannai who told them, there it is, all of it<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">He returned the money, against the rules.</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The debtors, afraid of losing their money, being responsible if it was stolen by the messengers.</i> took Rebbi Yose ben Kipper, whipped him with ropes, and took it<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">The part of the money which he had.</i> from him. When they returned here, he<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben Kipper went to complain to R. Yannai.</i> went to his father and told him, look what your son did to me! He asked him, what did he do to you? He answered, if he had taken my position, they could not have taken anything from us. He asked him<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yannai interrogated his son Dositheos.</i>, why did you act in such a way? He said, I saw that they were a unanimous court,<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">They had made up their minds; it was impossible to argue with them.</i> their hats were a cubit wide<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps wide-rimmed hats were the uniform of violent people.</i>, they were directing blows, my brother Yose was bound<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Following the text in <i>Qiddušin</i>. It seems that the word פטרוי, which in other contexts means “they freed him”, is a scribal corruption and should be removed from the dictionaries. Before correction, the scribe wrote ופרכוי “and they forced him”, which may be correct.</i> and the whip ascended and descended. I said, does my father have another Dositheos<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">He was afraid for his life; this justifies his breaking the rules.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, you say that for somebody who cannot give a warranty in good faith. But if somebody gives a warranty in good faith, one takes from this one and gives to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">If the messenger is empowered to sign a receipt for the money which shields the debtor from any claim of the creditor’s under any circumstances, he cannot ask the money back.</i>.
If somebody forgave a bond to another person. Rebbi Ḥanania and Rebbi Mamal: one said it is forgiven<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">An oral declaration by which the creditor engages himself not to claim a debt is valid without any act of acquisition by the debtor. This is the decision of <i>Šulḥan ‘Arukh Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> Chapter 12 §8, based on inferences from the Babli.</i>; the other said it is not forgiven unless he handed over the bond<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor has to take possession of the bond directly or by an indirect act of acquisition.</i>.
“ ‘Give this <i>mina</i> to X,’ then he died. If the heirs want to hinder [the delivery] they are powerless. It is unnecessary to say that it is so if he said, acquire for him, if he said, accept by my orders.<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">In a slightly different wording, this is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta 1:9</a>.</i>” Rebbi Abba barMamal said, this <i>baraita</i> refers to a sick person. If he is sick, does he not have to say: acquire for him, accept for him? Rebbi Mana said, I confirmed this by what Rebbi Abba bar Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: They treated verbal instructions by a sick person as if they were written and delivered<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 13a" href="/Gittin.13a">Babli (13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 15a" href="/Gittin.15a">15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 121a" href="/Bava_Batra.121a"><i>Baba Batra</i> 121a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175a" href="/Bava_Batra.175a">175a</a>) this is a statement of Rav Naḥman, universally accepted. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 11:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.11.1.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 11:1, Note 22</a>.</i>. But only if he died from that sickness, not if he recovered, he did not have to say: acquire for him, accept for him.
“ ‘Give this <i>mina</i> to Mr. X’ and he dies, they shall deliver it after his death”<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">If the gift was given when the giver was aware of his impending death, the heirs are bound to respect the wishes of the deceased.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The last clause of the Mishnah is essentially identical with the Tosephta discussed in the preceding paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: The Mishnah refers to a sick person.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas and says, “it was written before me,” but not “it was signed before me,” or “it was signed before me” but not “it was written before me”, “it was written before me but signed only partially before me”, “it was partially written before me but signed completely before me”, it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> is not followed.</i>. If one said “it was written before me” and another “it was signed before me”, it is invalid; if two people say, “it was written before us” but only one<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A third person.</i> says, “it was signed before me,” it is invalid; but Rebbi Jehudah declares it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah declares a bill of divorce valid if one witness testifies to the writing and one to the signing; he does not require that both be the same person.</i>. If one says, “it was written before me” but two say, “it was signed before us,” it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Any document is valid which is witnessed by two persons whose signatures are validated by two witnesses.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas,” etc. One understands “it was written before me” but not “it was signed before me”, but “it was signed before me” but not “it was written before me”? Is not a bill of divorce validated by its signatures? That must follow Rebbi Jehudah, for Rebbi Jehudah invalidates forms<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>, the anonymous majority permit the routine production of forms of bills of divorce where everything is written in advance and only the names of husband and wife, the date, and the statement of divorce are then inserted for the particular couple involved in the divorce; but R. Jehudah holds that the requirement that “he write for her” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>) can only be fulfilled if the bill was written specifically for that wife from the first letter to the last.</i>. Following Rebbi Jehudah outside the Land as for one who divorces in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.2-4">Halakhah 3:2</a>, practice is declared as following R. Jehudah.</i>. Are there forms of bills of divorce outside the Land for divorces outside the Land? It is necessary for him to know that it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the agent is to be interviewed about the writing of the bill, it is obvious that he could not answer if the bill was a form in which only the names of husband and wife and the time were inserted. Even the anonymous majority of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>:2 must agree that forms are possible only in the Land.</i> and that he be appointed as an agent; he would not know that it was a bill of divorce unless it was written completely before him. Rebbi Ḥanin stated: It is permitted that he enter and leave<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In all matters of supervision, it is possible for the supervisor to leave the place occasionally as long as he can return at any moment and the person to be supervised, in this case the scribe, does not know in advance when the supervisor will return. The same <i>baraita</i> is accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 6a" href="/Gittin.6a">Babli, 6a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked: If he said, “it was written and signed before me, but it was in the sole possession of the husband between writing and signing”<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the meantime, the husband could have substituted an invalid document for the valid one which was written in the presence of the agent.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “If one said ‘it was written before me’ and another ‘it was signed before me’, it is invalid.” That is, because one said, “it was written before me” and another, “it was signed before me”; this implies<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the case is not mentioned in the Mishnah; neither here nor in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a> is there any mention that the document has to be seen by the agent between writing and signing.</i> that if one said, “it was written and signed before me, but it was in the sole possession of the husband between writing and signing”, it is valid. Rav Ḥisda asked: half of an institution and half as biblical decree<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement that the bill be written specifically for that wife is biblical, the requirement that the agent be present at writing and signing is purely rabbinical. It was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.2">Halakhah 1:1 (Note 14</a>) that the husband is not suspected of doing anything which would make the divorce recognized by the court when it is invalid in the eyes of Heaven (since then he would commit a sin without hurting his ex-wife). Therefore, the answer given to R. Yose’s question is reasonable.</i>? If he said, “it was written and signed before me, but it was in the sole possession of the husband between writing and signing”? Let us hear from the following: “If one said ‘it was written before me’ and another ‘it was signed before me’, it is invalid.” That is, because one said “it was written before me” and another “it was signed before me”; this implies that if one said, “it was written and signed before me, but it was in the sole possession of the husband for an hour,” it is valid.
Rebbi Eleazar said, Rebbi Abun asked: If he said “it was written and one witness signed before me,” and two<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Two other witnesses.</i> testify about the signature of the second witness. Rebbi Immi the Babylonian said, nothing can be more valid than this. Rebbi Abba did not say<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin’s question was trivial.</i> so but: If he said “it was written and one witness signed before me, and I am the second witness.” He is like a party interested in his testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">He becomes party and can no longer be a witness. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 15b" href="/Gittin.15b">Babli, 15b</a>, the reason is given that a bill of divorce can be validated either as a document, by the independent testimony about the signature of the witnesses, or by the special rule of divorces which allows an agent to declare that it was written and signed before him, but not by a combination of both because then the witness would validate himself.</i>.
If there were four signatures and he said, “it was written and signed by two witnesses before me; I do not know anything about the other two.” That is the disagreement of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, who disagreed: If he said to ten people, sign the bill of divorce, and some signed on that day and some the next day. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is valid, for the remainder are only because of the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The first two witnesses validate the document as a bill of divorce The others only sign because he made a condition that the bill be valid only with ten signatures, to make a public statement that he would no longer be responsible for his wife’s debts.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said it is invalid unless all sign on the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot split the conditions; all ten signatures are needed to validate and, therefore, must be affixed on the date specified in the document. Otherwise the document would be invalid as predated.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 18b" href="/Gittin.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, the attributions are switched. This means that the Babli, which always prefers R. Joḥanan to R. Simeon ben Laqish, follows what is declared here to be R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion. The Yerushalmi does not decide between the opinions.</i>.
Rebbi Immi said, where do they disagree? If the document is presented by him personally. But if it is presented by another person, even the rabbis will agree that it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the clause in the Mishnah where two people say that the bill was written in their presence and a third person testifies that it was signed in his presence. R. Immi holds that the rabbis declare the bill invalid if it is presented by the single person who saw the signing, whose testimony is incomplete, whereas R. Jehudah accepts the bill as valid if two people testify to any state of the writing of the bill. But if the bill was delivered by one of the witnesses to the writing, then he and the witness to the signing are two and the rabbis will agree that two messengers who bring a bill of divorce do not have to declare that it was written and signed before them.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah agrees with Rebbi Immi. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, where do they disagree? If the document is presented by another person. But if it is presented by him personally, even Rebbi Jehudah will agree that it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Ze‘ira, R. Jehudah agrees that the bill is invalid if writing and signing are attested to by different persons. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 17a" href="/Gittin.17a">Babli, 17a</a>, both opinions are recorded in the name of R. Immi, held at different times.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira objected to Rebbi Immi: If the document is presented by him personally, does Rebbi Jehudah declare it valid even in the first case<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, the bill is unanimously declared invalid if writing and signing are attested to by different persons. One has to assume that R. Jehudah agrees.</i>? Rebbi Immi brought his father-in-law Rebbi Jonah who stated for him: If one said “it was written before me” and another “it was signed before me”, it is invalid, but Rebbi Jehudah declares it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> shows that R. Jehudah accepts the bill if different people testify to writing and signing, showing that R. Ze‘ira’s objection is unjustified.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, the formulation of a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze‘ira: <i>In that case</i>, Rebbi Jehudah declares it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>; only in one case does R. Jehudah accept the bill as genuine.</i>. But it is difficult following Rebbi Ze‘ira: If the document is presented by another person, that is when they do not treat the messenger as two. When do they treat him as two? When he testifies to both writing and signing<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since in that case, any later protest by the husband is disregarded (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.2">Chapter 1, Note 9</a>), it must be that the messenger who testifies to both writing and signing of the bill has the status of two witnesses whose testimony cannot be attacked.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it is presented by him personally<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last clause of the Mishnah, where one witness has seen the writing and two the signing. The singular implies that the person presenting the bill of divorce is alone; he is the person attesting to the writing.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira was looking at him<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Disapprovingly.</i>. He asked, why are you looking at me? Even if it is presented by another person<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">He changed his mind and accepts that two witnesses to the signature make any other witnesses superfluous.</i>. It meansthat Rebbi Yasa before he changed his mind followed Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, even after he changed his mind it can be explained following him. There is a difference between two witnesses to the writing and two witnesses to the signing. Signing before two [witnesses] is empowered<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Any document is confirmed by two signatures if the latter can be notarized in court.</i>, writing before two is not empowered<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Except for bills of divorce, the circumstances of writing a document are irrelevant.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it was written during daytime and signed during daytime, in the night and signed in the night, in the night and signed during daytime, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jewish calendar day starts at nightfall, all these documents are signed on the day indicated as date in it.</i>. During daytime and signed in the night it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since every night belongs to the next day in the calendar, the document is predated.</i>. Rebbi Simeon declares it valid since Rebbi Simeon says that all documents which were written during daytime and signed at night are invalid except for women’s bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the date of the writing of a divorce document has no monetary consequences; the duty of the husband to pay the <i>ketubah</i> and to return the dowry to his wife starts with the date of delivery of the bill.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If it was written during daytime and signed during daytime,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is invalid because of the usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the husband’s right to the yield of his wife’s property ends with the signing of the bill of divorce, not with its delivery. A predated bill of divorce is a predated document about money and, therefore, invalid automatically.<br>The Babylonian theory, given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.3.2-4">Halakhah 4:3</a>, requires a date as a matter of criminal law. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 17" href="/Gittin.17">Babli, 17a/b</a>, both opinions are attributed either to R. Joḥanan or to R. Simeon ben Laqish; cf. <i>Sefer Haʻiṭṭur</i> 1, 6d, Note 26.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish told him, then you should also be suspicious of the bills of divorce brought from overseas; maybe they were written during daytime and signed in the night<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The messenger has to testify that he was present at the writing and signing of the bill, so he can be asked whether the document was written and signed specifically for the woman to be divorced. Why does he not have to testify that it was signed on the date of writing?</i>! He answered him, there the forgery is intrinsic<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If the bill was not written specifically for the woman for whom it is intended, it is worthless.</i>, here it is extrinsic<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the writing and the signing are done for the correct purpose, the document is executed correctly. It becomes unenforceable by an outside circumstance.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon stated this only for the night; but on the next day even Rebbi Simeon will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon accepts documents from a Gentile court (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>), he must accept Gentile dating for which the night follows the day. But the next day is a different date even in the Gentile calendar.</i>. But Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, there is no difference, whether night, or the next day, or after many days<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that for R. Simeon the date of a bill of divorce is irrelevant.</i>. What is the next day? Rebbi Ḥanania and Rebbi Mana, one said the next day, the other said the day after the next<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is difficult to understand.</i>. The statement of Rebbi Joḥanan about Rebbi Simeon (ben Laqish)<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, “ben Laqish” is a copyist’s error since the reference is to the Tanna, ben Ioḥai. Cf. <i>Sefer Haʻiṭṭur</i> 7c, Note 62.</i> is difficult! <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This is a quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.4.5">Chapter 1:5, Note 132</a>.</i> So says Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: It turns out that Rebbi Simeon (ben Laqish)<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, “ben Laqish” is a copyist’s error since the reference is to the Tanna, ben Ioḥai. Cf. <i>Sefer Haʻiṭṭur</i> 7c, Note 62.</i> agrees with Rebbi Eleazar. Just as Rebbi Eleazar said, even if there are no witnesses signed on it, it is valid, so Rebbi Simeon said, even if there are no witnesses signed on it, it is valid. Rebbi Samuel, the brother of Rebbi Berekhiah, said: Here, if he wants the document to be signed, there if she does not want the document to be signed. If he said to ten people, sign the bill of divorce, and some signed today and some the next day. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is valid for the remainder [are signing] only because of the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The first two witnesses validate the document as a bill of divorce The others only sign because he made a condition that the bill be valid only with ten signatures, to make a public statement that he would no longer be responsible for his wife’s debts.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said it is invalid unless all sign on the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot split the conditions; all ten signatures are needed to validate and, therefore, must be affixed on the date specified in the document. Otherwise the document would be invalid as predated.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 18b" href="/Gittin.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, the attributions are switched. This means that the Babli, which always prefers R. Joḥanan to R. Simeon ben Laqish, follows what is declared here to be R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion. The Yerushalmi does not decide between the opinions.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: There was a case that one said to ten people, sign the bill of divorce, and some signed on that day and some the next day. The case came before the rabbis who reluctantly declared it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">42,</sup><i class="footnote">The case is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 18b" href="/Gittin.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, as coming before R. Joshua ben Levi's court where the latter declared that "R. Simeon is important enough that one may follow him in an emergency"; the husband had disappeared and it was impossible to obtain a replacement bill from him.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">From here on there exists a Geniza fragment whose readings are given by ג</i>. This supports Rebbi Joḥanan from the position of the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since they permitted a bill signed on two different dates only in order to save the wife from being married to a husband who had disappeared, they agree with him that his rule represents the normal case.</i> and disagrees with Rebbi Simeon. This supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish from the position of Rebbi Simeon and disagrees with the rabbis.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One writes with anything, with ink, with vermilion, with gum, and with copper sulfate<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since they permitted a bill signed on two different dates only in order to save the wife from being married to a husband who had disappeared, they agree with him that his rule represents the normal case.</i>, and anything that leaves a record. One does not write with drinks, or fruit juice, or anything not permanent. One writes on anything, on a leaf of the olive tree, on a cow’s horn if one delivers the cow to her<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is paralleled in <i>Šabbat</i> 12:5 (13d 1. 29); the readings from there are noted <b>ש</b>.</i>, on a slave’s hand if one delivers the slave to her. Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, one does not write on anything living nor on food.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One writes with anything,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is paralleled in <i>Šabbat</i> 12:5 (13d 1. 29); the readings from there are noted <b>ש</b>.</i>“He shall write<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>: “He shall write her a scroll of divorce and deliver it into her hand.”</i>”, not engrave. “He shall write”, not dropping points. “He shall write”, not pouring. “He shall write”, not engrave; some Tannaïm state, he even may engrave. Rav Ḥisda<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20a" href="/Gittin.20a">Babli, 20a</a>, this statement of the Babylonian Rav Ḥisda is in the name of the Galilean R. Eleazar.</i> said, he who says not to engrave, if the writing stands out as on a denar. He who says even to engrave, as on a writing tablet<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">On a wax-covered writing tablet (πίναξ), one writes by engraving. The same is true for writing cuneiform on clay tablets. Therefore, engraving can be called “writing”.</i>. “He shall write”, not dropping points; Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom and Rebbi Mattaniah: One said, if he did not connect the points; the other said, even if he connected the points<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Even though one can produce letters that look as if written, they are not written.</i>. “He shall write”, not pouring. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, those Orientals are very sophisticated. If one of them wants to write a secret<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μυστήριον “mystery or secret rite”. In the parallel sources the word is more appropriately the adjective μυστηρικός “of or for mysteries”.</i> letter to another, he writes with juice of gall-nuts<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of the expression in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 19a" href="/Gittin.19a">Babli, 19a</a>. But the word might also mean “apple juice, fruit juice”, from Greek μῆλον “apple, or other fruit”.</i>. The recipient pours ink without gall over it, which is absorbed at the place of the writing<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">If the ink is washed off the paper, the writing becomes visible.</i>. If one did that<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Is pouring a fluid over a paper to make invisible writing visible an act of writing, which is forbidden on the Sabbath?</i> on the Sabbath, what? There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 12:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.12.5.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 12:5</a>.</i>: “Writing on top of writing is not prosecutable.” Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both said, only if he wrote ink on ink or vermilion on vermilion<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the writing has no visible effect.</i>. But if he wrote ink on vermilion or vermilion on ink, he is guilty. Rebbi Isaac bar Mesharshia in the name of the rabbis from there: He is doubly guilty, once for erasing and once for writing<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 19a" href="/Gittin.19a">Babli, 19a</a>, this is quoted as the common opinion of R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.
If the witnesses do not know how to sign. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one sketches for them in ink and they sign with vermilion, or with vermilion and they sign in ink. Rebbi Joḥanan told him, because we are occupied<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Following the reading in <i>Šabbat</i>. The reading of the two texts in <i>Giṭṭin</i>, “because they are not occupied”, does not make sense. The meaning is also confirmed by the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 19a" href="/Gittin.19a">Babli, 19a</a>.</i> with the rules of the Sabbath, should we permit a married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Because writing with ink on vermilion is a desecration of the Sabbath, it does not follow that if illiterate witnesses draw their signatures in ink over vermilion letters that this is valid testimony. As the sentence after the next points out, the witnesses in that case paint the first writer’s letters; they do not sign in a way that could be notarized.</i>? But he brings an blank sheet of paper and cuts it before them<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">They draw with a pen along the lines cut into the paper. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 19a" href="/Gittin.19a">Babli, 19a</a>, this method is attributed to Rav.</i>. Would it not have been the first person’s handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The objection raised against R. Simeon ben Laqish can also be raised against R. Joḥanan. But for his method, the objection can be overcome by not cutting lines into the paper but cutting out small strips where the witness has some leeway where to draw his lines which spell his name.</i>? One has to widen the cuts. Rebbi Mana said, why do we not say that one sketches for them with water? If he would come and protest, his protest would be accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the witnesses just wrote over the letters, irrespective of how these were drawn, the signatures could not be notarized and the husband could claim that there are no signatures. The method indicated by R. Joḥanan is the only acceptable one.</i>. If somebody tears into skin<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Before it was turned into leather which was used as writing material in those times. Since it is permitted to write with a stylus, a permanent inscription can be made on hide and this is valid.</i> in the form of writing it is valid. If somebody sketches on skin<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Writing on untanned skin can simply be wiped off; this is not permanent writing and therefore is invalid as stated in the Mishnah. The parallel in <i>Šabbat</i> deals with the laws of Sabbath and characterizes the first writing as incurring guilt, because permanent, and the second as not prosecutable, because not permanent.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:4</a>, <i>Šabbat</i>11:8, the terms “valid, invalid” and “guilty, not prosecutable” are switched.</i> in the form of writing it is invalid.
“This is your bill of divorce on condition that you give it to me,” is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If the bill of divorce has to be returned immediately, there is no divorce. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires that the bill of divorce be “given into her hand”, i. e., be legally in her power.</i>. “On condition that you return it to me,” is valid. Is that not the prior case? Rebbi Yose said, after you went to court<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Once the document has served its purpose, it may be returned without invalidating the divorce. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20b" href="/Gittin.20b">Babli, 20b</a>, the formulation is: “ ‘This is your bill of divorce but I remain the owner of the paper’ is invalid; ‘this is your bill of divorce on condition that you return the paper to me’ is valid.” Here also it is understood that the paper will be returned only when it is no longer needed.<br>The Geniza text reads: “after you acquired it.” The Leiden text is preferable since for the Geniza text the explanation which follows is unnecessary.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, “after you have acquired all the rights for which it was made, return it to me.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with R. Yose; he only gives the legal formula for what the Babli calls “a transfer of ownership on condition that it be returned.” This is everywhere considered a valid transfer of ownership and, therefore, satisfies <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>.</i>” Similar to that: <i>Etrogim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Persian <i>turungān</i> (a word of Indian origin) designates all citrus fruits. It was used for <i>citrus medica</i>, rabbinic Hebrew <i>etrôg</i>, which was identified as the “fruit of the tree of splendor” mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev</i>. 23:40</a> together with palm branches, brook willows, and a branch of the thick-leaf tree (traditionally the myrtle).</i> were scarce over there. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob gave an <i>etrog</i> to his son and said to him, if you have acquired it and fulfilled its obligation, return it to me<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev</i>. 23:40</a> reads: “You shall take <i>for yourselves</i> on the first day palm branches …”. The prescribed ritual can only be fulfilled if the four kinds mentioned are the personal property of the celebrant. Therefore, if a person gives his “four kinds” to another person for the celebration, it cannot be a loan but must be “a transfer of ownership on condition that it be returned.” In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 41b" href="/Sukkah.41b"><i>Sukkah</i> 41b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta <i>Sukkah</i> 2:11</a>) the conditional transfer of ownership is a tannaïtic institution.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he wrote it on a golden cup and said, the writing is yours but the space between the lines belongs to me? Is that writing<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the Geniza text: “Does the text fly?” There is no text if there is no space between the letters. In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20b" href="/Gittin.20b">Babli 20b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>) the formulation is: If he retains ownership of the paper, the bill is invalid.</i>? Torn leather is valid, if it was torn it is invalid. If it was not torn by the tear of a court, but if it was torn by the tear of a court<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This text clearly is defective. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta, 7:11</a>, the text reads:נקרע כשר נתקרע פסול נקרע בו קרע של בית דין פסול “torn is valid, if it was torn it is invalid; if the tear is that of a court it is invalid.” The Geniza text is similar. In the opinion of most commentators, the meaning is that a bill of divorce written on a piece of leather that has a tear is valid but if it was torn after the document was written and signed but before it was delivered, it is invalid. In the opinion of S. Lieberman, נתקרע means that the piece of leather had many tears before it was used.</i>. What is the tear of a court? Between the text and the witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>ketubah</i> was paid under the supervision of the court, the court will tear the bill of divorce to make sure that the divorcee cannot use the bill to collect the <i>ketubah</i> a second time in the jurisdiction of another court. Since the witnesses have to sign immediately after the text, to avoid the fraudulent insertion of additional text, a document in which the signatures are separated from the text is automatically invalid.</i>
“On a leaf of the olive tree.” Is that not like torn<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The leaf is much to small for the text of the bill, even if written in miniature letters.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, Bina bar Shila<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the Geniza ms. and the Constantinople print.</i> stated: Even if he only wrote: “I X am divorcing my wife,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The name of the wife must be mentioned, the fact that she is his wife then need not be mentioned; the fact of the divorce must be mentioned.</i>” it is valid. This follows what Rebbi Ila said, if he detailed it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the full text is written it must comply with all rules to be valid.</i>, if he did not detail<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If he only wrote his and her names and the fact of the divorce.</i> it is valid.
“On a cow’s horn.” The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires that the cow be delivered to the wife as bill of divorce.</i> in case he says to her, here is your bill of divorce. But if he says to her, here is your bill of divorce and the remainder is for your <i>ketubah</i>, her bill of divorce and the payment of her <i>ketubah</i> were received together<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The moment she accepts the horns carrying the bill of divorce, she acquires the animal as part payment of the <i>ketubah</i>. There is a small problem here which is not mentioned in either Talmud: A bill of divorce can be given to a wife against her will but the <i>ketubah</i> can be delivered in merchandise, instead of coin, only with her consent. Since transfer of property of an animal <i>always</i> requires an act of acquisition, the husband who writes the bill of divorce on the horns of a cow gives up his right to unilateral divorce.</i>. If he said to her, here is your bill of divorce and the payment of your <i>ketubah</i> together<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, the payment of the <i>ketubah</i> was a consequence of the delivery of the bill of divorce. As it is explained at the end of the paragraph, one may interpret the verse as meaning that the bill of divorce has to come into the wife’s hand unconditionally, not as part of an acquisition of anything else. In this opinion, the requirement that the payment of the <i>ketubah</i> be simultaneous with the divorce, not a consequence of the divorce, invalidates the proceedings. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20b" href="/Gittin.20b">Babli, 20b</a>, the example is a bill of divorce engraved on a plate of gold and Rav Naḥman states that the simultaneous delivery of divorce document and <i>ketubah</i> is valid, in contrast to the conclusion of the Yerushalmi.</i>? Rebbi Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Geniza. The reading of the Leiden ms., R. Ze‘ira, cannot refer to R. Ze‘ira, the head of the Academy of Tiberias, who lived in the second generation after R. Mana I and two generations before R. Mana II. A R. זְעוּרָה, student of R. Mana I, is quoted a few times in other places in the Yerushalmi.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: If he delivered the halter to her, what<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A bridled animal can be acquired by the buyer by taking the halter in his hand and causing the animal to walk one step at his command.</i>? In commercial law, the buy is acquired, do you say so here<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In the first case, the payment of the <i>ketubah</i> was a consequence of the delivery of the bill of divorce. As it is explained at the end of the paragraph, one may interpret the verse as meaning that the bill of divorce has to come into the wife’s hand unconditionally, not as part of an acquisition of anything else. In this opinion, the requirement that the payment of the <i>ketubah</i> be simultaneous with the divorce, not a consequence of the divorce, invalidates the proceedings. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20b" href="/Gittin.20b">Babli, 20b</a>, the example is a bill of divorce engraved on a plate of gold and Rav Naḥman states that the simultaneous delivery of divorce document and <i>ketubah</i> is valid, in contrast to the conclusion of the Yerushalmi.</i>? Or is it a difference since it is written: “he shall deliver into her hand,” until it is completely in her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce has to come into the wife’s hand by being delivered by the husband, not by an active act of acquisition on her part. The formulation of this paragraph implies that this delivery of a bill of divorce is classified as invalid.</i>!
What is Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s reason? “Scroll.” Since a scroll is special in not being a living being, so no living being. Since a scroll is not food, so no food<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21a" href="/Gittin.21a">Babli, 21a</a>. The interpretation of the rabbis’ position is quite different in the Babli.</i>. What is the rabbis’ reason? “Scroll.” Since a scroll is detached, so everything detached<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> where it is stated that a bill of divorce cannot be written on anything connected to the ground. The bill of divorce cannot be written on the walls of a house and the house given to the wife.</i>. Are stalks of food in Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s opinion like food<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The stalks of fruits which usually are harvested together with the fruits have the status of fruits in the laws of impurity; this is the theme of Tractate <i>Uqeṣin</i>. The question is whether R. Yose the Galilean will forbid these inedible stalks as materials for bills of divorce since they are subject to the impurities of foodstuffs.</i>? Let us hear from the following: If he wrote it on a deer’s antlers<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> must be attributed to R. Yose the Galilean since for the rabbis the entire deer could have been given to the wife. The antlers are inedible bony structures attached to the living deer just as stalks are inedible wooden structures attached to fruits.</i>, shaved it off, had it signed, and gave it to him<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This should be “her”, correctly in the Geniza text. As explained in the next Halakhah, “signing” covers not only the signatures of the witnesses but also the insertion of the necessary data into the formulaic text; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.2">Note 100</a>.</i>, it is valid. Because he shaved it off before he had it signed. Therefore, not if he had it signed and afterwards shaved it off<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This proves that the antler is an integral part of the deer for R. Yose the Galilean. It is reasonable to assume that a stalk for him is an integral part of the fruit.<br>This rule also follows the rabbis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> whose position is explained in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b">Babli (21b</a>) from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>: “he writes and hands over to her” that no necessary action may intervene between signing and delivery of the document.</i>. Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In the Geniza text: Aḥa. While it is known that R. Aḥa was a student of R. Miasha’s, the reading of the Leiden ms. cannot be rejected out of hand since Rabbis Abba and Aḥa were contemporaries.</i> in the name of Rebbi Miasha: Only if he wrote on the male horn<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to horns of cattle and goats rather than deer. These horns consist of a horny sheath (the female horn) over a bony spur (the male).</i>. But if he wrote on the sheath<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Greek νάρθηξ; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.2.3"><i>Berakhot</i> 5:2, Note 67</a>.</i> it is as if separated<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> would certainly object and require that the entire animal be handed over to the wife; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.8">Note 88</a>.</i> and is valid. Rebbi Jonah asked: Is that the same for preparation of produce<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">While food is under rules of impurity that are much stricter than those for vessels, etc., harvesting alone does not transform produce into food for these rules; only intentional moistening will have that effect, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1-2"><i>Demay</i> 2:3, Note 136–141</a>.</i>? If he desired that it should rain on an animal and [the rain] dropped from the animal on food<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the moistening was desired by him for the animal but not for the food. Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> reads: Any fluid which at the start is desired, even if at the end it is not desired, or if at the end is desired, even if at the start it was not desired, fulfills the condition “that it be given” (i.e., it prepares for impurity by the terms of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev</i>. 11:38</a>: “If water be given on seeds … it shall be impure for you”.) For the anonymous rabbis it is obvious that drops from water which was desired for the animal will prepare food for impurity by this Mishnah. But R. Yose the Galilean, who disqualifies animals as writing materials for bills of divorce, might equate animals to things connected to the soil which are disqualified for bills of divorce in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, and for objects of desire which act in impurity as explained in the following.</i>. There, Rebbi Yose said “a scroll”, since a scroll is special in not being a living being, so no living being; and here, does he say so<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Does he exclude animals from the category of objects which prepare for impurity if watered? There seems to be no reason why this should be so.</i>? There is a difference since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>.</i>, “any drinkable drink in any vessel shall be impure”. Then also if it rained into cisterns, ditches and caves? There is a difference here, for it is written: “a vessel”<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i> 8(2): “I could think also [if it rained] into cisterns, ditches and caves? It is written: ‘a vessel’; since one of the characteristics of a vessel is that it is separated from the ground, so only things separated from the ground.” Everybody agrees that water collected in cisterns, etc., does not prepare for impurity; the reason for this has nothing to do with the controversy over writing materials.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not write on anything connected to the ground. If he wrote on anything connected, detached it, signed,<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Not only affixed the signatures of the witnesses but also inserted the essential data of the document; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.2">Note 100</a>.</i> and delivered it, it is valid, but Rebbi Jehudah declares it invalid unless both writing and signing took place when it was detached. Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra says, one writes neither on erased papyrus nor on διφθέρα<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic usage, hide incompletely tanned; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.4.1"><i>Soṭah</i> 2:6, Note 151</a>. The hide does not absorb the ink which can be wiped off without leaving a trace.</i> because these can be falsified but the Sages declare valid.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not write on anything connected to the ground,” etc. This does not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, writing the essential text with the formula is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">100,</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>, in which permission is given to scribes to prepare the formulaic text of divorce documents, so that only the names of husband and wife and the date have to be inserted, without violating the commandment that the document be written specifically for the woman concerned. It is not mentioned in the Mishnah whether the text which turns the document into one of divorce, "this is your bill of divorce and you are permitted to every man" is part of the formulaic text or has to be written with that particular woman in mind. R. Johanan permits this sentence to be written as a formula, R. Simeon ben Laqish prohibits. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, the attributions are switched, probably because the Babli insists that practice follow R. Johanan.) In the interpretation of the Babli, the divorce formula may be prepared in advance for R. Eleazar, for whom only the witnesses to the delivery of the text are important, but not for R. Mei'r, for whom the witnesses signing the document are those who validate the divorce. It is impossible to know whether the Yerushalmi would agree to this interpretation.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The Geniza text has a longer sentence: "R. Ze'ira said, they disagree in this, R. Johanan said, writing the essential text with the formula is valid, R. Simeon ben Laqish said..." This text adds no new information.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, writing the essential text with the formula is invalid. Samuel bar Abba asked: If he wrote and signed it when detached, then he connected it again, detached it, and delivered it to her; what does Rebbi Jehudah say<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bill was written according to the rules, does a later unnecessary action invalidate the document? No answer is given; it is difficult to see why the answer should be “valid”.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, Rebbi Abin asked: If he wrote and signed it when detached, then he connected again, signed, and detached it, and delivered it to her; what do the rabbis say<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This text seems to be self-contradictory since “signing” is mentioned twice. It is difficult to amend the text against the concurrent testimony of the ms. sources available. <i>Sefer haʻIṭṭur</i> I 26a, quotes an identical text for both questions, one addressed to R. Jehudah and one to the rabbis. Rashba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 21b, end) reads in the question addressed to the rabbis: If he wrote it when detached, attached, signed, detached, and delivered it, what do the rabbis say? In this case the obvious answer is: invalid. The text as it stands can be interpreted to mean that the bill was written when detached, including date, both names, and the statement that it was a divorce document which freed the wife to marry any man, but that the witnesses signed when it was attached to the ground. Then it would be invalid for R. Meïr and valid for R. Eleazar the Tanna.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <i>Sefer haʻIṭṭur</i> 26d (Note 52); the variant readings are given by<b>ע</b> . While medieval authors cannot be expected always to quote exactly, the text is quoted as support for the Geniza readings.</i> Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> said, where do they disagree? About bills of divorce. But for contracts even Rebbi Jehudah will agree<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 22b" href="/Gittin.22b">Babli, 22b</a>, R. Eleazar says that the Sages agree with R. Jehudah that contracts may not be written as palimpsests or on erasable leather.</i>. He explains it if the document is on a smooth surface and the witnesses on the erasure<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Then the document cannot be forged and one hopes that the signatures can be verified either by the signers or by comparing the signatures with those on other known documents.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it makes no difference; they disagree both about bills of divorce and contracts<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 22b" href="/Gittin.22b">Babli, 22b</a>, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan.</i>. He explains it if the document is on a smooth surface and the witnesses on the erasure. It is difficult for Rebbi Eleazar: If the witnesses [sign] on the erasure, do the Sages declare it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to agree that the signatures cannot be forged.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Geniza. The reading of the Leiden ms., R. Ze‘ira, cannot refer to R. Ze‘ira, the head of the Academy of Tiberias, who lived in the second generation after R. Mana I and two generations before R. Mana II. A R. זְעוּרָה, student of R. Mana I, is quoted a few times in other places in the Yerushalmi.</i> said before Rebbi Mana: The Sages follow the argument of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna who dismisses the signatures on the bill of divorce as irrelevant and requires delivery of the bill in the presence of witnesses to the delivery. This is the explanation of the Babli in the name of the Amora R. Eleazar.</i>, for he explains that the document is on the erasure and the witnesses on a smooth surface<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses to the delivery must read the document before delivery to verify that it is what it is declared to be. They would detect a forgery.</i>. Samuel said, everything is on the erasure<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The entire document is a palimpsest. This explanation is implied by the language of the Mishnah; such a document is declared valid in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 163a" href="/Bava_Batra.163a">Babli (<i>Baba batra</i> 163a</a>) in the name of Rav.</i>. Does not Rebbi Jehudah say it correctly? Rebbi Abba said, it is recognizable whether there was an erasure once or twice<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The editors of the Babli agree, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 164a" href="/Bava_Batra.164a"><i>Baba batra</i> 164a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Everybody is acceptable to write the bill of divorce, even an insane, a deaf-and-dumb, or a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakha, they may write only the formulaic text which does not have to be written specifically for the divorcing couple. This justifies the inclusion of the deaf-and-dumb.</i>. The wife may write her bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">She has to hand the completed bill to her husband who must have it signed by the witnesses and return it to her in front of witnesses.</i> and a man his receipt<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor who pays his debt may present the creditor with a receipt which the latter signs. The text of the receipt does not have to be in the creditor's hand.</i>, for the certification of the bill of divorce is only effected by its signatures. Everybody is acceptable to bring the bill of divorce except an insane, a deaf-and-dumb, a minor, a blind person<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">It is explained in the Babli that the blind person is disqualified only for bills of divorce transported across the border since he cannot affirm that he saw its writing and signing.</i>, or a Gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rules of divorce are biblical, they can be fulfilled only by a person subject to the biblical rules.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Everybody is acceptable to write the bill of divorce,” etc. Rav Huna said, only if a sane person watches over them<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If an incompetent person (insane, deaf-and-dumb, or minor) writes the text, they must be directed by a person who knows for what they write. The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 22b-23a" href="/Gittin.22b-23a">Babli, 22b/23a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan asked<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the Geniza text and <i>Sefer ha‘Iṭṭur</i> (27b).</i>: But is it not written: “He shall write for her,” in her name<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>, the anonymous majority permit the routine production of forms of bills of divorce where everything is written in advance and only the names of husband and wife, the date, and the statement of divorce are then inserted for the particular couple involved in the divorce; but R. Jehudah holds that the requirement that “he write for her” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>) can only be fulfilled if the bill was written specifically for that wife from the first letter to the last.</i>? Samuel said, it is necessary that one reserve the essential text for him<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The essential text must be written by a responsible person under the husband’s direction. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23a" href="/Gittin.23a">Babli agrees, 23a</a>.</i>. This follows what Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, writing the essential text with the formula is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>, in which permission is given to scribes to prepare the formulaic text of divorce documents, so that only the names of husband and wife and the date have to be inserted, without violating the commandment that the document be written specifically for the woman concerned. It is not mentioned in the Mishnah whether the text which turns the document into one of divorce, “this is your bill of divorce and you are permitted to every man” is part of the formulaic text or has to be written with that particular woman in mind. R. Joḥanan permits this sentence to be written as a formula, R. Simeon ben Laqish prohibits. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, the attributions are switched, probably because the Babli insists that practice follow R. Joḥanan.) In the interpretation of the Babli, the divorce formula may be prepared in advance for R. Eleazar, for whom only the witnesses to the delivery of the text are important, but not for R. Meïr, for whom the witnesses signing the document are those who validate the divorce. It is impossible to know whether the Yerushalmi would agree to this interpretation.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the minor received it and became an adult, the deaf-and-dumb and started talking, the blind and became seeing, the insane and became sane, the Gentile and converted, it remains invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires that the husband hand the divorce document over to his wife, he can be represented in this transaction only by a duly appointed representative. But since an incompetent person cannot be a representative, nor a Gentile be an agent in a Jewish religious transaction, any such delivery could not be considered as the husband’s.</i>.<br>But if a hearing person became deaf-and-dumb, then regained his hearing, a seeing person became blind, then regained his vision, a sane person became insane, then regained his sanity, it is valid. This is the principle: Whenever the beginning and the end was in full competence, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Even if there was an interval in which delivery could not be made, on condition that the responsible person remember his appointment as the husband’s representative.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the minor received it and became an adult,” etc. There came a case before Rebbi Immi that a slave brought a bill of divorce; [he declared it] valid<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23a" href="/Gittin.23a">Babli, 23a</a>, this was a theoretical question and R. Assi was the opponent.</i>. Rebbi Abba said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya the Elder.</i> state that if a slave brought a bill of divorce, it would be invalid? Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Babylonian name of R. Yasa, the constant companion of R. Immi. The version of the Geniza ms. which reads “R. Immi” for all occurrences of “R. Assi” is preferable.</i> said, if not for Rebbi Abba, we would have permitted a married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who receives an invalid bill of divorce remains married to her husband.</i>! Even if you say that Rebbi Assi did not accept what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, did he not accept what the rabbis said? Let us hear from the following which Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya bar Abba, student of R. Joḥanan and teacher of R. Ze‘ira. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23b" href="/Gittin.23b">Babli, 23b</a>, this is quoted by Rav Samuel bar Jehudah in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is reasonable that a slave could accept a bill of manumission [for delivery]<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He can bring another slave’s bill of manumission since he himself may be manumitted. In the Babli this is restricted to another owner’s slave.</i> but he should not accept [a bill of divorce] as we do<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the Geniza text, supported by the Leiden ms. in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a>, 60a 1. 50. Since a slave cannot legally marry, he cannot divorce and like a Gentile is barred from acting in a divorce proceeding. R. Immi, who initially accepted the bill of divorce, must have held with R.
