Meïr that the slave’s hand is his master’s hand (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a>, 60a 1. 49).</i>. They thought, he formulated “he should not accept,” therefore, if he transgressed and accepted, it would be valid<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">It is not formulated as an absolute impossibility.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rav Hoshaiah: It happened that she was a Cohen’s wife<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Cohen may not be married to a divorcee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev</i>. 21:7</a>), in all cases in which there may be the slightest doubt about the validity of a divorce given by a Cohen, the court will force the husband to give a second, unquestionably valid divorce.</i> and they were not afraid of a hint of invalidity<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">They declared the bill of divorce in the hand of the slave as non-existent and permitted the wife to stay married to her Cohen husband.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Even women who cannot be believed if they say that [a woman’s] husband died are trustworthy to bring her bill of divorce: Her mother-in-law, her mother-in-law’s daughter, her co-wife, her sister-in-law, and her husband’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">These are presumed to hate her, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:4:1-5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.4.1-5.4">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 15:4</a>; it is assumed that they would not mind tricking her into adultery.</i>. What is the difference between divorce and death? The writing is the proof. A woman may herself bring her own bill of divorce, but she still is required to say that it was written and signed before her.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Also those women who cannot be believed if they say that [a woman’s] husband died,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <i>Yebamot</i> 15:4, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.8">Notes 91–94</a>. The variants are denoted by <b>י</b>.</i> Would you believe her if it was written but not signed [in her presence]? If she did not say ‘it was written in my presence, it was signed in my presence’, would you permit her to remarry<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, it is not the text which allows the wife to remarry but the other woman’s testimony.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that parallels what Rebbi Abun said, he is not suspect to damage her before Heaven. Before the court he is suspect to damage her. But since he knows that if he comes and protests, his protest is void, he will choose qualified witnesses to sign<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">An additional sentence, extant in <i>Yebamot</i>, is missing here to the effect that if one of these women would recant her testimony she also would not be believed.</i>.
Think of it, if she stayed in Rome ten years and remarried<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Since the woman is divorced once the bill is delivered into her hands, does the Mishnah mean that a woman married for the second time has to keep her bill of divorce and show it to the local court any time she moves to a new location? This is impossible since the bill is torn up by the court once the <i>ketubah</i> was paid to her in full.</i>! Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah if he said to her, you shall only be divorced [by this document] at place X<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">She is divorced as soon as she declares at her place of destination that the bill was written and signed in her presence. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24a" href="/Gittin.24a">Babli, 24a</a>, agrees after a lengthy and tortuous discussion.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Every bill of divorce which was not written for a particular wife<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If the scribe did not intend to write for one particular woman. As will be clear from the sequel, the scribe not only has to know the wife’s name but her identity as well.</i> is invalid. How? If one was walking in the market and heard the scribes dictating<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A school of scribes and the teachers were dictating a text as example of a bill of divorce. At the end of the course, every student would have forms available for all situations.</i> “Mr. X divorces his wife Y from place Z” and said, these are mine and my wife’s names, it is invalid and cannot be used to divorce by. In addition, if he wrote [a bill] to divorce his wife but had second thoughts, and a fellow townsman came and said to him, my name is identical with yours and my wife’s name is identical with your wife’s, it is invalid and cannot be used to divorce by. In addition, if he had two wives with identical names and wrote a document to divorce the older one by, he cannot use it to divorce the younger. In addition, if he said to the scribe: write that I may divorce either one<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Of the two who have identical names.</i> I wish, it is invalid and cannot be used to divorce either one.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Every bill of divorce which was not written for a particular wife,” etc. Who is dictating? School teachers<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24b" href="/Gittin.24b">Babli, 24b</a>, “a training course for scribes.”</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24b" href="/Gittin.24b">Babli, 24b</a>, it appears that the remainder of this paragraph is a <i>baraita</i>.</i> What means “in addition”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In what is the case of the man who wants to use somebody else’s bill different from the case of the bill written as a writing exercise?</i>? There it was not written as a bill of divorce but here it was written as a bill of divorce. What means “in addition”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The question is what is the difference between the bill destined for the older one and the bill destined for either one of the two.</i>? There it was written neither in his name nor in her name but here it was written in his name but not in her name. What means “in addition”? There it was written in his name but not in her name but here it was written in his name and in her name but it was not written for divorce from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires that the husband “write her a scroll of divorce”; it must be written from the start for one and only one divorce.</i>.
Rav said, none of these are invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.6.2">Chapter 2, Note 132</a>, if a Cohen divorces his wife with a document which is found to be invalid but which possibly might have been valid, (the technical term is: the document has the “smell” of a bill of divorce) one forces the Cohen to deliver a valid divorce document to his wife and he is prohibited from changing his mind and not divorce her. Rav states that all the bills of divorce mentioned in the Mishnah except the last do not have a “smell” of a bill of divorce; they are pieces of paper, not bills of divorce, neither valid nor invalid.</i> except for the last which is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The bill written for two wives with identical names is invalid but if the husband is a Cohen he is forced to divorce the wife to whom the bill was delivered.</i>. Issi<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24b-25a" href="/Gittin.24b-25a">Babli, 24b/25a</a>, Rav’s opinion is atributed to Rav Assi (= Issi) and vice versa.</i> said, all are invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">And force a definitive divorce if made by a Cohen. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24b" href="/Gittin.24b">Babli, 24b</a>, this position is attributed to Rav.</i> except for the first which is not invalid. Rebbi Abba bar Ḥinena<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of this name is not otherwise known. In the Geniza text, the name is Ze‘ur bar Ḥinena, known from both Talmudim as the name of a third-Century Amora who almost was killed by Queen Zenobia of Palmyra.</i> said, all are invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 24b" href="/Gittin.24b">Babli, 24b</a>, this appears as Samuel’s opinion.</i>. The word of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish implied that all are invalid since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he wrote the essential text with the formula, it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The sentence about R. Simeon ben Laqish is missing in the Geniza text. R. Simeon ben Laqish considers the statement that the document is one of divorce so important that its writing without the explicit instruction by the husband invalidates the bill; cf. Chapter 2, Notes 100,101. But his choice of the word “invalid”, not that “there is no ‘smell’ of a divorce”, shows that in all cases he holds that any material expression of the husband’s desire to divorce prohibits the Cohen to remain married to his wife.</i>. The word of Rebbi Joḥanan implied that none are invalid since Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he wrote the essential text with the formula, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan strictly follows the Mishnah that only the names of husband and wife and the date have to be written by the explicit instruction of the husband. Therefore, he also follows the language of the Mishnah, not that the bills of divorce are simply invalid but are “invalid to be used in divorce”, meaning that the use of any of these documents cannot imply any divorce in any form. (The classical commentators all feel obliged to switch the positions attributed to R. Simeon ben Laqish and R. Joḥanan; this position cannot be sustained against the testimony of both mss.) The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 25a" href="/Gittin.25a">Babli, 25a</a>, quotes R. Joḥanan in the sense given here but explains his disagreement with the position attributed here to Rav by the fact that he never admits retroactive validation of documents; therefore, he cannot admit that the husband has the power to decide after the writing of the document to which wife it shall apply.</i>. Does the word of Rebbi Eleazar imply that none are invalid? Since Rebbi Eleazar asked: if one wrote a bill of preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a> states that a preliminary marriage can be effected either by a transfer of valuables, or a contract, or sexual relations. It is then explained [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.2-33"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a> (58c 1. 29), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 9a" href="/Gittin.9a">Babli 9a</a>] that the contract is a written statement by the groom declaring his future bride to be his wife; the marriage is effected by the acceptance of the letter by the adressee (or, if she is underage, her father).</i> not for a specific name<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning: not for a specific woman; since no name has to be written in the statement, it may simply read: By accepting this document, you are my wife. The document contains no formulaic text.</i>, does preliminary marriage take hold? If he had a problem with divorce, would he ask about preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If he thought that any of the bills of divorce mentioned in the Mishnah would possibly count as active for a Cohen’s wife, he would have to ask about circumstances of divorce, where the problems are real and grave.</i>? Since in the case of divorce, where the document is required to be for a specific name and he did not write for a specific name, divorce will not touch her, is it not logical that in the case of preliminary marriage, where a specific name is not required and he did not write for a specific name, preliminary marriage take hold with her<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Geniza text has a longer argument: “Since he has a problem with divorce where [the documents] are not invalid; therefore he asks about preliminary marriage. If you say he has a problem with divorce, he should not ask about preliminary marriage. Since in the case of divorce, where it is required that the document be for a specific name and he did not write for a specific name, divorce did not touch her; in the case of preliminary marriage, where a specific name is not required, if it was not for a specific name preliminary marriage should take hold of her. It should be so. That is impossible. Since in the case of divorce, where it is required to be for a specific name, if it was not for a specific name, divorce did touch her; in the case of preliminary marriage, where a specific name is not required and he did not write for a specific name, preliminary marriage should take hold of her.” It is difficult to make coherent sense of this text.</i>? That should be obvious to him. Really, the following is obvious to him: Since one inferred the bill of preliminary marriage only from divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The validity of a preliminary marriage by a symbolic bride purchase is given biblical status by reading <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> “if a man take a wife” as “if a man buy a wife” since in Mishnaic Hebrew the verb לקח means “to buy” [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.2-33"><i>Qiddušin</i>1:1</a> (58b 1.35), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 4b" href="/Gittin.4b">Babli 4b</a>]. The possibility of marriage by document is inferred from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:2" href="/Deuteronomy.24.2"><i>Deut</i>. 24:2</a>: “She [the divorcee] left his house, went and became another man‘s.” Since “becoming a manʻs” means to marry him, the verse connects leaving (divorce) with becoming (marriage). Since leaving is effected by a document, so becoming can be effected by a document [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.2-33"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a> (58b 1.33), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 9b" href="/Gittin.9b">Babli 9b</a>]. Therefore, a document invalid for leaving must be invalid for becoming.</i>, and in the case of divorce she is not divorced, in the case of preliminary marriage she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 9b" href="/Kiddushin.9b">Babli agrees, <i>Qiddušin</i> 9b</a>. There, the question is attributed to R. Simeon ben Laqish; the argument agrees with the latter’s position as given here.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar bar Yose asked before Rebbi Yose: Concerning what you say that one bill of divorce is invalid for two women<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last clause in the Mishnah, when the husband, married to two identically named women, orders a bill of divorce in their name without specifying to whom it will refer.</i>, should not two bills of divorce be invalid for two women? He asked him, what is the question? He answered, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.4.1">Mishnah 9:5</a>.</i>: “If two people sent two [identical]<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the Geniza text. The same messenger carries two bills of divorce from two men, both called X ben Y, to two wives, both called Z bat U.</i> bills of divorce which were intermingled<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Added from the Geniza text. The same messenger carries two bills of divorce from two men, both called X ben Y, to two wives, both called Z bat U.</i>, he gives both documents to both women.” There, it is a divorce for this one and a divorce for the other one; the mix-up caused [the problem]<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If each wife receives both bills it is certain that each one received the one intended for her.</i>. But here, neither this one is divorced in her name nor is that one divorced in her name.
After it was delivered to her<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last clause in the Mishnah, when the husband, married to two identically named women, orders a bill of divorce in their name without specifying to whom it will refer.</i>, could it not be considered as if written in her name from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bill was written for one of two women, can one not say that the delivery made it clear that from the start it was written for the recipient?</i>? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi [Abin bar Ḥiyya]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the Geniza.</i>: It follows Rebbi Simeon, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 1:14.</i>: “If one said to a craftsman, make me two bells, one for the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: For the door. “For the ground” means the same, i. e., to be affixed to a structure connected to the ground. A cowbell can become impure (Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 1:13) but a doorbell cannot become impure.</i> and one for an animal, make me two mats, one to sit on and one for a tent<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A mat to sit on can become impure in many ways; a mat used as part of a tent cannot become impure.</i>, make me two sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Textile sheets.</i>, one to lie on and one for [a pattern]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the Geniza text, supported by the Tosephta. A bed sheet can become impure like a mat; a sheet on which a picture is embroidered to serve as a pattern to be copied is not a utensil and is pure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 24:13" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.24.13">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 24:13</a>; concurrent explanation by <i>Arukh</i>, Maimonides, and R. Simson of Sens, based on a Gaonic source.) The Leiden ms. text, “for hides” is difficult to understand since a sheet used as wrapping is an implement and subject to the impurity of utensils.</i>, <sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta the text reads: “All these accept impurity up to the moment when he spells out;” if a utensil is made which can serve a dual purpose, it can become impure (once finished) unless it is clearly designated for a use which shields it from impurity. The common testimony of both ms. texts leaves no room for the addition of the Tosephta but its interpretation is similar: All these utensils are subject to impurity unless he spells out which one is pure, i. e., is shielded from impurity.</i> only if he designated them in purity. Rebbi Simeon declares them pure unless he designated them for impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since a manufactured item only accepts impurity if it is used as a vessel or a tool, R. Simeon rejects any retroactivity in the designation of dual use items and declares that impurity can affect the item only in the future, after it was designated as tool or utensil, even if it was exposed to impurity beforehand (cf. D. Pardo, Commentary on the Tosephta, Part 4-1, ed. S. Lieberman, New York - Jerusalem 1970, p. 84). In the Tosephta, R. Simeon agrees that if an item is manufactured for sale as a utensil it is subject to impurity up to the moment a buyer designates it for a protected use.</i>.” As he said there, only if it was a utensil from the start, so he says, unless it was a divorce in her name from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects any retroactivity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.3">Note 17</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya, [this is Rebbi’s opinion.]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">From the Geniza text, missing in the Leiden ms. but confirmed by Nahmanides (<i>Milḥamot Hashem</i>, on Alfasi #556). One still discusses the case of the man with two wives whose names are identical; Rebbi rejects the bill of divorce since he holds that its meaning must be clear before it is delivered; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.6">Note 45</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, and it was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The same <i>baraita</i> is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84b" href="/Gittin.84b">Babli, 84b</a>.</i>: “All conditions<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce is called סֵפֶר כְּרִיתוּת “a scroll of cutting” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>. This is interpreted to mean that all ties between husband and wife have to be cut; a divorce in which the husband retains any influence in his wife’s life is invalid.</i> invalidate a bill of divorce, the words of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Any condition written into the bill invalidates the bill. If the husband wants to attach a condition to the divorce he has to negotiate this separately with his wife before delivering the bill.</i>. But the Sages say, what invalidates orally invalidates in writing, and what does not invalidate orally does not invalidate in writing.” “You are permitted to marry anybody except Mr. X,” because this invalidates orally it invalidates in writing<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This reserves an influence of the husband’s for the rest of the wife’s life and clearly invalidates the divorce.</i>. “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>”; because this does not invalidate orally it does not invalidate in writing<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The divorce becomes valid immediately; the wife accepts a monetary obligation which the husband eventually could enforce by foreclosure. If the stipulation had read: on condition that you pay me 200 <i>zuz</i> within 30 days, then the divorce would become valid if and only if the money had been paid within 30 days. If it had read: only if you pay me 200 <i>zuz</i>, the divorce would be invalid since without a time limit there is no separation. The Babli’s concerns, whether the stipulation was written in the declaration of divorce or attached to it, are unknown to the Yerushalmi and all explanations of the Yerushalmi text based on the Babli have to be rejected, as noted by Naḥmanides (Note 39).</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, when do they disagree? If he rescinded his condition. But if he did nor rescind his condition, even Rebbi agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, Rebbi agrees with the Sages on what is or is not an acceptable condition. He differs in that (a) he does not permit any erasures in a bill of divorce and (b) he stipulates that there cannot be any change in conditions after the delivery of the bill.</i>. Otherwise, we would have to say that all conditions which were stated for bills of divorce do not follow Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It is unreasonable to assume that Rebbi formulated an entire series of Mishnaiot about conditions attached to divorce documents (Chapter 7) if he himself held that these are impossible.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena said, there when he writes “on condition,” here if he gives “on condition.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The previous argument is invalid; the Mishnaiot are about conditions attached to, but no written in, the bill of divorce.</i>” Rebbi Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.4.3">Chapter 2, Note 80</a>.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: Does this imply that Rebbi does not recognize formulaic texts<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does not admit any conditions to be written in the text of the bill, he does not make any distinction between the formulaic text and the essential entries. Does this imply that he prohibits the scribe from preparing bills where only the names, the date, and the declaration of the divorce can be entered with a specific couple in mind?</i>? He answered him, about whom do you ask? About Rebbi! He follows Rebbi Jehudah, for Rebbi Jehudah invalidates formulaic texts<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, everybody agrees that all conditions invalidate a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">No condition can be written in the bill; if written it would have to be a separate codicil.</i>. There came a case before Rebbi Jeremiah and he acted following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since the condition was written in the bill, he declared it invalid.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to him, does one neglect Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Who permitted written conditions under certain cirsumstances.</i> and act following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? He answered him, is Rebbi Joḥanan’s instruction an instruction but Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s instruction not an instruction<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli would take this as a statement of fact.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, it is not that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Who only was No. 2 in R. Joḥanan’s court.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan, but he understood a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The text is not spelled out.</i> and acted accordingly. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, not that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish fights against Rebbi Joḥanan when he disagrees with him only to extract a ruling from him. If he finds a <i>baraita</i>, he depends on it; if he does not find a <i>baraita</i>, he retracts his opinion before Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A scribe.</i> writes formulaic texts of bills of divorce he has to leave space for the man, the woman, and the time. For bonds, he has to leave space for the lender, the borrower, the sum, and the time. For commercial deeds, he has to leave space for the buyer, the seller, the sum, the field<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The descriptions of the boundaries of the parcel.</i>, and the time<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">All predated documents having financial implications are invalid; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.2.3">Chapter 2, Note 16</a>.</i>, because of good order<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">There is a dispute between the Talmudim about the meaning of this clause.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah invalidates all of these. Rebbi Eleazar validates all of them except women’s bills of divorce, for it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.2">Chapter 2, Note 100</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 26a" href="/Gittin.26a">Babli, 26a</a>, reports only R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion, in the name of Samuel.</i>: “He shall write for her”, in her name.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody writes formulaic texts of bills of divorce,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, writing the essential text with the formula is valid. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, writing the essential text with the formula is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.2">Chapter 2, Note 100</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 26a" href="/Gittin.26a">Babli, 26a</a>, reports only R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion, in the name of Samuel.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If somebody writes formulaic texts of bills of divorce he has to leave space for the man, the woman, and the time.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed by the questioner that the text to be written for that woman only includes the sentence that by the document she is divorced.</i>” He explains it, with the essential text<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah has to be explained in a restrictive way: Only the spaces mentioned in the Mishnah have to be left blank.</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Joḥanan: Do the Sages declare valid in case he wrote the essential text with the formulaic text? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: From its invalidity you learn its validity. If he wrote everything<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Including the divorce sentence.</i> in her name but the husband’s and the wife’s names not in her name, would that be valid? Similarly, if he wrote everything not in her name but the husband’s and the wife’s names in her name, it is valid. Rebbi Yose asked Rebbi Jeremiah: Think of it, if it was paired<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If there were two couples in town, in both cases the husband was called X ben Y and the wife Z bat U, would this not require a special characterization such as “X ben Y who dwells in house W”, and the space left blank would not suffice for the characterizations.</i>? He said, so said his teacher Rebbi Ze‘ira to him: Since pairing is infrequent, even if there was pairing it is as if there was no pairing<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even if there were two couples with identical names, if the bill of divorce is written without special characterizations it is valid since one assumes that the bill was intended for the woman who presents it together with her <i>ketubah</i> to collect her due. (The Babli permits only in emergencies to divorce such a couple without due characterization or in the presence of the other couple; 66a.).</i>. Then it is difficult for Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Even if he did not write the essential text in the formula, Rebbi Jehudah declares invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of R. Simeon ben Laqish has no basis in the Mishnah which either does not permit prepared forms or requires only the personal data and the date to be filled in.</i>! Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ze‘ira: Rebbi Jehudah refers to something else; it follows what Simeon bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: An empty space of two lines<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses have to sign on the second line after the end of the text; not directly after the text in order to show the difference between the text and the witnesses nor two lines apart because then the document could be falsified: a line could be added after the witnesses signed. A document signed by witnesses at a distance of more than one line is invalid in all cases (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.7.2-4">Halakhah 9:8</a>, 50c 1. 10; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 162b" href="/Bava_Batra.162b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 162b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:11</a>).</i> about one subject<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Venice printer read אחר instead of אחד; this led the commentators into difficulties. (The ms. text has אחר in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.2-10"><i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a>; this belongs to another editorial team. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.7.2-4">Halakhah 9:8</a>.)</i> is invalid in any case.
What is “the good order”? Rebbi Sabbatai in the name of Ḥizqiah: Because of the good order of Jewish women, lest they frequently be divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The forms must contain blank spaces to introduce an artificial complication forcing the husband to go to a scribe to get a bill of divorce.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac asked Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: It is valid, and you say so? If it were invalid, then [the explanation] would be acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The answer given fits the position of R. Jehudah who prohibits the preparation of forms.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, for the good order of the scribe, that his livelihood be guaranteed<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">All legal formalities contain an element of protection for lawyers, to make sure their services are needed. This is the only explanation given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 26a" href="/Gittin.26a">Babli, 26a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Jehudah for bills of divorce and Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a> and in 9:4 the name is R. Eleazar, but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.3.1">Mishnah 9:4</a> in the Babli (editio princeps and Munich ms.) as well as in the Cambridge ms. of the Mishnah the reading is Eliezer. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>, two sources read Eliezer, one Eleazar. Since “Eleazar” is the reading of all Medieval commentators of the Babli, it is accepted here.</i> in commercial documents. Could we not say, practice follows Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since he agrees with R. Jehudah about bills of divorce.</i>? Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Eleazar for bills of divorce and Rebbi Jehudah in commercial documents. Could we not say, practice follows Rebbi Jehudah? But because Rav and Samuel both say that practice follows Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">That the witnesses to the delivery of the bill effectuate the divorce. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 86b" href="/Gittin.86b">Babli agrees, 86b</a>.</i>; in order not to let you think that here it is the same, it was necessary to state that practice follows Rebbi Jehudah for bills of divorce and Rebbi Eleazar in commercial documents<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The reason for the rejection of divorce documents based on prepared forms is that of R. Jehudah, i. e., that both writing and signing are part of the biblical requirement of “writing for her”. The statement of R. Abba is rejected.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody transports a bill of divorce and loses it, if he finds it on the spot it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">One does not fear that the bill might be written for another couple with identical names from identical places.</i>, otherwise it is invalid. If he finds it in a small bag or in a box<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Greek γλωσσόκομον “case, box, chest”.</i>, if he recognizes it, it is valid. If somebody transports a bill of divorce from an old or sick man, he delivers it to her under the presumption that the man is alive<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband died childless, the widow is freed from ḥalîṣa or levirate marriage as long as the husband’s death was not known at the moment of delivery of the bill.</i>. The daughter of an Israel who is married to a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">And who is childless at the moment of her husband’s departure. At the moment of her husband’s death she loses her right to priestly food.</i> and whose husband went overseas, may eat heave under the presumption that he is alive. If somebody sends his purification offering from overseas, it is sacrificed under the presumption that he is alive<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A dead person’s purification offering cannot be sacrificed since “the dead are free” (from obligations) (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 88:6" href="/Psalms.88.6"><i>Ps</i>. 88:6</a>). The last two statements are included to illustrate the principle that in matters of ritual law one may always act on the assumption of the permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody transports a bill of divorce and loses it,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph also appears as Halakhah 16:6 in Yebamot, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.7.2-4">Notes 133–144</a>. The readings from there are noted<b>י</b> .</i> What means “on the spot”? Rebbi Joḥanan said, as long as nobody passed by there<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is missing in <i>Yebamot</i>. In the Babli, it is attributed to the Tanna R. Simeon ben Eleazar and the Amora R. Abba bar bar Ḥana (27b,28a); there it is declared as practice. It is also the only explanation offered in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta 2:11</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: as long as three people did not pass by there. If a Gentile passed by there? Let us hear from the following: Abba bar bar Ḥana transported a bill of divorce; he lost it and a Saracen found it. The case came before Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of <i>Yebamot</i>, “before the rabbis” is preferable since the verb is in the plural.</i>; they declared [the bill] valid. This means that if a Gentile passed by it is valid. Could we say that he had a mark to identify it? But was it not stated: There are no marks for bills of divorce. That is, if he said, two or three lines. But here, the ה in it had a spot. Rebbi Ezra asked before Rebbi Mana: Why is it invalid here? I say, it was another [husband] whose name was like his name. Think of it, if they checked out that entire place and did not find another man whose name was like his name! But it is because of the severity of adultery. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 16:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.16.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 16:6</a></i>: “They went and found nobody but let his wife remarry; they went there, did not recognize him, and let his wife remarry”? Rebbi Mana told him: This man had two bills of divorce in his hand, one valid, the other invalid. He lost the valid one and threw away the invalid. When he found it, I say that what he found was the invalid bill<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is explained away, as referring to a most unlikely hypothetical case. In the Babli, the Mishnah is accepted practice; the contrast between the two Talmudim is noted in <i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> 36a.</i>.
Bar Qappara stated: Even if he left [the man] when he was 100 years old and spent another 100 years on the way, he delivers it to her under the presumption that he is alive<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 28a" href="/Gittin.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, explains this <i>baraita</i> away, that a person who has reached 100 years of age might go on to live much longer, and implies that a person between the ages of 81 and 99 can be presumed to die soon. Since the Yerushalmi quotes the statement without commentary, it appears that it endorses an unrestricted application of the permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i> until further notice.</i>.
But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.12">Tosephta 4:12</a>, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 28a" href="/Gittin.28a">Babli 28a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 69b" href="/Yevamot.69b"><i>Yebamot</i> 69b</a>.</i>: From where that “if somebody says to his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">She is childless.</i>, this is your bill of divorce one hour before my death, or he says to his slave girl, this is the document of your manumission one hour before my death, each would be immediately forbidden to eat heave?” There, she is disabled immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bills were executed in the expectation of his death, whose time is unknown, it has to be expected immediately.</i>, but here she will only be disabled when he has died<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband expects to return from his trip overseas, the wife does not have to expect his death and can apply the principle of permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>. The Tosephta does not contradict the principle.<br>The explanations of the Babli in <i>Giṭṭin</i> and <i>Yebamot</i> are inconsistent with one another and the Yerushalmi.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar ben Parṭa said three things before the Sages and they confirmed his words: [People in] a city which was surrounded by palisades<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Besieged by a hostile army.</i>, on a ship in emergency at sea, or a person about to be judged<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In a capital case.</i>, are presumed to be alive<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The wife of a man in one of these situations has to assume that her husband is alive.</i>. But on [people in] a city conquered from palisades, a ship lost at sea, or a person led to execution, one puts the restrictions of the living and the dead: neither an Israel’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If she is childless; otherwise she remains in her husband’s clan after the husband’s death.</i> married to a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">She has to assume that her husband is dead and she has lost the right to priestly food.</i> nor a Cohen’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If she is childless; otherwise she remains in her husband’s clan after the husband’s death.</i> married to an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">She has to assume that her husband is alive and she has not regained the right to priestly food.</i> may eat heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah said three things before the Sages in the vineyard at Jabne: About [people in] a city which was surrounded by palisades.”<sup class="footnote-marker">99,</sup><i class="footnote">This is not a quote from the Mishnah; it might be from a <i>baraita</i> which seems to have disappeared from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta 2:12</a> (quoted in the next paragraph) which is missing an introduction.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:9:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.9.2-6">Ketubot 2:10</a> (noted כ), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.3-4">Notes 163-171</a>. (Note the spelling ליסטים, the final ם representing ס, a sure sign of Babylonian spelling.)</i>What are palisades? Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi: For example if bells, chains, and dogs, and geese, and chickens, and soldiers surround the town. And Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi said, it happened that a blind woman was able to flee from there. If there was one breach, it saved all. If there were hiding places, it is questionable. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Abba bar Zavda, Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqula, in the name of Rebbi Yudan the Prince: Only if there were palisades of that government. But palisades from another government are like robbers.
<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta 2:12</a>.</i> They added three additional cases: One who was dragged away by a wild animal, one who was swept away by a river, and one buried by a collapse<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Collapse of a house or a landslide.</i>. One puts the restrictions of the living and the dead on her<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The wife of a man who is the victim of one of these accidents.</i>: neither an Israel’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If she is childless; otherwise she remains in her husband’s clan after the husband’s death.</i> married to a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">She has to assume that her husband is dead and she has lost the right to priestly food.</i> nor a Cohen’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If she is childless; otherwise she remains in her husband’s clan after the husband’s death.</i> married to an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">She has to assume that her husband is alive and she has not regained the right to priestly food.</i> may eat heave.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody is transporting a bill of divorce in the Land of Israel when he falls sick, he may send it by somebody else<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since the messenger does not have to declare that the bill was written and signed before him, there is no reason why the original messenger would have to deliver the bill.</i>. If [the husband] had told him, take from her such and such<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife wants the divorce, the husband can require to be paid for it.</i>, he cannot send it by somebody else since he<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The husband. A person entrusted to handle valuables cannot transfer his responsibilities to another person without becoming liable for any damages that may occur; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:5, Note 144</a>.</i> does not want to see his valuables in somebody else’s hand.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody is transporting a bill of divorce in the Land of Israel when he falls sick,” etc. Because he falls sick; therefore not if he did not fall sick<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Without special authorization, the messenger who transports the bill of divorce cannot appoint a substitute. In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 29a" href="/Gittin.29a">Babli 29a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta 2:13</a>) any messenger is empowered to deliver the bill to a substitute unless the husband explicitly restricted his powers.</i>.
This does not disagree with what Rebbi Joḥanan said, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, if an agent handed over to another agent, the first one is responsible<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the principal gave his prior agreement. The main source is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4</a> (60b 1.48). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11b" href="/Bava_Kamma.11b">Babli (<i>Baba Qama</i> 11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 56b" href="/Bava_Kamma.56b">56b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 36a" href="/Bava_Metzia.36a"><i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 36a</a>) the matter is in dispute. In this opinion, the messenger who chooses a substitute if there are valuables involved accepts responsibility for any accidents that may befall the substitute for his mishandling of the matter.</i>. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya: Because of a condition of the divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the Mishnah as stating that the divorce shall become valid only if (a) the bill of divorce was delivered and (b) the valuables were delivered by the wife to the designated messenger. A substitute can fulfill (a) but not (b), invalidating the divorce.</i>. What is the difference between them? If the husband came earlier and took it<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The valuable he wanted from the wife.</i>. If you say, because of a condition of the divorce, that is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah which states that the messenger <i>cannot</i> appoint a substitute, meaning that the appointment of a substitute invalidates the divorce.</i>. If you say, because I do not want to see my valuables in somebody else’s hand, his valuables are not in somebody else’s hand<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, one has to read the Mishnah as stating that the messenger <i>should not</i> appoint a substitute, but such an appointment does not invalidate the divorce.</i>. Is it the same for preliminary marriages<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, can an agent appointed to conclude a preliminary marriage by proxy appoint a substitute agent?</i>? What is the difference between divorces and preliminary marriages? One can divorce through the agency of anybody<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Since the divorce does not need the consent of the wife, the choice of the agent is the husband’s and is irrelevant.</i>. One cannot preliminarily marry through the agency of anybody<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">An agent not approved by the prospective bride is useless.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody transports a bill of divorce overseas and falls sick, he assembles a court to send it and declares before them: “it was written and signed before me.” The later messenger does not have to say: “it was written and signed before me,” but he says: “I am an agent of the court.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that if the original bill of divorce was certified by a court, no messenger has to say that it was written and signed before him but that he is a court appointee, or, in modern terms, that a bill of divorce certified by a competent court can even be sent by mail to the court which has jurisdiction over the wife.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If somebody transports a bill of divorce overseas and falls sick,” etc. Rebbi Mana said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">His problem is, if the second messenger is an agent of the court, why does he have to declare anything (in front of two witnesses at the moment he hands over the bill of divorce)?</i> is only necessary if he already has arrived in the Land of Israel. Lest you say, since he arrived in the Land of Israel it should be treated as a bill of divorce from the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since it had been certified by a court in the Land of Israel.</i>, and for bills of divorce from the Land of Israel one does not have to say: “it was written and signed before me.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Nor does he have to say: I am an agent of the court.</i>”
If the messenger<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The one appointed by the dying first messenger.</i> fell sick. Rebbi Ḥanina said, a case came before the son of Rebbi Abba and he sent to ask Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Yasa and Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The three successors of R. Joḥanan.</i>. They instructed him that the first messenger<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">He is called “first” because he appoints a new messenger but really he is a second or later messenger to whom the bill was entrusted. The problem is that the Mishnah only empowers the original messenger to appoint a new one; nothing is said about successive later messengers.</i> does not have to say: “it was written and signed before me<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is a substitute, he could not say that.</i>,” but he says, “I am an agent of the court.<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">He can say that and appoint a new messenger by the local court. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 29b" href="/Gittin.29b">Babli, 29b</a>, reads the expression in the Mishnah: “the later messenger” as “<i>any</i> latter messenger”, to the effect that the appointment of successive messengers is explicitly authorized by the Mishnah. There, the main actor is the son of R. Abba<i>hu</i>.</i>”
Does he have to deliver the entire agency to him<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband had entrusted the first messenger not only with the bill of divorce but charged him with additional messages, is the transfer of agency valid if only the delivery of the bill is given to a subsequent messenger or is the agency one and indivisible? This is a new question since its protagonist, R. Jeremiah, was a student of R. Ḥiyya (bar Abba)’s student R. Ze‘ira.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, he has to deliver the entire agency to him. Rebbi Abin bar Cahana said, he need not deliver the entire agency to him. Rebbi Abin bar Cahana brought it from this: “The later messenger does not have to say: ‘it was written and signed before me,’ but he says: ‘I am an agent of the court.’<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the Mishnah as stating that the later messenger has only to identify himself as an agent of the court for the delivery of the bill without further comment.</i>” Rebbi Jeremiah said, Rebbi Abin bar Cahana retracted this, because of: “The later messenger does not have to say: ‘it was written and signed before me,’ but he says: ‘I am an agent of the court.’<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The same Mishnah can be read in the opposite sense: Since obviously no substitute messenger can say that the bill was written and signed before him, the mention of writing and signing for a later messenger can only mean that he stands for the first in all respects.</i>”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A farmer gave a loan to potential recipients of his heave and tithes with the understanding that they do not have to repay the loan but that the farmer will separate both heaves for the account of the Cohen, sell the heaves to another Cohen, and credit the receipt to the Cohen’s account with him, reducing the amount owed. Similarly, he will separate first tithe on the Levite’s account and tithe of the poor for the poor man, deduct the fair value of the produce from his account and eat the tithes himself with his family, since tithes are permitted food for anybody.</i> lent money to a Cohen, a Levite, or a poor person to separate on their account, he can proceed on the assumption that they are alive and does not worry whether the Cohen or Levite died or the poor person became rich<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Once the poor person becomes disqualified for public assistance, he has to inform the farmer.</i>. If they died, he has to receive permission from the heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">They might want to pay off the outstanding balance to establish a record of good credit.</i>; if the loan was executed in court he does not need permission<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If the setup was part of a public document, the heirs would have to negotiate with the farmer to change its terms.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody lent money to a Cohen,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This follows Rebbi Yose; as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 2:8" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.2.8">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 2:8</a>. The Mishnah speaks of a rancher whose ewe gave birth to twin lambs, one of which is a firstling but it is not known which. A firstling has to be given to a Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:19" href="/Deuteronomy.15.19"><i>Deut</i>. 15:19</a>); it may not be shorn (or, if a calf, used for work). The rancher can fulfill his monetary obligation by giving one of the two lambs to the Cohen; the other lamb has to be put out to graze until it develops a defect; then it can be eaten by its owner (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:21-22" href="/Deuteronomy.15.21-22"><i>Deut</i>. 15:21–22</a>). If it was known which lamb was a firstling, that one would have to be given to the Cohen and if the other lamb were slaughtered, some parts would have to be given to a Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut</i>. 18:3</a>). But in the case under consideration, R. Yose holds that no gifts to the Cohen are due since potentially the lamb was the Cohen’s and a Cohen who slaughters does not have to give away anything. He equates potential possession and real possession. The application to the Mishnah here is that the Cohen can dispose of the heave as if he had received it.<br>R. Meїr holds that the gifts are due since they would be due if it was known which one was the firstling.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30a" href="/Gittin.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, this is Ulla’s opinion.</i>: “Rebbi Yose says, anything whose replacement is in the Cohen’s hand is freed from the gifts, but Rebbi Meїr obligates.” Did Rebbi Yose not speak only if it did exist? But here, he still needs to sow<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody can acquire anything nonexistent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 113</a>), R. Yose’s argument cannot be applied to our case.</i>! Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Mishnah speaks of acquaintances of Cohanim or Levites<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The expression is Biblical (<i>2K</i>. 12:6,8); it refers to people who regularly give all their priestly gifts or tithes to the same Cohen or Levite. Then heave and tithes have the status of annuities and cannot be said to be nonexistent.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30a" href="/Gittin.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, this is Rav’s opinion. Samuel requires that heave or tithes be actually given to a third party Cohen or Levite, acting as recipient for the debtor, who then returns the produce to the farmer. That opinion has no parallel in the Yerushalmi.</i>. But did we not state “a poor person”? Does a poor person have acquaintances<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The tithe of the poor has to be given to the first poor person who applies.</i>? There came a case before Rebbi Immi: A Cohen or Levite who owed money to an Israel and told him<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">After the loan was given, the debtor then asked to be excused from repayment using the method outlined in the Mishnah.</i>, separate from my part for my account. He said to him, did we not state: “If somebody lent money to a Cohen, a Levite, or a poor person to separate on their account.” When the loan was given under these conditions. Therefore, not if it was not a condition of the loan! Rebbi Ze‘ira said, even if it was not a condition of the loan. Rebbi Ze‘ira’s force is from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 7:15" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).7.15">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 7:15</a>. The Levite cannot tell the Israel to arrange with other farmers that they pay him to give tithes for them and deduct the sum from the Levite’s debt, since no Levite can dispose of another Levite’s tithes.</i>: “Similarly, a Levite who owed money to an Israel and said to him, separate on my account; only he should not collect and separate because no Levite makes a Levite.” He only said, he should not separate<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the text: Other people’s tithes.</i>, but from his own he may separate.
There is no “he does not worry,” since he has to worry lest the poor became rich. It was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text is slightly corrupt; the translation follows the text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>: הַמַּלְוֶה מָעוֹת אֶת הֶעָנִי וְהֶעֱשִׁיר אֵין מַפְרִישִׁין עָלָיו. שֶׁאֵין מַפְרִישִׁין עַל הָאָבוּד.  This is also the Yerushalmi text quoted in many medieval authors, cf. <i>Tosefta ki-Fshuta Giṭṭin</i> p. 824, Note 16. The last clause means that if the poor person temporarily became rich (i. e., was ineligible for public welfare as defined in <i>Peah</i> 9:9) and then poor again, the lender cannot use the tithe of the poor of the time of ineligibility but must give it away to another poor person since that tithe was lost for the borrower. The Yerushalmi text considers poor and rich as different persons residing in the same body since they fall in different legal categories.</i>: “If somebody lends money to a poor and rich person he cannot separate for him, for one does not separate for what was lost.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose in the name<sup class="footnote-marker">139a</sup><i class="footnote">This probably should read “son of”.</i> of Rebbi Ḥanina: The Mishnah speaks of the heirs in priesthood and Levitic status<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor has to renew the contract if the heirs are priests or Levites. This excludes the poor from this rule, as well as cases in which a Cohen or Levite has only daughters married to Israel husbands.</i>. But concerning the poor, the poor has no inheritance. Bar Qappara stated: No person is immune from this<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Poverty.</i>; if not he then his son, if not his son then his grandson. This<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">“This” is not Bar Qappara’s homily but the Tosephta stated earlier and repeated later, that the poor debtor who became rich cannot continue the arrangement but must pay off the debt. This means that if the circumstances change, the debtor can change the terms to terms less favorable to him.</i> means, he can change the terms. Can the creditor change the terms? Let us hear from the following: “If somebody lends money to a first rich and then poor person he cannot separate for him, for one does not separate for what was lost and the poor acquired what is in his hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence is also in the Tosephta, Note 139, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30b" href="/Gittin.30b">Babli, 30b</a>. If the poor remains rich, the creditor has no way to recoup his money.</i>” This implies that the creditor cannot change the terms<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta, 3:1</a>, this is an explicit statement. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30a" href="/Gittin.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, both the statement that the debtor can change the arrangement but the creditor cannot are attributed to the late Amora Rav Papa; both Talmudim are unaware of the Tosephta.</i>.
Does this<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that after the Cohen’s death the creditor has to renew the agreement with his heirs.</i> not disagree with Rav? Since Rav said, an heir is like a holder of encumbered property. Just as a loan given before witnesses cannot be collected from encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic practice, mortgages are not written on specified parcels but are liens on all real estate the debtor owned at the time the loan was given. If such real estate is later sold, the lien of the mortgage holder is not removed; if the debtor cannot pay, the creditor can foreclose from the buyer. A loan arrangement made orally before witnesses is not a mortgage; the loan can be foreclosed neither from any buyer nor from debtor’s property subject to another person’s lien. If the loan cannot be foreclosed, there seems to be no reason why the creditor can ask anything from the heirs.</i>, so it cannot be collected from heirs. He explains it for a documented loan<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">A mortgage.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A loan given before witnesses can be collected from heirs, on condition that they inherited real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees that a loan cannot be foreclosed from encumbered property, but if the debtor has unencumbered property, free of any lien, it can be foreclosed on the testimony of the witnesses. Therefore, as long as the heirs inherit any real estate (in the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30b" href="/Gittin.30b">Babli, 30b</a>, at least the area covered by a needle), the oral loan remains in force and the creditor has reason to approach the heirs.</i>. As<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">This is an illustration of the Mishnah, not of the preceding rules.</i> those from the house of Rav Neḥemiah, who lent money to the public<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">They advanced money to the public welfare fund, to be repaid from future collections. The question was raised whether money collected for the relief of the poor can be diverted to repay the capitalists. The answer is positive since the committee overseeing charity always will have clients on whose behalf they took the money in the first place and on whose behalf they may repay.</i>. The case came before the rabbis who said, the public is neither all rich nor all poor.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sets aside<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">At the beginning of the harvest he sets aside a quantity of produce so that for the remainder of the harvest he simply can declare that all heave and tithes due at the moment of declaration should be taken from a certain spot of the set-aside. He can do that from a distance and does not have actually to go to the produce and separate heave and tithes for the next batch. For the rules of heave, see Tractate <i>Terumot</i>.</i> produce to be used for future heave and tithes, or money to be used for Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">To redeem the Second Tithe the moment it becomes due; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>.</i>, can refer to them on the presumption that they exist. If they were lost, one worries about them for 24 hours back<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Any heave and tithes which were supposed to be referred to the produce set aside earlier than within 24 hours of the discovery that they were missing are still considered in good order; only produce referred to in the last 24 hours before discovery has to be tithed a second time.</i>, the words of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna, ben Shamua.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, three times a year one has to check the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">That it remained wine and did not turn into vinegar, since vinegar cannot be used to tithe wine.</i>: At the time of East wind after Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">A fall <i>ḥamsīn</i> wind.</i>, when the vines blossom, and when fluid starts to enter unripe grapes.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sets aside produce,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar ben Antigonos in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Yannai: This means that nothing is in disorder for 24 hours but the last day<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Any heave and tithes which were supposed to be referred to the produce set aside earlier than within 24 hours of the discovery that they were missing are still considered in good order; only produce referred to in the last 24 hours before discovery has to be tithed a second time.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said: Nothing is in order for 24 hours but the first day<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the words of R. Eleazar meaning that one worries about all the times except the first 24 hours; all produce processed later than that date have to be tithed again. Both opinions are also reported in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 31a" href="/Gittin.31a">Babli, 31a</a>, with the Babli leaning towards the interpretation of R. Joḥanan as the correct one.</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Miqwa’ot</i> 2:2: If a <i>miqweh</i> was measured and found deficient (containing less than 40 <i>se’ah</i> of water), all things and persons purified by immersion in the <i>miqweh</i> after the last preceding check are considered impure. As pointed out by M. Margalit, R. Joḥanan proves too much since by his reason everything should have to be re-tithed, even what was put in order during the first 24 hours.</i>: “If a <i>miqweh</i> was measured and found deficient.” Ḥinena the son of Rav Assi bar Mamal in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora, ben Pedat.</i>: That is to negate the words of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna, ben Shamua.</i>, as we have stated: “If they were lost, one worries about them for 24 hours back, the words of Rebbi Eleazar.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in <i>Miqwa’ot</i> is inconsistent with that in <i>Giṭṭin</i>. This proves that the Mishnah here, which is formulated in the name of a single person, represents a minority opinion. The Mishnah in <i>Miqwa’ot</i> is from the anonymous majority. The same argument is quoted in R. Eleazar’s name in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 31b" href="/Gittin.31b">Babli, 31b</a>.</i>”
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Demai</i> 7:5; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.1-2">Notes 60–62</a>. The Mishnah states that one may drink Samaritan wine (which is kosher but certainly not tithed) immediately on condition that one declare that one will take a stated amount from it as heave and tithe as soon as the wine is in storage.</i>: “If someone buys wine from Samaritans.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 7:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.7.4.2">Halakhah <i>Demai</i> 7:5, Note 63</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon prohibit to do so, for the wine bag might spring a leak and it may turn out retroactively that he drank <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭevel</i> is untithed produce, whose consumption at times other than during the harvest is a deadly sin.</i>.” Does the Mishnah here not follow Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah permits to tithe by relying on produce stored elsewhere, and which might have disappeared in the meantime.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">RR. Yose and Simeon may agree with the Mishnah here. In the case of Samaritan wine, it was known that the wine was not tithed, but here there is a presumption following the <i>status quo ante</i> that everything is in order.</i> it was not in order from the start. But here it is not in order from now to the future.
For freshly pressed wine one can allow for up to forty days presuming that it is wine<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to judge whether fermenting freshly pressed cider will turn out to be wine or vinegar. Since in most cases one obtains wine, the fermenting juice can be considered to be wine and if a vessel full of fermenting cider was set apart to serve as heave for the entire harvest, one may declare parts of it to be heave for successive batches up to 40 days.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, up to a break point<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">One of the three meteorologically dangerous dates listed by him in the Mishnah when wine is apt to turn into vinegar.</i>. You find that there is a leniency and a restriction following Rebbi Jehudah, and a leniency and a restriction following the rabbis. <sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The following two sentences are garbled. One should read: A <i>restriction</i> following Rebbi Jehudah, if a break point arrived before forty days had passed, <i>he cannot give heave</i>, and a <i>leniency</i> if forty days passed without break point that he <i>can</i> give heave. A <i>restriction</i> following the rabbis, if forty days passed without break point that he cannot give heave, and a <i>leniency</i>, if a break point arrived before forty days had passed that he <i>can</i> give heave.<br>Since wine and vinegar are considered two different products for the purpose of heave, it is forbidden to give heave for wine from vinegar and vice-versa (Mishnah<i>Terumot</i> 3:1).</i> A leniency following Rebbi Jehudah, if a break point arrived before forty days had passed, and a restriction if forty days passed without break point that he cannot give heave. A leniency following the rabbis, if forty days passed without break point that he cannot give heave, and a restriction, if a break point arrived before forty days had passed that he cannot give heave. Rebbi Simon asked: If the fortieth day arrived and he did not check. He was lazy for two or three days, then he came and found it to be vinegar. Is it retroactively considered to be vinegar or only from there on to the future? What is the difference? If he had transgressed and given heave. If you say that retroactively it is considered to be vinegar, his heave is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The previous paragraph shows that in this sentence one has to read: His heave is no heave. In the next sentence, one has to read: His heave is heave. The text already was corrected in <i>editio princeps</i>.</i>. From there on to the future it is not heave. <sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah there exists a parallel text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.2-8"><i>Baba Batra</i> 6:1</a> (15b/c) edited differently. The first part also has a parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 96a" href="/Bava_Batra.96a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 96a</a>.</i> If he had checked an amphora to continuously give heave from it<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave has to be given from the finished agricultural product, it is in order to set aside a small amphora of the first wine of the season to be used to provide heave for the entire harvest. From the moment the first small volume of wine is declared as heave, the entire contents of the amphora can be consumed only by a ritually pure Cohen. This does not alter the fact that most of the contents are still profane and can be used for future declarations of heave; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.1">Note 151</a>.</i>. When he returned, he found it to be vinegar. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The first three days it certainly is wine, the last vinegar, and the middle ones are in doubt. Rebbi Abbahu said, I heard that from Rebbi Joshua ben Levi; Rebbi Joḥanan did not say so but the first three days it certainly is wine, after that it is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Joḥanan’s opinion is supported by a <i>baraita</i>. There it is explained that they differ in the analysis of the process which turns wine into vinegar. This cannot be the background of the Yerushalmi since then the comparison to the status of the needle would be impossible.</i>. Do they differ in the treatment of doubt? What Rebbi Joḥanan said refers to the case that he came and found it to be stale vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">It was exposed to oxydation for a prolonged period after turning into vinegar.</i>. What Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said refers to the case that he came and found it to be strong vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Turned into vinegar only recently.</i>. Or their difference equals another difference, for what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 3:5" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.3.5">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 3:5</a>.
