There, “pure” means “cannot be impure” and “impure” “can become impure”. The only items which can become impure are humans, food, vessels, and tools. An impure tool or vessel is purified when it becomes unusable.</i>: “A rusty or broken needle is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In this state it is not usable. It is stated in Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 3:10 that if it was impure, rusted and became pure, then was rubbed clean, it returned to its original impurity.</i>.” It was stated: He put it down smooth, then he came and found it rusty. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The first three days it certainly is impure, the last pure, and the middle ones are in doubt. Rebbi Abbahu said, that is what Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said; Rebbi Joḥanan did not say so but the first three days it certainly is impure, after that it is in doubt. Rebbi Ila, Rebbi Abba and Rebbi Eleazar in the name of all rabbis who are frequenting the House of Study: Concerning wine and needle practice follows Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.2-8"><i>Baba Batra</i> 6:1</a>, practice is decided following R. Joshua ben Levi only in the case of the needle. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 96a" href="/Bava_Batra.96a">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 96a</a> implies that practice follows R. Joḥanan in the case of the amphora (understood in this sense by Maimonides and R. Moses of Coucy.)</i>.
Rebbi Crispus asked: Does he check every year or only once every three years<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This question is asked only for R. Jehudah who identifies times dangerous for wine.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This belongs to trade rules, one of the topics of <i>Baba Batra</i>. If wine turns into vinegar before Tabernacles, usually in October, the buyer can declare the transaction as executed under false pretenses, return the wine, and request restitution of his money.</i>: If somebody sells unspecified wine to another person, he has to warrant it until after Tabernacles. Rebbi Yudan<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">This is the name in <i>Baba Batra</i>. There is no Amora called “R. Judah” without additional qualifier.</i> said, explain it for those Galileans who harvest only after the beginning of Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since harvesting is forbidden on Tabernacles, they harvest overripe grapes to make sweet wine after the end of the holiday (for Tokay wine this is called <i>Ausbruch</i>). This wine has to be guaranteed until the next Tabernacles and automatically has to be checked at R. Jehudah’s three break points.</i> and you cannot deduce anything. That which was said, “old from the preceding year, very old aged three years<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 6:3</a>. Wine sold as old is warranted to be aged at least a full 12 months.</i>”, should we say because of the tasting? This means that any time it is declared old he has to warrant it for two years<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The year it was made and the next.</i>. This means that very old [wine] has to be warranted until Tabernacles. This means that he has to check every year<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The seller who does not check all his wine after Tabernacles cannot sell his wine without risking to have to take it back. The entire paragraph only deals with money matters, not with heave since it is forbidden to give heave from one year’s produce for that of another year.</i>. How does he check? May one check one amphora on which all others depend that they would not become vinegar? Rebbi Shammai said, there are people who knock on the barrel from the outside and know what is inside<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Vintners do not have to open the amphoras and taste; they learn how to check quickly and accurately; the rules of warranty do not require an undue amount of work.</i>.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sent a bill of divorce to his wife and caught up with the agent, or he sent another agent after him and said: the bill of divorce I gave you is annulled, it is annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">It is not active and cannot be reactivated. If the husband changed his mind again, he would have to write a new bill.</i>. If he met his wife or he sent an agent to her and said: the bill of divorce I sent to you is annulled, it is annulled. After the bill of divorce came into her possession, he no longer can annul it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sent a bill of divorce to his wife,” etc. This disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said that a person invalidates the agent appointed by him by a declaration<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If a person can declare the agency void by a declaration at his place without informing the agent, why does the Mishnah accept voiding the agency only if either the agent for delivery or the recipient of the bill is informed of the annulment? R. Joḥanan’s statement is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.8.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:9</a>; in the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59a" href="/Kiddushin.59a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 59a</a>, his reason is that words can be invalidated by words; he is opposed by R. Simeon ben Laqish. As an Amora, R. Joḥanan should not have the authority to contradict a Mishnah.</i>. He explains it by the stringency of the rules of incest<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Adultery is always included under the rules of incest. R. Joḥanan may hold his opinion in matters of agency in civil law, but marriages and divorces are also matters of criminal law since as long as a woman is married, she is forbidden by criminal law to have relations with men other than her husband. Therefore, agency for delivery of a bill of divorce has to follow the standards of criminal law.</i>. If he appointed an agent to deliver the bill of divorce, the latter has to deliver it in the presence of two [witnesses]; the agent is not counted as one of the two<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the agent represents the husband, he is party and cannot be a witness under the rules of criminal procedure.</i>. If another agent went to invalidate the bill of divorce, he has to invalidate it in the presence of two [witnesses] and the agent is counted as one of the two<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the rules of civil procedure since the husband could have invalidated the agency alone and without witnesses.</i>. If he met him on the road and asked him, what about the bill of divorce I gave you? He answered, I delivered it to her<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot invalidate the divorce retroactively.</i>. While they were talking there, he dropped it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce fell from the agent’s hand. This seems to indicate that it was not delivered and the husband still can invalidate it.</i>. He said, did you not tell me that you delivered it to her? He explained, I delivered it to her and she said, keep it for me. What? Can he be believed since it is in his power to divorce, or can he not be believed because it is in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered; it can easily be resolved by asking the (ex-)wife.</i>? If the agent<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The second agent, sent to inform the first that his agency was terminated. It already was stated in Chapter 3 that the original agent can appoint successor agents.</i> fell sick, how do you treat this? By the rules of divorces or the rules of preliminary marriages? By the rules of divorces one can divorce through the agency of anybody. By the rules of preliminary marriages one cannot preliminarily marry through the agency of anybody<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.5.3">Chapter 3, Notes 114–115</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In earlier times, he could go to a court at another place and annul [the bill of divorce]; Rabban Gamliel the Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Hillel’s grandson, the grandfather of Rabban Gamliel of Jabneh.</i> instituted that one should not do that because of the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If it was not assured that both the agent and the wife were informed of the annulment, all kinds of complications could arise which are described in the Halakhah.</i>. In earlier times, his name and her name and the names of his and her towns could change<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if he lived in the city where the bill was written under a name different from the one he used earlier in the city where his wife still lives, the court in his wife’s city will not recognize the bill as valid for divorce of the wife.</i>; Rabban Gamliel the Elder instituted that one should write Mr. X and all his names, Mrs. Y and all her names, because of the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear, and there were different interpretations given in rabbinic practice, whether the Mishnah requires that a bill enumerate correctly all names under which either party ever lived, or only a statement that the parties (or the town) also were known by other names.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “In earlier times, he could go to court,” etc. Does this<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This question is based on the interpretation of the term “court” in the Mishnah as an <i>ad hoc</i> court, that the husband was able <i>ad hoc</i> to assemble three people as a court, and declare before them that he was revoking the agency given to the agent. This is the explanation accepted in the Babli in the name of Rav Naḥman, 32b.</i> not disagree with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said that nobody can annul an agency by speech<sup class="footnote-marker">2,</sup><i class="footnote">If a person can declare the agency void by a declaration at his place without informing the agent, why does the Mishnah accept voiding the agency only if either the agent for delivery or the recipient of the bill is informed of the annulment? R. Joḥanan’s statement is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.8.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:9</a>; in the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59a" href="/Kiddushin.59a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 59a</a>, his reason is that words can be invalidated by words; he is opposed by R. Simeon ben Laqish. As an Amora, R. Joḥanan should not have the authority to contradict a Mishnah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For money transactions, this is credited to R. Johanan in the Babli, 32b.</i>. Explain it in a fully constituted court<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He will hold that even in earlier times the agency could be revoked only by a public act of the local court who was presumed to transmit a copy of the document to the court at the wife’s place of residence.</i>.
“Because of the public good.” They say in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: That she should not risk bastardy<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 33a" href="/Gittin.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, this is R. Joḥanan’s opinion.</i>. Some say in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: That she should not remain anchored<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This is a technical term. Just as an anchored ship cannot move, so an abandoned woman without a clear bill of divorce cannot move into another man’s house.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 33a" href="/Gittin.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, this is R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion.</i>. He who said, that she should not remain anchored, [is afraid that] she turns out to be anchored if she thought that he annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">That the bill of divorce delivered to her was worthless since the husband could have annulled it at a place unknown to her.</i> when he did not annul. He who said, that she should not risk bastardy, [is afraid that] her children will be bastards if she thought that he did not annul when he annulled<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">She received the bill in due form in the presence of two witnesses. The husband can later reappear when she is remarried and declare her divorce void and her children from the second husband bastards born in adultery.</i>, she goes and marries without a bill of divorce. If she thought that he annulled when he did not annul, another man comes and marries her preliminarily; the preliminary marriage is valid but she thinks it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Any adulterous or incestuous marriage is null and void (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 64</a>).</i>. She waits until her first husband dies, then she goes and marries [another man]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Then in reality she is [preliminarily] married to the man to whom she thinks she is not married; her new [definitive] marriage is adulterous and her children bastards. In all these cases, the adultery is unknown to her and the world; the children are bastards only in the eyes of Heaven; it is the duty of the judicial system to see to it that such cases do not occur.</i>; the result is that her children will be bastards. Rebbi Mana said, I went with a caravan and heard Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa and Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: That she should not risk bastardy. Rav Huna said, even the one who said that she should not remain anchored also agrees that she should not risk bastardy.
If he transgressed and annulled? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 33a" href="/Gittin.33a">Babli 33a</a>. Did Rabban Gamliel the Elder decree that any annulment not in the presence of agent or wife was null and void?</i>: “If he annulled it is annulled, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he can neither annul it nor add to his condition.” Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says it correctly<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the names of Rebbi and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel are switched here and in the next sentence. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 33b" href="/Gittin.33b">Babli, 33b</a>, decides practice following Rebbi.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi? It is the word of the Torah that he has the power to annul<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical text decrees that the husband has to hand his wife a bill of divorce if he has decided to divorce her. The text contains no hints to any restriction as to the exercise of his free will.</i> but they<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the rabbinic authorities of the first half of the first century C. E.</i> said, he shall not annul. Can their word uproot the words of the Torah? But is it not from the Torah that one may [give as heave] olives for oil and grapes for wine, but because of robbing the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumot</i> 1:4, Mishnah and Note 137. Biblical heave is due only for “grain, cider, and oil”. The prohibition to satisfy the tithing duty with unprocessed grapes and olives is strict only according to the House of Hillel; they hold that if olives were given as heave for olive oil then both the olives and the oil remain <i>ṭevel</i>, untithed, by rabbinic decree and their consumption is a deadly sin.</i> they said not only that one may not give heave [in this way] but if he transgressed and gave, his heave is not heave. Rebbi Oshaia bar Abba said to Rebbi Yudan the Prince: Who could tell exactly what your ancestor<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In modern Hebrew, סבא means “grandfather”. In the Talmudim, generally it means “old man”; since “grandfather” usually is formulated as ,אֲבִי אַבָּא סַבָּא can also mean “great-grandfather”. The interpretation of the word depends on the reading of the names in this discussion.</i> meant?
<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is quoted by R. Nissim Gerondi in his commentary to Alfassi (#456). While his spelling is still more babylonized than the ms. text and it is not clear that his deviations from our text are from his text or are his interpretation, his text is very clear in its meaning and is followed in the commentary.</i>“Mr. X and all his names.” Think of it, if his name was Reuben and he went away<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nissim: אפיך “he changed it to”.</i> under the name of Simeon. But: “I am X and<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nissim: עם כל שם “with every name”, i.e., all the names have to be spelled out. This is the ms. text later in the name of R. Ilai, so probably the ms. text here is original.</i> every other name which I have.” <sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Similar texts are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 34b" href="/Gittin.34b">Babli, 34b</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.5">Tosephta 6:5</a>.</i>“In addition, they said if he had two wives, one in Judea and one in Galilee, and he is known by two names, one in Judea and one in Galilee. He used [his name] in Judea to divorce the one in Galilee and [his name] in Galilee to divorce the one in Judea. Neither one is divorced.” This <i>baraita</i> if he was in Judea<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The translation uses the text of R. Nissim: כשהיה ביהודה. The ms. text could be read as: If he used the name from Judea,</i> and wrote to divorce in Galilee or in Galilee and wrote to divorce in Judea. But if he was in Judea and wrote to divorce in Judea, or in Galilee and wrote to divorce in Galilee, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If only the local name was used. The divorce is valid only if both the witnesses signing the document and the witnesses to the delivery are able to identify the husband; therefore, both names (possibly with an indication where which name is being used) are required.</i>. Rebbi Ilai said, according to the rules he has to say: “I am X with every other name which I have in Galilee.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nissim: עם כל שם “with every name”, i.e., all the names have to be spelled out. This is the ms. text later in the name of R. Ilai, so probably the ms. text here is original.</i>” If he was at another place, he can divorce either one he wishes<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nissim: באיזה מהם שירצה “with any [name] he chooses”. If he is not known locally, he can identify himself in any way he chooses (as long as the name is recognized as his at the place of delivery of the bill.)</i>. Rebbi Yose said, one says that if he did it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If the bill was written without asking for rabbinical advice. This is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 34b:3:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.34b.3.1">Tosaphot 34b, <i>s. v.</i> והוא</a>.</i>. But to start out<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If he asks for instructions, he has to be told to enumerate all names under which he is known. (It is not clear whether the reference, in the edict on Jewish names by the German Emperor Joseph II, to Jews having different names at different places, refers to a reality in the 18th Century or to the talmudic discussion.)</i> he has to follow Rebbi Ilai. Rebbi Abin said, if he is known by (another) name at another place, he has to mention all three.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 10:4</a>. The statements of rabbis Abba and La (Ilai) are also quoted there in Halakhah 10:4.</i>: “One writes a bill of divorce for a man even if his wife is not with him, and a receipt<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A receipt for the amount of the <i>ketubah</i> due her at her divorce.</i> for a woman even if her husband is not with her, and the husband pays the fee<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s fee.</i>.” Rebbi Abba said, he<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe.</i> has to know both of them. Rebbi La<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Ilai. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 167b" href="/Bava_Batra.167b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 167b</a>, his statement is attributed to Rav.</i> said, he has to know the husband for the bill of divorce and the wife for her receipt. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi [ <sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, a name is missing. R. Abun bar Ḥiyya is known to have asked questions of R. Ilai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 3:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.3.5.2-4"><i>Yoma</i> 3:5</a>).</i> ]: Think of it, if he brought another woman and divorced by means of her<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Can the agent of an absent husband not fraudulently give the bill of divorce to another woman, not the wife, and have that woman foreclose the <i>ketubah</i> from the husband’s local property?</i>. He said to him, let the witnesses come and testify. But did not Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.3.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:3, Note 56</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 10:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.10.3.3"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:5, Note 96</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:10:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.10.2-3"><i>Baba Batra</i> 10:16</a>, 14d 1.26); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 3a" href="/Gittin.3a">Babli 3a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 18b" href="/Ketubot.18b"><i>Ketubot</i> 18b</a>. Signatories of documents cannot claim that they never signed unless they claim that the signatures are forgeries.</i> that they considered witnesses who signed a document as if their testimony had been cross-examined in court? There, if they said, we did not sign at all. But here if they say, we signed this but not that<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses can identify the persons for whom they signed; this is not a second testimony.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Abba: “In earlier times, his name and her name and the names of his and her towns could change.” If he knows them, why would he change their names<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the scribe knew all persons involved, he could not be tricked into writing a misleading document.</i>? There are people who know others by sight but do not know their names. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Ilai: “Rabban Gamliel the Elder instituted that one should write Mr. X and all his names, Mrs. Y and all her names<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah treats husband and wife on an equal footing but R. Ilai does not.</i>, because of the public good.” The Mishnah about one who is forced to divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">One of those cases in which the wife can force a divorce (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.8.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:10</a>) when the husband’s cooperation cannot be taken for granted.</i>, Rebbi Ila speaks about one who divorces on his initiative; some want to say if he divorces at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Where he has to prove his and his wife’s identities to the scribe.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This discusses the statement of the Tosephta that the husband has to pay the expenses for the divorce document. The statement that the husband cannot change the conditions of the divorce once the document is no longer in his hands was discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.6">Chapter 3, Note 45</a>.</i> For example Doso, Dodo’s brother, divorced his wife. The case came before the rabbis who said that she should pay the fee<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 168a" href="/Bava_Batra.168a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 168a</a>, agrees that current rabbinic practice requires the wife to pay the scribe to prevent the husband from dragging his feet once the couple has decided on a divorce.</i>. But did we not state: “the husband pays the fee”? Rebbi Ilai in the name of Samuel: If she forgives him her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If he makes money out of the divorce, he will not drag his feet and one can make him pay.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A widow can be paid from the orphans’ property only by an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If the widow continued to live in her husband’s house, she is suspected to have taken from her husband’s property more than was necessary for her guaranteed support and, therefore, if she decides to leave that house she cannot collect her <i>ketubah</i> without swearing that nothing of her <i>ketubah</i> already came into her hand, similar to a woman who had received a down payment on her <i>ketubah</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.7"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.8.2-5">9</a>.</i>. When they avoided letting her swear<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis became worried that the widow while caring for the orphans took things which she thought were payment for her work but which legally should be counted as part payment of the <i>ketubah</i>; if she then swore that she had received nothing, the widow involuntarily transgressed the prohibition of false oaths and the rabbis the prohibition of “putting a stone in the path of the blind.”</i>, Rabban Gamliel the Elder instituted that she should make a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A vow that she would prohibit on herself the use of anything (food, vessel, place) chosen by the orphans if she had received any down payment for her <i>ketubah</i>. For these “vows of mortification”, see Introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>.</i> on the instruction of the orphans for anything they would decide on and collect her <i>ketubah</i>, and that witnesses sign the bill of divorce because of the public good. Hillel instituted <i>prozbol</i> for the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Prozbol</i> is a document which turns a private debt (subject to the laws of revocation in the Sabbatical year) into a public debt (exempt from these laws) in order to maintain an operating banking system; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:3 ff.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A widow can be paid from the orphans’ property only,” etc. Earlier they swore falsely and buried their children, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 2:30" href="/Jeremiah.2.30"><i>Jer</i>. 2:30</a>. For the statement that children die for the parents’ sins of vows cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:7, Note 66</a>.</i>: “For vain [vows] I smote your children.” In addition, they fear vows more than oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For example, there is an annual revocation rite, <i>Kol Nidre</i>, for vows with oaths only mentioned as an afterthought; cf. introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>, Note 4.</i>. If she transgressed and swore? Rav Huna said, if she swore, she swore<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If she swears on her own initiative to expedite the process, it is valid and she has to be paid. The same statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 35a" href="/Gittin.35a">Babli, 35a</a>.</i>. Rav said to his daughter-in-law: If I were not a person readily making concessions, even the cover on your head would be my property. Samuel said, she acquires the garments which she is wearing<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The same disagreement, whether or not garments, which the husband has to supply during the marriage, are counted against the <i>ketubah</i>, is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 54a" href="/Ketubot.54a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 54a</a>.</i>. A Mishnah supports Samuel: “Neither his wife’s garments nor his children’s are his property<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>‘Arakhin</i> 6:5. The family’s clothing cannot be counted as the husband’s property in bankruptcy proceedings. In the Babli, nevertheless practice is decided following Rav, against the Yerushalmi.</i>.”
Rav Huna said, why did they require a date on the bill of divorce? Because of an actual case. It so happened that a man was married to his sister’s daughter who committed adultery when she was still married. He predated the bill of divorce and said, it is better she should be judged as single rather than as a married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 17" href="/Gittin.17">Babli, 17a/b</a>, R. Joḥanan refers to this story; the editors of the Babli assumed that this Yerushalmi was generally known.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:3, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.2-6">Notes 83–88</a>. The Leiden ms. is denoted by <b>ל</b>, the Rome ms. by <b>ר</b>.</i> Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The readings from <i>Ševi‘it</i>, “<i>Rebbi</i> Ḥuna”, “<i>Rebbi</i> Jacob”, are the only ones which make sense in place and time.</i> said, I asked before Rebbi Jacob ben Aḥa: Following him who says tithes are from their words. But following him who says tithes are from the Torah, does Hillel institute anything against the words of the Torah? Rebbi Yose said, from the moment that Israel was exiled to Babylonia, did they not become free from all commandments connected with the Land, but the remission of debts applies both in the Land and outside the Land from the words of the Torah? Rebbi Yose turned and said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:2" href="/Deuteronomy.15.2"><i>Deut</i>. 15:2</a>) “this is the word of the abandonment, remit” as long as abandonment is followed in the Land as a word of the Torah, remission of debts applies both in the Land and outside the Land from the words of the Torah, but when abandonment is followed in the Land as their word, remission of debts applies both in the Land and outside the Land from their word. There, they say that even one who holds that tithes are from the Torah will hold that the Sabbatical is from their word. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:2" href="/Deuteronomy.15.2"><i>Deut</i>. 15:2</a>): “This is the word of the remission, remit!” Rebbi says, two remissions are the Sabbatical and the Jubilee. As long as the Jubilee is operative, the Sabbatical is from words of the Torah. If the Jubilees are abolished, the Sabbatical is operative from their words. When were the Jubilees abolished? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:10" href="/Leviticus.25.10"><i>Lev</i>. 25:10</a>) “For all its inhabitants.” In the time when they lived on it, not when they went from it into exile. If they lived on it but did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly is a scribal error; see the variant readings.</i> intermingle, the tribe of Judah in Benjamin, and the tribe of Benjamin in Judah, I could think that the Jubilee was operative. The verse mentions its inhabitants, “All its inhabitants;” you find that when the tribes of Reuben, Gad, and half the tribe of Manasseh went into exile, the Jubilees were disestablished.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a slave was kidnapped and ransomed<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">A Jewish community at another place ransomed him. Since he is under the laws of the Torah, any Jewish community is obligated to ransom him.</i>, if it was as a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If they knew that he was a slave and ransomed him as such.</i> he shall serve<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">His former master, who has to indemnify the community which provided the ransom.</i>, if as a free person he shall not serve<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">A person who was a free Jew even for one moment cannot be enslaved.</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, in any case he shall serve<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a slave can be freed only by a formal act of manumission, otherwise he will be forbidden to marry a Jewish woman. Since the act of ransom does not change the slave’s status in marriage law, it cannot change it in civil law.</i>. A slave whom his master gave as mortgage to others and then freed<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The act of manumission breaks the chattel mortgage; this is declared as a general principle in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 40b" href="/Gittin.40b">Babli, 40b</a>.</i> in strict law does not owe anything<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since the evil act was his master’s, not his own.</i>, but for the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Lest the creditor come and enslave a free person.</i> one forces the master to formally manumit him and he<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The slave, who owns no money.</i> writes a bond for his own value. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only the one who manumits writes<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The slave is free and free from debt.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a slave was kidnapped and ransomed,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Ḥizqiah: It would be logical that even as a slave he should not have to serve<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 37b" href="/Gittin.37b">Babli, 37b</a>, Ḥizqiah is quoted in a discussion whether the occurred when the owner still had hope to recover his slave or whether he had given up this hope. Since the case that the owner had given up hope ransom mentioned in the Mishnah and therefore relinquished his claim to ownership is discussed later, the Yerushalmi must hold that the Mishnah deals with the case that the slave still was a slave in fact, not only in name, i.e., that the original owner had not given up hope to recover him. As R. Yose explains later, the slave should not have to serve since he was ransomed as a Jew.</i>. And why did they say, he has to serve? That a slave should not run away to the kidnappers<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Offer himself to kidnappers as a sure way to gain his freedom.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, a <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta 3:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 37b" href="/Gittin.37b">Babli 37b</a>.</i> implies this: “His master pays his ransom and frees him.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If the master wishes, he may free him once the community which provided the ransom is reimbursed.</i>” They said that only about his master, not anybody else<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what is the meaning of the <i>baraita</i> clause that the master frees the slave. The Tosephta implies that the community which ransoms the slave sends his master the bill with the slave. No other person has an interest in the slave.</i>. Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Yasa: It would be logical that even as a free person he should have to serve<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since there was no formal manumission, there seems to be no reason why the slave, redeemed in the erroneous belief that he was a free Jew, should not remain a slave.</i>. Why did they say that he does not have to serve? Not to damage the reputations of free persons<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If it were possible later to enslave a person redeemed as a free person, nobody would be safe.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, but it is so: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, in any case he shall serve; just as Israel are obligated to ransom free persons so they are obligated to ransom slaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta 3:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 37b" href="/Gittin.37b">Babli 37b</a>.</i>” The first Tanna is of the opinion that they are obligated to ransom free persons but they are not obligated to ransom slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rabban Gamliel has to spell out his opinion in the Tosephta, it follows that the dissenting opinion also applies in this case. The slave of a Jew is obligated to keep the Sabbath and eat only kosher food; since he cannot do that as slave of a Gentile, the Jewish community is responsible for him.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, it is a Saboraic principle that “practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in his statements in the Mishnah” almost always (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 77a" href="/Ketubot.77a"><i>Ketubot</i> 77a</a>); the Babli does not have to state that practice follows him.</i>.
Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Yossi ben Shaul said in the name of Rebbi: A person who had given up hope to recover his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general rule that anything abandoned becomes ownerless and may be taken by anybody who finds it. If a person gives up hope to recover something he lost, this is abandonment (<i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 2:1, first line). It is stated later that an adult slave, being able to become a responsible person, acquires himself if he becomes ownerless. Since then he is a full Jew, if the owner later finds him, he cannot reduce him to slavery. But the former slave cannot marry a Jewish partner without a bill of manumission.</i> is not entitled to enslave him but has to write him a bill of manumission. Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39a" href="/Gittin.39a">Babli, 39a</a>, the entire statement is in R. Joḥanan’s name.</i> heard this and said, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi taught us that correctly. Did they not infer the rules of a bill of manumission from women<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is nowhere explicit in the Yerushalmi or the halakhic Midrashim; it is found in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 29b" href="/Gittin.29b">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 29b</a>,41b; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 10a" href="/Chagigah.10a"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 10a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23a" href="/Kiddushin.23a"><i>Qiddušin</i> 23a</a>) from a comparison of the language used for the manumission of a handmaid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev.</i> 19:20</a>) and the divorce of a wife (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut.</i> 24:1</a>).</i>? Just as a woman is not freed if [the husband] has given up hope<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">If a woman left her husband and the latter has given up hope that she ever would return to him, she still cannot remarry without a formal bill of divorce.</i>, but she needs a bill of divorce from him, so a slave is not freed if [the master] has given up hope, but he needs a bill of manumission.
Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a slave fled from the kidnappers to his master, the master may not enslave him<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was not ransomed, it is as if his owner had given up hope to recover him.</i> but has to write him a bill of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In order to permit him to marry a Jewish partner.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody declares his slave as ownerless he can neither enslave him nor write him a bill of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since a bill of manumission can be written only by the owner and this master has formally declined ownership, there is nobody who can write a bill of manumission for this slave. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39" href="/Gittin.39">Babli, 39a/b</a>, holds in the name of R. Joḥanan that the owner can only renounce his ownership in money matters; for ceremonial law he can divest himself of ownership only by writing a bill of manumission. The Babli agrees that if the original owner dies before writing the bill of manumission, his children do not inherit any rights to the slave and the latter cannot be rescued from his state <i>in limbo</i>, free but unable to marry.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said to him, everybody says that he needs one<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39a" href="/Gittin.39a">Babli, 39a</a>, this is a statement of Ulla in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>; do you say that he does not need? Would he hinder him from eating the Passah [sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A person is prevented from eating the Passah sacrifice if any member of his <i>familia</i> is not circumcised (or if a female slave, not inducted into the Jewish faith by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:43-45" href="/Exodus.12.43-45">Ex. 12:43–45</a>.</i>? As it was stated: “The slave of any man, acquisition of his money, if you circumcise him.” Anytime he serves his master, he prevents his master from eating the Passah; if he does not serve his master, he does not prevent his master from eating the Passah<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the slave declared ownerless is no longer part of his owner’s <i>familia</i>; the master no longer is a patron.</i>.
If [the master] blinded [the slave’s] two eyes simultaneously, or broke two of his teeth simultaneously, the slave thereby obtains his freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex</i>. 21:26–27</a> ordains freedom to a slave whose owner destroys one of his eyes or teeth. By rabbinic interpretation, this is extended to all important body parts.</i>. One after the other, he obtains his freedom from the first [injury] and [the master] has to pay for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">For the second injury, the slave can already require the damages due to a free person.</i>. Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Following the opinion that [the master] does not have to write a bill of manumission for him. But following the opinion that [the master] has to write a bill of manumission for him, can you say that he has to pay him<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42a" href="/Gittin.42a">Babli, 42a</a> (quoted also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 24b" href="/Kiddushin.24b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 24b</a>), this is a dispute among Tannaïm which is resolved by deciding that a bill of manumission is not needed for injury to teeth or eyes since the verse represents the bill of manumission, but is needed for all other body parts whose loss results in freedom to the slave only by rabbinic interpretation. The following sentence shows that this interpretation is not accepted in the Yerushalmi. In case a bill of manumission is required, damages can be claimed only after delivery of the bill.<br>It seems that the <i>baraita</i> in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Mišpaṭim</i> 9, contains a polemic against the argument here since it emphasizes that the verse requires to send the slave to freedom for his tooth or for his eye; the singular implies that the slave can request payment for the second injury independent of his legal status.</i>? Not only does this<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Even an authority who requires a bill of manumission for the slave freed by the Torah will not insist that the rules of these bills be followed to the letter but all forms of manumission accepted in Gentile courts are acceptable.</i> apply to one who gains his freedom for a tooth or an eye, from where also that he may gain his freedom by a cap<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">A Phrygian cap given to the slave by his master as a sign of freedom.</i>, or הרנירק טיאונוס<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">These words are unexplained. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20a" href="/Gittin.20a">Babli 20a</a>, speaking of manumission in general, not of the slave freed by the Torah, it is stated that a bill of manumission engraved on a writing tablet is valid, but not one written on a cap and אנדוכתרי (var. אנדרכתן, אדוכתן, אנדרוכתרי) explained in <i>Arukh</i> as text woven into the cloth (but see next paragraph).<br>In <i>Massekhet ‘Avadim</i>, end of Chap. 3, the <i>baraita</i> quoted here appears in one ms. as: “If he broke his two teeth simultaneously, or blinded his two eyes simultaneously, the slave goes to freedom and is not paid anything, one after the other he obtains his freedom for the first and is paid for the second. If he cut his flesh or destroyed a tooth which did not come out yet, he is freed by אנטוקטא, a tablet, a writing tablet, but not by קיפה of kings.” The word אנטוקטא was read by Pineles, דרכה של תורה, as <i>vindicta</i>, a reading accepted by all later lexicographers. It is likely that אנדוכתרי and its variants also represent forms of <i>vindicta</i> in Babylonian spelling, which does not show the usual exactness of transliterations in the Yerushalmi, standing for <i>manumissio per vindictam</i>, manumission by having the slave touched with a <i>vindicta</i>, or staff, by a duly authorized government official. Such a manumission is invalid in any case not ordered by the Torah.</i>, or the king’s freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The acts of manumission recognized in Roman law, <i>manumissio per vindictam, inter amicos</i>, and per epistulam, also manumission in Egyptian law by declaration before a notary public and payment of the applicable tax (cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Greco-Roman Egypt in Light of the Papyri</i>, New York 1944, p.73).</i>? The verse says, “he shall send him to freedom”, in any way. What is the status of his<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">This should read “her” here and in the sequel since a male slave cannot marry and has no family relationship with his children.</i> children? The children of him who leaves for a tooth or an eye are slaves, the children of him who leaves by the loss of hope are free. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that is unreasonable; it is the other way around. The children of one who leaves for a tooth or an eye are free because the Torah freed him. The children of one who leaves by the loss of hope are slaves because the Torah did not free him.
If a convert died<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">He failed to start a Jewish family. Since he no longer is a member of his Gentile family and did not acquire Jewish heirs, his property becomes ownerless.</i> and Jews plundered his property, if there were slaves, either adult or underage, they gain their freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since they become ownerless, they go free, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.4">Note 94</a>. This Tanna holds that the ownerless slave does not need a bill of manumission. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39a" href="/Gittin.39a">Babli, 39a</a>, Rav Naḥman explains that since the rules of bills of manumission are derived from those of bills of divorce, since a widow does not have a bill of divorce, the slave who gains freedom by his master’s death does not need a bill.</i>. Abba Shaul said, the adults gain their freedom, the minors do not become free. Why? They are no longer property after their owner’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">And can be acquired by anybody who takes them.</i>. Then even adults? Rebbi Abba said, since adults are competent to acquire themselves, they gain their freedom. Minors, who are not competent to acquire for themselves, do not gain their freedom. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, practice follows Abba Shaul<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39" href="/Gittin.39">Babli, 39a/b</a>, agrees and is ambivalent in the matter, as is the Yerushalmi.</i>. The opinions of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi are contradictory. There, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi and Rebbi Yossi ben Shaul in the name of Rebbi said: A person who has given up hope to recover his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general rule that anything abandoned becomes ownerless and may be taken by anybody who finds it. If a person gives up hope to recover something he lost, this is abandonment (<i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 2:1, first line). It is stated later that an adult slave, being able to become a responsible person, acquires himself if he becomes ownerless. Since then he is a full Jew, if the owner later finds him, he cannot reduce him to slavery. But the former slave cannot marry a Jewish partner without a bill of manumission.</i> is not entitled to enslave him<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">And he asserts that the master has to write a bill of manumission for the slave. But the adult slave who gains his freedom by his master’s death does not need a bill of manumission.</i>. They said, what Rebbi Joshua said about enslaving, if he did not give, he did not give<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The owner cannot be forced to write a bill of manumission for the absconded slave.</i>; perhaps practice would be to enslave him, therefore he has to write him a bill of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">It may be that any ownerless slave is automatically free without a written document; it also may be that the runaway slave whose master has given up hope of recovery is not free. Therefore, that slave cannot marry in the Jewish community without being formally freed.</i>. And here, he says so! We heard that Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi taught us that correctly, perhaps practice follows Abba Shaul<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Practice follows Abba Shaul, not as a necessity but as recommended action.</i>. The slave girl of Rabba bar Zuṭra ran away and he gave up hope to recover her. He went to ask Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. They said, he is not entitled to enslave her<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he found her later, he could not bring any action to recover her as a servant.</i>. How is it to write her a bill of manumission? They said to him, if you wrote, you did the right thing<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot be forced to write a bill but, since the runaway girl probably will reappear as a Jewish woman in some distant community and marry there, for the benefit of the community it is appropriate that the former owner write her a bill of manumission and appoint a local person to receive it in her stead (since one may give benefits to absent persons without their knowledge) to legitimize the children from any marriage she might contract.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose: A slave who married as a free person<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 39b-40a" href="/Gittin.39b-40a">Babli, 39b/40a</a>, and the <i>editio princeps</i> read בַּת חוֹרִין “a free woman” instead of בֶּן חוֹרִין “a free man” in the ms. One has to assume that the Venice typesetter was influenced by the Babli since the ms. shows no correction by the editor of the print; the text makes perfect sense as it stands. Since a slave cannot marry, if the master organizes a marriage for him it is obvious (a) that the owner manumits the slave and (b) that the woman he marries must be free.</i> in the presence of his master gained his freedom. Rebbi Joḥanan said, yesterday I was sitting and formulating: If somebody writes a document of preliminary marriage for his slave or a document of definitive marriage for his girl slave, Rebbi says, they acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the marriage document as a bill of manumission; the slave acquires his own legal personality with the marriage document.</i>, but the Sages say, they did not acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since a slave’s hand is his master’s hand, a slave cannot acquire a document for himself unless he first was manumitted.</i>. The first Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael ben R. Yose.</i> follows Rebbi: he acquired.
<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.2-17"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16">1</a>. 24 ff.; variants noted <b>פ</b>.</i> Who frees<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last part of the Mishnah, <i>viz.</i>, the mortgaged slave who was manumitted.</i>? Rav says, either his first or his second master<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Both the owner and the mortgage holder can free the slave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 40a" href="/Gittin.40a">Babli 40a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan says, only his first master alone is able to free<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">A mortgage holder is not a proprietor.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai objected before Rebbi Yose: Does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree with Rav? If a Jew gave a loan to a Gentile on the latter’s leavened matter, it is permitted after Passover. If you say that the Jew has property rights<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows <b>פ</b>.</i> in it, it would be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:2</a> states that leavened matter pledged to a Jew is forbidden after Passover. This is explained in both Talmudim to refer to a contract which in case of foreclosure would retroactively transfer property rights to the creditor from the moment the loan was given. If the loan was given before Passover and foreclosed after Passover, the leavened matter was in the Jew’s possession during Passover and is permanently forbidden to Jews. In the Yerushalmi <i>baraita</i> one assumes that the contract states explicitly that the creditor would acquire property rights only after foreclosure.</i>. What does Rav do with this? Rebbi Yudan said, manumission is made easy, as it was stated: If somebody gives his slave as mortgage, if he sold him, he is not sold; if he freed him, he is freed<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:1, Notes 23–27</a>.</i>. The strength of Rebbi Joḥanan is from the following: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, only the manumittor writes<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The slave is free and free from debt.</i>. If somebody mortgaged his field to another, then went and sold it, can the creditor not come and foreclose? Rebbi Abbahu said, in this matter Rebbi Joḥanan opened for us a door to illuminate<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows <b>פ</b>.</i>. We do not find that a slave can again be enslaved after having been freed<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The moment the slave gains his freedom he is a full Jew and is protected by all biblical laws regarding treatment of converts.</i>. If that is so, he should not have to write a bond for his value! Rebbi Ila said, it is better that a person say to him, give me the 200 <i>zuz</i> which you owe me than say to him, you are my slave<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The only reason the rabbis require the ex-slave to write a bond to the creditor is for his own protection.</i>! The rabbis of Caesarea say in Rebbi Nasa’s<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows <b>פ</b>.</i> name: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel follows Rebbi Meïr. Just as Rebbi Meїr imposes a fine for words, so Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel imposes a fine for words. As it was stated: With a bond documenting both principal and interest one can collect neither principal nor interest, the words of Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10a" href="/Bava_Metzia.10a"><i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 10a 1.60</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 72a" href="/Gittin.72a">Babli 72a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30b" href="/Bava_Kamma.30b"><i>Baba Qama</i> 30b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 94b" href="/Bava_Batra.94b"><i>Baba Batra</i> 94b</a>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel holds that the manumission of a pledged slave is illegal and therefore the perpetrator has to pay.</i>. But the Sages say, one collects the principal but not the interest<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The bond is valid and those parts which conform to the law can be enforced.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person who is half slave and half free works for his master one day and for himself one day, the words of the House of Hillel. The House of Shammai say, you provided for his master but you did not provide for him. He can marry neither a slave woman<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The free part in him is forbidden any sexual relation with a slave.</i> nor a free woman<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The free woman is forbidden any sexual relation with the slave part in him.</i>: shall he be alone? But the world was created only for procreation and increase, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 45:18" href="/Isaiah.45.18"><i>Is</i>. 45:18</a>.</i>: “He did not create it to be empty, He formed it to be settled.” For the public good one forces his master to manumit him and he writes a bond for half his value<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">This entered Roman law by a decree of Justinian, c. 1. C. 7,7. Most earlier jurists rejected the idea of partial manumission; cf. R. Taubenschlag (Note 104), p. 75.</i>. The House of Hillel changed and instructed following the House of Shammai.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person who is half slave and half free,” etc. How is it possible that anyone be half slave and half free? Explain it either following Rebbi, since Rebbi said that a person can free half of his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 41b" href="/Gittin.41b">Babli (41b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23b" href="/Gittin.23b">23b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25b" href="/Temurah.25b"><i>Temurah</i> 25b</a>) one holds that in the opinion of the rabbis opposed to Rebbi a person cannot free half of his slave but the freedom of a slave can be bought in installments.</i>, or following everybody in the case of a slave belonging to two partners, when one of them manumitted his part. If he found anything of value, on his master’s day it belongs to his master<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since at least for R. Meїr a slave’s hand is his master’s hand (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:1, Note 10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.9.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 11:8, Note 70</a>), what he finds belongs to his master.</i>, on his own day it belongs to him. But would he not on the next day turn it over<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Reading יעברה for יעבדה. Since a slave cannot own property, all he has would be intermittently his owner’s. This is legally an impossible situation; even without the argument of the House of Shammai it would be necessary to totally free the partially manumitted person or to declare the partial manumission as void.</i> to his master? If he entered a preliminary marriage. On his master’s day, one would not consider his marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">As a slave, he cannot marry. A woman who receives valuables from a slave for a preliminary marriage is free to marry any other man without a divorce; she is not considered married.</i>, on his own day one would consider his marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">While the marriage could not be consummated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.5.1">Note 132</a>), there seems to be no reason why the preliminary marriage should not be valid according to the House of Hillel, who hold that on alternate days he is a free person.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya say in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: If a person who is half slave and half free entered a preliminary marriage, one does not consider his marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">By a rabbinic decree, the half-free person is disqualified from entering a preliminary marriage.</i>. In parallel, if he divorced one does not consider his divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If a half-free person entered a preliminary marriage and then, upon being informed that his act was invalid, gave the woman a bill of divorce; the recipient was never either married or divorced and is free to marry a Cohen.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Moed Katan 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Moed_Katan.1.10.1">Mishnah <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 1:7</a>.</i>, we have stated: “One does not marry women on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The intermediate days of the Passover and Tabernacles holidays when acquisitions are permitted and in biblical law the man could acquire a wife and the woman the <i>ketubah</i> rights.</i>.” Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of refraining from being fruitful and increase<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition to organize weddings on the intermediate days of a holiday is purely rabbinical, so that people should not refrain from marrying during the rest of the year in order to save money.<br>In <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 1:7 1. 26ff. and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 8b-9a" href="/Gittin.8b-9a">Babli, 8b/9a</a>, the reason for the prohibition is a matter of disagreement among several authors.</i>. They asked before Rebbi Yose: May a slave marry a woman on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The question is difficult to understand since the slave cannot marry as long as he is a slave but is a full Jew subject to all Jewish laws the moment he is manumitted. Maybe the question is about a slave manumitted on the holiday.</i>? He told them, let us hear from the following: “Shall he be alone? But the world was created only for procreation and increase!” And Rebbi Simeon bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Anybody commanded to be fruitful and increase is forbidden to marry on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Even if in this case the marriage has to be postponed for a few days. A slightly different version of this paragraph is in <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 1:7 80d l. 30, quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Chagigah 2b" href="/Tosafot_on_Chagigah.2b">Tosaphot <i>Ḥagiga</i> 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 41b" href="/Gittin.41b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 41b, <i>s. v</i>. לא</a>. Tosaphot point out that in a certain sense a male slave cannot fulfill the commandment to be fruitful because he cannot have any family relationship with his biological children.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sells his slave to Gentiles or outside the Land, the slave gains his freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Since a slave is required to obey all of the biblical prohibitions imposed on Jews, a sale to a Gentile is equivalent to the slave’s removal from Jewish practice. Also, there are more commandments to be observed in the Land than abroad. In both cases, it is a rabbinic decree that the master has to buy the slave back and then formally manumit him. (In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 259, this is connected to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:16-17" href="/Deuteronomy.23.16-17"><i>Deut</i>. 23:16–17</a>.)</i>. One does not ransom kidnap victims for more than the going rate<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">If the kidnap victims were sold as slaves on the open market. If it were known that Jews be ready to ransom their co-religionists for any price, no Jew would be safe from kidnapping.</i> because of the public good. One does not smuggle kidnap victims out because of the public good; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, because of the welfare of the kidnap victims<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The majority holds that one never tries to free kidnap victims without paying because then the robbers would put their next victims in chains and otherwise mistreat them. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel holds that one may not try to steal the kidnap victims if one cannot free all of them in the hand of a group of kidnappers, because those left behind then would be mistreated. He does not fear an adverse reaction in a larger context.</i>. One does not buy Torah scrolls, phylacteries, and <i>mezuzot</i> from Gentiles for more than their worth because of the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Gentiles should not get the impression that stealing Jewish cult objects might be a particularly profitable business.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sells his slave to Gentiles,” etc. To a Gentile even in the Land. Who frees him? His original owner pays for him and the second frees him<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence and the next should be switched since if the slave was sold to a Gentile it is obvious that the Jewish owner has to manumit the slave; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:5</a> states that the manumission of a Jewish slave by the rules of a Gentile court is invalid. The original text probably was close to Tosephta <i>‘Avodah Zarah</i> 3:18: “If somebody sells his slave to a place outside the Land he gains his freedom and the second master has to write him the bill of manumission.” The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 44b" href="/Gittin.44b">Babli, 44b</a>, infers from the Tosephta that the sale in itself is valid, otherwise the buyer would not have the power to manumit the slave.</i>. “Or outside the Land,” even to a Jew. Let us say that if the second owner knew<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">That the sale was illegal by rabbinic rules.</i>, both of them split the price<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The seller has to repay only half the sale price.</i>; otherwise the first pays for him and the second frees him. “I X ben Y sold my slave Z to Antiochia”, he gains his freedom. But if he is in Lydda, he did not gain his freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">This is much clearer in the Tosephta, <i>loc. cit</i>., in the name of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel: “ ‘I X ben Y sold my slave to Antiochia’, he gains his freedom; ‘to Z the Antiochian who dwells in Lydda’ he does not gain his freedom.” This is qualified in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 44b" href="/Gittin.44b">Babli, 44b</a>, by the statement that the Antiochian has to own an apartment in Lydda.</i>. It was stated: A slave goes outside the Land with his master<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta and the Babli, this is restricted to Syria. There is no indication in the Yerushalmi of any restriction on a person to take his slave with him on a trip as a valet.</i>. Therefore, if he sold him there, he is sold<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slave was legally taken on the trip. The sale is only illegal if contracted in the Land.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">If a diaspora Jew comes to the Land for a visit, his slave cannot refuse to return with him. But if he came to immigrate, the slave acquires the rights of residence. A slightly garbled version is in the Tosephta.</i> came from outside the Land to the Land with the intention to return, one forces him to return. With the intention not to return, one does not force him to return. If he fled from outside the Land to the Land, he<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the slave can take him back. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:16" href="/Deuteronomy.23.16"><i>Deut</i>. 23:16</a>,17 refers to the slave of a Gentile owner.</i> may return him. If he fled from the Land to outside the Land, Rebbi Joshia in the name of the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya said, it is permigtted<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">To forcibly return the slave to the Land.</i>. The great Rebbi Hoshaia said, it is permitted. Rebbi Marinos, the son of the great Rebbi Hoshaia said, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The question arises how the son can disagree with his father.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they do not disagree. He who says, it is permitted, if he is used to flee there. But he who says, it is forbidden, if he is not used to flee there. Rebbi Yose said, they do not disagree. He who says, it is permitted, if he can return him from there; but he who says, it is forbidden, if he cannot return him from there<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the slave gained his freedom when the owner gave up hope to recover him; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.6">Note 88</a>.</i>.
There could be terms<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">If the slave was bought on condition that he could be resold outside the Land, the deal might be valid.</i>. If he entered into terms with him<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If the condition was stipulated with the slave, it is valid.</i>, it is permitted. That means, with adults. With minors, the terms can be with their masters. That is, if the master was a Jew. If he was Gentile? Rebbi Tevele sold his slave and entered into terms with his master<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This could even be a sale within the Land to a Gentile if the buyer guaranteed that the slave could continue following Jewish law, keeping the Sabbath and eating kosher.</i>. Rebbi Isaac the goldsmith had slaves who fled to Bnoton<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Name of an unidentified place, probably a corruption. As a noun, the word means “their daughters”.</i>. He came and asked<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">When the slaves were caught outside the Land, he asked how he could dispose of them there within the rules. The explanation of this passage follows J. N. Epstein, <i>Tarbiz</i> 8 (1937) 316–318, with a note by I. Ostersetzer, <i>Tarbiz</i> 9 (1938) 395–397.</i> Rebbi Immi who instructed him that a sales contract<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὠνή “sales contract, purchase”. The outright sale of the slave outside the Land is forbidden.</i> is forbidden, a loan for use<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀντίχρησις “loan for use,” reading אנטיכריס for אנטריס, which is possible in such a heavily Babylonized text. An antichretic loan is a loan in which the creditor obtains the use of the mortgaged object, and the rental fee, instead of being paid to the borrower, is used to amortize the loan (cf. Taubenschlag, Note 104, p. 218, on antichretic loans of slaves.) Since the slave remains the property of the owner, there is no sale. J. N. Epstein points out that R. Immi in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.2.2"><i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 5:3</a> (10b l.20) explicitly states that antichretic mortgages are valid and do not violate interest prohibitions. (In an earlier article, Epstein had read ἀντίδοσις “exchange”.)</i> is permitted, a cession<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Greek παραχώρησις “cession, surrender, assignment.” Rabbinic law accepts the division between possession (possessio) and ownership (dominium) found in Ptolemaic and Roman law. Parachoresis means handing over the pledge (in this case, the slave) by the debtor to the creditor for possession to be turned into ownership if the debtor fails to repay the loan. It is now stated that the sale of the slave in form of a fictitious chattel mortgage (when the owner of the slave has no intention to repay the sum he received) is of questionable character; it would be valid if the owner had the intention of repaying the loan and taking back his slave since, as antichresis shows, the (temporary) transfer of possessions without transfer of ownership is perfectly legal. Cf. Taubenschlag, pp. 172,316. (In an earlier article, Epstein had read ἐπίχειρον “wages, pay of manual labor”.)</i> is questionable. The slave girl of Rebbi Abba bar Ada fled to Cilicia. He came and asked Rebbi Mana, who instructed him following Rebbi Immi: a sales contract is forbidden, a loan for use is permitted, a cession is questionable.
“One does not ransom kidnap victims for more than the going rate<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">If the kidnap victims were sold as slaves on the open market. If it were known that Jews be ready to ransom their co-religionists for any price, no Jew would be safe from kidnapping.</i> because of the public good. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, because of the welfare of the kidnap victims.” That they should not be put in chains<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">The majority holds that one never tries to free kidnap victims without paying because then the robbers would put their next victims in chains and otherwise mistreat them. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel holds that one may not try to steal the kidnap victims if one cannot free all of them in the hand of a group of kidnappers, because those left behind then would be mistreated. He does not fear an adverse reaction in a larger context.</i>. “One does not smuggle kidnap victims out because of the public good.”
It was stated: “It is prohibited to buy from a Gentile who sells Torah scrolls, phylacteries, and <i>mezuzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Since probably he is a fence. Babylonian sources in theory (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 45b" href="/Gittin.45b">Babli 45b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.3.2">Tosephta <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 3:6</a>) permit to buy from the Gentile if the writing follows all established rules, in practice disapprove, 45b.</i>.” But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 45b" href="/Gittin.45b">Babli 45b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.3.2">Tosephta <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 3:7</a>. This Tosephta is a polemic against the Yerushalmi baraita.</i>: “It happened that a Gentile in Sidon sold phylacteries and <i>mezuzot</i>. The case came before the Sages who said that it was permitted to buy from him.” That one was a Gentile who reverted to his error<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">He was a proselyte who had learned to write Jewish sacred texts. Since the status of being Jewish cannot be lost, when he reverted to Gentile ways he remained a Jew. Therefore, he is entitled to write these texts; he is not a fence. The Babli has the same explanation, 45b.</i>.

Halakhah 7
