<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody divorces his wife because of bad reputation<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">She has the reputation of an adulteress but he has no proof.</i>, he cannot take her back<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, to protect the wife’s children in a subsequent marriage. This is in the public interest.</i>; because of a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Because of a vow she made.</i>, he cannot take her back<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, to protect the wife’s children in a subsequent marriage. This is in the public interest.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, for any vow known in public<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Which according to R. Jehudah cannot be dissolved; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.4.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 5:4, Note 56</a>.</i>, he cannot take her back; not known in public, he can take her back. Rebbi Meїr says, for any vow which needs investigation by a Sage, he cannot take her back; if it does not need investigation by a Sage, he can take her back<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">A vow made in error which never was binding.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, they forbade one because of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Jehudah said, it happened in Sidon that one said to his wife, a <i>qonam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">He forbade certain things on himself if he did not fulfill the conditions he imposed on himself; cf. Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>, Introduction.</i> if I do not divorce you, and he divorced her, and the Sages permitted him to take her back<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">This is unproblematic since he fulfilled his vow. The case is quoted only to show that the prohibition to take her back is due only to a vow of hers, not his.</i>, for the public good.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody divorces his wife because of bad reputation,” etc. And it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 46a" href="/Gittin.46a">Babli 46a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>.</i>: “Why did the Sages say that he who divorces his wife because of bad reputation cannot take her back<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The court will not let him take her back. While a divorce as a matter of principle is a private affair between husband and wife and needs only two witnesses for the document and two for the delivery, a divorce because of probable cause of adultery is a public affair. The husband can divorce his wife without paying her <i>ketubah</i> only if he can show probable cause in court. If he divorces her and pays the <i>ketubah</i>, it is not classified as “divorce because of bad reputation”.</i>? For if he divorced his wife because of bad reputation, when she went, married another, and bore him children, after some time it turned out that the accusations were baseless and he said, if I had known that the accusations were baseless, even if somebody had offered me a hundred minas for my wife I would not have divorced her, then the bill of divorce would be invalidated<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> make the divorce dependent on the husband’s will, if later he retracts that will, he retroactively invalidates the divorce. This is also accepted in the Babli; in modern practice the husband has to agree that any later change of heart must be disregarded.</i> and her children bastards. But since he knows that if he divorces her, she will be forbidden to return to him, from the start he gives her a perfect bill of divorce.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>.</i>: “Why did they say that he who divorces his wife because of a vow cannot take her back? For if he divorced his wife because of a vow, when she went, married another, and bore him children, after some time it turned out that the vow was void and he said, if I had known that the vow was void, even if somebody had offered me a hundred minas for my wife I would not have divorced her, then the bill of divorce would be invalidated and her child a bastard. But since he knows that if he divorces her, she will be forbidden to return to him, from the start he gives her a perfect bill of divorce.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, you can see that he was looking for a pretext to divorce her since it must have been a vow which does not need investigation by a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">The only invalid vows are those which do not need an Elder to be nullified. Therefore, the husband could have annulled the vow (<i>Nedarim</i> Chapters 10–11).</i>! Rebbi Ze‘ira said, you can see that he was looking for a pretext to divorce her since it must have been a vow which was not public knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Also for a public vow, the situation described in the Tosephta cannot arise.</i>! <sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:9</a>, explained there in Notes 117,118.</i>“Rebbi Eleazar said, they forbade one because of the other.” The law should be that he can take her back even if it was a vow which needs investigation by a Sage, for the Elder uproots the vow. Why did they forbid a vow which needs investigation by a Sage? Because of a vow which does not need investigation by a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">In which case the situation described in the Tosephta could arise.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody divorces his wife because she is a she-ram<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">A woman lacking secondary female sex characteristics; she is presumed to be infertile. If she was married when very young and then failed to develop, the husband can claim the the marriage and his obligation of <i>ketubah</i> was entered into in error. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.25"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 65</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:7, Note 77</a>. R. Jehudah holds that a she-ram should not be married, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.5.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 6:5</a>.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah says, he cannot take her back, but the Sages say, he may take her back. If she was married to another man, had children by him, and requests her <i>ketubah</i>, Rebbi Jehudah says, he tells her: Your silence would be better than your talking<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This gives R. Jehudah’s rationale. If she was divorced without payment of the sum promised her in the <i>ketubah</i> and it turns out that she is not infertile, the husband tells her that if she goes to court, he will declare that he would never have divorced her, had he known that she was not infertile (or, according to Rashi, that he would have to pay the <i>ketubah</i>), and turn her children from the second husband into bastards.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>.</i>: “Why did they say that he who divorces his wife as a she-ram cannot take her back? For if he divorced his wife as a she-ram, when she went, married another man, and bore him children, and he said, if I had known that she can have children, even if somebody had offered me a hundred minas for my wife I would not have divorced her, then the bill of divorce would be invalidated and her child a bastard. But since he knows that if he divorces her, she will be forbidden to return to him, from the start he gives her a perfect bill of divorce.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, you can see that he was looking for a pretext to divorce her since there are women she-rams who are married and since [their husbands] are satisfied with them they keep them<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the Mishnah and its implications. If the court would tell him that he was lying when he claimed that he was divorcing her because of infertility, it could force him to pay the <i>ketubah</i> or let him take her back.</i>.
“Rebbi Jehudah says, he cannot take her back,” and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah prohibits him to take her back and he knows that the divorce is irrevocable, how can he then claim that he would not have divorced her?</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no Rebbi Jehudah but Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Who is represented by the “Sages” in the Mishnah. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 46b" href="/Gittin.46b">Babli, 46b</a>, disagrees and holds that for R. Meїr the bill of divorce is valid and immune from attack by the first husband unless the bill of divorce was delivered with the explicit declaration that it be valid if and only if she is a sterile she-ram.</i>. It was stated thus in Rebbi Meїr’s name: Your silence would be better than your talking.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sells himself and his children to Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">As slaves.</i>, one does not buy him back<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">With public money.</i>, but one buys his children back after their father’s death. If somebody sold his field<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In the Land of Israel.</i> to a Gentile and then a Jew bought it back from him<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">During the growing season, when the produce already was exempt from the obligation of First Fruits as Gentile property.</i>, the buyer brings first fruits because of the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In order to encourage people to buy agricultural properties in the Land.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sells himself and his children to Gentiles,” etc. The Mishnah, if he sold himself repeatedly. But if he sold himself once, one buys him back<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 46b" href="/Gittin.46b">Babli agrees, 46b</a>.</i>, but if he sold himself to the games<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">As a gladiator.</i>, one does not buy him back. There was a case of one who sold himself to the games; the case came before Rebbi Abbahu, who said, what can we do? He did it for his livelihood<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise he would have starved. He agreed to buy him back with money given for charity. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47a" href="/Gittin.47a">Babli, 47a</a>, this scenario is restricted to the case of a gladiator who continues to eat only kosher.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <i>Demay</i> 5:9, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:5-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.5-6">Notes 104–109</a>. Variants are denoted by <b>ד</b>.</i> The Mishnah is Rebbi Meїr’s, since Rebbi Meїr says that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to remove it from tithes; Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon say, a Gentile may acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The reason of Rebbi Meïr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:46" href="/Leviticus.25.46"><i>Lev</i>. 25:46</a>): “You shall transmit them by inheritance to your sons after you, to inherit by the rules of real estate.” This brackets slaves with real estate. Just as you may buy slaves from them but they cannot buy from you, so real estate you may buy from them but they cannot buy from you. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct reading in <b>ד</b>.</i>, the following supports Rebbi Meїr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:23" href="/Leviticus.25.23"><i>Lev</i>. 25:23</a>): “The land shall not be sold permanently,” absolutely. He said to him, that in itself<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This is the correct reading in <b>ד</b>.</i> supports Rebbi Simeon. “It shall not be sold”, because if it was sold, it would be sold absolutely. Rebbi Huna from Sepphoris said, Rebbi Ḥanina instituted in Sepphoris following Rebbi Simeon.
Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Even though Rebbi Meїr said that a Gentile may not acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to remove it from tithes, he agrees that he has property rights. What are property rights? Rebbi Abba said, the usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">All the yield rightfully belongs to the Gentile; the land is not ownerless and taking anything from there would be theft.</i>; as we have stated: “the buyer brings First Fruits because of the public good,” otherwise he could have brought First Fruits by the word of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">If the fruits were not the Gentile’s, his possession could not have freed the field from the biblical obligation of First Fruits.</i>.
If somebody bought a field during a Jubilee period<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">This discussion is purely theoretical since the institution of the Jubilee was abolished with the exile of the Ten Tribes, not to be re-instated; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.3.4">Note 66</a>; <i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:1 Note 7, 10:3 Notes 83–88.</i>. Rebbi Ila said, he acquired ownership of the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">A sale of ancestral agricultural land in a Jubilee period is really a long-term lease since the land returns to the owner in the Jubilee year. The buyer certainly acquires possession; the question is whether he also acquires ownership (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.6.3">Note 169</a>).</i>, Abba bar Mamal said, he did not acquire ownership of the ground. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected to Rebbi Ila: In your opinion, since you say that he acquired ownership of the ground, why can he not dig cisterns, ditches, and caves<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition to alienate agricultural lands from their status is not found otherwise in either one of the Talmudim There may be a hint of this in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 5 7" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_5.7"><i>Sifra Behar, Pereq</i> 5(7</a>); also <i>Parašah</i> 4(6) where it is stated that cisterns, ditches, and caves follow the rules of houses, which sometimes are not returned in the Jubilee year, but not those of agricultural land, which always is returned. The buyer/lessee has no right to change the legal status of the land; this seems to indicate that he has no ownership.</i>? He said to him, the Torah said<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:28" href="/Leviticus.25.28"><i>Lev</i>. 25:28</a>.</i>, “he shall return to his inheritance,” as it was. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa brought this topic before them and did not know that they still disagreed: Rebbi Joḥanan said, he brings and recites; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he does not bring and recite<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47b-48a" href="/Gittin.47b-48a">Babli, 47b/48a</a>, it is clearly stated that R. Joḥanan holds that possession is like ownership, R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that possession and ownership are distinct. In this, practice of the Babli strictly follows R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yose: Everybody agrees that he brings<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Possession of the land is sufficient for the property to be subject to the duty of first fruits but not enough for the farmer to declare (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:10" href="/Deuteronomy.26.10"><i>Deut</i>. 26:10</a>) “I brought the First Fruits of the land which You gave me, o Eternal”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.7.2">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 1:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.6.1">6</a>.</i>. Where do they disagree? Rebbi Joḥanan said, he brings and recites; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he brings but does not recite.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For tort victims one estimates with best quality land<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If a person is due damages and is not paid, he can ask the court for foreclosure. If the guilty party owns several pieces of land, the tort victim is indemnified, up to the value of his claim, from the land which carries the highest value per unit of surface area. A creditor whose mortgage does not specify a particular piece of land is indemnified from land which the court’s experts assess to be of average value among all of the debtor’s holdings.</i>, for a creditor with average quality, and for a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> with lowest quality. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In all other Mishnah sources mentioned in M. S. Feldblum, <i>Diḳduḳe Soferim - Gittin</i> (New York 1966), the name is R. Meїr.</i> says, also for a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> with average quality.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “For tort victims one estimates with best quality land,” etc. “By an inference <i>de minore ad maius</i>, for Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A statement by R. Aqiba in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Mišpaṭim</i> 14, also quoted by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 48b" href="/Gittin.48b">Babli, 48b</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Abba bar Pappaios said before Rebbi Yose, where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">What does the statement about Temple property mean?</i>? If to qualify as damages, is that not what we stated “his neighbor’s ox<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex</i>. 21:35</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 4:3</a>. The rules of damages do not apply to Temple property, not for damages inflicted by Temple animals nor damages done to them. Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 49a" href="/Gittin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>.</i>” but not the ox of Temple property? If for bodily damages, is that not what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: A private person can claim damages but not the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation is ambiguous; it also could mean that the Temple never pays damages.</i>? But we must deal with one who said, I obligate myself to give 100 minas to the Temple, <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The following clause is missing in the quote of the passage by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 49a:6:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.49a.6.1">Tosaphot <i>Giṭṭin</i> 49a, <i>s. v.</i> שור</a>, and in the Constantinople edition of the Yerushalmi. If one accepts the text as it stands, one has to explain that the person making the vow thought that he could pay his vow in cash, but before he could do this, he had to pay damages and now he is short of cash. If one does not read the clause, then there is a straightforward statement that debts to the Temple in all cases are privileged like debts for damages (a statement considered and rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 49a" href="/Gittin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>). The interpretation of the Babli, that damages inflicted on Temple property always must be paid in full, is incompatible with the Yerushalmi.</i> when his ox went and did damage. You should not say, [the Temple] is a creditor and should collect from average quality land. Therefore, it was necessary to say that “for tort victims one estimates with best quality land, by an inference <i>de minore ad maius</i>, for Temple property.”
Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “Full and partial damages are collected from encumbered property.” One understands full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:36" href="/Exodus.21.36"><i>Ex</i>. 21:36</a> requires the owner of an animal with a history of causing damages to pay all damages in full. If the animal’s owner cannot pay cash, the Mishnah decrees that the damages be liquidated by the best available land, even if mortgaged.</i>. Are partial damages not collected from the animal’s body<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex</i>. 21:35</a> requires the damages inflicted by an animal which never before had caused damage to be paid from proceeds of a sale of the animal itself.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, explain it when it was a docile ox which did damage and then his owner went and sold it. Its body already was encumbered to the injured party<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The “encumbered property” mentioned in the <i>baraita</i> is not mortgaged land but the animal causing the damage, which can be repossessed by the owner of the damaged property from a buyer of the agressive animal.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea said, explain it if he converted it<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the injured animal did not insist on immediate payment but agreed that the debt be liquidated <i>as if it were</i> a loan.</i> into a loan. Then it should be foreclosed only by average quality land! Since the debt originated in a damage claim, he may collect from best quality.
“His field<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:4" href="/Exodus.22.4"><i>Ex</i>. 22:4</a>: “If a person destroys a field or a vineyard through animals by sending his livestock to graze on another’s field, <i>the best of his field or the best of his vineyard</i> he shall give in payment.” This is the basis of the Mishnah requiring that damages be paid with best quality real estate.</i>”, except if it was mortgaged<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">“<i>His</i> field” excludes third-party interests.</i>. “His vineyard”, except Temple property. Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">What relation can Temple property have with damages due from a private person?</i>? If somebody caused damage and then dedicated to the Temple, that is what we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 6:2: “If somebody dedicated his property to the Temple while he owed a <i>ketubah</i> to his wife or a debt to a creditor, neither the woman nor the creditor can collect from the Temple; but the redeemer redeems on condition to pay the <i>ketubah</i> to the woman or the debt to the creditor. If he dedicated 90 minas but his debt was 100 minas, [the redeemer] adds another denar and redeems these properties on condition to pay the debt to the creditor.” In order to combine two conflicting principles, viz., that vows cannot be used to escape obligations towards third parties, and that Temple obligations override all others, it is decreed that the Temple has to put the dedicated properties up for sale but collects only for the amount by which the value of the properties exceeds the obligation, with the third party buyer accepting the obligation to pay off the liens on the property. (It has to be a third party buyer since the person making the dedication would have to add another 25% to the redemption amount, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:19" href="/Leviticus.27.19"><i>Lev</i>. 27:19</a>.) Claims for damages have to be handled in the same way.</i>: “If he dedicated 90 minas but his debt was 100 minas.” If somebody dedicated to the Temple and then caused damage, that is what we have stated: “ ‘His neighbor’s ox<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex</i>. 21:35</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 4:3</a>. The rules of damages do not apply to Temple property, not for damages inflicted by Temple animals nor damages done to them. Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 49a" href="/Gittin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>.</i>’ but not the ox of Temple property.” Rebbi Yudan said, explain it if an ox of Temple property grazed on a private field. Rebbi Mana told him, we require Temple real estate and you say “on a private field”? But we must hold about one who said: I am obligating myself for 100 minas to the Temple; then went and caused damage. You should not say that between damages and a loan given before witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Not in documented form; the witnesses do not sign anything. Such a loan is not a mortgage and is not privileged.</i> damages are privileged, and here damages are privileged against the Temple; therefore it was necessary to say “his vineyard”, except Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The statement about Temple property is not to exclude Temple property from damage claims but to privilege Temple property relative to all civil claims.</i>.
“His field,” except movables. “His vineyard,” except potential property which is not similar in status to that in possession. “ ‘His field,’ except movables.” About those who did not claim movables from the start. Is this different from what we stated: There is not only real estate; from where if he wanted to pay money? The verse says: “Money he shall return to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex</i>. 21:33</a>, speaking of somebody digging a hole in the public domain which then causes injury to another person’s animal. This damage has to be paid in money (or, in the Babli’s interpretation, in money’s worth). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 7a" href="/Bava_Kamma.7a">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 7a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 14b" href="/Bava_Kamma.14b">14b</a>, money is legal tender to liquidate all debts. The Yerushalmi gives the injured party the right to claim real estate in those cases in which the verse prescribed payment in real estate.</i>.” What did you mean to say that real estate is primary? Or<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">אי is Babylonian spelling for Galilean אוֹ “or”, אִין “if”, אֵי “not”. The first alternative applies here.</i> should we say that money is primary? Then it should be irrevocably given to [the claimant] from the start! Do we not see rabbis who irrevocably give him real estate! “ ‘His vineyard,’ except potential property which is not similar in status to that in possession.” Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Nisa: If his father dug a cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the public domain, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.1.5">Note 18</a>.</i> which caused injury during his father’s lifetime. Property fell to him after his father’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The injury claim was not paid during the father’s lifetime. By accepting the inheritance, the son became liable to pay the injury claim. If later he received inheritance from another source, e. g., grandparents, the later inheritance is not liable for claims stemming from a prior inheritance even if the son was the only known heir and therefore the grandparent’s property was potentially his.</i>. I might say that this property should be encumbered for that damage; therefore, it was necessary to say “his vineyard” to except potential property which is not similar in status to that in possession.
“The best of his field and the best of his vineyard <i>he</i> shall pay,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:4" href="/Exodus.22.4"><i>Ex</i>. 22:4</a>: “If a person destroys a field or a vineyard through animals by sending his livestock to graze on another’s field, <i>the best of his field or the best of his vineyard</i> he shall give in payment.” This is the basis of the Mishnah requiring that damages be paid with best quality real estate.</i>” i. e., the party responsible for the damage, the words of Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 48a" href="/Gittin.48a">Babli, 48a</a>, the attribution of names is switched; the same is implied by <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i>, ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 296; the interpretation of R. Ismael is quoted anonymously in <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i>, ed. Epstein-Melamed p. 196, together with the interpretation given later to the opinion of R. Aqiba.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, “the best of <i>his</i> field and the best of <i>his</i> vineyard”, of the person suffering the damage. It is difficult for Rebbi Aqiba, he suffered damage, and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Why should the person responsible only pay corresponding to the best remaining parts of a ruined field?</i>? But the court see what the field was worth before it was damaged and estimates accordingly. As it was stated, it is possible if the grazing was on best quality land, one estimates from best quality, from lowest quality one estimates from lowest quality. How is this? The best of the lowest quality is quite good. If you say so you provide a windfall for the person suffering the damage<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Aqiba, the holdings of the person suffering the damage are graded into best, average, and lowest quality. In each category, the value of the damage is established and paid by the person responsible with his best real estate in the given category, up to the value established in court.</i>. One looks at the field as if it was full of first quality [produce] and estimates for him from the best quality land; least quality one estimates from least quality land. It turns out that he pays with best quality for best quality and from least quality for least quality. From where has Rebbi Aqiba that “for tort victims one estimates with best quality land”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reads the verse as referring to the method of estimating the damage, not payment, the Mishnah has no biblical foundation.</i>? It is not from the Torah, it is a decree. <sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This is a <i>baraita</i>, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 49b" href="/Gittin.49b">Babli, 49b</a>, in the name of R. Simeon (in some sources, R. Simeon ben Eleazar.) A similar, anonymous, text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Ketubot (Lieberman) 12:2" href="/Tosefta_Ketubot_(Lieberman).12.2">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 12:2</a>.</i>“Why did they say, ‘for tort victims one estimates with best quality land’? Because of the robbers<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since a robber of land is also inflicting damage on the person robbed.</i>. That a man should say, why should I have robbed [land] in my hand, or land subject to a suit for damages on my hand? Tomorrow, the court will see my good field, take it from me, and support it by the verse, ‘the best of his field and the best of his vineyard he shall pay.’ ” So far his real estate. His pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the person responsible cannot pay directly, any pledge taken should be of his most valuable possessions.</i> was inferred from his real estate. Since his real estate [is taken] from the best quality, also his pledge [is taken] from the best.
<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A continuation of the Tosephta (Note 26). In the Babli, R. Jehudah agrees that loans should be foreclosed only by least quality land but R. Meїr states that to support credit one has to allow loans to be foreclosed from average quality.</i>” ‘For creditors, average quality,’ because of tricksters. That a man should not see a desirable field in his neighbor’s hand, lend him money, and contrives to take it from him. Then one should estimate only from the least quality! Rebbi Jehudah said, that is correct; but not to lock the door before people needing to borrow. For tomorrow he may need to borrow and does not find [a lender.]”
Rebbi Simai preached: “And the man,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:11" href="/Deuteronomy.24.11"><i>Deut</i>. 24:11</a>, about a creditor taking a pledge, quoted in full later in the paragraph.</i>” that is the agent of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 276 seems to read וְהָאִישׁ as “ ‘and any man’, to include the agent of the court” since the verse obviously refers to the creditor.</i>. For if the creditor enters, he removes the best. If the borrower enters, he removes the worst. How is this? The agent of the court enters and removes from average quality. Rebbi Ismael says, it is a word from the Torah that the creditor<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The context would require to read “debtor”; this is an Amoraic statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 50a" href="/Gittin.50a">Babli, 50a</a>.</i> should enter, for it is written: “The man whose creditor you are should bring the pledge out for you.” That is, his pledge. His real estate was inferred from his pledge. Since his pledge [is taken] from average quality, also his real estate [is taken] from average quality.
<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:2, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.8">Notes 75–77</a>, variant readings noted <b>ש</b>. The Mishnah notes that court judgments are not subject to the rules of the Sabbatical.</i> This is obvious: A loan that is disputed is not remitted. A loan that was disputed but is now a [confirmed]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">By judgment of the court.</i> loan, is remitted. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Is this also true for judgments? A loan that was disputed can be collected. A disputed loan turned into a [confirmed] loan is collected from property of average quality.
If there is only best quality, one collects from the best quality, [only] average quality one collects from average quality, [only] worst quality one collects from worst quality. Best and average, torts are assessed from best quality, creditors and a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> from average. Average and worst, torts and creditors are assessed from average, a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> from worst. If his best quality was like everybody’s best quality, it is best quality. If his average quality was like everybody’s average quality, it is average quality. If his worst quality was like everybody’s worst quality, it is worst quality<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The statement up to here is a <i>baraita</i>, quoted as such in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 7b" href="/Bava_Kamma.7b">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 7b</a>. One can foreclose only existing property; the only question which arises is how to classify claims if there are properties of two different qualities but claims for all three. The Babli formulates this as: Do the notions of best, average, and worst refer to the available land or are these general notions used by realtors to classify properties?</i>. If his best quality was like everybody’s average? Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Ila asked: Let us hear from the following: Average and worst, torts and creditors are assessed from average, a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> from worst. But just as you need in the case that you have best and average quality, torts push creditors to the average, so in this case, of average and worst quality, should not torts push creditors to the worst quality<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If his best quality is classified by realtors as average, nevertheless it is his best, reserved for satisfaction of tort judgmens, and creditors should be satisfied with second best quality, even if that is worst in universal classification. This seems to prove that the classifications mentioned in Mishnah and <i>baraita</i> are universal, not referring to the debtor.</i>? Rebbi Yose bar Abun in the name of Rav Ḥisda: Explain it that he had best quality which he sold, and the creditors were already pushed to average quality<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">His explanation is also given by Rav Ḥisda in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 8a" href="/Bava_Kamma.8a">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 8a</a>. As Rashi explains, the loan was taken when there was best quality land; then automatically the average quality land was pledged to the creditor. When later the best quality land was sold, the pledge was not removed. When then a claim for torts was entered, it only could be additional to the existing lien. (The Babli lists three other possible explanations, all agreeing that in a normal case in which only two qualities of real estate are available, the better one is declared best, reserved for the satisfaction of tort claims.)</i>.
“For a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> with lowest quality.” <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a rearrangement of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.5.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:5 (Notes 54–59</a>) and one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 3:7:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.3.7.17">Peah 3:9 (Notes 182–189</a>).</i> Rebbi Jeremiah said, they taught only for a <i>ketubah</i> of a mina or 200 [<i>zuz</i>], but a <i>ketubah</i> of a thousand denar she collects from average quality<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The obligatory minimum she collects only from the lowest quality land; any excess she can take from average quality. The Yerushalmi does not give a reason why a woman can collect only from lowest quality real estate; the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 49b-50a" href="/Gittin.49b-50a">Babli (49b–50a</a>) after a lengthy discussion decides that this is a necessary institution to induce men to get married.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even a <i>ketubah</i> of a thousand denar she collects only from lowest quality. It turns out that this disagreement parallels the following disagreement: “While she lives in her father’s house, she always can collect her <i>ketubah</i>; if she lives in her husband’s house, she can collect her <i>ketubah</i> for 25 years.”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 12:5</a>.</i> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: One stated this only for the <i>ketubah</i> of a mina or 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. But a <i>ketubah</i> of 1’000 denar she collects forever. Rebbi Abbahu quoted in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even a <i>ketubah</i> of 1’000 denar she can collect only up to 25 years. It turns out that Rebbi Yose follows Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Jeremiah Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 3:9.</i>: “If somebody signs over his property to his sons and signs over some real estate to his wife, she loses her claim to <i>ketubah</i>.” Rav said, if he sells through her<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Peah</i>, “if he lets them acquire through her agency.” One has to assume that this is meant here also.</i>. Samuel says, if he distributes in her presence. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, they stated here a relaxation of the laws of <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Abba said, the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina is that it applies not only to a <i>ketubah</i> of a mina or two hundred, but even regarding a <i>ketubah</i> of one thousand denar they proclaimed a relaxation of the laws of <i>ketubah</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not collect from encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">“Encumbered property” is property sold when there was a lien on it. If the lienholder cannot get satisfaction from the debtor, he can have regress on the buyer. But as long as the debtor has any real estate left in his possession, it has to be foreclosed before any claim can be raised against a buyer.</i> as long as there is free property, even if the latter is of lowest quality. One may collect from orphans’<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the orphans are underage.</i> property only from the lowest quality.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One cannot collect for usufruct,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.7"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:8</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:7:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.7.3-4">Notes 187–197</a>. The variant readings (including a parallel text in <i>Šebuot</i> 5:5) are given in <i>Ketubot</i>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: One pays from an orphan’s property only a document on which interest is due, and some say, also a woman’s <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Yannai said, because of her sustenance. Rebbi Mattaniah said, who is worried about sustenance? Rebbi Simeon! Since Rebbi Simeon said, it depends on the collection. What about it? For attraction, that everybody should be eager to marry her. Some say, also for robbery and torts. Rebbi Yose said, we stated both of these. Robbery from the following: “If it was mortgageable, he has to pay.” For torts from the following: “One pays from an orphan’s property only from the least valuable.” So is the Mishnah: One pays for torts from an orphan’s property only from the least valuable. But was it not stated: If the son took his father’s place, one estimates torts from the most valuable land, creditors from average quality, and a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> from the least valuable. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there about an adult orphan, here<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the orphans are underage.</i> about an underage orphan.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One cannot collect for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If a robber took land by force, had its use for some time, and then sold it, the land itself can be reclaimed from the buyer but payment for the illicit use of the land can be exacted only from the robber himself.</i>, for improvement of the land<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the buyer of some property makes improvements, such as planting fruit trees, and then loses the property through the foreclosure of a prior lien, he can recoup his expenses for the improvements only from the original seller directly.</i>, or for sustenance of the wife and the daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is a required condition in a <i>ketubah</i> that after the husband’s death his widow and unmarried daughters be sustained by the estate (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:10:2-11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.10.2-11.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:11–12</a>). While the <i>ketubah</i> itself is a mortgage lien on all real estate acquired by the husband, the additional conditions cannot be foreclosed as a mortgage.</i> from incumbered property, for the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">All other Mishnah sources have an additional clause, either as original text or addition: “The finder shall not be made to swear, for the public good.” Since the statement is quoted in the Halakhah (Note 88), it should be read here also.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not collect from encumbered property,”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is not in its place. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> and the list of items which cannot be subject to foreclosure from a buyer.</i> etc. Rebbi Ḥanina said, because they have no fixed amount<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Mortgage liens are only fixed amounts. The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 50b" href="/Gittin.50b">Babli, 50b</a>.</i>.
If [Reuben]<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the text but clearly demanded by the sequel.</i> robbed a field from Simeon and sold it to Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases discussed, Levi is presumed to be an innocent buyer who did not know that the seller had no title.</i>, then Simeon came<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Who could prove his ownership in court.</i> and foreclosed it from Levi; then Levi collects from Reuben’s encumbered property and Simeon collects the usufruct from Levi’s free property<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Mishnah and is unproblematic.</i>. If Levi in the meantime improved [the property], Levi collects the capital from Reuben’s encumbered property and the improvement from Simeon’s free property. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, only if a third party’s buying preceded the improvement by this one<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If Simeon sold property before he knew that Levi had improved the property which he is going to retake, the eventual obligation of Simeon towards Levi is no lien on the real estate sold to the third party. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 51b" href="/Gittin.51b">Babli, 51b</a>, a similar but tannaïtic statement applies only in case the field was sold by the buyer from the robber to another person, whose innocent purchase is protected.</i>. But if the improvement by this one preceded a third party’s buying, Levi can collect the capital even from Simeon’s encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion, whose problematic aspect is discussed later, Simeon’s potential liability is an automatic lien on all his properties.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said: With usufruct one proceeds in the same way<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There also, it depends on when the obligation of payment started. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 14b" href="/Bava_Metzia.14b">Babli agrees, <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 14b</a>, where the situation described here is alluded to.</i>. If he spent four denars and the improvement is worth six, Levi collects four denars from Reuben’s encumbered property and another two from Simeon’s free property. Rebbi Abin in the name of the rabbis there: Since he did not enter with permission, his hand is weak against Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since for Simeon all that Levi did in his field was unauthorized, Simeon does not have to pay at all since he would pay only an amount equal to the smaller of investment or improvement, but since the investment is collected from Reuben, there is no enforceable claim against Simeon. (Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.3.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 15:3, Note 71</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of the rabbis there: Since he entered with permission, his hand is strong against Reuben<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since Reuben sold the field to Levi, the latter can collect from Reuben the value of the improvement, not only the amount of investment.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is discussed by Rashba in a letter to Meïri (<i>Responsa attributed to Naḥmanides</i>, #96). The text is practically identical with that of the ms.</i> Reuben robbed a field from Simeon and sold it to Levi; Levi went and gave it to Jehudah as a gift. Then Simeon foreclosed it from Jehudah. Jehudah has no regress on Levi since it was a gift. Levi has regress on Reuben since it was a sale. Did he not give it away as a gift? He can say to him, it was my intention to give it to him so he would return me favors<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">While goodwill cannot be easily quantified in monetary terms, it is of value and can be used in a claim against a robber.</i>. Reuben robbed a field from Simeon and gave it to Levi as a gift; Levi sold it to Jehudah. Jehudah has regress on Levi since he sold it; Levi has no regress on Reuben since it was a gift; as Rebbi Yose said in the name of the rabbis: No easement is created by a gift.
Reuben robbed a field from Simeon and sold it to Levi, and he<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">“He” seems to be Simeon, who has to go to court and obtain a document which gives him the right to take the field from Levi.</i> did not have time to write the foreclosure document before he died: whose is it<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">It should be obvious that the field is Simeon’s and after him his heirs. The paragraph is difficult to understand; the commentators all emend the text to fit their explanations, which can be disregarded. It seems that the question is to whom do the heirs have to address their foreclosure document.<br>The problem is an old one; Meïri, after Rashi the second most important Medieval commentator, asked Rashba for an explanation (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.4">Note 64</a>). Meïri’s text read טרפו “his forclosure document,” Rashba’s read תרפו “his sales document.” This is Rashba’s anwer:<br>“It is my opinion that our text is defective and it should be as follows: Reuben robbed a field from Simeon and sold it to Levi, <i>then Reuben bought it from Simeon</i> but before he could write the sales contract, he died. That is the case about which Samuel asked Rav in (Babli) <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> (15b), viz., if it turns out that the field was not his, but he went and bought it from the original owners, what are the rules? He told him, the first (the robber) sold to the second (the buyer) any present and future rights to the parcel.… And this is the explanation of this text according to my understanding: Reuben had robbed a field from Simeon and sold it to Levi who did not realize that it was robbed. Afterwards, Reuben bought the parcel from Simeon, but Simeon had not yet written the sales contract when Reuben died: who is the owner? Rav Huna and Ḥiyya bar Rav: One says if the document was first, if the sale was completed before he died, the sale was final and Reuben did not transfer [the field] to Levi; this shows that Reuben did not intend to leave the field in Levi’s hands, ostensibly to leave it to his heirs. He does not spell this out since the sales document was not yet written. The other one said, there is no difference between written and not written, he wants the field to be Levi’s since he did not dispose of the field in a will. (The same Rav Huna and Ḥiyya bar Rav disagree in the Babli <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> (16a) about the time available to the robber to act to protect his credit.) Rebbi Mana has a different explanation: He says that in any case the field belongs to Reuben, following Rami bar Ḥama (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 16a" href="/Bava_Metzia.16a">Babli, <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 16a</a>) who said that Levi’s contract was not worth the paper it was written on. But Rebbi Yose bar Abun is of the opposite opinion, since Levi can tell him, is the field not now before you that you can turn the sale into a valid one. This follows Rava (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 16a" href="/Bava_Metzia.16a">Babli, <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 16a</a>) who told Rami bar Ḥama that Levi acquired the property by the trust he put in Reuben.<br>But following your reading, since you read טרפו with ט, it is possible that this refers to a foreclosure document which Levi obtained against the robber after he had lost the field to Simeon, and that is the same disagreement we find there (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 16a" href="/Bava_Metzia.16a">Babli, <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 16a</a>); how long does a person have credit, Rav says until the start of court proceedings, Ḥiyya bar Rav says, until the foreclosure document was signed, and Rav Papa said, until the public sale.”</i>? Rav Huna and Ḥiyya the son of Rav: One said, if he wrote the foreclosure document, it is Reuben’s, if not, it is Levi’s. The other one said, whether he wrote or did not write, it belongs to Levi. Rebbi Mana said, it is reasonable that it does not belong to Reuben since he can say to him, I sold you something which was not mine. Rebbi Yose said, it is not reasonable that it should not be Levi’s, since Reuben<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba reads "Levi" as text, not as correction.</i> can tell him, is this not before you? You are good to confirm the sale.
Can one collect dowry from encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since providing a dowry for daughters is one of the obligatory conditions attached to a <i>ketubah</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.6.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:6</a>.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.6.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:6</a> (30d 1.59), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.10">Notes 93–97</a>. Variant readings are noted <b>כ</b>.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira said that Rebbi Joḥanan does not collect. Who collects? Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Ila collect. Rebbi Yasa was appointed custodian of orphans’ property. There were orphan [girls] who asked for dowry. He brought the case before Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Simeon bar Yaqim. Rebbi Simeon bar Yaqim said, is it not better to provide for them from their father’s estate rather than from charity? Rebbi Eleazar said to him: If such a case came before our teachers, our teachers would not touch it; would we act? Rebbi Yose said, I shall give to them, and if some orphans would get up and complained, I would give it to them. They stood up and complained<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opposite of what is asserted in <i>Ketubot</i>.</i>.
A judgment was rendered<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.6.4"><i>Ketubot</i> (6:6, Note 98</a>) it is only asserted that a judgment was given following R. Ḥanina.</i> for Ṣippor bat Absalom. Rebbi Simon and Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Abba: I and our teachers collected for her from movables<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Following the principle that local custom is the overriding determinant in the judicial interpretation of <i>ketubah</i> obligations; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:4, Note 67</a>.</i>, following the custom of her place.
Rav wrote to Rebbi, who transferred it to the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Ḥiyya wrote between the lines. If the orphans sold against the rules<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since by talmudic rules, all obligations of a <i>ketubah</i> are to be satisfied by real estate, any sale while some of the obligations are still open is against the rules.</i>, one collects for dowry but one does not collect for sustenance<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The daughters, who do not inherit if there are sons, have a claim of (in general) 10% of their father’s estate as dowry. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.6.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:6</a>. Since this claim exists even during the father’s lifetime, it has precedence over the widow’s claim to sustenance which starts only at her husband’s death.</i>. If they accepted a lien<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This was Rav’s question. The lien is a mortgage obligation.</i>. I do not know what this lien is<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Ḥiyya’s note. Was the mortgage taken during the father’s lifetime or after his death? In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 69a" href="/Ketubot.69a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 69a</a>, the question from the start was about a mortgage the heirs took out on real estate of their inheritance.</i>. Levi stated: Both for a lien made by the father and for a lien made by the son, one collects for dowry<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">From real estate sold or pledged.</i> but one does not collect for sustenance<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This is also quoted as Rebbi’s opinion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 69a" href="/Ketubot.69a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 69a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, there is no mention of a lien made by the father, only a lien made by the son. If one cannot collect during the father’s lifetime<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:6</a>.</i>, not so much less after the father’s death? Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: If the daughters are married, the widow is sustained by them<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 51a" href="/Ketubot.51a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 51a</a>, the question never arises since the claims of the widow and the daughters are accorded equal status; this rule is quoted in <i>Yebamot</i> 15:3 (14d 1.68), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.2-15"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8</a> (29a 1.25).</i>. Rebbi Abba follows his own [opinion] and Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 51a" href="/Ketubot.51a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 51a</a>, the question never arises since the claims of the widow and the daughters are accorded equal status; this rule is quoted in <i>Yebamot</i> 15:3 (14d 1.68), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.2-15"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8</a> (29a 1.25).</i> follows his own [opinion]. For the sister of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥaggai: Even if they died, the widow is sustained by them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8, Notes 200–201</a>. Variant readings are given there.</i> What is the situation of grandchildren? Rebbi Mana said, grandchildren are like children. Rebbi Yose said, grandchildren are not like children. Rebbi Samuel, son of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Mattaniah were sitting together. They wanted to say, the same situation applies to grandchildren here as there. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said to them, inheritance by biblical law jumped on grandchildren.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuʻot</i> 6:1. The subject is the biblical oath imposed on a person who denies an obligation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>). The rabbinic interpretation of the expression אֲשֶׁר יֹאמַר כִּי הוּא זֶה is “if he [the defendant] agrees that there is a case.” If the defendant in a civil suit, in which there are no witnesses and no documents, denies the entire claim, he does not have to swear a biblical oath (he may have to swear a rabbinical oath). But if he agrees to part of the claim, he has to swear a biblical oath to free himself from the remainder. Only if the claim is advanced as tentative, then any admission by the defendant is a gift to the claimant and by rabbinic rule no oath of any kind is due.</i>, we have stated: “You<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The speaker is the claimant.</i> have in your hand a mina of my father’s; you<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The speaker is the defendant.</i> have to get from me only 50 denars. He does not have to swear since he is like a person who returns a find<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The heir is not conversant with all the details of his father’s business dealings. Since he cannot swear that the father had not received payment, he has no case if the defendant denies the entire claim. The partial admission of the claim is the equivalent of a gift by the defendant to the claimant.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, in this case they instituted a regulation as they instituted for a find, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">All other Mishnah sources have an additional clause, either as original text or addition: “The finder shall not be made to swear, for the public good.” Since the statement is quoted in the Halakhah (Note 88), it should be read here also.</i>: “The finder of a lost object shall not swear because of the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner claims to have lost more than the finder returns, the finder cannot be made to swear that he did not retain anything for himself, since otherwise nobody would return any find.</i>.” Rebbi Abba said, this Mishnah [applies] when he said to him, you found my two oxen<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As explained later, one ox was found but the owner claims to have lost two. Returning one ox is not accepting part of a claim of two.</i>. But if he<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The finder.</i> said, I found your two oxen and returned both of them, but the other<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The original owner.</i> said, you returned only one, in that case they did not institute the rule<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">He has to swear since both parties claim to be sure of their case.</i>. Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In this case they did institute<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">This is a case of returning a find and the finder is protected.</i>. But in the first case<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As explained later, one ox was found but the owner claims to have lost two. Returning one ox is not accepting part of a claim of two.</i>, it is a word of the Torah, as it was stated: I could think that if he said to him, you found my two oxen<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This cannot be more than a conjecture; the claimant cannot make a defendant swear about a hypothetical case.</i>, but the other said, I found only one, should he be obligated [to swear]? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:22" href="/Leviticus.5.22"><i>Lev.</i> 5:22</a>, speaking of the sacrifice required of the person who swears falsely in the situation described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i> says, “or if he found a find and disowned it;” that excludes this one who did not disown. But if he said to him, I was standing on my roof when I saw you dragging away two tied oxen, two tied oxen you dragged away from me<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The claimant asserts as a certainty that he saw his two oxen in the finder’s possession.</i>, and he says, only one I dragged away; in that case they made no regulation. Similarly: “I was standing with my father when he was dying and he claimed from you a mina and you agreed to it<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">This is an assertion of a certain claim which falls under the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>,” and the other says, “I agreed only for 50;” in that case they made no regulation.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If orphans rely on a property owner<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If orphans are adults (older than 13 years) but not able to act in real estate matters (younger than 20 years) and they seek assistence from a relative or acquaintance, that person is not a guardian but by rabbinic usage he has some rights and obligations of a guardian, in particular to see to it that the produce of the orphan’s fields is tithed so it becomes available for sale or for their own use. In general, a third person cannot give heave and tithe for others; this is another example of a “regulation for the public good.”</i> or if their father appointed a guardian<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐπίτροπος, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.5.1"><i>Bikkurim</i> 1:5, Note 82</a>.</i> for them; these are obligated to tithe their produce. A guardian appointed by the orphans’ father shall be made to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">At the end of his service, the court shall require him to swear that he retained nothing of the orphan’s property for himself.</i>; if he was appointed by the court he shall not be made to swear. Abba Shaul says, it is the other way around<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The guardian appointed by the father’s will shall not be made to swear, so people will not refuse to serve. The court appointee, who does not have the deceased father’s trust, can be made to swear.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If orphans rely on a property owner, etc.” <sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Terumot</i> 1:1, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.3.3">Notes 61–69</a>. Variants are denoted by <b>ת</b>.</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>) “you” excludes partners, “you” excludes guardians, “you” excludes one who gives heave from what is not his. Did we not state (<i>Terumot</i> 3:3): “Partners who gave heave one after the other?” But one must be for Great Heave, the other for heave of the tithe. Did we not infer the laws of the Great Heave from heave of the tithe? But one is for practice, the other for action. “You” excludes guardians; but did we not state: “Orphans dependent on a home owner, or for whom the father had appointed a guardian, must tithe their produce.” The colleagues say, here for a permanent guardian, there for a temporary guardian. Rebbi Yose asked, does that apply to: “he may sell slaves but not real estate?” But here one deals with an adult orphan, there with an underage orphan.
<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">There is a different but parallel treatment of the subject of this paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:4:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.4.2-6"><i>Baba Qama</i> 4:5</a> (4b 1.50).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, as a matter of principle one does not appoint guardians for orphans to their detriment, only to their profit; but if they are detrimental, they are detrimental<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If the estate of underage minors is sued, the court does not appoint a guardian to conduct their defense but one tells the claimant to wait until the orphans are adults. But if the estate has a claim to pursue, the court may appoint a guardian to prosecute their case; if the guardian is not successful, the orphans cannot sue him.</i>. Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, neither as a matter of principle nor as a reaction, neither for profit not for detriment<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">All suits involving an estate, whether by a defendant or a claimant, have to be postponed until the orphans are adults.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: “One appoints a guardian for them<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 1:5</a>: If an animal of an irresponsible person (a minor, an insane, or a deaf-and-dumb person) did damage, the court has to appoint a guardian to take care of the matter. This seems to imply that one appoints guardians for underage orphans who are defendants in a damage suit.</i>” There is a difference about an ox, lest it continue to cause damage<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not express a biblical principle but a rabbinic rule for the public good, that a dangerous animal should be slaughtered to prevent more damage.</i>. If it did do damage, from whom does one take payment? Rebbi Joḥanan said, from the orphans. Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, from the guardian<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If the animal did additional damage when the guardian already was in charge.</i>. And even if Rebbi Joḥanan never taught this, does not his word imply it since he said if in the end they are detrimental, they are detrimental?
A guardian appointed by the orphans’ father shall be made to swear, for usually he is not checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">A private person usually does not have the ability to judge the honesty of his friends.</i>. If he was appointed by the court he shall not be made to swear for usually he was checked out. Abba Shaul says, it is the other way around; a guardian appointed by the orphans’ father<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: “appointed by the court”.</i> shall be made to swear, for he is like one who is paid<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he is not paid, the reputation he obtains by publicly being trusted by the court may be worth money to him. Therefore, a person will not refrain from serving even if he has to swear in the end. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 52b" href="/Gittin.52b">Babli agrees, 52b</a>.</i>; if he was appointed by the court<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: “appointed by the father.”</i> he shall not be made to swear for for he can say to him, I am doing this as a favor to you. It turns out that Rebbi Joḥanan follows the rabbis and Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina Abba Shaul. Does Rebbi Joḥanan follow the rabbis? Even if he holds with Abba Shaul, a person acts as a trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 52b:10:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.52b.10.1">Tosaphot (52b, <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. הלכה</a>) quotes a slightly different text: בעי אינש מיתן זוזי “a person would be ready to pay money”.</i> in order to be declared trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, he can be made to swear.</i>. Does Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina [follow] Abba Shaul? Even if he holds with the rabbis, a person is apt to slip away from an oath but not from payments<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If people know they will have to swear, they will not be available to be appointed guardians. They will think that they are competent enough not to have to pay; very few people will refrain from becoming guardians because of the potential liability.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone causes impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following cases all concern invisible defects which cannot be detected by inspection. Causing impurity to food can imply a diminution of value, since the food cannot be sold to people eating only in purity, or it can make it unfit for use, if the item was heave or any other sanctified food.</i>, or <i>dema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demaʻ</i> is profane food mixed with heave, which is forbidden to lay persons and only permitted to priests, who will pay only a fraction of the value. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.21"><i>Demay</i> 1:3, Note 175</a>.</i>, or libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Wine used in a pagan libation rite. Such wine is prohibited for all usufruct and makes all food and drinks it comes in contact with forbidden in the most minute amount.</i>, if it was unintentional he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since the damage is invisible, it does not fall under the tort laws.</i>, intentional he is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">By a rabbinic rule for the public good (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.7">Tosephta 3:7</a>), that people should not do damage with impunity.</i>. If Cohanim in the Temple intentionally made [a sacrifice] <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">פִּגּוּל by biblical definition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:7" href="/Leviticus.19.7"><i>Lev.</i> 19:7</a>) is sacrificial meat on the third day after slaughter. The root seems to be preserved in Arabic فجل “to be soft”, as in Aramaic פּוּגְלָא “radish”, i. e., a soft, edible root. Applied to meat, which starts to decompose, probably a better translation would be “to be mushy”.<br>In rabbinic interpretation, the deadly sin is not to eat the meat when it becomes unsanitary but to slaughter and offer the blood to the altar with the idea of eating the sacrifice after its allotted time. Since the blood is only handled by the Cohen, not the person offering the sacrifice, the Cohen could disqualify the sacrifice and inflict a heavy financial loss on the faithful bringing the sacrifice simply by telling him that he handled the sacrifice with the wrong intention. Since this also is an invisible damage, it is clear that some penalties are in order for the public good (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:8" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.8">Tosephta 3:8</a>).</i>, they have to pay.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yudan did not go to the assembly. He was standing with Rebbi Mana and asked him, what new was said? He said: So says Rebbi Yose, a person causing impurity does not have the status of a perpetrator<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Because an outside observer could not notice any change in the food which was made impure.</i>, fromthe following<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.2.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 9:2</a>. If somebody stole heave and it became impure in his possession, he can hand it back to the owner and tell him, this is yours. But pure heave was food and impure heave can only be used as fuel; the difference in value cannot be claimed in court.</i>: “Heave which became impure.” He answered: Explain it, if it became impure by itself<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The passive formulation in the Mishnah invites this interpretation.</i>; then you cannot infer anything, because of a Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bekhorot</i> 4:4. The Mishnah is part of R. Yudan’s argument since it states that if a person who is not duly ordained exercizes the function of a rabbi or judge, he is personally responsible for any damage he causes by a wrong decision. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 53b" href="/Gittin.53b">Babli, 53b</a>, the same argument is made by Rav Papa.</i>: “If somebody judged, absolved the guilty and obligated the innocent, declared the pure impure and the impure pure.” For Rav Jeremiah said in the name of Rav: If he touched it with his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to pay in this case is not by the declaration that something is impure, but by the act of the incompetent judge who handles the material in question when he declares it impure. Since a person can make impure only by an action, either touching or moving, he equally will be responsible under the laws of torts.</i>. Samuel said: If he touched it with his hand. Rebbi Jehudah ben Rebbi says, it should have been logical that even intentionally he should have been free. Why did they say that he is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that “free” and “obligated” have to be interchanged. Since the prior argument showed that causing impurity is causing damage under the laws of torts, the person causing unintentional impurity should have to pay. The reason that courts refrain from applying the rules of torts in this case is that the damage is not visible and, in order to guard the owner of the impure food and others from unintentional sin, it is necessary to induce the person causing the damage to come forward and tell by granting him immunity.</i>? That he should tell. And Rebbi Joḥanan said, it should have been logical that even unintentionally he should have been obligated.
