Why did they say that he is free<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, it is obvious that “free” and “obligated” have to be interchanged. Since the damage cannot be detected, it cannot be claimed in court. The obligation to pay is not derived from the laws of torts but is a regulation “for the public good” in line with the other rules of this chapter.</i>? Because of a fine. The strength of Rebbi Joḥanan comes from this: “If someone causes anything to become impure, or <i>dema‘</i>, or libation wine, if it was unintentional he is free, intentional he is obligated,” and Rebbi Joḥanan said, he pays a fine. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “A person who puts a yoke on another’s cow is free in human law but guilty in Heaven’s law.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In the times of the Temple, a red (probably meaning reddish-brown) cow without any black hair was worth millions (as material for the purification from the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>) provided it never was used for work and never wore a yoke. Putting a yoke on such a cow for the shortest of times robs its owner of an enormous sum of money but, since the damage is not visible, it is not claimable in a human court. This follows the doctrine of R. Joḥanan that damages which cannot be observed on the object cannot be claimed in court.</i>”
So far if he thought that it was profane<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in the Mishnah that the person who inadvertently causes damage by making food impure does not have to pay. The only food where there would be a measurable diminution in value is sanctified food, of which heave is the most common example.</i>. If he knew that it was heave but he thought that it was permitted to make it impure? Let ushear from the following: “If Cohanim in the Temple intentionally made [a sacrifice] <i>piggul</i>, they have to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah does not state that “the Cohanim who cause <i>piggul</i> in the Temple have to pay”, it follows that there must be a scenario in which a Cohen could cause <i>piggul</i> inadvertently.</i>” Could the Cohen think that it was profane? Is there <i>piggul</i> of profane things? But we must hold that he knew that it was holy but thought that it was permitted to make <i>piggul</i> of sacrifices. And here, if he knew that it was heave but thought that it was permitted to make it impure.
Rav Huna in the name of Rav: If he mixed<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">He took wine which was used for a pagan rite and is forbidden for any use by a Jew, and mixed it with permitted wine of the same kind, so that the damage was not visible. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 52a" href="/Gittin.52a">Babli, 52a</a>, this interpretation of “libation wine” in the Mishnah is attributed to Samuel.</i>. If he made a libation and then mixed it<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is the interpretation of Rav.</i>, at the start he committed a capital crime and at the end he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since rabbinic doctrine excludes multiple punishment for one offense (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:4-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.4-14"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 19–70</a>) and idolatry is a capital crime, it seems difficult to understand why the culprit should pay. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 52b" href="/Gittin.52b">Babli, 52b</a>, notes that the argument is faulty in its understanding of the double penalty clause. The libation and the later mixing are two different acts. Even if there is only a libation, not followed by mixing with other wine, the libation can only be performed after the wine was poured into a vessel and the vessel lifted. Since we are speaking of wine belonging to a person other than the perpetrator, the latter becomes liable for all damages by lifting the vessel, some time before he commits the capital crime. It is clear, therefore, that the Yerushalmi view of the double punishment clause does not allow for splitting one action into separate parts.</i>? Rebbi Abba objected: Think of it, if he killed somebody and broke the flask, at the start he committed a capital crime and in the end he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody committed murder by administering poison in another person’s flask and in the act broke the flask, nobody will require the murderer to pay for the flask.</i>? Rebbi Yose objected: Think of it, if he set fire to somebody’s grain stack on the Day of Atonement, at the start he committed a capital crime and in the end he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Everything forbidden on the Sabbath is forbidden on the Day of Atonement (except that the crime will be punished by Heaven, rather than by the earthly court.) Since both making a fire and arson on the Day of Atonement are crimes punishable by Divine extirpation, no money is due (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.13"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Note 62</a>.)</i>? “If someone causes anything to become impure, or turn into <i>dema‘</i>, or libation wine, if it was unintentional he is free, intentional he is obligated,” and Rebbi Joḥanan said, he pays a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since the payment is a fine, not a judgment following biblical law, the principle of single punishment does not apply. This argument is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 53a" href="/Gittin.53a">Babli, 53a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan ben Gudguedah testified about a deaf-mute girl<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">She is presumed not to be able to read and write. Since she has no means of communication with the outside world, she is incompetent in law. She herself could not conclude a marriage valid by biblical standards, but her father can marry her off when underage in a biblically valid marriage. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 14:3" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.14.3"><i>Yebamot</i>, Mishnah 14:3, Notes 20–24</a>.<br>“Testified” usually means a statement of practice from Temple times accepted by the Jabneh Academy as binding.</i> who was married off by her father, that she must be divorced by a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since a divorce is a unilateral act by the husband as long as she is able to receive the bill. An insane woman, who cannot keep her bill of divorce, cannot be divorced.</i>, and about an underage girl who was married to a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the father had died and she was married in a rabbinically valid marriage by her mother or brothers, from which she may walk out without formality before she reaches adulthood (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.7"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:2, Note 38</a>).</i>, that she may eat heave and her husband inherits from her if she dies, and about a stolen log which was used to build a house<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian versions of the Mishnah, it is added “for the benefit of repentant sinners”, as explained in the Halakhah.</i>, that [the owner] has to take the value<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot sue the robber for restitution of the original log.</i>, and about a purification sacrifice of a stolen animal<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">It is a sinful act to use any stolen or robbed animal as a sacrifice. If such a sacrifice would be declared invalid, no Cohen would volunteer to serve in the Temple for fear of unwittingly committing the sin of eating from an invalid sacrifice. Therefore, a sacrifice can be rejected only if it is known that it was obtained by illegal means.</i> which was not known in public, that it atones because of the order of the altar.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 14:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.14.2.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 14:2, Notes 25–28</a>. The readings from there are noted <b>י</b>.</i>“Rebbi Joḥanan ben Guedgedah testified,” etc. Rebbi Ḥananiah asked before Rebbi Immi. His testimony seems reasonable for a hearing woman of sound mind, that she is divorced with or without her acquiescence. But a deaf-mute woman who has no will should not be divorced except with her acquiescence. But is it not testimony “that is an example”<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text from <i>Yebamot</i>. The text here, “his father is the same”, does not make any sense; it indicates that the text in <i>Yebamot</i> is original.</i>; does one add to a testimony? Rebbi Ḥanania is of the opinion that this refers to a hearing girl who was married off by her father (and mother<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of the mother is an addition here; legally the mother has no authority over her daughter.</i>) and became deaf-and-dumb. Rebbi Yose is of the opinion that this refers to an underage girl who was married off by her father (and mother<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of the mother is an addition here; legally the mother has no authority over her daughter.</i>) and became divorced.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, why did they say “an Israel underage girl eats heave,” that all should be eager to marry her for her attractiveness<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">And not be reluctant to take a very young girl off her parent’s hands.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said, only if she knows how to keep her [valuables given for] preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">He wants to abolish the theoretical age of 3 years and one day for the marriage of girls and requires that she know the difference between something given for a legal obligation and a toy.</i>. Rebbi Immi said, a Mishnah said so, “married to a Cohen she eats heave.<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah changes the topic from a deaf-mute to a minor it implies that only an Israel underage girl, which will reach understanding, but not a deaf-mute, is able to eat heave. It follows that as long as the girl is like a deaf-mute, having no understanding, she is not included. (A similar Mishnah, <i>Niddah</i> 5:4, speaking of a girl 3 years and 1 day old, could be used as counter-argument since such a girl, if married off by her father, is married even if mentally handicapped.)</i>” Rebbi Ḥananiah said, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, for her attractiveness, that means, even if she does not know how to keep her [valuables given for] preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan could have said that a minor can eat heave because she has understanding, to emphasize the difference between the minor and the deaf-mute. Since he chose to represent the ruling as rabbinical, to help marrying off underage girls, it follows that he does not require the girl to understand the situation. If the girl is given in marriage by her father, she is married by biblical rule, is part of the priestly clan, and eats heave by biblical law. If she was an orphan married off by her mother or brothers, if she actually lives with her husband she is part of her husband’s household and was acquired by his gift of valuables (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:11" href="/Leviticus.22.11"><i>Lev.</i> 22:11</a>).</i>.
Rav Hamnuna in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he always is called Rav Assi.</i> Assi: An underage girl has no definitive marriage to let her eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">An underage Israel girl married to a Cohen acquires the right to eat heave only by living with her husband, not by the definitive marriage ceremony.</i>. Rebbi Immi said, a Mishnah said so, “married to a Cohen she eats heave.<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Niddah</i> 5:4. The Mishnah does not say “by definitive marriage ceremony” but “being married to”, i.e., living with her husband.</i>” Rebbi Abin asked: If she was definitively married but had no intercourse, [the husband] sent her to her father’s house and she grew up, may she eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since an adult married to a Cohen can eat heave immediately, during the wedding meal, before sleeping with her husband.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “Based on her earlier definitive marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">There is a <i>baraita</i>, not otherwise attested to, which does not require any additional action to give all her rights to the adult married woman. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 58a" href="/Yevamot.58a">Babli (<i>Yebamot</i> 58a</a>) quotes dissenting opinions without deciding the matter.</i>”
“If somebody stole a log and used it in building. The House of Shammai say, he has to tear down the entire building and return it. But the House of Hillel say, for the benefit of repenting sinners<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.1.2-5"><i>Baba Qama</i> 9:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.5">Tosephta <i>Baba Qama</i> 10:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 55a" href="/Gittin.55a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 55a</a>.</i>, that he has to pay its full value.” Rebbi Eleazar said, where do they disagree? If he stole it planed, but if he stole and planed it himself, he owes him money<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">If he stole a piece of raw wood and turned it into building material, it cannot be returned to its original state. The robber acquired ownership of the material by changing it into something new; at the same time he incurred a debt to the original owner in the value of what he had taken (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.2.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 9:2</a>).</i>. If he stole and planed it on the spot<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Does acquisition by changing the object require removing it from the domain of the original owner?</i>, what is the rule? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 8:10, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.6.2"><i>Baba Qama</i> 10:6</a>,7; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.1.2-5">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Qama</i> 9:1</a> (6d 1.28); as an object of controversy <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 101a</a>.</i>: “If somebody appropriates another person’s ruin and rebuilds it without prermission, one appraises it and his hand is weak<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Tosephta, the owner of the bundle pays the builder the smaller amount either of the increase in value of his land and the building expenses.</i>. If he wants to take his logs and stones<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Which already form part of the building.</i>, one does not listen to him.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: It was asked in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: If somebody stores his bundle in somebody else’s ruin and wants to take it from there, does one not listen to him? He had not heard that Rebbi Jacob bar Idi<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This should be “Aḥa”.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Because of civilisation<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">One does not destroy houses except for an urgent need, either to avoid a collapse or to improve the housing stock. The question (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.5">Note 160</a>) is answered in the negative.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this is correct. We would have thought that they disagree only in the Land, but not outside the Land. Since Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, because of civilisation<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation is universal יִשׁוּב “civilization”, not narrow יִשׁוּב אֶרֶץ יִשְׂרָאֵל “settlement of the Land of Israel”.</i>, this implies that there is no difference between the Land and outside the Land. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The illegal builder.</i> wants to take [the value of] his logs and stones, what is the rule? One tells him, wait until he<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the land.</i> dwells in it. Samuel said, one pays him immediately.
Ulla bar Ismael: By law, it should not atone even if it was [not] known in public<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 55a" href="/Gittin.55a">Babli 55a</a>. There, Ulla explains that the sacrifice is not acceptable once the original owners have given up hope of recovery, since while abandonment makes a thing ownerless (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.6">Chapter 4, Note 88</a>), ownership requires a conscious act of acquisition by the person in actual possession..</i>. Why did they say that it atones? Not to let the altar be deserted<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">It is a sinful act to use any stolen or robbed animal as a sacrifice. If such a sacrifice would be declared invalid, no Cohen would volunteer to serve in the Temple for fear of unwittingly committing the sin of eating from an invalid sacrifice. Therefore, a sacrifice can be rejected only if it is known that it was obtained by illegal means.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli this is attributed to Rav Jehudah and it is explained that abandonment by the former owner makes the object the property of the person in possession.</i> said, by law, it should atone even if it was known in public. Why did they say that it does not atone? Not to let the altar accept robberies. How many are “public”? Rebbi Gorion in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Three people. Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “You brought robbed goods with the lame and the sick.<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 1:13" href="/Malachi.1.13"><i>Mal</i>. 1:13</a>.</i>” Since lame and sick are obvious, so everything must be obvious.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There was no law of <i>siqarii</i><sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">סִיקָרִיקִין or סִקָרִיקוֹן (in the Halakhah and everywhere in the Babli) is usually interpreted to refer both to <i>sicarii</i> (members of organized crime bands armed with a <i>sica</i>, “curved dagger”, and the rules promulgated to deal with these.<br>A. Gulak [<i>Siqariqon</i>, Tarbiz 5 (1934) pp. 23–27] objects to the derivation of <i>siqariqon</i> from <i>sicarii</i> since it is inconceivable that Rebbi’s court, in the early years of the third Century C.E. had to deal with the aftermath of the first revolt against the Romans, in the first half of the first Century. He proposes to see in the word a compression of σὺν κήρυκι to *συγκηρυκι “by herold” referring to the public sale of properties confiscated by the government which always was done by publicly announced auction. {While the Babli clearly connects the law of <i>siqariqon</i> with the first revolt against the Romans, the Yerushalmi squarely refers it to the revolt of Bar Kokhba. We know of no general confiscation of Jewish land after the first revolt; after the second there was at least the seizure of all Jewish property in and around Jerusalem. Since both Talmudim emphasize the cordial relations of Rebbi with the administration of the Severan dynasty and Rebbi clearly addressed a current problem, it seems rather that the law refers to a problem arising from a breakdown of internal security. Talmudic law certainly authorizes buying of property confiscated for nonpayment of taxes. Gulak’s interpretation is difficult to square with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.7.2">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 1:2</a>.}<br>A forced sale is intrinsically invalid since the seller can hope that in the future, when law and order are re-established, he may have a chance to appeal to a court and regain his property. But if a band of known <i>sicarii</i> give a property owner the choice of selling his land for a pittance or be killed together with his family, one may assume that he willingly gives the land to stay alive. In that situation, the land becomes the <i>siqarii</i>’s not only in possession but also in ownership, and may be sold by the current owner to any buyer. It is a matter of judgment of the court as to which situation does apply.</i> in Judea for those killed in the war<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">“The war” refers to the revolt of Bar Kokhba and its aftermath, as shown by the parallel in <i>Ketubot</i> to the first sentences in the Halakhah. S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Giṭṭin</i> p. 844) has noted that the reading “R. Joshua” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.7.1">Mishnah 7</a> is clearly a scribal error since Rav, a student of Rebbi, states both in the Yerushalmi (Note 205) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 58b-59a" href="/Gittin.58b-59a">Babli (58b/59a</a>) that he himself was a member of the court relaxing the <i>siqariqon</i> rules. Also, the Halakhah never refers to R. Joshua or any of his contemporaries. Therefore, one has to read in the Mishnah, with all sources other than the Mishnah in the Yerushalmi, “Rebbi assembled a court” in the first quarter of the third Century, to deal with the problem of organized crime in his own day. Together with the rules about kidnappings, this shows that the forces of the Roman Empire (at least starting with the Severan dynasty) were barely able to guarantee the external, but not the internal, security of the Empire.<br>The Mishnah states that land taken from Jews during the war can be bought by anybody who has the means, without regard to any prior owner. (In this case, <i>sicarii</i> is a code name for Roman soldiers.) The Babli connects the Mishnah to a long string of tales about the first war against the Romans; this should not be taken as indication that the Mishnah refers to that war.</i>. After those killed in the war there was a law of <i>siqarii</i>. How is this? If one bought from a <i>siqarius</i> and afterwards from the original owner, his acquisition is void<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">It was decided that the original owner retains claim to title even though he probably sold the land to save his life. Therefore, a potential buyer must first buy the right to title before buying the land.</i>; from the original owner and afterwards from the <i>siqarius</i>, his acquisition is valid. If one bought from a husband and afterwards from the wife, his acquisition is void<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The husband is not the owner of his wife’s (paraphernalia) properties, but their administrator. While the details of the sale must be contracted by the husband, prior consent of the wife is needed; otherwise, the husband is not authorized to deal and anything he agrees to is illegal.</i>; from the wife and afterwards from the husband, his acquisition is valid. This is the earlier Mishnah; a later court said that he who buys from a <i>siqarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Without needing prior consent. This is a regulation “for the public good”, to retain as much of the Land as possible in Jewish hands.</i> gives a quarter to the original owner.<br>When is this? If they have no means to buy, but if they have the means to buy, they precede everybody else<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">If the original owner has the cash available, he can pay the full price to the buyer and take back his title to the land.</i>. Rebbi (Joshua) assembled a court who voted that if it was in the <i>siqarius</i>’s hand for twelve months, any who wants to buy shall buy<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">After 12 months, the original owner lost his right to regain the land.</i> and give a quarter to the original owner.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There was no law of <i>siqariqon</i> in Judea” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence and part of the next is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.5.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 1:5 (Notes 196–199</a>). Variants are given by <b>כ</b>.</i> In earlier times they decided on a persecution in Judea because they had a tradition from their forefathers that Jehudah had killed Esaw, as it is written: “Your hand is on your enemies’ neck.” They went and enslaved them, took their fields and sold them to third parties. The original owners could come and repossess<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">After the Jewish court system was reestablished under R. Jehudah.</i>; therefore the land was left absolutely in the hand of the <i>siqariqon</i> since they refrained from buying<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the court held that a forced sale was invalid.</i>. “They decreed that the law of <i>siqariqon</i> should not apply in Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody had the right to reclaim from third parties land he had given away to save his life.</i>. When was this said? About war killings before the war<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">In the anarchy between the begin of the revolt and the occupation of the Land by Roman forces.</i>. But about anybody killed in and after the war, the notion of <i>siqariqon</i> does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>: “But about anybody killed in and after the war, the notion of <i>siqariqon</i> applies.” The Yerushalmi text should <i>not</i> be corrected based on Tosephta or Babli. Since one refers to the war of Bar Kokhba and the Hadrianic decrees in its aftermath, it is clear that the situation during and after the war was worse than before the war. One does not speak of a forced sale but of the rights of the heirs of a person who was killed and his land taken by Roman soldiers or civilians. It is clear that there was no sale. Since the greater part of the Jewish population of Judea was either killed in the war or deported as slaves, it made no sense for the organizers of the survivors to take notice of prior ownership. One has to assume that “Judea” means the Judean hills, to exclude the settlements in the plain centered around Lydda.</i>.” But are those killed before the war not like those killed after the war? Explain it that a <i>siqariqon</i> came and robbed and suppressed; there was no time left to write the sales contract before murder engulfed the entire world, that practice should not be partial<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Robberies during the anarchy before the Roman invasion cannot be separated from what happened during the war; in both cases no documentation can be recovered.</i>.
“The law of <i>siqariqon</i> always applies in Galilee.<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>. In Galilee, the Hadrianic decrees were never applied to dispossess Jewish farmers.</i>” The law of <i>siqariqon</i> does not apply to movables<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">If one buys movables from an organized crime enterprise, one does not have to give anything to the former owners since these had given up hope to recover (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.6">Note 169</a>).</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a>.</i>: “The law of <i>siqariqon</i> does not apply to foreclosures for a debt<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">If a Gentile forecloses for a loan, the Jewish buyer does not owe anything to a former Jewish owner.</i> and for tenant fees<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">A. Gulak has shown that ἀναφορά (“offering, installment, means of repairing a deficiency”) was the equivalent of Roman <i>vectigal</i>, the fee due by hereditary farmers of government land (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.5.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 10:5, Note 91</a>; <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Giṭṭin</i> p. 846). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 58a" href="/Gittin.58a">Babli, 58a</a>, treats ἀναφορά as robbery. It is not clear whether the term was also used for fees payable by hereditary tenants to landlords other than the government.</i>. [A foreclosure for nonpayment of] tenant fees waits twelve months for the owners<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">If a hereditary tenant is evicted for non-payment of the <i>vectigal</i>, a Jewish farmer cannot apply to fill his place before 12 months after his eviction, to give the original tenant time to settle with the government. {The text is confirmed by <i>Sefer haʻIṭṭur</i> II, 68b; the explanation follows S. Lieberman in his commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 3:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).3.11">Tosephta 3:11</a>.}</i>.” Rebbi Judah ben Pazi said, after twelve months one announces on four consecutive Sabbaths<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">After twelve months, there will be public anouncements that the twelve months’ grace period for the property (either one taken by organized crime or foreclosed for non-payment of the tax) has expired without action by the former owner.</i>. He explained it in two ways. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sicarius</i> did not sell the property but installed a tenant farmer to produce a continuous stream of income.</i> took it from a tenant and gave it to a tenant and the first wants to foreclose, he says to him, are you not a tenant? Leave it for me and what you cut, you cut<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>sicarii</i> are not interested in selling, the original tenant cannot bid for the property. But during the 12 months accorded to him, he can offer the new tenant (who moved in in violation of the 12 months’ rule) not to sue him in rabbinic court, to enter the property under the terms accorded by the <i>siqariqon</i>, and let the new tenant keep any yield he already had reaped.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">A similar situation in which the <i>sicarii</i> are not interested in selling, exists where the new tenant can arrange with the original owner to stay in place if and when he manages either to buy from the organized crime group or to recoup his title with government help.</i> took it from the owner and gave it to a tenant, the [owner] wants to foreclose, he tells him, what use do you have if you take it from me and he will take it from you, but what I can afford I shall give to you.
So far, if there were two contracts<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the part of the Mishnah in which husband and wife sell from the wife’s real estate. It is clear that the Mishnah text underlying the Yerushalmi is not the Mishnah of the ms. (which frequently represents a different tradition) but that of the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Mishnah (Napoli) and the Vienna ms. of the Tosephta: “If one bought from a wife and afterwards from her husband, his acquisition is void; from the husband and afterwards from his wife, his acquisition is valid.” S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah Giṭṭin</i> p. 847) considers this reading as “incomprehensible” and, against his declared intention, does base his text on the Vienna ms. in this case. But it seems that the text can be well understood, since all depends on the legal status of the property, whether it is mortmain or paraphernalia (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:1, Note 1</a>). Mortmain is the husband’s property; at the dissolution of the marriage the wife is entitled to receive it or its value back; during the marriage all she has is a mortgage lien on her husband’s properties. Therefore, if she sells mortmain properties, she sells what she does not have; this is invalid. But if the husband sells, she has a right of regress on the property if it should turn out that the estate is insufficient to cover her <i>ketubah</i> claim. A buyer may want to buy insurance against such a possibility by paying the wife for her final assent to the sale. If she receives a consideration for her signature, she cannot claim later that she only signed to please her husband. The Mishnah speaks of paraphernalia property, which is the wife’s and of which the husband only has rights of administration.</i>. If there was one contract<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the buyer might not pay separately for the wife’s signature.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Ila both say, certainly the sale is invalid; i. e., if he bought from the wife and then bought from the husband. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Bevon both said, certainly the sale is valid; i. e., if he bought from the husband and then bought from the wife. But is not all the wife’s property administered by the husband? For the yield<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Of the paraphernalia property.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: So it is: If he sold to a first person and she signed, to a second and a third and she signed, she can collect from the last<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">She signed on the sales contract after her husband and claims that she did so to please her husband, not as a commercial transaction. The court will uphold this claim. But the earlier buyer can claim that when he bought the real estate, there were left sufficient assets to cover the wife’s <i>ketubah</i> mortgage and his property should be immune to the wife’s claims.</i>. If it is insufficient, she collects from the one before the last. If this is insufficient, she collects from the one before the penultimate. On this, Rav said: For property which she brought him in her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Dos aestimata</i>, the value of which is written in the <i>ketubah</i> document and which establishes a mortgage on all the husband’s properties.</i>. But property which she brought to him as paraphernalia<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Dos non aestimata</i>, property she inherited or otherwise acquired during the marriage; any sale by the husband is invalid even if the wife signs the sale document after her husband; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:1, Note 1</a>.</i>, she collects from any she wants.
(The deaf-mute communicates by sign language.)<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">This heading is misplaced; it belongs after the next Mishnah. Probably it should read “Halakhah 7” and quote <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> They say<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>. Who has to bring proof if the heirs of the original owner say that they had the means to buy back the property but the actual buyer disputes their assertion?</i>, we had the means to buy; the buyer says, no. Rebbi Yose says, the field is always in the possession of the current owner<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sefer ha‘Iṭṭur</i> II, 66d “the buyers”. The commentary (Note 176) lists all other authors who copied from there.</i> since the burden of proof is on the claimant.
Rav said, I was of the voters<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the court convened by Rebbi which decreed that after 12 months the original owners or their heirs lost the right of first refusal but retained the right to an indemnity. The Mishnah does not spell out whether a third of the real estate should be returned or one speaks of a payment.</i>. There is no difference between a quarter in real estate or a quarter in money<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 58b" href="/Gittin.58b">Babli, 58b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, real estate for a denar a γραμμάριον<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">Greek γραμμάριον, the equivalent of Latin <i>scrupulus</i>, two obols, a third of a denar’s (or drachma’s) weight. As measures of land, the denar is <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>96</sub> and the scruple <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>288</sub> of a <i>iugerum</i> of 28’800 Roman square feet. R. Yose ben R. Abun holds that the “quarter” mentioned in the Mishnah is computed from above, a quarter of the total expense which is a third of the price paid to the criminal. The computation from above is that of Samuel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 58b" href="/Gittin.58b">Babli, 58b</a>.</i>, in money a quarter is a <i>tremis</i><sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">A third of an <i>aureus;</i> a large coin mentioned in <i>Threni rabba</i> 1(14) as טרימיסיא.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A deaf-mute communicates in sign language, and Ben Bathyra says, in pantomimes<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">This is Rashi’s explanation, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 5:10" href="/Job.5.10"><i>Job</i> 5:10</a>. Maimonides’s explanation is based on a meaning “to jump” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 2:8" href="/Song_of_Songs.2.8"><i>Cant</i>. 2:8</a>) meaning that the deaf-mute has to bring and handle the object.</i>, referring to movables. Underage children: their buy is a buy and their sale is a sale, referring to movables.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Ben Bathyra says, in pantomimes<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">This is the quote from the Mishnah but it is not discussed.</i>.” He hires and is hired<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">The deaf-mute, by sign language.</i>. The artless, small children<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">The uncommon Semitic word פָּעוּט is compared to Greek ἀποίητος “artless”, παιδία “childhood”. (Explanation of H. L. Fleischer.) S. Lieberman (<i>Tosephta kiFshutah</i> p. 847) explains אפיוטות, הפיוטות as Greek ἑπτέτης, ἑπταετής, adj., “seven years old”. There is no reason to single out that age.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of their livelihood. Rebbi Mana said, even though Rebbi Yose raises difficulties in collecting forfeits<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that nobody signs a contract that imposes a fine on himself with the idea that he really will have to pay the fine. Therefore, a contract which contains a forfeit clause for each party may be <i>asmakhta</i> and invalid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 117</a>); the court is reluctant to enforce such a clause. (In modern Hebrew, עִיצוּמִים are labor union actions just short of a strike.)</i>, he agrees that those who give their children to artisans that they collect forfeits because of the livelihood of people<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">If an artisan bows out of a contract for an apprentice, he will have to pay a fine since the child needs to learn a trade to be able to make a living.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following was said for communal peace: A Cohen reads first, after him a Levite, after him an Israel, for communal peace<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">In any public reading from the Torah, a Cohen has to be given precedence (if there is one present), followed by a Levite. If there is no Levite present, the Cohen is called for a second reading since all Cohanim are Levites (<i>Maʻaśer Šeni</i> 5:5, Note 90).</i>. One puts an <i>eruv</i> in an old house, for communal peace<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">In a common courtyard serving many houses with different owners one cannot carry on the Sabbath unless one symbolically unites all houses by depositing some common food in one of them. This is called עֵרוּב חֲצֵרוֹת “the combination of courtyards”. If one house was the depository for some time, one cannot change the place of deposit without insulting the owner of that house; therefore, it is forbidden to change.</i>. A cistern which is close to a water canal is filled first, for communal peace<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">The order of filling cisterns (or irrigation of fields) is by the length of the side canal used by cistern or field, to avoid quarrels.</i>. Finds of a deaf-mute, or an insane person, or a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">These cannot legally acquire anything they find since they have no legal standing. But it is forbidden to act on this fact.</i> fall under the category of robbery, for communal peace; Rebbi Yose says, complete robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will explain when taking finds from an incompetent person is a prosecutable offense.</i>. Traps for wild animals, birds, or fish fall<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">If set in the public domain under rules which permit setting up such traps, the catch is private property and protected as such.</i> under the category of robbery, for communal peace; Rebbi Yose says, complete robbery. If one knocks off [olives] from high up in the olive tree<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Olives are harvested by shaking the olive tree and collecting under the tree. Those which did not fall off are left for the poor. The person who climbs into the tree to knock off the remaining olives is a poor person, not the owner of the orchard. What he knocks off and causes to fall under the tree is protected as his property. (The Mishnah in the Babli and in several Mishnah mss. is more explicit in speaking about the poor.)</i>, it falls under the category of robbery, for communal peace; Rebbi Yose says, complete robbery. One does not hinder poor Gentiles [from taking] gleanings, forgotten sheaves, or <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">Since one supports the Gentile poor for communal peace.</i>, for communal peace.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following was said for communal peace,” etc. The saying of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai implies that it is of the words of the Torah, for Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated: “Moses wrote this Torah and gave it to the Cohanim, the Levites (etc.)” and afterwards “to all the Elders of Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:9" href="/Deuteronomy.31.9"><i>Deut</i>. 31:9</a>. The implication is that in all matters of reading the Torah the Cohanim and Levites have precedence over Israel Elders. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 58b" href="/Gittin.58b">Babli, 58b</a>, the author of the statement is given as the Amora R. Mattaniah.</i>” The saying of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi implies that it is of their words, as Rebbi Joshua said, never did I recite the benediction before a Cohen and I did not allow an Israel to do it before him<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">If it were a Torah obligation to give precedence to Cohanim, there would be no reason to point it out as a sign of particular religiosity. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 28a" href="/Megillah.28a">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 28a</a>, the statement is attributed to the wonder-working R. Perida. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:4:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.4.2-8">Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i> 5:5</a> (9d 1.3), the reference is that R. Joshua ben Levi never let any non-Cohen publicly recite Grace in the presence of a Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.5">Halakhah 5:5, Notes 163</a>,164.)</i>. The saying of Rebbi Ḥanina implies that it is of their words, as Rebbi Ḥanina said, in a city of only Cohanim, an Israel reads first because of communal peace<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">If it were a Torah obligation, then even to avoid quarrels it would not be possible to call an Israel.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 5:4:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.5.4.2-8"><i>Berakhot</i> 5:5</a>, explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:5-7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.5-7.1">Notes 167–175</a>. The readings of the Leiden ms. are noted by <b>ב</b>, of the Rome ms. by <b>ר</b>,</i> Rebbi Aḥa and Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Simai: In a city of only Cohanim, all of them lift their hands. For whom do they recite the blessing? For their brothers to the North and South. Who answers after them? Women and children<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">There may be a material difference between the two texts since קטנים refers to all underage children but טף only to those who still need their mothers. The <i>Berakhot</i> text might allow almost adult children to join their fathers in the priestly blessing.</i>. Abbaie bar Benjamin stated: People who stand behind the Cohanim are not included in the blessing. For those in front of them, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that even an iron wall does not interrupt. Those who stand to their side? Let us hear from the following: “If he intended to sprinkle before himself but sprinkled behind himself, behind himself but sprinkled before himself; his sprinkling is invalid. Before himself but sprinkled sideways, his sprinkling is valid.” This implies that those who stand to their side are included in the blessing. Rav Ḥisda said, it is necessary that the organizer be an Israel. Rav Ḥisda<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The name attributions in <i>Berakhot</i> seem preferable since this style of repetition is not usual.</i> said, if there was one Cohen, he says “Cohen.” For two, he says “Cohanim.” But Rav Huna said, even for one he says “Cohanim” since he only calls the tribe, lest you say, probably person X is an adulterer or a murderer,<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">This is an exaggeration since a homicide is barred from reciting the priestly blessing. It simply means that people might be offended if others think that they could dispense blessings to them. It has to be emphasized that the Cohen is just fulfilling a hereditary duty; his person is not giving the blessing.</i> and he shall bless us? The Holy, praise to Him, said: Who blesses you? Am not I blessing you? As it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev</i>. 6:27, the verse following the text of the priestly blessing.</i>: “They shall put My name over the Children of Israel and I shall bless them.”
“One puts an <i>eruv</i> in an old house.” Rebbi Abin said, the Mishnah speaks of a prior dweller<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Not the house is offended if the <i>eruv</i> is removed from it but the owner of the house. Therefore, if there is a new owner in an old house, one may move the location of the <i>eruv</i>. The statement is copied in <i>Eruvin</i> 6, 23d 1.40.</i>.
“A cistern which is close to a water canal is filled first.” Only if it passes through it<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 60b" href="/Gittin.60b">Babli, 60b</a>, this is one interpretation given by Samuel, that the owner of the cistern cannot be forced to install a lock.</i>. Samuel said, even if it is close by. There<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia. This is not mentioned in the Babli, which prefers to have the situation handled without set rules. “Drink” may mean “being irrigated”.</i>, they say what is farther away but easy to drink from precedes one which is close by but difficult to drink from.
<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">The text is from <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i> 4:4, Notes 80–90. Variants are noted <b>מ</b>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.6">Note 236</a>.</i> Following the opinion of the rabbis there it is acceptable since there, they say in the name of Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: One to whom one gives a nut and he throws it away, a pebble and he keeps it, what is found in his hand is as if found on a garbage heap; a nut and he keeps it, a pebble and he throws it away, what is robbed from him is robbed because of communal peace; a nut or a pebble he takes, hides them, and produces them later, what is robbed from him is total robbery. He can acquire for himself but not for others. Rav Huna said, just as he can acquire for himself so he can acquire for others. Everybody agrees that his gift is not a gift since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>): “If a man give.” The gift of a man is a gift, but the gift of a minor is no gift, the words of the Sages. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, robbing from him is not clear<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">מחזר is a misspelling for מְחֻוָּר, a Babylonian spelling for Yerushalmi מְחוּבָּר “(logically) connected; clear”. It is not derived from the Aramaic root חור “pale”. Cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Zur Aussprache des Bet in talmudischer Zeit</i>, Bulletin des Vereins jüdischer Lehrer und Kantoren der Schweiz 21 (1977), 4–5.</i> unless he grew two pubic hairs. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, that is, to recover from him by a law suit, but to have to bring a sacrifice for [a false] oath only if he grew two pubic hairs. But following the rabbis here, Rebbi Yose asked that even for himself he should not be able to acquire since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>): “A man to his neighbor”, until he be like his neighbor. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac explained it by the method of the artless. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is from here, this shows that the original is in <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i>.</i>: “For underage children, their buying is buying and their selling selling, for movables.” But did we not state there<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 4:4.</i>: “He cannot make them acquire through his minor son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave, because their hand is like his hand.” The rabbis of Caesarea say, here a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The talmudic expression תִּינּוֹקוֹת שֶׁל בֵּית רַבָּן “schoolchildren” shows that the word denotes older children. The root is ינק “to suckle”.</i> with knowledge, there a minor without knowledge.
“Traps for wild animals, birds,” etc. That is<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">The difference of opinion between the Sages, who hold that taking from them is only rabbinically forbidden, and R. Yose, who holds that taking from traps is biblically a sin.</i>, about large ones<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Where animals are trapped but not immobilized; they cannot be considered acquired by the owner of the trap, in contrast to small traps which immobilize the prey. In the Babli, 60a/61b, in the interpretation of Rashi and R. Hananel, it is stated similarly that animals or birds trapped in nets are acquired by the trapper but those caught in larger contraptions constructed of planks are subject to the controversy of the Mishnah.</i>. But small ones are as if inside his house.
“If <i>a poor person</i> knocks off [olives] from high up in the olive tree, <i>what is under him</i> falls under the category of robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">The longer version of the Mishnah (indicated by <i>italics</i>) is close to the text of the Babli and most Mishnah mss.</i>, for communal peace; Rebbi Yose says, complete robbery.” That means, on the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">What falls down on the ground is only protected by rabbinic rule. But what the poor person picks and puts in a vessel is his property by biblical standards.</i>. But if he plucks with his hand, his hand acquires for him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">The text, to the end of the Halakhah, is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:3:8-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.3.8-9"><i>Demai</i> 4:6, Notes 76–79</a>. Variants from there are noted <b>ד</b>; a (Babylonian spelling) variant from the Venice print by ו. The text there is probably copied from a ms. which underwent fewer changes than the text here.</i>“One does not hinder poor Gentiles [from taking] gleanings, forgotten sheaves, or <i>peah</i>, for communal peace.” It was stated: “In a town where Gentiles and Jews live together, one appoints overseers of charity from Gentiles and Jews; one provides for the Gentile and Jewish poor, visits Gentile and Jewish sick, buries Gentile and Jewish dead, and consoles Gentile and Jewish mourners. Also, one washes Gentile and Jewish clothes for the sake of peaceful coexistence.”
A weaver asked Rebbi Immi, what about a Gentile wedding feast? He wanted to permit it to them from this: “Because of peaceful coexistence.” Rebbi Abba told them, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state that the holiday of a Gentile wedding feast is forbidden? Rebbi Immi said, if it had not been for Rebbi Abba, we would have come to permit their strange worship; praised be He Who separated us from them.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is from <i>Ševiʻit</i> 5:9, Notes 80–98. The variant ms. readings from there are noted <b>ש</b>. The last two paragraphs are also from <i>Ševiʻit</i> 4:3, Notes 50–57.</i> A woman may lend to her neighbor, whom she suspects in matters of the Sabbatical, a coarse sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This is also quoted as Rebbi’s opinion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 69a" href="/Ketubot.69a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 69a</a>.</i>, a fine sieve, grindstones, or an oven<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:6</a>.</i>, but she should neither cull nor grind with her. The wife of a Fellow may lend to the wife of a vulgar<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 51a" href="/Ketubot.51a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 51a</a>, the question never arises since the claims of the widow and the daughters are accorded equal status; this rule is quoted in <i>Yebamot</i> 15:3 (14d 1.68), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.2-15"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8</a> (29a 1.25).</i> a coarse sieve and a fine sieve, she may cull, grind, and sift with her, but she may not touch hers once water has been put in<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8, Notes 200–201</a>. Variant readings are given there.</i> because one does not support transgressors<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuʻot</i> 6:1. The subject is the biblical oath imposed on a person who denies an obligation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>). The rabbinic interpretation of the expression אֲשֶׁר יֹאמַר כִּי הוּא זֶה is “if he [the defendant] agrees that there is a case.” If the defendant in a civil suit, in which there are no witnesses and no documents, denies the entire claim, he does not have to swear a biblical oath (he may have to swear a rabbinical oath). But if he agrees to part of the claim, he has to swear a biblical oath to free himself from the remainder. Only if the claim is advanced as tentative, then any admission by the defendant is a gift to the claimant and by rabbinic rule no oath of any kind is due.</i>. All this has only been said for communal peace. One<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The speaker is the claimant.</i> encourages Gentiles in the Sabbatical year but not Jews. One greets them for communal peace.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A woman may lend to her neighbor,” etc. Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Mana: Does our Mishnah deal with the implicit case, rather than the explicit? He said to him, is the implicit case not explicit? He said to him, I can say a coarse sieve to count coins, a sieve to sift sand, grindstones to grind spices, a stove to put in flax bundles.
Rebbi Phineas asked: For what did they impose a fine? At a place where they sow and eat or at a place where they sow but do not eat? What is the difference? If one saw him buying from Saracens. <sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">See variant readings and the translation in <i>Ševiʻit</i> for the text that probably was omitted here.</i> It is forbidden; if you say at a place where they sow and eat, if one saw him buying from Saracens it is permitted.
Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina asked: Has this been said for all of this chapter or only for this Mishnah? The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Judah bar Titus: From the fact that we state this Mishnah in <i>Giṭṭin</i> it follows that it was said only for this Mishnah.
There, we have stated: One does not knead or form bread with a baker working in impurity. And it was stated additionally: One should neither cull nor grind with him. And here, it says so? Rebbi La said, here for profane [bread], there for heave. Can you say heave at a baker’s? The colleagues say, one if he washes, there if he does not wash. The Mishnah supports the colleagues: “But she may not touch hers once water has been put in.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of the paragraph is missing in this copy; cf. the full text in <i>Seviʻit</i> 4:3, (Notes 50–57). The paragraph should not be taken as a variant of the text but as a hint to look up the original.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Ammi. One said: “Plough it well then, I will rent it from you after the Sabbatical.” The other one said: “May you succeed” For him who says, plough it well, then I will rent it from you after the Sabbatical, what means “one greets them” in a Jewish way? “May you succeed.” For him who says to the Gentile “may you succeed”? Explanation: Rebbi Ḥinena bar Pappa and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman passed by one of the ploughmen in the Sabbatical. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said to him, are you not Jewish? Rebbi Ḥinena bar Pappa said to him, did not Rebbi teach us (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 129:8" href="/Psalms.129.8"><i>Ps</i>. 129:8</a>): “The passers-by did not say, the blessing of the Eternal be on you,” these are the nations of the world who pass out of the world, they did not say to Israel “the blessing of the Eternal is on you.” What does Israel say to them, “we bless you in the Name of the Eternal.” All blessings that come into the world because of us are not enough for you; not only do you not say, come and take from these blessings for yourselves, but you roll over us pro-rated contributions and fines, head taxes and <i>annonae</i>.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who says, accept this bill of divorce for my wife, or bring this bill of divorce to my wife, may retract if he wants to retract<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband appoints an agent, he may annul the agency any time as long as the bill was not delivered.</i>. If a woman said, accept the bill of divorce for me, he<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The husband cannot retract the bill of divorce once it has been delivered to the wife’s agent whose hand acts for her hand.</i> cannot retract even if he wants to retract. Therefore, if the husband said, it is not possible for me that you accept for her but bring and deliver it to her, he may retract if he wants to retract<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The husband may refuse to recognize the messenger as the wife’s agent but appoint him as his own agent. Then the messenger acts uniquely as the husband’s agent.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even if she says, take my the bill of divorce for me, he cannot retract even if he wants to retract<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the wife’s agency was not spelled out clearly.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who says, accept this bill of divorce for my wife,” etc. Everywhere you say that “accept” is the equivalent of “obtain,”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If A says to B: “accept this gift for C”, then if B has accepted the gift it is C’s property and A cannot retract the gift. Why can the husband annul the bill of divorce when he empowered the agent to receive the bill of divorce for his wife?</i> but here you say that “accept” is the equivalent of “bring”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The divorce takes effect only by delivery of the bill into the woman’s hand.</i>? There is a difference, for one can obtain something for a person without him being present<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 11b" href="/Gittin.11b">Babli (11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 11a" href="/Ketubot.11a"><i>Ketubot</i> 11a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 118b" href="/Yevamot.118b"><i>Yebamot</i> 118b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23a" href="/Kiddushin.23a"><i>Qiddušin</i> 23a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 24a" href="/Kiddushin.24a">24a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 81b" href="/Eruvin.81b"><i>Eruvin</i> 81b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12a" href="/Bava_Metzia.12a"><i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 12a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 63a" href="/Chullin.63a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 63a</a>): “One may give a benefit to a person without his knowledge but one cannot put a detriment on him without his knowledge.” Since a divorce is considered detrimental to the wife, the husband has no power to appoint an agent to receive the bill for his wife; his instruction has to be re-interpreted to mean that he has to deliver the bill to the wife.</i>. Rebbi Shammai objected: Think of it, if she was shouting to obtain a divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Then the divorce would be a benefit to her.</i>? I say, maybe she changed her mind. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2:8, Note 139.</i>: “A woman herself may bring her bill of divorce”? Could you say that she may have changed her mind<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband hands the bill of divorce to his wife and stipulates that the divorce become valid only at the moment she brings the bill to the court at her place of residence which is not his place, can she change her mind, return to her place, and not showing the bill, being then married to her husband without his knowledge?</i>? There, she is damaging herself<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">By accepting to be her husband’s agent, she accepted the obligation to become divorced; this is with her knowledge and cannot be compared to the agency of a third party. This interpretation is by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 118b" href="/Yevamot.118b">Rashba (on <i>Yebamot</i> 118b</a>), quoted by <i>Nimmuqe Yosef</i> (on Alfassi <i>Yebamot</i>, end of Chapter 15).</i>; here it is to her detriment.
<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A similar discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 62b-63a" href="/Gittin.62b-63a">Babli, 62b–63a</a>, quoting the same authors but in a different setting. (In the Babli, the husband shows that he does not quite believe the agent; here the husband acts on the agent’s word.)</i> Rav Naḥman bar Jacob said: If she said, bring me my bill of divorce, but he went and said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The wife’s agent speaking to the husband.</i>, your wife said, accept the bill of divorce for me<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wife can empower an agent to accept the bill of divorce for her, “accept” in this context must mean “acquire”. The agent violated the terms of his agency.</i>, even after the bill came into her hand it is not a valid bill of divorce, since he<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The husband.</i> thinks that she will be divorced by the acceptance of the agent but she can be divorced only by her own acceptance<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires that the husband put the bill of divorce into the wife’s hand, it is a biblical requirement that both husband and wife know the moment of divorce. In the case before us, the defect of the divorce is biblical and cannot be remedied except by a new bill.</i>. Rebbi Huna bar Ḥiyya objected: But did we not state that if a woman said, accept the bill of divorce for me, it is not a valid bill of divorce until it reaches her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The quote is elliptic; most probably it refers to a text close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a> where the wife appoints an agent to <i>receive</i> her bill of divorce and the husband instructs him to <i>deliver</i> the bill into her hand.</i>? Then even if the bill came into her hand it should not be a valid bill! For she thinks that she will be divorced by the acceptance of the agent but she can be divorced only by her own acceptance. If he knows that practice is that “accept” and “obtain” mean “bring”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">While the wife can appoint an agent to legally receive the bill in her stead, the husband cannot appoint such an agent and, as explained in the preceding paragraph, for him “receive” means “deliver”. The husband is free to interpret the wife’s instructions in terms of his own vocabulary. {The Babli, obviously dissatisfied with this solution, holds that the husband’s instructions must be followed to the letter but since the wife can be divorced against her will, the husband’s instructions override those of the wife and the latter’s instructions were given only subject to the husband’s approval.}</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah 6:3</a>.</i>: “If an underage girl said, accept the bill of divorce for me, it is not a valid bill until the bill of divorce reaches her hand.” Then it should not be a valid bill even if the bill of divorce reaches her hand. For she thinks that maybe she is divorced by the acceptance of the agent, but she can be divorced only by her own acceptance. If he knows that a minor cannot appoint an agent<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">While an underage girl becomes emancipated from her father’s <i>potestas</i> by marriage (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.3.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:2, Note 42</a>), she can become an adult only by reaching the age of puberty. If she is divorced while being underage, she becomes “an orphan during her father’s lifetime” and will need an adult guardian to execute legal acts in her behalf. The husband in this case has to empower the agent to deliver the bill; he cannot rely on the underage wife’s instructions.</i>.
Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: A woman cannot appoint an agent to accept her bill of divorce from her husband’s agent<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli, 63b</a>, and opposed there by the Galilean R. Ḥanina. Rabbinic practice follows R. Ḥanina. The Babli conjectures that a possible reason for Rav’s statement is that if the wife refuses to accept the bill from the husband’s agent but appoints her agent to receive the bill, she does this only to hinder the husband’s agent to fulfill his mission to deliver the bill to her. In that case, the wife would be entitled to appoint her agent if her appointment precedes her husband’s. Another conjectured reason is that there is a very remote possibility that things can go wrong if the bill is not actually delivered from hand to hand but deposited on her (or her agent’s) property. The basis for the possible complication is a rule which is not alluded to in the Yerushalmi and, therefore, should not be used to explain a statement in that Talmud.</i>. Samuel heard this from him and repeated after him forty times.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The major source of this paragraph and the next is in <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i> 4:7, Notes 118–129. Since the introduction is different and some essential passages are missing here, it is clear that this passage is derivative. But since the text has suffered some rearrangement, it cannot be a direct copy (as noted in the Introduction.)</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: So far if she said, bring me, but [the agent] said [to the husband] “receive for me”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since the agent was not appointed by the husband, he cannot legally deliver the bill.</i>. If she said, receive for me but [the agent] said [to the husband] “bring me”? If was found stated in both cases, this is a bill of divorce and if he wants to retract he cannot retract<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Probably a text similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63a" href="/Gittin.63a">Babli 63a</a>) following the three main sources of the Tosephta (a text rejected by S. Lieberman.) While the bill is considered delivered only when the wife receives it, the agent is not empowered to return it to the husband. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63a" href="/Gittin.63a">Babli, 63a</a>, explicitly states that the divorce is valid from the moment the agent receives the bill.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This inference from the repetition of the expression וְנָתַן בְּיָדָהּ in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>,3 is missing in other sources; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.2-5">Halakhah 8:1</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 41a" href="/Kiddushin.41a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 41a</a>, derives the possibility of divorce by agent (שָׁלִיחַ) from the expression וְשִׁלְּחָהּ in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> For the general principle that “an agent takes the place of a principal” cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a> (62a 1.41), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 41b" href="/Gittin.41b">Babli 41b</a>. It is not trivial that a bill of divorce can be delivered by an agent since <i>ḥalîṣah</i> cannot be performed by an agent.</i>: “He shall deliver into her hand,” that includes not only her hand, from where through his agent’s hand, into her agent’s hand, from his agent to her agent, and from her agent to his agent; the verse says, “he shall deliver, he shall deliver.” Who said, “it is impossible for me”? Rebbi! <sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the text from <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i>, Notes 120 ff. The quote in <i>Ma’aser Šeni</i> is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.1">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:1</a>; the text here, while almost identical, has a totally different meaning. In the Tosephta, the husband instructs the agent to act for him; in the text here, the husband does not say anything but gives the bill to the agent who delivers it to the wife. It then is stated that the act of delivery validates the divorce.</i> As it was stated: “If she said, bring me my bill of divorce, but he said to him, your wife said, accept my bill of divorce for me: If he brought it to her, gave it to her, he acquired it for her, it was received for her; if he wants to retract, he may not<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta mss., “not” is missing.</i> retract, the words of Rebbi. Rebbi Nathan says, ‘bring it to her, give it to her,’ <sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i>, missing here but essential for an understanding of the text. The omission from להחזיר to להחזיר is easily explained.</i>[if he wants to retract, he may retract; ‘acquire it for her, receive it for her’, if he wants to retract, he may not retract. Rebbi says, in any of these cases] he may not retract except if he says, ‘It is impossible for me that you accept it for her, but give it to her.’<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband explicitly appoints the agent as his own.</i>” It is difficult for Rebbi, “here you have it from my word”, if he wants to retract, he may retract<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband gives the bill and tells the agent to deliver, it should automatically invalidate the wife’s commission and let him retract.</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Nathan, “here you have it from her word”, if he wants to retract, he may retract<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband tells the agent to do for him what the wife instructed him to do, he did not accept the agent as her agent and should be able to retract.</i>! Rav Ḥuna said, he becomes his and her agent<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In either case mentioned here, it is not clear whose agent he is; the divorce is questionable and does not permit the wife to remarry. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli 63b</a>, Rav Huna in the name of Rav).</i>. Assi said, everywhere we have stated “his and her agent”, she is both divorced and not divorced.
Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose: Is not this disagreement the same as that disagreement, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i> 4:7.</i>: “If a man was talking with a woman about her bill of divorce or her preliminary marriage and gave her the bill of divorce or [some valuable for] preliminary marriage and did not spell it out<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">There were two witnesses present but the man did not alert them that he was going to divorce or marry, neither did he warn the woman that he was now going to act on his word.</i>, Rebbi Yose says it is sufficient<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is sufficient that everybody involved understood what was going on.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says he has to be explicit.” Then Rebbi follows Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Nathan Rebbi Jehudah. He<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose to R. Ḥaggai.</i> said to him, what do you have in your hand, did not Rebbi Zeïra, Ḥiyya bar Abin, Abba bar Taḥlifa, say in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia, when do they differ? [If they thought about] other things but if he still was on the same subject, it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Jehudah will agree that if two witnesses are present during the writing of a bill of divorce they do not have to be alerted for the delivery. But this has no implications for our case where the question is the due form of the appointment of an agent. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 6a" href="/Kiddushin.6a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 6a</a>, this is the concurrent opinion of Rav and Samuel.</i>. But here, even if they are still on the same subject there is disagreement.
Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Mana: Is it the same for a gift? Can a person appoint an agent for anything that is not his? He said to him, there the Torah gave her the right to a bill of divorce and she appoints an agent to receive what is rightfully hers. Can you say in regard to a gift that a person can appoint an agent for anything that is not his? In addition, from what Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, if somebody said to give a gift to another and he wants to retract, he may retract.
