Rebbi Yose stood near Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi and said to him, is that a just “yes”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev</i>. 19:36</a>: “Your <i>hîn</i> shall be just”. The הִין is a liquid measure (half a <i>se’ah</i>). But since biblical כֵּן “yes” became rabbinic הֵן, the verse is interpreted to mean that in money matters any “yes” has to be honest. The formal argument is that the verse already mentioned the <i>ephah</i> as correct measure; therefore the smaller <i>hîn</i> requires symbolic interpretation. [This learned pun is in the name of R. Yose ben R. Jehudah in the Babli, <i>Baba meṣiʻa</i> 49a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 13b" href="/Bekhorot.13b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 13b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 8 7" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_8.7"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i> 8(7</a>)].</i>? He said to him, at the moment he said it, it was a just “yes”.
If [the husband] said to [another person]: Bring my wife’s bill of divorce, or her daughter’s, or her sister’s, then he went and delivered it to her, it is no divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The text is not in good condition. A similarly corrupt text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>, but from both texts the original can be approximated: If the husband said to a third party: Bring my wife’s, or (<i>your</i> or <i>his</i>) daughter’s or (<i>your</i> or <i>his</i>) sister’s, and he went and delivered it to her, it is no divorce since the third party was never appointed an agent for delivery. But if a woman appoints an agent, who goes and requests a bill of divorce from her husband and receives the bill without being appointed the husband’s agent of delivery, it is a perfectly valid divorce the moment the agent takes possession of the bill. (According to Rav Huna, this situation is preferable to one in which the agent also becomes the husband’s agent, unless the husband explicitly invalidates the wife’s commission.)</i>. But if [the other] said: Give a bill of divorce to his wife, and [the husband] said, here is my wife’s bill of divorce, or her daughter’s, or her sister’s, and he went and gave it to her, it is a divorce. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun and Rebbi Avin in the name of Rebbi Shammai: These are Rebbi’s words. But the Sages say, only if she says, it should be mine in your hand<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi accepts an implicit commissioning while the rabbis require an explicit statement.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanina said, practice follows Rabban Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>: if she said “take for me, it should be mine in your hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Not only the expression טוֹל לִי mentioned in the Mishnah but any equivalent expression is sufficient to appoint an agent for the wife. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli agrees, 63b</a>.</i>” A man sent a bill of divorce to his wife. She told [the agent]: “It should be mine in your hand.” The casecame before Rav, who said, if he wants to retract he cannot retract<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bill was never delivered into the wife’s hand, the husband then claimed that there was no divorce; but Rav decided that the appointment of the husband’s agent as the wife’s agent represented delivery of the bill.</i>. Does he disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">It is not quite clear with what Rav is supposed to disagree. A similar story is told in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli, 63b</a>, about a woman whose hands were dirty and who told the husband’s agent to keep the bill, that it should be in his hand. Rav Naḥman, in the generation after Rav, decided that this was no delivery.</i>? There is a difference between “it should be in your hand” and “it should be mine in your hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The second version is clearly an acceptance of the bill whereas the first one is not.</i>” Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, I know the beginning and the end. A woman appointed an agent to accept her bill of divorce from the husband’s agents. The case came before Rav, who said, if he wants to retract he cannot retract. Rav’s opinion has switched! There, Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: A woman cannot appoint an agent to accept her bill of divorce from her husband’s agent<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 63b" href="/Gittin.63b">Babli, 63b</a>, and opposed there by the Galilean R. Ḥanina. Rabbinic practice follows R. Ḥanina. The Babli conjectures that a possible reason for Rav’s statement is that if the wife refuses to accept the bill from the husband’s agent but appoints her agent to receive the bill, she does this only to hinder the husband’s agent to fulfill his mission to deliver the bill to her. In that case, the wife would be entitled to appoint her agent if her appointment precedes her husband’s. Another conjectured reason is that there is a very remote possibility that things can go wrong if the bill is not actually delivered from hand to hand but deposited on her (or her agent’s) property. The basis for the possible complication is a rule which is not alluded to in the Yerushalmi and, therefore, should not be used to explain a statement in that Talmud.</i>, and here, he says so? I say, he was standing behind the door and heard her voice<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The relations between husband and wife were so bad that not only did the husband not want to deliver the bill to his wife who was nearby but the wife did not even want to receive it from the husband’s agent. Since the appointment of an agent by the husband under these circumstances was intended as an insult to his wife, her appointing her own agent was an adequate response. Since the husband was present during the entire proceedings, he must have agreed with his wife’s appointing her own attorney and there could be no reason to invalidate the proceedings. The statement of Rav Huna can be applied only to cases where not both parties are present.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A woman who said, accept my bill of divorce for me, needs two groups of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since a divorce is a public act it must be executed in front of at least two witnesses. The agent certainly needs two witnesses for the delivery since the woman herself must receive the bill in front of two witnesses. It is only natural that the agent needs two witnesses to legitimize his status (or be appointed by a duly witnessed document.)</i>; two who say that she appointed in their presence and two who say that in their presence he received it and tore it up<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is not necessary that the bill be torn up after delivery; this clause is added only to show that the divorce is valid even if the document never reaches the wife because it was torn up after delivery. (This has nothing to do with current rabbinic practice to cut up the bill of divorce after delivery in order to prevent outsiders from questioning the validity of the document.)</i>, even if the first ones are the latter ones, or one of the first together with one of the latter and a third person is associated with them<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">There do not have to be four different people; there may also be two or three.</i>. A preliminarily married adolescent girl<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Between the ages of 12 years and 12 years and six months; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:1, Note 1</a>. Since she is over 12 years of age, she is able to act in law. But since she is not definitively married, she is still under her father’s authority.</i>, or her father, can accept her bill of divorce. Rebbi Jehudah says, no two hands can acquire together; only her father alone can accept her bill of divorce. Any female<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is independent of the preceding one; it deals with an insane woman or an underage girl who was definitively married and, therefore, is emancipated from her father’s power. If she is too young to understand the meaning of divorce and the need to keep the document until she has collected all the monies due her, she cannot be divorced and the husband cannot escape the obligation to feed and house her.</i> who cannot take care of her bill of divorce cannot be divorced.<br>If an underage girl said, accept the bill of divorce for me, it is no valid bill until the bill of divorce reaches her hand. Therefore, if the husband wants to retract he can retract since an underage person cannot appoint an agent. But if her father said to [an agent]: Go and receive my daughter’s bill of divorce, if [the husband] wants to retract he cannot retract.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This was explained in the preceding Halakhah.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A woman who said, accept my bill of divorce for me,” etc. Two say that she appointed in their presence and two say that in their presence he received and tore it. But if he<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The husband. The wife only needs testimony that the bill was delivered in order to be able to remarry. Why does the Mishnah not state that the agent together with another person can testify in her behalf?</i> agrees, the agent should be able to be added. Explain it, it he was a relative<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A close relative who is inadmissible as witness.</i>. This implies that a relative can act as agent.
<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a>; the readings there are noted <b>ק</b>.</i>“A preliminarily married adolescent girl<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Between the ages of 12 years and 12 years and six months; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:1, Note 1</a>. Since she is over 12 years of age, she is able to act in law. But since she is not definitively married, she is still under her father’s authority.</i>, or her father, can accept her bill of divorce.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, like the disagreement about divorce is the disagreement about preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis allow the adolescent girl to contract a preliminary marriage; R. Jehudah reserves this right to the father. The same discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 43b-44a" href="/Kiddushin.43b-44a">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 43b–44a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, everybody agrees about preliminary marriage that [her father]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Reading from <i>Qiddušin</i>. The text here has “mother”; but the mother has no say during the father’s lifetime.</i> contracts but not she herself. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees about a definitive marriage that she is not empowered to marry herself off and let her father lose her earnings<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since the father has the right to his adolescent unmarried daughter’s earnings, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:6</a>. One must assume that R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that the girl must deliver to her father any valuables she received for her preliminary marriage.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan she has no legal standing relative to her father<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">But an orphan girl or a definitively married one becomes able to act in law (and appoint agents) once she reaches the age of 12 years and one day.</i> and cannot appoint an agent. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish she has legal standing relative to her father and can appoint an agent. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: <sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a>.</i>“A man contracts a preliminary marriage by himself and through his agent and a woman contracts a preliminary marriage by herself and through her agent.” He explains it about an adult woman. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:3</a>.</i>: “If an underage girl said, accept the bill of divorce for me, it is no valid bill until the bill of divorce reaches her hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah confirms that the girl cannot appoint an agent, it seems to contradict the statement that the adolescent has no standing in law since she is able to accept her bill of divorce.</i>” He explains it about an orphan<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Including an “orphan during her father’s lifetime”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.3">Note 20</a>.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:3</a>.</i>: “If her father<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah speaks about an orphan, there could be no father.</i> said to [an agent]: Go and receive my daughter’s bill of divorce, if [the husband] wants to retract he cannot retract.” He explains that the Mishnah deals with cases; the first part about an orphan and the second part if she has a father. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. <sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a>.</i>“A man may contract a preliminary marriage for his adolescent daughter either by himself or by his agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is a sentence in the same Mishnah which stated that a woman may agree to a preliminary marriage, the absence of any mention of the power of the girl implies that she cannot enter a preliminary marriage on her own.</i>” He explains that following Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 44a" href="/Kiddushin.44a">Babli agrees, <i>Qiddušin</i> 44a</a>.</i>, since Rebbi Jehudah said, “no two hands can acquire together; only her father alone can accept her bill of divorce.” Rebbi Yose said, a rabbi came out from the assembly and said, a case was decided following Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The court denied the right of an adolescent girl to marry on her own initiative.</i>. Can one rely on that<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">There is no name attached to this information; it is impossible to check the standing of the informant in the legal hierarchy.</i>? It was no question of relying on him since it was as from the sea to the frying pan<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The statement was made immediately after judgment was rendered. For the expression “from the sea to the frying pan” cf. <i>Berakhot</i> 4:5, Note 233; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 44a" href="/Kiddushin.44a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 44a</a>.</i>.
The colleagues said, they<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish disagree about the legal standing of a minor. The corresponding discussion in the Babli is <i>Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 10b. This paragraph really refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i> disagree about courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli, <i>Baba Meṣiʻa</i> 11a, explains in the name of the Galilean R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina, a fenced-in property acquires for a person even if he is not aware of it. For example, if somebody lost an article without distinguishing marks on somebody else’s fenced-in courtyard, that article becomes automatically the courtyard owner’s property since it cannot be reclaimed by the person who lost it. Nobody else is allowed to take it.<br>In the Babli, the disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish is explained to the effect that R. Joḥanan considers the courtyard as an extension of its owner’s hand. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> states that a minor legally can receive her bill of divorce, she has a “hand” and, therefore, her courtyard acquires for her. R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that the courtyard is its owner’s agent and since the minor cannot appoint an agent, her courtyard cannot acquire for her.</i> and four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If a person stands still in the public domain and nobody else is within 8 cubits from her, anything found or delivered to her at a distance of at most 4 cubits belongs to her (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.1">Mishnaiot 8:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:9:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.9.2-4"><i>Yebamot</i> 3:9</a>). The question is whether this rule extends to emancipated underage females (it certainly does not extend to underage males.)</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, she has both courtyard and four cubits. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, she has neither courtyard nor four cubits. Rebbi Eleazar said, they disagree about the courtyard but she has no four cubits. What<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">מִי is a Babylonian Aramaic particle used as a general question mark. In Galilean Aramaic, it should be מַה.</i> did Rebbi Joḥanan say about outstretched hands<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.2-3">Halakhah 8:2</a>, R. Joḥanan states that an adult woman standing in the public domain is divorced if the husband throws her the bill (in front of two witnesses) and it lands close enough for her to take it up without moving her feet. According to R. Eleazar, who states that R. Joḥanan agrees that an underage girl has no 4 cubits, it is questionable whether throwing the bill within the reach of the underage wife’s hands is delivery in the meaning of the verse. The question is not answered.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar asked, does an agent’s courtyard act as his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Joḥanan, is a woman divorced if the husband throws the bill into her agent’s courtyard in the presence of two witnesses? This question is not answered either.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is explained by R. Nissim Gerondi, Commentary to Alfasi Chapter 6, #510.</i> If she is definitively married, she but not her father<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah which gives the father the right to receive the bill of divorce of his adolescent, preliminarily married daughter, excludes the definitively married daughter over which the father has no power and who can be divorced only by direct delivery of the bill to her.</i>. An underage girl, her father but not she<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">To divorce a preliminarily married underage girl, the bill must be delivered to the father.</i>; if she is definitively married, both she and her father<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The definitively married underage girl who lacks understanding (as defined in this paragraph by R. Joḥanan) cannot be divorced since her standing is the same as her father’s. Since her father cannot receive her bill of divorce because by definitive marriage she became emancipated from him, so she cannot receive her bill of divorce, as explained in the Mishnah.</i>. If she has understanding, she but not her father. Who is an underage girl who needs to be divorced [by herself]? Rebbi Joḥanan said, any female who is given her bill of divorce together with something else and she produces it after some time.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says, deliver this bill of divorce to my wife at place X<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">This is formulated as a condition of the appointment. In the next sentence, the place is mentioned only as an indication.</i> and he delivered it at another place, it is invalid. But if he said, she is at place X, and he delivered it at another place, it is valid.<br>If a woman says, accept my bill of divorce at place X, and he accepted it at another place, it is invalid, but Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah 5</a> holds that instructions about delivery are not binding on the agent. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65a" href="/Gittin.65a">Babli disagrees (65a</a>) and holds that R. Eleazar (the Tanna) considers the husband’s instructions as binding, but not the wife’s.</i> declares it valid. Bring my bill of divorce from place X and he brought it from another place, it is valid.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If an underage girl said, accept the bill of divorce for me,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The reference should be to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>, not 3.</i>” etc. The Mishnah does not follow Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah 5</a> holds that instructions about delivery are not binding on the agent. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65a" href="/Gittin.65a">Babli disagrees (65a</a>) and holds that R. Eleazar (the Tanna) considers the husband’s instructions as binding, but not the wife’s.</i>. But following Rebbi Eleazar she is (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The context and the parallel about preliminary marriage by agent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.3.2-3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:4</a>) show that this word has to be deleted.</i> divorced unless [the husband] tells [the agent]: Do not divorce her except at place X<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The context and the parallel about preliminary marriage by agent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.3.2-3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:4</a>) show that this word has to be deleted.</i>. “If he said, she is at place X and he delivered it at another place,” Rebbi Eleazar agrees that he only indicates a place; the rabbis agree that he only indicates a place.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Bring my bill of divorce”, she may eat heave until the bill is delivered to her. “Receive my bill of divorce,” she is immediately forbidden to eat heave. “Receive my bill of divorce at place X,” she may eat heave until the bill arrives at that place; Rebbi Eleazar forbids immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, since R. Eleazar permits the agent to pick up the bill anywhere, he might have received it as soon as he left the house.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is essentially <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.7.2-3">Halakhah 4:7</a> in <i>Demay</i> (<b>ד</b> Notes 81–85). Except for the omission of a sentence, the differences in readings are explained by the Babylonian spelling in <i>Giṭṭin</i>. The reference should be to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.4.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>: “If a woman says, accept my bill of divorce,” etc. What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar? I say that he found it behind the door. There, we have stated: “If someone tells a person who is not trustworthy, buy for me from somebody who is trustworthy, or from somebody who tithes, he is not trustworthy. It was stated: “Rebbi Yose said, even if he specified, from X, he is not trustworthy unless he said, you buy and I will pay.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? I say that he found someone close by and bought from him. It turns out that Rebbi Eleazar is like Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Yose is like Rebbi Eleazar (ben Rebbi Yose.)<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna R. Eleazar is R. Eleazar ben Šamua’. The addition here is a corruption of the following sentence from <i>Demay</i>.</i> [Rebbi Yose says more than Rebbi Eleazar;]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Demay</i>, cf. the variant readings.</i> does not Rebbi Eleazar agree that if she says to him, do not accept it except at such and such a place, she continues eating until the bill of divorce reaches that place? But here, even if he says, only from X, he is not trustworthy, except if he said, you buy and I will pay. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? I say that he found someone close by and bought from him.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says: “Write a bill of divorce and deliver to my wife, divorce her, write a letter<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.2.1">Mishnah 9:3</a>, the bill of divorce is called “letter of abandonment”.</i> and deliver to her”, they should write and deliver. “Discharge her, provide for her, treat her according to the law<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Greek νόμος.</i>, do what is necessary;” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">These expressions are unspecific; they do not have to be interpreted as instructions for a divorce.</i>. Earlier, they said that if somebody was led away in a neck-iron<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>collare</i>. While, in general, the instruction to write a bill of divorce does not include an instruction to deliver, it was decided that the court had the authority to wave the separate instruction for delivery in an emergency situation when it is clearly implied.</i> when he said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, they should write and deliver. They reconsidered and said, also one who leaves on a ship or in a caravan. Rebbi Simeon from Shezur said, also the dangerously sick.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says: “Write a bill of divorce and deliver to my wife,” etc. If somebody says in Aramaic “divorce her,” it is as if he said in Hebrew “divorce her.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65b" href="/Gittin.65b">Babli, 65b</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta, 4:5</a>, in a parallel to the Mishnah, תרכו is used as substitute for גרשוה. It should be obvious that the command to write a bill of divorce can be given in any language understood by husband, scribe, and witnesses. The statement implies that in the Aramaized Hebrew of the educated person in Palestine, a mixture of Hebrew and Aramaic terms was completely acceptable.</i>”
“ ‘Discharge her<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65b" href="/Gittin.65b">Babli, 65b</a>, wants to make a difference between the meaning of פטר in <i>qal</i>, פְּטָרוּהָ, “discharge (my obligations towards her), provide for her”, and <i>pi‘el</i> פִּטְּרוּהָ “get rid of her, discharge her (from the marriage)”.</i>, provide for her, treat her according to the law, do what is necessary;’ he did not say anything.” I say, he meant sustenance.
Rebbi Mana said, not only a neck-iron of mortal danger<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The chain put on a person condemned to death.</i> but also a neck-iron of money<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the person arrested can expect to have to pay a fine. The Babli disagrees in the interpretation of Rashi (on the Mishnah, 65b) but not in that of <i>Sefer haʻIṭṭur</i> (I, 28b, Note 154).</i>. For every neck-iron is a mortal danger. Rebbi Eleazar asked: Does his gift follow the rules of the gift of a sick person<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Some formal rules of gifts are waived for a person on his deathbed. If a sick person directs that gifts should be given and he dies, the gifts are valid even though no act of acquisition was performed for the benefit of the recipients. But if he recovers, his instructions are void in the absence of a formal act of acquisition; cf. <i>Peah</i> 3:9; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 146b" href="/Bava_Batra.146b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 146b</a> ff.</i>? Rebbi Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jacob the Armenian: That which is a problem for Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one should read “R. Eleazar”; this is Rashba’s reading in his <i>Novellae</i> (who reads “R. Jacob the Red” instead of “R. Jacob the Armenian”.)</i> is obvious for Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 1:4.</i>: “One may have use for the hair of a woman who was executed<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Even though a human corpse is forbidden for all usufruct [<i>‘Avodah zarah</i> 5:12 (45b 1.4), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 29b" href="/Gittin.29b">Babli 29b</a>], this does not include the hair which is a dead part of the living body.</i> but an animal which was executed is forbidden for all use<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which is stoned because it has killed a human becomes forbidden, together with its hide and hair, not by stoning but by the sentence passed upon it by the court [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>; Mekhilta dR. Ismael (Horovitz-Rabin) p. 282, dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai (Epstein-Melamed) p. 179].</i>.” Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, if she said, my bonnet should be given to my daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli (‘<i>Arakhin</i> 7b), R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina is quoted as questioning whether in this case a wig worn by the woman at her execution can be inherited by her daughter.</i>.
As the following: Ganiba was lead out to be executed<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">It seems, by the Persian government as a political trouble maker. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 7a" href="/Gittin.7a">Babli 7a</a>).</i>. He said, One should give money<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 65b" href="/Gittin.65b">Babli, 65b</a>, 400 <i>zuz</i>. It seems that R. Abuna was Ganiba’s only student (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 44a" href="/Chullin.44a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 44a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 50b" href="/Chullin.50b">50b</a>). He also was Rav Huna’s student.</i> to Rebbi Abuna from the wine of Kefar Panaya<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 66a" href="/Gittin.66a">Babli, 66a</a>, the heirs were forced to pay by Rav Huna.</i>. They did not give him<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 66a" href="/Gittin.66a">Babli, 66a</a>, the heirs were forced to pay by Rav Huna.</i>; they were considering what Rav said, “an heir is like the buyer of encumbered property.” Just as a loan before witnesses cannot be collected from encumbered property, so it cannot be collected from heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.7.5">Chapter 3, Note 146</a>. The opinion ascribed here [and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:7:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.7.2-5">3:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.1.2-7"><i>Baba qama</i> 10:1</a> (7b 1. 39), <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 1:6 (8a 1.26)] to Rav is quoted as common to Rav and Samuel (i. e., established Babylonian practice) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175b" href="/Bava_Batra.175b"><i>Baba batra</i> 175b</a>.</i>. If they did not give it as payment of a debt, they should have given as a gift! They were considering what Rebbi Eleazar said, for Rebbi Eleazar asked: Does his gift follow the rules of the gift of a sick person<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Some formal rules of gifts are waived for a person on his deathbed. If a sick person directs that gifts should be given and he dies, the gifts are valid even though no act of acquisition was performed for the benefit of the recipients. But if he recovers, his instructions are void in the absence of a formal act of acquisition; cf. <i>Peah</i> 3:9; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 146b" href="/Bava_Batra.146b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 146b</a> ff.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he counsels R. Abina to appeal to Rav Huna.</i> brought Rebbi Isaac from Atosha who stated for him: A gift is like a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Which is valid without formality if ordered by a person sentenced to death.</i>. Rebbi Abba said to him, perhaps like bills of divorce which one cannot deliver if not delivered during his lifetime, so a gift is not called a gift if not delivered during his lifetime? He said to him, here comes the rabbi with sophistication to let Rebbi Abuna lose money! You cannot, for we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:6</a>.</i>: “Give a mina to X; then he died. One shall give after death.” Following Rebbi Simeon. For Rebbi Joḥanan disagrees, they should give him, as Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A loan before witnesses can be collected from the heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted as opinion shared by R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish (i. e., common Galilean practice) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175b" href="/Bava_Batra.175b"><i>Baba batra</i> 175b</a>.</i> on condition that they inherited real estate. Rebbi Yose said, that is, if somebody was with him until the moment of his death<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Who can testify that from the moment of the gift to the donor’s death nothing was given to the recipient of the gift.</i>. But if he goes in and out, I am saying that already he gave him. The word of Rebbi Ḥanina implies that it<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Ganiba’s gift.</i> was not given since Rebbi Ḥanina reported this fact: Levi Parisa<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i> 3:4 (6a 1. 63), he is called Levi Sarisa. He may be Levi ben Sisi, known as a commuter between Galilee and Babylonia.</i> was going from place to place when Ganiba’s relatives came to eat with him. He said, the money should be given to Rebbi Abuna; they started quarelling with him.
It was stated: Also the sick person. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Practice follows Rebbi Simeon from Shezur about the dangerously ill<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 55a" href="/Ketubot.55a">Babli (<i>Ketubot</i> 55a</a>) agrees in the name of R. Jonathan.</i>. What is the difference between a dangerously ill and a sick person? A sick person is a normal case; any who gets sick suddenly is dangerously ill. <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, the text is from <i>Peah</i> 3:9 (<b>פ</b>), Note 155.</i> In a normal case, the relatives visit him immediately; unrelated persons visit him after three days. If he falls ill suddenly, everybody visits him immediately. An example: Rebbi Ḥuna, Rebbi Phineas, and Rebbi Ḥizqiah went to visit Rebbi Yose after three days. He said to them: Do you want to sustain the <i>baraita</i> in my case?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody was thrown into a cistern<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, the text is from <i>Peah</i> 3:9 (<b>פ</b>), Note 155.</i> and said, anybody hearing my voice should write a bill of divorce for my wife<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Naturally, he has to declare his and his wife’s full names as well as their place of residence. The people hearing him do not have to ask for further identification.</i>, they should write and deliver<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he did not say that the bill should be delivered.</i>. If a healthy person said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, he wants to make fun of her<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not instruct anybody to deliver the bill, it is invalid.</i>. It happened to a healthy person who had said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, that he went on the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">After the bill had been written and (erroneously) been delivered to the wife when he was still alive.</i>, fell down, and died. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, the Sages said that if he jumped, it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce of the suicide retroactively becomes an emergency bill and is valid.</i>; if the wind pushed him, it is no bill of divorce.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody was thrown into a cistern,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">This text essentially is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 16:6:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.16.6.1-2"><i>Yebamot</i> 16:6, Notes 117–124</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, Rebbi Jonathan taught us, only if they saw a man’s shadow. There we have stated: “One testifies by the light of a candle or by the light of the moon and one permits to remarry on the basis of a disembodied voice”, and Rebbi Jonathan said, only if they saw a man’s shadow. Rebbi Aḥa bar Ḥanina, in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: That means, in the fields, but in town even without a man’s shadow. But did we not state: “If somebody had been thrown into a cistern,” and Rebbi Jonathan said, only if they saw a man’s shadow? Rebbi Abin said, damaging spirits were<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">This disclaimer of the current existence of spirits is not in the <i>Yebamot</i> text (which is an insert of the corrector, not a text by the original scribe), nor in the text quoted by Rashba (<i>Novellae</i> to 66a) The Babli definitely believes in evil spirits. The sentence is quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 66a:8:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.66a.8.1">Tosaphot (<i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. וליחוש, 66a</a>) as מְצוּיִין הֵן בַּשָּׂדוֹת “they are found in the fields.” But that text is not usable as witness since it is thoroughly babylonized, using תימא for תימר, בבואה for בובייה.</i> as frequent in cisterns as they are frequent on the fields.
If it was doubtful whether he jumped or the wind pushed him? Let us hear from the following: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if he fell down immediately, it is a bill of divorce; if he fell after some time it is no bill of divorce. But is “immediately” not a case of doubt whether he jumped or the wind pushed him? This implies that in case of doubt whether he jumped or the wind pushed him, it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The different versions of the Tosephta seem to disagree with the Yerushalmi (the topic is not treated in the Babli). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta 4:7</a> (Lieberman) “If a healthy person said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, climbed on a roof, and fell down, one writes and delivers as long as he still is alive. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if he jumped, it is a bill of divorce; if he fell down after some time, it is no bill of divorce since I may say that the wind pushed him.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:9" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.9">Tosephta 6:9</a> (Zuckermandel): “If a healthy person said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, climbed on a roof, and fell down, one writes and delivers as long as he still is alive. If he fell down after some time, it is no bill of divorce since I may say that the wind pushed him.” Tosephta (quote of Rashba): “If a healthy person said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, climbed on a roof, and fell down, one writes but does not deliver since I may say that the wind pushed him.” A discussion of the different versions is in J. N. Epstein, <sup>2</sup>מבוא לנוסח המשנה, p. 600–601.</i>.
If somebody slaughtered with the intention of sprinkling [the victim’s] blood in idolatry or to burn its fat for idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Any idolatrous sacrifice is forbidden for any use; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, the intention invalidates<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">If the slaughter were for idolatry, everybody would agree that the meat is forbidden for consumption. But here the slaughter is not intended for idolatry, only that later part of the blood and fat would be used for idolatrous purposes, for instance, if the animal is a Gentile’s property; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.6.4">Note 131</a>.</i>, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the intention does not invalidate<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Only an action can trigger the prohibition. If the Gentile takes part of the animal for himself for idolatrous purposes after he had sold most of the meat to Jews, there is no reason why the meat should be forbidden to Jews.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi objected before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 2:7" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.2.7">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 2:7</a>: “If somebody slaughters for a Gentile, his slaughtering is valid, but Rebbi Eliezer declares it invalid. Rebbi Eliezer said: Even if he slaughtered it only for the Gentile to eat the appendage of its liver, it is invalid since the Gentile’s thoughts are always for idolatry. Rebbi Yose said, that is a matter of a conclusion <i>de minore ad majus</i>. Since in a case where thought makes sanctified food unusable, everything is determined by the person who officiates; in the case of profane food which cannot become invalidated by thought, it is only logical that everything depend on the slaughterer.”<br>It is forbidden to eat from a sacrifice after its appointed time (<i>piggul</i>) or outside the appropriate sacred precinct. If the slaughterer intends the sacrifice to be eaten at the wrong time or place, the entire sacrifice becomes invalid. But if the owners had the same idea, their intentions are irrelevant; they could invalidate the sacrifice only by actually using it at the wrong time and at the wrong place. R. Yose argues that profane slaughter cannot have rules more strict than sacrificial slaughter. If the owner’s intentions are irrelevant for sacrificial slaughter, they must be irrelevant also for profane slaughter.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, that is a matter of a conclusion <i>de minore ad majus</i>. Since in a case where thought makes sanctified food unusable, etc.,” and thought invalidates for <i>piggul</i> and leftover<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice not eaten during its appointed time becomes forbidden as leftover even if it was not intended from the start to be eaten out of its time.</i>. He said to him, truly thought invalidates for <i>piggul</i> and leftover; but if somebody slaughtered with the intention of sprinkling [the victim’s] blood in idolatry or to burn its fat for idolatry, the intention does not invalidate<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that only for Jewish sacrifices does the wrong thought at slaughter invalidate the act; for all others the wrong thought invalidates the act but is not transferable to others. If the slaughter was for idolatry, the entire meat is forbidden. If the slaughter was for using part of the blood or fat for idolatry, that blood or fat becomes forbidden by being used; it has no influence on the other parts. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 39" href="/Chullin.39">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 39a/b</a>).</i>. If he slaughtered and then the blood was sprinkled in an idolatrous rite and the fat was burned in an idolatrous rite<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 39b" href="/Chullin.39b">Babli (<i>Ḥulin</i> 39b</a>): It was slaughtered and after that [the slaughterer] expressed an intention [of idolatry].</i>, there was such a case in Caesarea and they pronounced neither prohibition nor permission. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rav Ḥisda: This means that they were not apprehensive<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, the reference is to the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in the Mishnah, who holds that a later action indicates a prior thought. If suicide can validate a prior bill of divorce then idolatrous practice can invalidate a prior slaughter. Since this also explains the insertion of the paragraph here, it has to be accepted as explanation.</i>. If you say that they were apprehensive, they should have taught a prohibition. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav Ḥisda: This means that they were apprehensive. If you say that they were not apprehensive, they should not have dealt with the case<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">A rabbi could have dismissed the case without bringing it to the attention of the full court. But what they really said was that the scrupulous should not eat from the meat; those who did eat did not sin.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one said to two persons, give a bill of divorce to my wife<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband did not authorize a scribe, the appointed persons themselves have to write the bill of divorce.</i>, or to three persons, write a bill of divorce and deliver to my wife, they should write and deliver<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Since he specifically asked them to write, they cannot constitute themselves as a court and direct the scribe of the court to write the bill but must write it themselves.</i>. If he said to three persons, deliver a bill of divorce to my wife, they shall ask others to write since he appointed them as a court, the words of Rebbi Meïr. This instructions for practice did Ḥananiah<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:2-35" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.2-35"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:2</a> (18d last line) he is called Ḥanina; in Babli sources Rebbi Ḥanina.</i> from Ono send from jail: I know from tradition that if someone said to three persons, deliver a bill of divorce to my wife, they shall ask others to write since he appointed them as a court. Rebbi Yose said, we told the messenger that we also know from tradition that even if he said to the Supreme Court in Jerusalem, deliver a bill of divorce to my wife, they shall learn<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">If they do not know how to write a document.</i>, write, and deliver. If he said to ten persons, write a bill of divorce for my wife, one writes and two sign. All of you shall write, one writes and all sign. Therefore, if one of them died, the bill of divorce would be void.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one said to two persons, give a bill of divorce to my wife,” etc. The Mishnah follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The problem discussed is hinted at in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.2.2-3">Halakhah 7:2</a> but made explicit only in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 66b" href="/Gittin.66b">Babli (66b</a>, also 29a,71b,72b). R. Yose (ben Ḥalaphta, the Tanna of overriding authority) holds that “words cannot be delivered to an agent.” Therefore (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>), if a sick person says to the (at least three) persons attending him, write a bill of divorce to my wife; if they ask a scribe to produce the bill and then they all sign, the bill is invalid since the order to write the bill cannot be delivered through an agent. This contrasts with the Mishnah here, which states that if three persons were appointed, they can be considered a court and have the authority to order the document to be drawn up <i>as a court</i>, not as agents. There really is no doubt that the Mishnah is R. Meïr’s, since it is stated explicitly. The question is only what is R. Meïr’s reason in opposing R. Yose. It seems that R. Yose will agree that a court can cause the bill to be written if the husband in so many words appoints three persons <i>as a court</i>; he does not accept R. Meïr’s position that the appointment of at least three joint agents automatically constitutes them as a court; he does not admit that a person can appoint others (in any number, but not a formal court) to act as agents to have a document written for him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 66b" href="/Gittin.66b">Babli 66b</a>).</i>, from the next sentence: “If he said to three persons;” and Rav Ḥisda said, the reason of Rebbi Meïr is that we find everywhere that others write and the court signs, also here others write and the court affixes their signatures.
Rebbi Hillel ben Valens asked before Rebbi: What reason did they have to say that practice follows Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">There is no doubt that in the Mishnah, practice has to follow R. Yose even if the opinion of Ḥanania from Ono might have the authority of R. Aqiba behind it (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 3:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.3.1.5"><i>Terumot</i> 3:1, Note 23</a>).</i>? He said to him, because I never met him. If somebody questioned statements of Rebbi Yose, Rebbi said, we miserable ones asked against Rebbi Yose, for as there is a difference between the most holy and the most profane, so is there a difference between Rebbi Yose’s generation and ours. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose said, the difference between my father’s generation and ours is like that between gold and dust.
<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 4:13" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).4.13">Tosephta 4:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 67b" href="/Gittin.67b">Babli 67b</a>.</i>“If somebody said to ten persons, ‘bring this bill of divorce’, one transports in the name of all of them. ‘All of you shall bring’, they all transport and one delivers in the name of all of them; therefore, if one of them died, the bill of divorce becomes invalid.” <sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 67b" href="/Gittin.67b">Babli 67b</a>; quoted in the name of the Yerushalmi but in Babli language in <i>Sefer Ha‘Iṭṭur</i> I, 29d (Note 186).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Eleazar from Rome: The one who awards<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to understand what it means to “award” the right to deliver a bill of divorce. The Babli version (also ascribed to the Yerushalmi, Note 144), reads: “The one who counts is like one who says ‘all of you’,” which according to Rashi means that the husband says: you, 1, 2, 3, 4 (pointing at persons and counting the number of persons he appointed) shall deliver. One could try to explain the version of the text if the husband is a very well known personality and it is an honor to be his representative. Then, if he appoints his representatives by name without indication of any preferences between them, they all have equal rank and all are appointed jointly but not separately.</i> it like one who says “all of you”.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody had an attack of seizures<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The word קורדייקוס is taken as Greek κορδακικός “staggering, making drunken movements,” derived from κόρδαξ, Latin <i>cordax</i>, “extravagant dance in Greek comedy.” The nature of the seizures is not described in either Talmud, but it is implied that during an attack the person is not of sane mind.</i> and said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, he did not say anything. If he had said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, and afterwards had an attack of seizures and said, do not write, his later words are nothing. If somebody became paralyzed<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">He is of clear mind but has lost his power of speech.</i>, one asks him, shall we write a bill of divorce for your wife, and he nods his head, one checks him out three times. If he answers no for no, yes for yes<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If he repeatedly shakes his head sideways when asked whether not to write the bill but nods if asked whether to write the bill, his intentions and instructions are confirmed.</i>, they shall write and deliver.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody had an attack of seizures”, etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1"><i>Terumot</i> 1:1, Notes 38–50</a>. In the variants, <b>ת</b> denotes the Leiden ms. of <i>Terumot</i> and <b>ר</b> the Rome ms.</i> The symptoms of an insane person: One who goes out in the night, stays overnight in a graveyard, tears his clothing, and destroys what one gives to him. Rebbi Huna said, only if all of that is in him since otherwise I say that one who goes out in the night is a man-dog<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. E. Guggenheimer and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Notes on the Talmudic vocabulary</i>,<i>gndryps-qntrwpys</i>, Lešonenu 35 (1971) 201–207 (Hebrew); <i>Talmudic evidence for Greek spelling</i>, Studi classici in onore di Quintino Cataudella, vol. iv, U. Catania (1972) 1981, 313–314.</i>; he who stays overnight in a graveyard burns incense to spirits, he who tears up his clothing is [a choleric person]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>Terumot</i>, missing here.</i>, and he who destroys what one gives to him is a Cynic. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even only one of these symptoms is proof. Rebbi Abun said, what Rebbi Joḥanan said, even only one of these by itself is reasonable for him who destroys what one gives to him; even the greatest idiot does not destroy all one gives to him. The one attacked by seizures does not exhibit any of these signs. What is one attacked by seizures? Rebbi Yose said, a decrepit one. There came a case before Rebbi Yose of a weaver who, when one gave him red on black he was exerting himself, black on red he was exerting himself. This is the <i>cordiacus</i> described by the Sages.
<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.12"><i>Terumot</i> 1:1, Notes 52–55</a>. The slightly different <i>Terumot</i> text is given in the second column.</i>“If sometimes he is insane and sometimes healthy. When he is insane he is insane in all regards, when healthy he is normal in all regards.” A case came before Samuel who said, when he is of sound mind he should deliver the bill of divorce. Why? Samuel follows Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish; as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the next paragraph.</i>, when he regains his sanity. It follows that Samuel acted according to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, only if he is of sound mind may he deliver the bill of divorce; (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 38:16" href="/Isaiah.38.16"><i>Is</i>. 38:16</a>) “make me healthy and let me live!<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The verse is quoted correctly in <i>Terumot</i>.</i>”
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even while he is suffering an attack of seizures, one writes the bill of divorce and delivers it to his wife. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, when he regains his sanity<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 70b" href="/Gittin.70b">Babli, 70b</a>, the attributions are switched, following the later argument in this paragraph (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.4">Note 17</a>).</i>. The argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems to be inverted, since they disagreed: <sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This quote is incomplete; the text has to be completed from <i>Horaiot</i> (Note 10) and a related text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 12b" href="/Zevachim.12b">Babli, <i>Zebaḥim</i> 12b</a>. The completed text must read: “If somebody had eaten forbidden fat and already had prepared his separation sacrifice when he became…” A purification sacrifice is personal; if its owner died it cannot be transferred to another owner or another use, nor can it be redeemed. The question is whether insanity makes a person lose his individuality; if the answer is positive then during the period of insanity the sacrificial animal was ownerless and cannot then or afterwards be of any use; it has to be put away until it dies a natural death.</i> If he became deaf-mute<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The illiterate deaf-mute has lost his legal personality; cf. <i>Ketubot</i> Chpater 1, Note 134, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 14:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.14.1.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 14:1</a>.</i>, or insane, or became an apostate<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:2" href="/Leviticus.1.2"><i>Lev</i>. 1:2</a>: “If a person <i>from among you</i> present a sacrifice …” is interpreted to mean that the rules of obligatory sacrifices apply to converts but exclude apostates [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 5a" href="/Chullin.5a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 13b" href="/Chullin.13b">13b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 69b" href="/Eruvin.69b"><i>Erubin</i> 69b</a>; <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Paršata</i> 2(3)].</i>, or the Court ruled that fat may be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The High Court in the Temple ruled that the circumstances for which the person brings his purification offering do not imply that a sin was committed. Since a purification offering cannot be brought as a voluntary gift, the offering becomes unusable. It is stated here that if the court later reverses itself, R. Joḥanan holds that the sacrifice is not reinstated.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, his sacrifice of purification is pushed aside, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, his sacrifice of purification is not pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If circumstances change, the sacrifice may be re-instated.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi (Joḥanan) [Aḥa]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that the reading from <i>Horaiot</i>, R. Aḥa, is correct, since R. Joḥanan, who is quoted in the text here, has no influence over what future generations report in his name. R. Aḥa’s tradition is the source of the quote in the Babli (Note 11).</i> switches traditions, to avoid that a word of Rebbi Joḥanan contradict his own word. For Rebbi Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since both R. Samuel bar Abba and R. Simeon bar Abba were students of R. Joḥanan, it is impossible to decide between the readings here and in <i>Horaiot</i>.</i> bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One sprinkles the blood of a purification sacrifice or a reparation sacrifice for a person terminally ill<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">These sacrifices may not be offered for the deceased. The priest who was informed that the offering was for a terminally ill person can proceed under the assumption that the person is alive at the moment which validates the offering, when the blood of the sacrifice is sprinkled on the wall of the altar.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea said, Rebbi Ḥiyya and Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Horaiot</i>: R. Immi, the permanent companion of R. Yasa. It follows that the R. Ḥiyya mentioned here is R. Ḥiyya bar Abba, student and successor to R. Joḥanan.</i>, one follows the one, the other follows the other<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It is not stated who followed R. Joḥanan and who R. Simeon ben Laqish; from the following it seems that the Yerushalmi does not accept R. Aḥa’s relabelling of the opinions.</i>.
The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “If he had said, write a bill of divorce for my wife, and afterwards had an attack of seizures and said, do not write, his later words are disregarded.” He explains it: After he regains his sanity, his later words are disregarded. If he gave her a bill of divorce saying that it should be valid only the next day and then suffered an attack of seizures, this is the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan, the wife is divorced since the bill was delivered; for R. Simeon ben Laqish the attack of insanity invalidates the delivery. If the husband regains his sanity, he has to take back the bill from his wife and deliver it a second time.</i>. If he threw her bill of divorce to her saying that it should be valid only the next day and then suffered an attack of seizures, this is the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Rebbi Eleazar said that Rebbi Abin asked: If he separated heave<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The insane person cannot separate heave from grain; his actions are irrelevant in law (Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 1:1).</i> from his grain heap and said, it shall be heave only tomorrow but the next day he suffered an attack of seizures, is this the disagreement between Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, a <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish and he cannot explain it: If somebody had been crucified or mortally wounded<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">He is slowly but surely dying.</i> when he signalled to write a bill of divorce to his wife, one writes and delivers on the assumption that his soul hangs on in him<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A similar formulation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta (5:1</a>): “If somebody had been crucified or mortally wounded when he signalled to write a bill of divorce to his wife, one writes and delivers as long as he is alive.”</i>. Is it not impossible that his mind should not have been disturbed for an hour? This disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish and he cannot explain it.
<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are from <i>Terumot</i> 1:1 (<b>ת</b>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:4-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.4-10">Notes 19–32</a>). The Rome ms. is indicated by <b>ר</b>. Variants which are introduced by the hand of the corrector in <i>Terumot</i> are indicated by <b>מ</b>.</i> It was stated: “If a deaf-mute person gave heave, it is not heave. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, to what does this refer? If he was born deaf-mute. But if he was normal and became deaf and dumb, he writes and others confirm his signature.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One disagrees with this Tanna. They say, does not a Mishnah disagree: “If he became deaf-mute or insane, he may never divorce.” Why can he not write and have others execute his written instructions? They upheld it, if he was illiterate. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected, does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree: “If he wrote himself, or told the scribe to write and the witnesses to sign, even though the scribe wrote, the witnesses signed, they gave him [the bill] and he in turn gave it to her, it is no divorce.” Rebbi Assi said, complete the sentence and there is no disgreement: “it is no divorce unless they hear his voice.” But even if he gave a sign with his head you say it is invalid, (here also it is invalid). Rebbi Mana said, it is valid: hearing the voice has the same status as seeing him nod his head!
Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Ze‘ira lived too late to have known R. Mana I and was the teacher of R. Mana II’s teacher, the correct reading is that from <i>Terumot</i>: R. Ezra.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: Just as you say that three times he must give a sign with his head, (must one equally hear his voice three times)? He said to him, hearing his voice once, giving a sign with his head three times.
Rebbi Yudan said, there, if he said I did such and such, here, if he says do such and such. Rebbi Benjamin ben Levi inquired: He should have understanding for the future if he has understanding for the past; (if he has no understanding for the past,) he would not have understanding for the future! Rebbi Abba Mari said, here we deal with a mute; there is no agency for a mute. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, we deal with a sane person. Why is there no divorce? Because I say, he was occupied with his documents. We also stated thus: “When was this said? When he was paralyzed while being healthy. But if he was paralyzed by a prior sickness, once is enough.”
But only if it is intermittent<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last part of the Mishnah, about the bill of divorce to be written for a paralyzed man. As explained here, one asks questions in which the one which counts is alternating with other questions for which the answer must be “no” (or shaking of the head).</i>. Shall we write a bill of divorce for your wife? And he says, yes. For your mother, and he says, no. For your wife? And he says, yes. For your daughter, and he says, no. For your wife? And he says, yes. For your sister, and he says, no<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A paraphrase, not a parallel text, is quoted in Tosaphot 70b, <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. בודקין.</i>. Is it the same for testimony? Rebbi Abbahu came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is the same for testimony, since a person may testify while sitting<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut</i>. 19:17</a> requires only that the parties to a suit stand before the judges, not the witnesses. Therefore there is no biblical impediment to ascertaining testimony from a paralyzed person.</i>. The same holds for vows. Rav Sheshet stated: Just as one checks three times for bills of divorce, so one checks for inheritance, commercial transactions, and gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 71a" href="/Gittin.71a">Babli, 71a</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta, 5:1</a>. In both of these texts, testimony is explicitly included.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If they<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">An indeterminate plural always means 2.</i> said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As Rashi points out, he must be either a healthy person or a sick scholar, for whom the special simplified rules for the dangerously ill do not apply.</i>, shall we write a bill of divorce for your wife? If he said to them, write<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The husband did not appoint the people talking to him as a court since they are only two. Nor did he appoint them as witnesses to his order to the scribe, as explained in the Halakhah.</i>, and they told the scribe who wrote, and the witnesses who signed; even though they wrote, signed, and delivered it to him and he delivered it to her, this is an invalid bill of divorce unless he said to the scribe, write, and to the witnesses, sign.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If they said to him, shall we write a bill of divorce for your wife,” etc. If he said to two people, say to X that he shall write<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding Note, this legalizes the writing of the bill.</i> and to Y and Z that they shall sign. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He is identical with the Rav Jeremiah mentioned later in this paragraph.</i> Jeremiah, this happened in the days of Rebbi, and he said, such a thing should not be done in Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 67a" href="/Gittin.67a">Babli, 67a</a>, which attributes the statement to Rav, explains that the procedure is disapproved of since a wife might hire witnesses to falsely instruct the scribe to write and witnesses to sign.</i>. Samuel said, it certainly should be done. Rav Jeremiah asked Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A similar question, in the name of Rav’s Academy, is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 66b" href="/Gittin.66b">Babli, 66b</a>.</i>: If he said to two people, say to X that he write and you sign. He said to him, that is a bill of divorce but it needs study<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The woman is divorced but she cannot remarry based on that bill.</i>. The opinion of Samuel seems inverted. There, he said, it certainly should be done, and here, he says so? There is a difference, because they changed their testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is cryptic. It assumes that the question was: “If he said to two people, tell X that he write and you sign; they told the scribe to sign and others to sign.” (In the Babli, the question is inverted, with the witnesses being instructed to have others sign but they signed themselves.) The question then arises, is it sufficient that the bill of divorce be written correctly (which it is) but not signed according to instructions? (The arguments of the Babli about this case are irrelevant for the Yerushalmi.)</i>.
We understand<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This now is a discussion of the Mishnah. The persons asking whether a bill of divorce should be written cannot engage witnesses without special authorization. But why should the scribe not be able to write on a simple order since he is able to write the entire bill of divorce in advance, just leaving blanks for the names and addresses of the parties?</i>, to the witnesses, sign! But to the scribe, write? Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he wrote the essential text with the formula, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The problem of bills written in advance was treated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.3">Chapter 3, Note 17</a> ff.</i>. He explains it<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah here follows R. Jehudah who prohibits the scribe from using prepared texts.</i> following Rebbi Jehudah, since Rebbi Jehudah invalidates formulas.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce if I should die, this is your bill of divorce if I should die from this illness, this is your bill of divorce after my death,” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody can be divorced after his death. The next sentence makes it clear that a bill of divorce would be valid if given on condition to become activated one hour before the husband’s death. But from that moment he can no longer live with his wife without invalidating the divorce.</i>. “From today if I should die, from now if I should die,” that is a bill of divorce. “From today after my death” is no bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The husband’s intent cannot be determined from this language. Does he want to say that the divorce shall be valid from the moment the bill was handed to his wife if he should die (either from the illness he is currently suffering from, or on a trip overseas he is just starting, or any other foreseeable dangerous situation which causes him to write the bill), or does he really mean that the divorce should become effective only at the time of his death? In the first case, the wife becomes a divorcee after his death, in the second case she becomes a widow.<br>In some Bablylonian sources, the wording is “it is and is not a bill of divorce.”</i>; if he dies<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If he dies childless and has a brother, the wife cannot enter levirate marriage since she might be a divorcee; she cannot marry outside the family without <i>ḥalîṣah</i> since she might be a widow; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Yebamot</i>.</i> she must receive <i>ḥalîṣah</i> but is barred from levirate marriage.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘This is your bill of divorce if I should die, this is your bill of divorce from this illness, this is your bill of divorce after my death,’ he did not say anything.” <sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <i>Niddah</i> 3, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.4.5">Notes 93–97</a>. “Rebbi Jehudah the Prince” may be Judah II, Rebbi’s grandson; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.2">Note 51</a>.</i> But our teachers said, it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 72b" href="/Gittin.72b">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 72b</a>, reports the same but in 76b refers the decision to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.8.1">Mishnah 7:9</a>: “If he says, this is your bill of divorce if I do not return within 12 months; if he dies in the meantime, the bill of divorce is void.” In this case also, he did not specify <i>from today</i>. The Babli explains that in both cases they follow R. Yose who holds that “the date of a document is proof of its validity;” a bill of divorce executed before the husband’s death is valid (cf. below, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.5">Note 67</a>). The Yerushalmi’s interpretation is given in the second and third paragraphs following.</i>. Who are “our teachers”? Rebbi Jehudah the Prince and his court<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 (3d 1.20), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.2-6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:3</a> (48d 1.17), <i>Avodah zarah</i> 2:8 (41d 1.48); obliquely mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 72b" href="/Gittin.72b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 72b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 76b" href="/Gittin.76b">76b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 2b" href="/Ketubot.2b"><i>Ketubot</i> 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37a"><i>Avodah zarah</i>37a</a>.</i>. In three places is Rebbi Jehudah the Prince called “our teachers”, about bills of divorce, oil<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Avodah zarah</i> 2:9. The Mishnah has a list of foods that cannot be taken from Gentiles without kosher supervision since one cannot be sure that no forbidden ingredients were used, but which are not forbidden for usufruct. A first group includes milk, bread, and olive oil, with a note that “our teachers permitted olive oil” [to be used without supervision.] In the Babli, this permission is attributed to Rebbi, not his grandson R. Jehudah the Prince. (The chronology of the House of Hillel in the Third Cent. and the attribution of decrees between Rabbis Jehudah I, II, and III is in dispute.)</i>, and a sole<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">If a woman has a miscarriage in the shape of a sole (essentially an empty placenta) she is subject to all disabilities of the woman who gave birth to a male and a female. Rebbi Jehudah’s court restricted this to the case that a human shape was recognizable.</i>. They should have called him “permissive court” since any court which permits three [previously forbidden] things is called “permissive court.” Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Niddah</i> simply: R. Yudan.</i> said, his court disagreed with him about the bills of divorce.
