May she<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This cannot refer to the case in the Mishnah here, but must refer to Mishnah 10, about a man going overseas who gave his wife a bill of divorce to be valid if he should not return within 12 months. It is clear that the wife can remary after 12 months. The question is whether she can remarry within 12 months if news was received of her husband’s death (meaning that he would not be able to return) but the news was not brought by two witnesses testifying in court.</i> be allowed to remarry? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, she is allowed to remarry. Rebbi Yose: she is forbidden to remarry; I say that a miracle happened to him and he survived.
“ ‘From today if I should die, from now if I should die,’ that is a bill of divorce. ‘From today after my death’ is no bill of divorce.” Rebbi Yudan said, the rabbis and our teachers [discussed.] Our teachers objected to the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the sequel Rebbi (who is “our teachers”) objects to the rabbis, it follows that here one has to read: The rabbis (of the Mishnah) objected to our teachers (who declare the bills of divorce of the first series as valid.)</i>: Why do you say, it is a bill of divorce? Because he said, “if”<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Our teachers follow R. Yose (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.5">Note 67</a>) who declares that “the date of a document is proof”, meaning that the date of a document valid under certain conditions is proof of intent that it should be valid from that date on once the condition was satisfied. Therefore, the difference between the first and second series of bills of divorce is irrelevant; all are conditional in the same way.</i>! But in the last statement, he said “if” and you say, it is no bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">He really did not say “if” but “from today after my death” but this means the same as “from today on condition that I shall have died.” In any case, it is a dated document with an implied condition.</i>! But it must be because he said, “from today”. We also hold that “from today” is the main point<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">An undated document, while valid if executed unconditionally and delivered before witnesses at the time it was written, can never be validated after that time.</i>. Rebbi objected to the rabbis. Why do you say, it is not a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">“From today after my death”.</i>? Because he said, “from today”! But in the earlier statement<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The first two examples of the second series.</i>, he said “from today” and you say, it is a bill of divorce! But it must be because he said, “if”. I also am holding that “if” is the main  point<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">That every dated document containing a condition is to be read as valid from the date of execution once the condition is satisfied (unless it is stated otherwise in the document.)</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 72b" href="/Gittin.72b">Babli, 72b</a>, Rebbi is quoted to the effect that “from today after my death” is a valid condition.</i>, they switch attributions. Both Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rav say that the words of Rebbi [interpreted] there are the words of the rabbis [interpreted] here: Since he said “from today” it is as if he had said “on condition.” Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa, “on condition that I die<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Retroactively valid now if the husband dies from his current illness.</i>” or “on condition that it be after my death<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">That the divorce be valid only after the husband’s death.</i>”? He said to him, “on condition that I die.” Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: The words of Rebbi [interpreted] here are the words of the rabbis [interpreted] there: Since he said “from today” it is as if he had said “after death.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 72b" href="/Gittin.72b">Babli, 72b</a>, Rebbi is quoted to the effect that “from today after my death” is a valid condition.</i>”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.6.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 8:7</a>.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody writes his property over to his sons, he has to write: From today and after death<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">By a positive biblical rule, an inheritance has to be divided evenly among the male heirs (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:6-11" href="/Numbers.27.6-11"><i>Num</i>. 27:6–11</a>) except that the firstborn male in rabbinic interpretation receives a double portion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut</i>. 21:17</a>). If the father wants to distribute his property unevenly, or leave real estate to his daughters, he has to execute a will which has to become valid during his lifetime since nobody can act in law after his death.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, this is unnecessary.” What is Rebbi Yose’s reason? The date of the document is its proof<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since everybody knows that a will has to be activated during the testator’s lifetime, the date of the will automatically becomes the date of its validation except if this is disclaimed in the document itself. The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 136a" href="/Bava_Batra.136a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 136a</a>.</i>. The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This is no condition<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps compare Greek ὕστερος “later, subsequent” as in combination τῇ ὑστέρῃ προσβολῇ “later, subsequent, conditions added to a document.” (E. G.) Less likely is a relation between the <i>hapax</i> איסרטה and Arabic شرطة “stipulation, clause” which might be Aramaic שטר, Accadic <i>šeṭrum</i>,<i>šaṭārum</i> “document”.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It applies neither to bills of divorce nor to gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of the colleague’s statement: R. Yose’s statement about wills is applicable neither to divorces nor to gifts.</i>. Rebbi Ila<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Ze‘ira.</i> said, for a gift; since he said “from today”, the gift is irrevocable. Why did he write “after death”? To reserve the yield to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">During his lifetime.</i>. But in bills of divorce, since he wrote “from today” in the bill, it would be a separation<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If it is a divorce, the wife will be able to marry another man.</i>. Why did he write “after death”? To reserve her body<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The use of prothetic א for אל, על is Babylonian. He wants to prevent his wife from remarrying during his lifetime. Therefore, the mention of “after death” contradicts the statement “from today” and there is no divorce. Rashba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 72b) reads: “the bill of divorce is unclear” (He also reads לְשַׁייֵר לוֹ גוּפָהּ, a better Yerushalmi style).</i> to himself. Rebbi Bibon<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba reads: R. Bun. [Compare the Latin adjective <i>vivus</i> “alive” as equivalent of חַיִים; cf. also the Roman name <i>Bibulus</i>. (E.G.)]</i> bar Cahana said before Rebbi Ilai, not to reserve her earnings for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Then the divorce would be absolute and the wife entitled to remarry during her first husband’s lifetime.</i>? He answered, we do not find a woman married to one man and her earnings belonging to another. Rebbi Ze‘ira praised him for this and called him “son of the Torah.”
The same situation obtains for bills of manumission. “This is your bill of manumission valid after 30 days from now.” In Rebbi’s opinion, it is a bill<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Then the divorce would be absolute and the wife entitled to remarry during her first husband’s lifetime.</i>; in the rabbi’s opinion, it is no bill<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The document is self-contradictory and therefore invalid.</i>. The same situation obtains for abandoning. “My field shall be abandoned after 30 days from now.” In Rebbi’s opinion, it is abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">And freed from some of the obligations imposed by biblical law on agricultural properties in the Land; cf. <i>Sevi‘it</i> 9:6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.4.2">Note 85</a>.</i>; in the rabbi’s opinion, it is not abandoned. Is it the same for dedications<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Donations to the Temple or, in later times, for charitable purposes.</i>? Everybody agrees that “my speaking to Heaven is equivalent to my delivering to a person.”<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Formulated in the third person this is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>. Promises of charitable giving always are absolute and irrevocable.</i> Is it the same for preliminary marriage? It was found stated: Rebbi says, she is completely preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>3:1</a>: “ ‘(You are preliminarily married to me) after 30 days from today’, if another man married her preliminarily within the thirty days she is married and not married.” In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59b" href="/Kiddushin.59b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 59b</a>, Rav holds that she is permanently disabled from marrying anyone unless she receives a bill of divorce from both men; Samuel holds that she cannot definitively marry until the 30 day period has passed, when the claim of the second man disappears automatically. In contrast, Rebbi here asserts that the preliminary marriage of the second man never was valid in law.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce from today if I should die from this illness;” if he got up, walked on the market<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If he got out of bed but never left the house, then fell ill again and died, it is a valid bill of divorce even without an expert opinion on the cause of death.</i>, fell ill again and died, one estimates whether he died from the original sickness, in which case it is a bill of divorce; otherwise it is no bill of divorce. She may not be alone with him except in the presence of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid intimacy. If she has sex with him then either it is married sex and the bill of divorce is automatically invalidated, or it is illicit sex and sinful. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.4.1">Mishnah 8:4</a>, the House of Hillel invalidate any bill of divorce if husband and wife were alone together at any time between the signing of the document and its date of validation. The House of Shammai do not invalidate the bill. The Mishnah follows the House of Hillel.</i>, which may be even a male or a female slave except her personal slave girl since she has no shame in the presence of her personal slave girl. What is her status in those days? Rebbi Jehudah says, she is a married woman in all respects. Rebbi Yose says, she is divorced and not divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">She is divorced in criminal law but not divorced in civil law.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce from today,” etc. If he overate on groats it is as if he died from that illness, hazelnuts<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic فندق, from Greek κάρυον ποντικόν “Pontic (Black Sea) nut”.</i> alone and he did not get out of bed it is as if he did not die from that illness<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Overindulging in cereal is not in itself life threatening, but eating hazelnuts without anything else is (since it causes both vomiting and diarrhea at the same time).</i>.
“If he died from that sickness<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This is the condition on which the bill was delivered.</i>,” and a stone heap fell on him, or a snake bit him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">While the patient was still bedridden, he died from another cause.</i>, it cannot be a bill of divorce unless he died of that sickness. “If I should not recover from that sickness<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This is the condition on which the bill was delivered.</i>,” and a stone heap fell on him, or a snake bit him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">While the patient was still bedridden, he died from another cause.</i> it is a bill of divorce since he did not recover from that sickness<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 73a" href="/Gittin.73a">Babli agrees, 73a</a>.</i>.
It was stated: “Also the one in mortal danger.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, it happened in such a case that he died during intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A terminally ill person is forbidden intercourse not for legal but for medical reasons.</i>.
“Whether he died from the original sickness.” Are male or female slaves trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The validity of the bill of divorce may be the subject of a law suit. Can such a suit be decided on the testimony of slaves? Slaves cannot be witnesses in court because (1) their testimony is subject to the influence of their master and, more importantly, (2) in money matters they are not subject to the laws of perjury (since they do not have personal property) and “the testimony of a person not subject to the laws of perjury is worthless.”</i>? The witnesses know that at the time she was alone with him a male or female slave was present<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The slaves do not have to appear in court.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta 5:4</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 73b" href="/Gittin.73b">Babli 73b</a>; the text is partially quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.10"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:1, Note 52</a>.</i>“If she was alone with him with the knowledge of two witnesses, she needs a second bill of divorce from him<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since two witnesses establish a fact by biblical rules and the House of Hillel invalidate a bill of divorce if the parties were alone together between signing and time of validation, the knowledge of two independent witnesses in itself invalidates the bill.</i>. One witness, she does not need a second bill of divorce from him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A single witness has no standing in court in matters of validity of marriages or divorces.</i>. One in the morning and one in the afternoon, that was a case and Rebbi Eleazar bar Thaddeus<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The name is תַּדַּאי “Thaddeus” in <i>Soṭah</i>, the two parallel (and additional) Babylonian sources, against the reading “Tradion” here.</i> asked the Sages who said, this is not [testimony of] being alone<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Two single witnesses testifying about two different facts can never be considered as two witnesses for a pattern; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.1.10"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:1, Note 52</a>.</i>. If she was alone together with him long enough for intercourse, one suspects her about intercourse but not about preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Preliminary marriage (not intended to be definitive) can be effected by intercourse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a>); this is biblical standard. A marriage ceremony which is not witnessed is automatically invalid but it is possible to consider the witnesses to their being alone together as witnesses of intercourse. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.7.1">Mishnah 8</a>:11 the House of Hillel require only minimal standards from witnesses once the parties had been definitively married and living together.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one also suspects her about preliminary marriage.” Rebbi Jeremiah explains the <i>baraita</i>: If she was alone together with him long enough for intercourse, one suspects her about immoral intercourse but not about preliminary marriage. If the time was not long enough for intercourse, one does not suspect anything. Rebbi Yose explains the <i>baraita</i>: If she was alone together with him long enough for intercourse, one suspects her about immoral intercourse but not about preliminary marriage by intercourse. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one also suspects her about preliminary marriage by intercourse. If the time was not long enough for intercourse, one does not suspect preliminary marriage bymoney<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This is an extraordinary relaxation of the standards of testimony in the circumstances of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.9.1">Mishnah 8:11</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 73b" href="/Gittin.73b">Babli, 73b</a>, rejects any legal consequences of physical intimacy not confirmed by two eye-witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one also suspects her about preliminary marriage by money. Rebbi Abin said, It turns out that the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.4.1">Mishnaiot 8:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.9.1">11</a>.</i> parallels the rabbis and the House of Hillel Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah.
For what<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">What does R. Jehudah mean when he states that the woman who receives a bill of divorce which becomes valid at an indeterminate point in the future is a married woman in all respects?</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, to inherit from her<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife dies before her severely ill husband. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 73b-74a" href="/Gittin.73b-74a">Babli (73b/74a</a>) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta (5:4</a>), this is a tannaïtic statement.</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:6</a>. The reference is to the part of the Mishnah which is not quoted, viz., that the husband has the usufruct from the wife’s properties (and inherits from her should she die during the marriage.)</i>: “He has a claim on what she finds and on her earnings, to dissolve her vows.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this is what is implied by “she is a married woman in all respects.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The husband did not relinquish his right to his wife’s properties’ yield.</i>”
The opinion of Rebbi Jehudah seems to be inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:1:1-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.1.1-27">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 1:1</a>. On the Day of Atonement, the High Priest first has to atone for “himself and his house” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:6" href="/Leviticus.16.6"><i>Lev</i>. 16:6</a>). “His house” is interpreted to mean “his wife”; an unmarried High Priest is unable to officiate. R. Jehudah requires that the married High Priest preliminarily marry another woman lest his wife die in the week before the Day of Atonement and prevent him from serving. He cannot definitively marry another woman since he alone among all Jews is required to be monogamous (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:13" href="/Leviticus.21.13"><i>Lev</i>. 21:13</a>). The other rabbis consider R. Jehudah’s precaution to be excessive.</i>, he says that death is frequent but here, he says that death is not frequent<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">He could not say that the woman who received a bill of divorce “from now if I should die” is married in all respects if at any moment he would expect the husband to die and the wife to be divorced.</i>. There in public, here in private, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">תניא is Babylonian spelling for Yerushalmi תני. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 6:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.6.4.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 6:5</a> (belonging to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.5.1">Mishnah 6:7</a>). The Mishnah enumerates 13 chests which were maintained at the Temple gate for the public to deposit its contributions. One of the chests was labelled for “nests”, the pairs of birds required as a purification sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:8" href="/Leviticus.12.8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:8</a>) before a woman after childbirth could partake of family sacrifices. The women would deposit the money in the chest and every day the priests would offer the corresponding number of pairs of birds to purify the women. R. Jehudah holds that there was no such automatic arrangement in the Temple but that every woman after childbirth had to provide the pair of birds and personally hand them to a Cohen. His problem was that one of the birds was a holocaust but the other a purification offering and purification offerings might be presented only for living persons. If a woman died between the time she deposited the money in the chest and the next morning when the money was counted and the number of birds determined, then not only her purification offering would be invalid (it could not be offered, nor could it be released; it had to be kept in isolation until it died) but it would invalidate all other offerings of the same day (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 55b-56b" href="/Yoma.55b-56b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 55b/56b</a>). R. Jehudah goes to extraordinary lengths for fear of sudden deaths; his position in the Mishnah here becomes more difficult to understand.</i>: Rebbi Jehudah says there was no chest for nests in Jerusalem because of the mixing, maybe one of them would die and money for purification offerings destined to die would be mixed in with them.
What is the status of her intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">What is the legal status of a woman who had intercourse with another man after she had received a bill of divorce valid “from today when I shall die.”</i>? Some Tannaïm state, her intercourse is certain<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">It certainly is sinful.</i>, but some Tannaïm state, her intercourse is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband dies, it is clear retroactively that the intercourse was permitted.</i>. According to him who says that her intercourse is certain, he brings a “hung” sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If the man involved was unaware at the moment of the act that the woman might be married. The “hung” sacrifice is the expiation sacrifice prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17-19" href="/Leviticus.5.17-19"><i>Lev</i>. 5:17–19</a> for an unintentional sin whose nature eludes the perpetrator.</i>. According to him who says that her intercourse is in doubt, if [the husband] dies, he is free; if [the husband] does not die, he brings a purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A regular purification sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-35" href="/Leviticus.4.27-35"><i>Lev</i>. 4:27–35</a>) for unintentional adultery. No sacrifice can ever atone for intentional sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30" href="/Numbers.15.30"><i>Num</i>. 15:30</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>;” she is divorced and has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">She is divorced immediately but she cannot act on the divorce (either claim the <i>ketubah</i> or remarry) before she paid up.</i>. “On condition that you give me within thirty days,” if she paid within thirty days she is divorced, otherwise she is not divorced. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, it happened in Sidon that someone said to his wife “this is your bill of divorce on condition that you return to me my stole<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στολίς, στολή “garment” also “equipment, arms, etc.” Can the rabbinic Hebrew כֵּלִי “garment” (for biblical “vessel, equipment, arms”) be induced by analogy with the Greek (E. G.)? In Latin <i>stola</i> “garment, ceremonial robe”.</i>” and it turned out that his stole was lost; then the Sages said, she shall pay him its value<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since this statement is labelled as Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s, it follows that others either invalidate the divorce if the stole cannot be found or that she has to replace it in kind.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 74a" href="/Gittin.74a">Babli 74a</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 60b" href="/Kiddushin.60b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 60b</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.5">Tosephta 5:5</a>, this is formulated as a dispute: “If he died before she paid, she is subject to levirate marriage; after she paid, she is not subject to levirate marriage; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said …”</i>: If she did not manage to pay before he died, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, she pays his father or his brother and is free from <i>ḥalîṣah</i> and levirate marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband died childless, cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Yebamot</i>. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 74a" href="/Gittin.74a">Babli explains, 74a</a>, “anybody who says ‘on condition that’ means to say ‘valid immediately on condition that’ ” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta 5:6</a>). The delay in fulfilling the condition does not change the fact that the divorce preceded the husband’s death.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">While the statement on divorce is also quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.2-7"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:2</a> (63d line 16) as tannaïtic, the parallel about marriage is presented as amoraic inference. It is obvious that at the moment of preliminary marriage the groom has to give some valuables to the bride.</i> The same holds for preliminary marriage: “You are preliminarily married to me on condition that I shall give you 200 <i>zuz</i>;” if he did not manage to pay before he died, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, his father or his brother pay and she is subject to <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or levirate marriage.
Rebbi Yudan said, we deal with the case that it was lost by compulsion<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The reason Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel does not insist that the condition on which the divorce was given be fulfilled to the letter is that the wife is an unpaid trustee who is not required to pay for damages beyond his control. In contrast to the unpaid trustee who has to swear that it was beyond his control if he does not want to pay, the wife does not have to swear (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.4.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:4, Note 121</a>).</i>. They treated her like an unpaid trustee who may stipulate to be free from an oath. Rebbi Yose said, do we state here that the unpaid trustee is free from an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If R. Yudan’s explanation were correct, some reference should have been made to the rules of trustees. Since no reference was made, it makes no difference why the stole was lost. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel from the start treats the request for the stole as a request for money.</i>? But we deal with the case that it was lost by mishandling. They treated the stole according to its value. But the rabbis say that in any case it has to be treated as a case of condition of a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis who oppose Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel require fulfillment of the condition to the letter. If the stole cannot be produced for any cause whatever, the divorce is invalid. In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 38a" href="/Gittin.38a">38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 75a" href="/Gittin.75a">75a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 69a" href="/Bava_Kamma.69a"><i>Baba qama</i> 69a</a>, <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 38b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 174a" href="/Bava_Batra.174a"><i>Baba batra</i>174a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 77a" href="/Ketubot.77a"><i>Ketubot</i> 77a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 31b" href="/Sanhedrin.31b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 31b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 24a" href="/Bekhorot.24a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 24a</a>), practice is decided following the rabbis. This is reported as Babylonian practice in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:8:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.8.2-3"><i>Baba batra</i>10:14</a> (17d 1. 9).</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you serve my father,” “on condition that you breast-feed my son,” how long does she have to breast-feed him? Two years<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Meїr’s opinion; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.3.3"><i>Soṭah</i> 4:4, Notes 55</a> ff. She does not have to nurse the baby for two years after the divorce but only until he reaches the age of two years (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta 5:6</a>). Since the expenses of child-rearing are the father’s responsibility, the mother does not have to nurse her child if she is divorced before the child is weaned.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, eighteen months. If the son or the father died, it is a valid divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">This may mean that, if the father or the child died immediately after the bill of divorce was handed over, the divorce nevertheless stands. It is a matter of dispute in the Halakhah.</i>.<br>“On condition that you serve my father for two years,” or “on condition that you breast-feed my son for two years;” if the son died or the father said, I cannot stand you serving me (without being offended)<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">She did nothing to offend the father.</i>, it is no divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since the condition was not satisfied. The Mishnah in the Babli and most Mishnah mss. adds: “or if the father died in the meantime”.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, that is a divorce. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel stated a principle: Given any hindrance which is not from her side, it is a divorce.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you serve my father,” etc. Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe first wrote “R. Ḥiyya bar Abba” and then deleted “R. Ḥiyya”. The <i>editio princeps</i> has “R. Ḥiyya bar Abba”. But since the opposite opinion is referred to as R. Simeon ben Laqish’s, one of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba’s teachers, the final text of the ms. is the correct one, against the <i>editio princeps</i>.</i> said: During his lifetime, on condition that she serve all his needs, on condition that she breast-feed all his needs<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">His interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.6.1">Mishnah 6</a> is that the absence of any time limit in the condition implies that the divorcee has to be the husband’s father’s geriatric nurse for the rest of the latter’s life or to breast-feed her child for a full 24 months.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">It seems from the following that a note “Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said,” was omitted here. In the Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 75b" href="/Gittin.75b">Babli 75b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta 5:6</a>, it is a tannaïtic text.</i> Even if she did not serve all his needs, even if she did not breast-feed all his needs, but even if she served him for one hour, or breast-fed him for one hour<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He opts for a minimalist interpretation of the condition. Since no time limit was given, <i>any</i> service or <i>any</i> breast-feeding satisfies the condition and validates the divorce.</i>. After death<sup class="footnote-marker">123,</sup><i class="footnote">This may mean that, if the father or the child died immediately after the bill of divorce was handed over, the divorce nevertheless stands. It is a matter of dispute in the Halakhah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">It could also mean, "after the husband's death." Cf. S. Lieberman's commentary to Tosephta 5:6.</i>, even if she never served him or never breast-fed him. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “How long does she have to breast-feed him? Two years, Rebbi Jehudah says, eighteen months.” Rebbi Abin said, this was taught about the cost of a wet-nurse<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin reads the condition not as a request that the ex-wife breastfeed her child for 24 months and therefore be forbidden to remarry during all this time (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 4:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.4.3.3"><i>Soṭah</i> 4:4, Notes 55</a> ff.) but that the wife pay for a wet-nurse to care for the child in the husband’s house, with the wife being free to remarry 90 days after the divorce.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.2-7"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:2</a> (63d 1. 20), readings noted <b>ק</b>. The contract text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 21b:3" href="/Eruvin.21b.3"><i>Erubin</i> 3 (21b 1. 20</a>), noted <b>ע</b>.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The following is the contract text<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τὸ σύμφωνον “(written) agreement”. In general, a preliminary marriage was concluded by an act of the male, with passive acceptance by the female. Since a preliminary marriage can also be effected by contract (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a>), in certain circles in Galilee this was the preferred course of action (it is not mentioned in the Babli.) The main attraction of the contractual marriage for the woman was that it provided insurance against foot-dragging by the groom (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 13:5</a>).</i>: “I, X son of Y, contract a preliminary marriage with you, Z, daughter of U, on condition that I shall give you property A anddefinitively marry you by day B. If that day should pass without me having taken you in<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the parallel texts in <i>Qiddušin</i> and <i>Erubin</i>. The text here would read: “but not transfer real estate to you”.</i>, I shall have no claim on you<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The preliminary marriage is conditional. If the condition is not satisfied, the marriage is non-existent and the woman may marry another man without needing a bill of divorce from the first. She may even marry a Cohen, which would be impossible if the first preliminary marriage had been valid.</i>.” If anything intervened beyond his control? Rebbi Joḥanan said, matters beyond his control are as if he were inactive<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The condition is absolute. If the definitive marriage did not take place before the contracted date, the preliminary marriage is annulled.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, matters outside his control are as if he had acted<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">If the only reason that the definitive marriage did not take place were circumstances beyond the groom’s control, the preliminary marriage is <i>not</i> annulled.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, what would be necessary? “If that day should pass without you having taken me in, I shall have no claim on you.<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The condition has to be formulated not for the male, who actively has to take the woman in and therefore can claim unavoidable circumstances, but for the passive female. Since the contract is σύμφωνον, a mutual agreement, she gives her passive acceptance of the preliminary marriage only on condition that she be definitively married by a fixed date. If she refuses her agreement, no preliminary marriage is possible and no bill of divorce due.</i>” When Rebbi Joḥanan was dying, he told his daughters to act following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. He said, maybe in the future there might be a court which follows him; then his descendants might be in danger of bastardy<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">If a woman contracts a preliminary marriage by the terms of R. Joḥanan’s formulation of the contract and is not married by the time specified because of unavoidable circumstances, and then contracts another marriage without a bill of divorce in the domain of jurisdiction of a court following R. Simeon ben Laqish, her children from the second marriage will be illegitimate if the first groom claims her as his wife.</i>.
A person gave money for a ship when the canal dried up<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">This is in Babylonia, where most transport is by water. A person rented a ship for some future transport and then could not use it since in the meantime the canal had dried up. This shows that the disappearance of the old system of canals between Euphrates and Tigris cannot all be dated to the Arab conquest but that the neglect of the canal system already started early in Sassanid times.</i>. The case came before Rav Naḥman bar Jacob: There is the ship, bring the canal<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Naḥman holds with R. Simeon ben Laqish that circumstances beyond the contracting party’s control do not invalidate the contract.</i>! Abba bar Huna in the name of Rebbi Abba was praying that the canal should dry up and he could recover his money. He said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Abba are of the same opinion as Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Who holds in the Mishnah that any circumstance beyond the woman’s control does not interfere with her having complied with the terms of the divorce. (Rashba, <i>Novellae ad</i> 75b, prefers to switch the attributions. He reads אשכחת אמר “it turns out that one has to say”, making the sentence an editorial remark, not R. Abba’s statement.)</i>. If she cut off, she hindered. If her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In order to make sense of this statement, it is necessary with Rashba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 75b) and the commentators to read דַּד instead of ׃יָד “If the woman who was divorced under the condition of breast-feeding her child cut off her breast herself, she violated her contract. If her breast was cut off by others, the hindrance is not her fault and the divorce stands.”</i> (?) was cut off, it is not as if she hindered.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce if I should not return within thirty days from today;” if he intended to go from Judea to Galilee, came to Antipatris, and returned, it is invalidated following his condition<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The great trade route of the <i>via maris</i>, connecting Egypt with Damascus, leaves Judea at Antipatris, near today’s Roš Ha‘ayin, to enter Samaria. The Mishnah states that a person wanting to leave a country is considered to have left it already when he has reached the border, even if he did not actually cross the border. The person is considered to have left the country; if he then changed his mind and returned, he returned within 30 days and invalidated the bill of divorce.</i>. “This is your bill of divorce if I should not return within thirty days from today;” if he intended to go from Galilee to Judea, came to Kefar Othnay<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kafr ‘Uthnay</i> near the excavations of Megiddo was the border town at which the <i>via maris</i>, coming from the South, entered Galilee.</i>, and returned, it is invalidated following his condition. “This is your bill of divorce if I should not return within thirty days from today;” if he intended to go overseas, came to Acco<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">By the principle explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.7.1">Note 144</a>, if the person reaches a harbor where he could find a ship taking him overseas, he is considered to have gone overseas. The Mishnah also can be read as requiring the husband actually to have left the Land of Israel since Acco is partially inside and partially outside the Land (Chapter 1, Notes 64, 77).</i>, and returned, it isinvalidated following his condition. “This is your bill of divorce if I should be absent from you for thirty days;” even if he repeatedly came and left, it is a bill of divorce if he never was alone with her<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the opinion of the Yerushalmi “absent from you” means “not having marital relations with you.” In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 76b" href="/Gittin.76b">Babli, 76b</a>, this is the interpretation of Rav Huna, against R. Joḥanan who invalidates the divorce if the wife saw the husband within a 30 day period.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce,” etc. For what<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">What does “return” mean in the Mishnah?</i>? Rebbi Elazar said, for his house<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">“Return” means that he returns to his wife for marital relations.</i>; Rebbi Joḥanan said, for places<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">He already returned if he entered the community of his residence.</i>. The Mishnah supports him who said, for his house: “If he came to Acco<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">If we hold that Acco has the status of a foreign country, the part of the Mishnah dealing with Acco is superfluous. The important statement of the Mishnah then is that he has to return to his wife to invalidate the bill of divorce, following R. Eleazar.</i>, and returned, it is invalidated following his condition.” Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose, explain it following him who said that Acco is part of the Land of Israel for bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:2, Note 77.</i>. But even if you say that Acco is part of the Land of Israel for bills of divorce, does that mean also for conditions imposed on a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Conditions have to be interpreted following common use, not in legal jargon. For everybody, Acco is a border town of the Land.</i>?
He is forbidden to be alone with her immediately, for maybe he shall not visit her for thirty days and then her bill of divorce would be older than her child<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The condition was an indeterminate period of thirty days, not “from today”. If the husband impregnated his wife after delivering the bill of divorce and later absented himself for 30 days, the record will show that the woman was divorced more than 9 months before the birth of her child. While this has no influence on the duties of the father towards his child, it will damage the child’s reputation.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “This is your bill of divorce if I should not return within twelve months,” if he died within the twelve months it is no bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">After 12 months the woman is a widow, and a widow cannot be divorced.</i>. “This is your bill of divorce <i>from today</i> if I should not return within twelve months,” if he died within the twelve months it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">It is a valid bill of divorce the moment it has been ascertained that the husband will not be able to return within 12 months.</i>.<br>“If I should not return within twelve months from today write a bill of divorce and deliver to my wife;” if they wrote a bill of divorce within twelve months and delivered it within<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">This text is supported by a number of Mishnah mss. but all known Babli mss. read “<i>after</i> 12 months”. In any case the bill is invalid since the instructions of the husband were not followed to the letter.</i> twelve months, it is no bill of divorce. “Write a bill of divorce and deliver to my wife if I should not return within twelve months from today;” if they wrote a bill of divorce within twelve months and delivered it after twelve months, it is no bill of divorce; Rebbi Yose says, this is an example of a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose holds that the instructions of the husband were followed exactly since he mentioned “writing” before he mentioned a date. The opposing rabbis hold that the instruction may be interpreted to mean that no bill of divorce should exist before the end of 12 months.</i>. If they wrote after twelve months and delivered after twelve months but he had died, if the bill of divorce preceded the death it is a bill of divorce but if the death preceded the bill of divorce it is no bill of divorce. If it is not known, it is a case where they say, she is divorced and not divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The widow or divorcee can sue the heirs only for sums due to both widow and divorcee.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If I should not return within twelve months from today,” etc. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? Because he mentioned writing earlier<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose holds that the instructions of the husband were followed exactly since he mentioned “writing” before he mentioned a date. The opposing rabbis hold that the instruction may be interpreted to mean that no bill of divorce should exist before the end of 12 months.</i>. But did he not also mention delivering earlier, and you say that it is no bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered since it is obvious that the second sentence cannot be read as authorizing delivery before 12 months have elapsed.</i>?
The Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi: “This is your bill of divorce after the holiday.<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">If the language “after the holiday” (or possibly, after the holiday of Tabernacles”) was used in connection with a bill of divorce; either that the bill should be valid if the husband did not return “after the holiday” or that the bill should be written and delivered “after the holiday”, what is the interpretation of this term?</i>” He said to him, all of 30 days after the holiday are called “after the holiday”<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77a" href="/Gittin.77a">Babli, 77a</a>, accepts this only as Rebbi’s private opinion which is not followed in practice.</i>. Rebbi Bivon bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: “This is your bill of divorce on Passover eve”? Even a person who says that all of 30 days after the holiday are called “after the holiday” will agree that 30 days before Passover are not like “after Passover”<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">A reference to “Eve of Passover” means the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan and nothing else.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: If somebody said, a <i>qonam</i><sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">A formula of “a vow of deprivation”, cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Nedarim</i>, p. 422.</i> that I shall not taste wine at the end of the Sabbath? He said to him, after the end of the Sabbath he is forbidden, Sabbath eve he is permitted, the days in between are problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The night following the Sabbath is certainly called “after the Sabbath”. The following Friday is certainly not called “after the Sabbath”. The days in between are questionable. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77a" href="/Gittin.77a">Babli, 77a</a>, Sunday, Monday, and Tuesday are called “after the Sabbath”, Wednesday, Thursday, and Friday are called “before the Sabbath”.</i>. And so it is here, after the end of the Sabbath it is a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a condition imposed on a bill of divorce, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.8.3">Note 161</a>.</i>, Sabbath eve it is no bill of divorce, but the days in between are problematic.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody throws a bill of divorce to his wife while she is in her own house or her own courtyard, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> requires the bill of divorce to be delivered “into her hand”. Legally, “hand” means “possession” (as in Latin and many other languages). Since anything lying in somebody’s real estate is in his possession, delivery of the bill into the wife’s real estate is legal delivery.</i>. If he threw it to her inside his house or his courtyard, even if he was together with her in a bed, she is not divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife is in his real estate, only delivery into her hand or her personal belongings (the folds of her garment or her wool basket) is legal delivery.</i>. Into her bosom or her wool basket<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κάλαθος “wicker-basket”, e. g., basket for wool.</i>, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife is in his real estate, only delivery into her hand or her personal belongings (the folds of her garment or her wool basket) is legal delivery.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody throws a bill of divorce to his wife,” etc. “He shall write,” “he shall deliver into her hand.” Not only into her hand; from where also into her garden or into her courtyard? The verse says “he shall deliver, he shall deliver.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A shortened version of this argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77b" href="/Gittin.77b">Babli, 77b</a>. The argument follows R. Aqiba, who derives rules from every word which could be considered not absolutely necessary. Since “he shall deliver” is mentioned both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> and 24:3, he concludes that any legal delivery is acceptable.</i> Is this following Rebbi Ismael? It is following Rebbi Aqiba. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael holds that “the Torah speaks in everybody’s language.” Therefore, if “hand” is to be interpreted as “possession”, a verse has to be found in which this sense is documented. This is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 21:26" href="/Numbers.21.26"><i>Num</i>. 21:26</a> which notes that Siḥon the Amorite “took all the land from the Moabite king’s hand”. Since land cannot be grasped in one’s hand, “hand” must mean “possession”. (A hint of this argument is in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 269. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 56b" href="/Bava_Metzia.56b">Babli, <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 56b</a>, following only R. Aqiba, accepts the interpretation of “hand” as “possession” only in extraordinary cases.)</i>, “he took all his land from his hand, up to the Arnon.” Did he take it out of his hand? What means “from his hand”? From his possession.
But are her garden and her courtyard not subject to her husband’s having the usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a contract to the contrary, the wife’s dowry, brought into the marriage, becomes the husband’s property as <i>mortmain</i> subject to his obligation to restitute the full value at the termination of the marriage. Property coming to the wife during the marriage is <i>paraphernalia</i> property about which the husband assumes the duty of administration and from which he has the usufruct. In any case, it is impossible to say that (in the absence of a contract of separation of properties) the wife’s properties were in her actual possession during her marriage. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:1, Note 1</a>.</i>? Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">No R. Joshua is known among Amoraïm. One has to read with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 18a:13:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.18a.13.1">Tosaphot (<i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. מה</a>, 77b): R. Joḥanan.</i> in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Only if he writes to her, “I have nothing to do with your properties<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Ketubot 9:1" href="/Mishnah_Ketubot.9.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 9:1 (Notes 1–4</a>) about the different formulations of separation of properties.</i>.” Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqulah in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: Even if he did not write to her, “I have nothing to do with your properties.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the words of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 77a" href="/Gittin.77a">Babli, 77a</a>: In a divorce, her bill of divorce and her entering into full possession of her properties are simultaneous. Even if the husband had the administration of the properties when he dispatched the bill of divorce, the moment the latter landed on his wife’s property he has lost the administration, and all property rights revert to his ex-wife.</i>” It turns out that Rebbi Yannai follows the rabbis and Rebbi Hoshaiah follows Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23b" href="/Kiddushin.23b">Babli (<i>Qiddušin</i> 23b</a>), the attributions are switched: R. Meїr holds that neither slave nor wife have powers of acquisition separate from husband or master, and the rabbis disagree.</i>. Rebbi Yannai follows the rabbis, for the rabbis say, even though the slave’s hand is his master’s hand, he can acquire his document of manumission from [his master’s] hand<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">While anything the slave acquires automatically becomes his master’s property, the master by delivering the bill of manumission empowers the slave to own property, in particular, himself.</i>. But her garden and her courtyard are subject to her husband’s having the usufruct unless he writes to her, “I have nothing to do with your properties.” It is difficult for the rabbis: since the slave’s hand is his master’s hand, how can he acquire his document of manumission from [his master’s] hand<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a rhetorical question; the answer was already given, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.3">Note 11</a>.</i>? Rebbi Hoshaiah follows Rebbi Meїr, for Rebbi Meїr said since the wife’s hand is not her husband’s hand, she can acquire her bill of divorce from her husband. Her garden and her courtyard will not be subject to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The husband possibly is the administrator but not the owner.</i>, even if he has the usufruct, even if he did not write to her, “I have nothing to do with your properties.” If he inherited properties together with her, even if he wrote it is as if he did not write; [for example] if he had married his brother’s daughter, even if he wrote it is as if he did not write<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The right to joint property can be renounced only by transfer of property rights, not by a disclaimer.</i>. If another person gave her a gift and told her, on condition that your husband have no rights to it, but it is for you to trade on your own account, then even if he did not write it is as if he wrote<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband never had any rights of administration, he does not have to disclaim them. It is a recognized way of giving property to one’s daughter to shield it from the son-in-law; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Nedarim 11:8" href="/Mishnah_Nedarim.11.8">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 11:8 (Notes 69</a>,70).</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he threw it to her in a courtyard which belongs to neither of them, what is the rule<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If the bill of divorce is deposited in a private domain which belongs neither to the husband nor to the wife, can the wife legally acquire the document by lifting it up or drawing it close to her so that it could be lifted up. In the public domain this is possible as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: That is a problem for the rabbis. <sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This is also referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4</a> (60b 1. 32). The text of the following Tosephta (attributed to the Elder R. Ḥiyya) is slightly garbled here; it is translated following the text of <i>Qiddušin</i> (and its parallels in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 85a" href="/Bava_Batra.85a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 85a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta <i>Baba batra</i>5:2</a>).</i> Rebbi Abin said, it is a disagreement between Rav and the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya, for Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi said in the name of Rav: Drawing close does not acquire in a courtyard which belongs to neither of them<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4</a> states that “movables can only be acquired by being moved” (unless they are acquired together with real estate.) It is a peculiarity of rabbinic law (for which no biblical tradition is claimed) that transfer of money does not transfer ownership of the commodity bought. Delivery is effected and responsibility for the merchandise is transferred from the seller to the buyer uniquely by an act of acquisition by the buyer, which in theory should be actual moving of the merchandise.</i>. But Rebbi Ḥiyya stated in disagreement: “Under which circumstances did they say that movables are acquired by drawing close? In the public domain or in a courtyard which belongs to neither of them. But in the buyer’s domain he acquired the moment he accepted it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since “a person’s courtyard acquires for him” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.1">Note 1</a>.)</i>. In the seller’s domain he does not acquire unless either he lifts it up or moves it out of its prior owner’s domain. In a domain where it was deposited<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The merchandise was deposited with a third person. Then the owner of the property may grant the buyer the right (with or without payment) to use his property for the acquisition. Without an explicit grant, the merchandise can be acquired only by actual removal from the premises.</i> he does not acquire unless he either is explicitly empowered or he leases its place.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For Rav, a divorce document can be delivered on another person’s property only by direct delivery. For R. Ḥiyya, the delivery can be indirect as long as no party has an interest in the property. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 85a" href="/Bava_Batra.85a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i>85a</a>, recognizes only R. Ḥiyya’s position.</i>”
Ulla bar Ismael said, he never thought to have a right to her wool basket<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He explains why the wife is divorced if the husband throws the bill of divorce into her wool basket in his house. According to the Tosephta quoted in the preceding paragraph, the wife should not be able to acquire anything on the husband’s premises without bodily removing it from the premises. It is now stated that this excludes the wife’s personal things.</i>. Think of it, if her wool basket was of gold? Certainly he never thought to have a right to her wool basket. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only if it is being used<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If she is using the wool basket at the moment, it is part of her outfit. If the husband puts the bill of divorce into one of her bags which is not being used, it is no delivery.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, only if she is using it at that moment<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose only makes R. Joḥanan’s statement clear.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if it is tied to her; Rebbi Yose said, if she lifts it for her purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Similar statements of the parties involved are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78a" href="/Gittin.78a">Babli, 78a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaiah stated in support of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even if he threw it into her <i>maloš</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>hapax</i> is unexplained; the hypothetical meaning “baking trough” from לוש is adopted by the classical commentaries. {Perhaps explain as Greek μαλλός, ὁ “flock of wool, tress”, as contents of her κάλαθος (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.1">Note 3</a>) (E. G.). In Galilean speech, no difference existed between שׁ, שׂ and ס.}</i> she is divorced.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he said to her, “take this bond,” or she found it behind his back, she read it and it turned out to be her bill of divorce, it is no bill of divorce until he says to her “this is your bill of divorce”. If he put it into her hand while she was asleep, after she awoke she read it and it turned out to be her bill of divorce, it is no bill of divorce until he says to her “this is your bill of divorce”. If she was standing in the public domain when he threw it to her and it landed close to her it is a bill of divorce, close to him it is no bill of divorce, half and half she is divorced and not divorced.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he said to her, ‘take this bond,’ ” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan says, this follows Rebbi Eleazar ben Simeon, since Rebbi Eleazar ben Simeon says it never is a bill of divorce unless he says at the moment of delivery: “this is your bill of divorce.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78a" href="/Gittin.78a">Babli (78a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>), quotes a disagreement between Rebbi, who accepts the husband’s declaration even after delivery of the bill, and R. Eleazar ben Simeon, who requires a repeat delivery if the declaration was not made at the moment of delivery. In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 269, the opinion of R. Eleazar ben Simeon is quoted anonymously and referred to the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> about the bill of divorce “… hands it over to her and sends her out of his house;” meaning that handing the bill to his wife and making it clear that she is divorced must be simultaneous.</i>”
How close is “near”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This starts the discussion of the last part of the Mishnah.</i>? Rav said, four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A person standing in the public domain acquires anything movable that is within a circle of four cubits (about 2.2 m) of him if (1) it is his intent to acquire and (2) no other person is present in that circle; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.9.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 3:9, Note 120</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ilai the heir said, four cubits. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Stretching out hands, and we have stated: “stretching out hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce is legally delivered only if the wife can pick it up by stretching out her hands without moving her feet. (It seems that this includes the possibility that she has to kneel down to reach the document.)</i>” Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rav’s colleague who in the Babli is called Rav Jeremiah.</i>, in the name of Rav: Stretching out hands. The argument of Rav is inverted; there, he says four cubits and here he says so? There in theory<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The principle explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.3">Note 29</a> is generally accepted in money matters. For example, if one finds something in the street, he can claim it for himself and sue anybody who would snatch it away from him if the two conditions of acquisition are met. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79" href="/Gittin.79">Babli, 79a/b</a>, goes to great lengths to uphold the principle; its arguments are irrelevant for the understanding of the Yerushalmi.</i>, here in practice<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In practice, a divorce is definitive only if the document was picked up by the wife. The Babli emphatically agrees in the names of Samuel and Rav Ashi, 79b.</i>. “Rebbi Eleazar stated: Even if it was closer to her than to him but a dog came and took it away, she is not divorced.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>. The sources are divided on whether to read Eliezer or Eleazar. In the Tosephta, R. Eleazar disagrees with the restriction of the distance to four cubits; this is not mentioned in the Yerushalmi. The corresponding text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79b" href="/Gittin.79b">Babli, 79b</a>, is noncommittal in the matter.</i>” Samuel agrees with Rebbi Eleazar and disagrees. The clearest way is that he puts it into her hand. If there was room for stretching out hands between him and her, Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan [states]: If it was closer to her than to him she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he was standing within 4 cubits of her when he threw the bill to her, which would prevent her from acquiring ownerless property.</i>, closer to him than to her she is not divorced, half and half she is divorced and not divorced. Abba bar Rav Jeremiah said: In all cases in which we have stated “divorced and not divorced”, support is on him<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since she cannot remarry unless she receives a new, clean bill of divorce, for all consequences in civil law she remains married.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The same applies for preliminary marriages<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 3:9" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.3.9">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 3:9, Note 120</a>.</i> and for [liquidation of] debts. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor (who is explicitly mentioned in most Mishnah and Babli mss.)</i> said to him, throw to me<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the public domain.</i> what you owe me and he threw it close to the lender<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The borrower threw it in the public domain close to the lender. The borrower is free even if the creditor is unable to pick up the money.</i>, the borrower is acquitted, close to the borrower, the borrower remains obligated, in the middle both of them must share it<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If the money is lost, the borrower and the creditor each have to cover half the loss.</i>. If she was standing on top of her roof and he threw it to her<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The flat roof has the same standing as a courtyard; delivery to the roof is legal delivery (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>).</i>, as soon as it reached the roof’s airspace<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the wife never could pick it up since, as explained in the next sentence, it might have landed in a puddle and become illegible or landed in a fire and be destroyed.</i> she is divorced. If he was on top and she below<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In her own domain.</i>, once it left the domain of the roof she is divorced even if it was blotted out or burned.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The same applies for preliminary marriages”, etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The following is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:2:3-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.2.3-8"><i>Peah</i> 4:2, Notes 30–58</a>. (Variant readings <b>פ</b>).</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Abba Cohen Bar Dalaia: A person acquires a find within four cubits of himself. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 22:14" href="/I_Chronicles.22.14"><i>1Chr</i>. 22:14</a>) “Look, in my poverty I prepared for the House of the Eternal 100’000 <i>kikkar</i> of gold, 1’000’000 <i>kikkar</i> of silver, bronze and iron unweighed because it was so much; I prepared wood and stones, and you should add to them.”
Rebbi Jonah said that Rebbi Hoshaiah asked: What are we talking about? If this was within four cubits from him, he was rich. If it was outside of four cubits, may anybody dedicate anything that is not his? We confirmed it if he dedicated it piece by piece.
Rebbi Abun said, what means “in my poverty?” That there is no wealth before Him Who commanded and the world came into existence! Another explanation: בְּעוֹנְיִי “in my deprivation,” because he fasted and donated the price of his meal to Heaven.
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi objected before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Did we not state: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 1:4</a>) “If somebody saw a find and fell on it, when another person came and grabbed it, he who grabbed it had the rights to it?” He answered him: Explain it if the first one did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did we not state: “If he fell on it or spread his talith on it, one removes him from it?” He said to him, that is the same, if he did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:2</a>) “If two were pushing one another because of a sheaf and another person came and grabbed it from before them, the one who grabbed is entitled to it. He said to him, it is the same, they did not say that our four cubits should acquire it for us.
Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: That refers to divorce documents, but it does not apply to a gift. Rebbi Joḥanan adds something, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish adds something. Rebbi Joḥanan adds something. Since a find which cannot be acquired by the knowledge of another person can be acquired within four cubits, should this rule not apply <i>a fortiori</i> to a gift that is acquired by the knowledge of another person? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish adds something. Since a gift which could only be acquired within four cubits by the knowledge of another person, should this not <i>a fortiori</i> apply to a find?
Rebbi Zeïra objected before Rebbi Yasa: But did we not state: The same applies to marriage? He answered him, divorce documents and marriage contracts have the same rules. But did we not state: The same applies to debt? He said to him, because if he said to him: Throw it into the sea and your debt will be forgiven, it would be forgiven. But if that is true, even if it fell down close to the debtor, the debtor should have the benefit! But we have stated, if it falls down close to the debtor, the debtor is still obligated. For he said to him, throw it so that it will enter my domain, but it did not yet enter his domain. Rebbi Abbahu said, all those objections that Rebbi Zeïra raised before Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish raised before Rebbi Joḥanan. Did he accept them from him? He solved them with those same solutions.
Rebbi Abin in the name of Ḥizqiah: The second scribe earns money<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Mishnah stated that the woman is divorced if the bill reached the airspace of her property, the practice explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.3">Note 33</a> forces the parties to have a second document written since otherwise the woman could not remarry and, consequently, the husband could not escape his obligation to support her. {The corrector added a word: נשכר הלבלר שיני שנו “they taught that the second scribe earns money”. The attempts of the classical commentators to replace שיני by שנו are most unconvincing.}</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah speaks of a roof with a parapet when it descended lower than the parapet<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in which the bill of divorce is considered as delivered if it arrived at the wife’s roof must consider a roof shielded from the wind by a parapet (which by biblical standard must be at least 10 handbreadths high; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:8" href="/Deuteronomy.22.8"><i>Deut</i>. 22:8</a>).</i>, or one without a parapet when it descended lower than within three handbreadths of the roof, since anything within three handbreadths of the roof is as if it were part of the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Any opening which is less than 3 handbreadths wide can be disregarded. [In the terminology of the Babli, this is called לְבוּד “glued (to the wall or the ground)”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 97a" href="/Shabbat.97a"><i>Šabbat</i> 97a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16b" href="/Eruvin.16b"><i>Erubin</i> 16b</a>.] This explanation is also quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 26b:10" href="/Eruvin.26b.10"><i>Erubin</i> 10 (26b 1. 39</a>).</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Abba bar Hamnuna in the name of Rav Ada bar Aḥawa: This refers to the rules of the Sabbath, where anything within three handbreadths of a partition is considered part of the partition<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden on the Sabbath to move anything from a private (enclosed) domain to the public domain. The corresponding rules have nothing to do with property rights.</i>. Rebbi Yasa said, they are not comparable, for bills of divorce are about the top, the rules of the Sabbath about the below<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce is delivered as soon as it enters the roof space defined by the parapet from the top; the rules of “gluing” a hanging wall to the ground refer to the three handbreadths closest to the ground.</i>.
