For bills of divorce even if it never came to rest<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As the Mishnah explains, even if the bill was burned in a fire on the top, the delivery is valid.</i>, for the Sabbath only if it rested<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the rules of the Sabbath, moving an object has three stages: Lifting the object from a state of rest, transporting it from one domain to another, and depositing it (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1). If any of the three stages is missing, the biblical law was not violated. Therefore, if a document was moved from one domain to another (as from the husband’s courtyard to the wife’s roof) and burned while still flying, it was delivered as a bill of divorce but not moved by the laws of the Sabbath. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:9, Note 188</a>).</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Only if it descended to within the partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in the Mishnah. If the husband throws the bill from his roof to her courtyard, it is possible to say that the bill was delivered the moment it cleared the roof only if the walls of the wife’s courtyard are higher than the husband’s roof. Otherwise it would be delivered only if the bill fell below the level of the courtyard walls. (The same argument is quoted in Samuel’s name in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79a" href="/Gittin.79a">Babli, 79a</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Immi asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: Does the Mishnah follow Rebbi, since Rebbi considers partitions as solidly filled up<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi considers it a violation of the Sabbath if the object was brought within the walls of the domain for which it was intended; he does not require it actually to come to rest (<i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 2c 1. 63, 11:1 12c 1. 59; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 4a" href="/Gittin.4a">Babli 4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 5a" href="/Gittin.5a">5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79a" href="/Gittin.79a"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 79a</a>).</i>? He said to him, here in the matter of bills of divorce it is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79a" href="/Gittin.79a">Babli agrees, 79a</a>, that the delivery of bills of divorce is governed by the rules of property rights, not those of the Sabbath.</i>. Could one not object that Rebbi said, if it is roofed? And you say, it is not roofed?<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah and Halakhah <i>Šabbat</i> 11:1: “If somebody throws an object from a private domain to the public domain or from the public domain to a private domain, he is guilty. From a private to a private domain across the public domain, R. Aqiba declares him guilty but the Sages declare him not prosecutable.” It is then explained that throwing from the public to a private domain is prosecutable only if the object came to rest. On that it is noted that Rebbi does not require it to come to rest (only to be received within the walls), but R. Abba bar Huna in the name of Rav said that “Rebbi did only find him guilty if the private domain was roofed.” It is noted there that R. Joḥanan objected to the latter statement, which explains that no answer is given to the question raised.</i> What is the difference between bills of divorce and the Sabbath? Rebbi Abba said, about the Sabbath it is written: “You shall not do any work;” it may make itself automatically<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As long as it was not intended that the object should come to rest by the force of the thrower (but it was moved along by some mechanical contraption not directly controlled by the thrower), no violation of the Sabbath occured. But for delivery of a bill of divorce, it is the fact of delivery rather than its mode which counts.</i>. But here “he shall deliver into her hand,” into her domain.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, a man may send away his wife with an old bill of divorce, but the House of Hillel forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah 9:11</a>, the House of Shammai forbid divorce except for adultery (whether or not it is provable in court). Therefore, the House of Shammai can assume that the parties hate one another and will not sleep together. But the House of Hillel, who permit divorce for any (or no) reason, must be afraid that the wife could become pregnant after the bill was written and this would put her child in an untenable position. In any case, everybody agrees that the bill of divorce is valid in biblical law.</i>. And what is an old bill of divorce? If he was again alone with her after he wrote it for her.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The House of Shammai say,” etc. Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: And any one of them if she was married should not leave, in order not to cause talk about her children<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since the bill of divorce is valid in biblical law, there is no reason to punish any woman who remarries based on such a bill. The court has no reason to force her to leave the new husband. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 79b" href="/Gittin.79b">Babli, 79b</a>, agrees and even notes that if the first husband is unavailable to give a corrected bill, the court will instruct the woman to remarry on the basis of the old bill.</i>.
We stated: “After he wrote it for her.” Some Tannaïm state “after he delivered it to her.” He who says “after he wrote it for her” supports the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the House of Shammai in Mishnah 11: “If a person divorced his wife and stayed with her in a hostelry, she does not need a second bill of divorce from him,” since after a divorce for adultery we do not assume that the husband would sleep with his ex-wife.</i>; he who says “after he delivered it to her” supports the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">They always expect a man and a woman to take any occasion to sleep together if they were once married.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he wrote in the name of an inappropriate government<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">If the document was dated by the regnal years of a king who did not rule over the place at which the bill was written.</i>, in the name of the government of Media<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The government of Media had ceased with Cyrus, many centuries earlier. An astronomer like Ptolemy could base his computations (during the reign of Antoninus Pius) on the era of the Assyrian king Nabonassar, but a legal document had to refer to the years of the Princeps in Rome or the Parthian, later the Persian king in Babylonia.</i> or the government of Greece<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Probably this refers to any one of the successors of Alexander.</i>, from the construction of the Temple or the destruction of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This way of dating probably was proscribed by the Roman government.</i>; if he was in the West and wrote “in the East” or in the East and wrote “in the West”<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If the place of writing the document was described incorrectly.</i>, she needs a bill of divorce from both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The situation of a woman remarrying based on an invalid bill of divorce is identical with that of a woman wrongly informed of the death of her husband who remarried based on that information; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 10:1" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.10.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 10:1, Notes 3–6</a>.</i>, she has neither <i>ketubah</i> nor usufruct nor used clothing from either of them, and if she took anything she must return it. Any child from either of the men is a bastard; neither of them may defile himself for her. Neither man has any claim on what she finds or earns, or on invalidation of her vows. If she was the daughter of an Israel, she is disabled from priesthood, the daughter of a Levite from tithe, the daughter of a Cohen from heave. The heirs of neither man inherit her <i>ketubah</i>. If they died, the brothers of both of them perform <i>ḥaliṣah</i> but not levirate. If he changed his or her name or the name of his or her city<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">As noted in the Halakhah, only this section of the Mishnah represents practice; it has given rise to an enormous literature dealing with the correct spelling of Jewish names of persons and places; cf. the Introductions to E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Jewish Family Names and Their Origins</i>,<i>An Etymological Dictionary</i>, Ktav Publishing 1992; <i>Etymologisches Lexikon der jüdischen Familiennamen</i>, K. G. Saur 1996.</i> she shall be divorced from both of them and all the indicated consequences apply to her.<br>Referring to all the close relatives about whom they said that their co-wives are permitted, if any of the co-wives went and married otherwise but the relatives turned out to be she-rams, she shall be divorced from both of them and all the aforementioned consequences apply to her<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">A “she-ram” is an infertile woman lacking secondary sex characteristics. Her marriage is considered non-existent by biblical standards. It is the basic tenet of the House of Hillel that if a man dies without issue and any of his wives is forbidden to any of his brothers, all of his wives are forbidden to all the brothers and are free to marry outside the family without further ceremony (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 1:1</a>). If the relative turns out to be a she-ram, who cannot be married, the release of the other wives turns out to have been erroneous and their new marriages incestuous for those who hold that the outside marriage of a candidate for levirate marriage is incestuous (which is an opinion of R. Aqiba rejected by his successors.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he wrote in the name of an inappropriate government, in the name of the government of Media,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: They declared the child a bastard because of the danger<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">There is no biblical reason why a bill of divorce should be invalid as long as it is possible to identify its date. The only reason that people are made afraid of the consequences of incorrect dating is to avoid repressive measures by the Roman government. The Babli agrees, 80a.</i>. Rav Huna in the name of Rav: This entire Chapter is Rebbi Meїr’s<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">None of the rules which require a double divorce are practice except that which forbids changing the names of the parties or their places of residence. The Babli agrees implicitly by labelling the rules as R. Meir’s.</i> except for “if he changed his or her name or the name of his or her city.” Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Yasa: Also “if somebody divorced his wife”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The following Chapter 9.</i>? He said to him, not your Chapter but our Chapter<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Only the rules in Chapter 8 which require a double divorce.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody married his sister-in-law while her co-wife went and married somebody else, if the sister-in-law turns out to be a she-ram, the co-wife shall be divorced from both of them and all the aforementioned consequences apply to her<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">A “she-ram” is an infertile woman lacking secondary sex characteristics. Her marriage is considered non-existent by biblical standards. It is the basic tenet of the House of Hillel that if a man dies without issue and any of his wives is forbidden to any of his brothers, all of his wives are forbidden to all the brothers and are free to marry outside the family without further ceremony (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 1:1</a>). If the relative turns out to be a she-ram, who cannot be married, the release of the other wives turns out to have been erroneous and their new marriages incestuous for those who hold that the outside marriage of a candidate for levirate marriage is incestuous (which is an opinion of R. Aqiba rejected by his successors.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody married his sister-in-law,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.4.2">Yebamot 10:5, Notes 116–121</a>.</i> If she should not have married but entered levirate, this is the case of a sister-in-law who married without <i>ḥalîṣah</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, this man performs <i>ḥalīṣah</i>, the other one keeps her. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: She must leave. Rebbi Yose asked Rebbi Phineas, how does the rabbi hold? He said, with Rebbi Jeremiah. He said to him, change your mind, for otherwise I shall publicly call you a rebellious Elder. Rebbi Zevidah said, a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “She must leave both of them and the thirteen items apply to her, following Rebbi Meïr who said it in the name of Rebbi Aqibah. But the Sages say, there is no bastard from a sister-in-law.” Therefore she must leave.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the scribe wrote<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources more in detail: “If the scribe wrote a bill of divorce for the husband and a receipt for the wife and then …” The husband has the money ready to pay the ketubah immediately and the wife will hand him the receipt if she obtains the money with the bill of divorce.</i> and then in error handed the bill of divorce to the woman and the receipt to the man and they exchanged them, then later the bill of divorce is found with the man and the receipt with the woman<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Who had remarried in the meantime, thinking that she was divorced when in fact she was not divorced since she handed the bill of divorce to her husband instead of receiving it from him. One has to assume that both husband and wife were illiterate.</i>, she shall be divorced from both of them and all the indicated consequences apply to her.<br>Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah and Babli sources are divided on whether to read Eliezer or Eleazar; the Halakhah later reads “Eleazar”.</i> says: If it was found out immediately, this is no bill of divorce. If it was found out later<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">After the wife had remarried one suspects the first husband to have obtained the bill of divorce illegally to cause trouble.</i>, it is a bill of divorce since the first cannot destroy the rights of the second.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the scribe wrote,” etc. Where do we hold? If he made a mistake, even Rebbi Eleazar agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe agrees that he made a mistake, R. Eleazar agrees that no divorce took place.</i>. If he made no error, even the rabbis agree<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If the scribe denies making a mistake, the rabbis also have to assume that the bill of divorce in the hand of the first husband is fraudulent.</i>. But we must hold that it was unexplained<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe does not remember or is not available to be asked.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar suspects that possibly he made no mistake; the rabbis suspect that possibly he did make a mistake.
“Rebbi Eleazar says: If it was found out immediately, this is no bill of divorce. If it was found out later, it is a bill of divorce since the first cannot destroy the rights of the second.” What is immediately? Rebbi Ze‘ira says, before she was preliminarily married, or when she was preliminarily married. Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqula said, before she was definitively married, or when she was definitively married<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe does not remember or is not available to be asked.</i>. The Mishnah supports Rebbi Ze‘ira: “Since the first cannot destroy the rights of the second<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The second husband acquires his rights to the bride by the preliminary marriage.</i>.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he wrote a bill to divorce his wife and then changed his mind, the House of Shammai say, he disabled her from the priesthood<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">A priest is forbidden to marry a divorcee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev</i>. 21:7</a>). The House of Shammai forbid a woman to her priestly husband if the bill of divorce was written, even if it never was delivered.</i>. But the House of Hillel say, even if he handed her [the bill of divorce] and attached a condition, if the condition was not satisfied he did not disable her from the priesthood<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the divorce was not effective, the priestly husband can take his wife back.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he wrote a bill to divorce his wife and then changed his mind,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: There is no problem because of the purity of the priestly line and the court will not denigrate her<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If a woman became a widow who had been given a bill of divorce but who was not divorced because the condition attached to the divorce was not fulfilled, the court will not intervene if she marries a Cohen.</i>. There came a case before the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and he acted following the House of Hillel. Rav asked him, why did you bring yourself into this great doubt? He answered, Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The greatest authority of his time.</i> was with me. He quoted about them: “Two are better than one.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:9" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.9"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:9</a>.</i>”
“A woman who engages in sex play<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This meaning of the root סלד (usually “to jump”) is conjectural, similar to the corresponding root סלל (Biblical “to build a road”) in the Babylonian parallels [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 65a" href="/Shabbat.65a"><i>Šabbat</i> 65a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 76a" href="/Yevamot.76a"><i>Yebamot</i> 76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 69b" href="/Sanhedrin.69b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 69b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta <i>Soṭah</i> 5:7</a> (ms. Erfurt סלסל “to adorn”)]. The text is more explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta <i>Soṭah</i> 5:7</a> (Vienna ms.): “If a woman engages in sex play with her son who touches her genitals, the House of Shammai disqualify from the priesthood, the House of Hillel qualify.” The son must be less than 9 years of age for his sex play to be dismissed as meaningless (cf. <i>Yebamot</i> 10:13–14). {Also compare the Latin verb <i>salio</i>,<i>-ui</i>, “to jump; leap, cover”, also said of copulation of animals (E. G.).}</i> with her son, the House of Shammai disqualify, the House of Hillel qualify.” Two women who mutually engage in sex play, the House of Shammai disqualify, the House of Hillel qualify<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 76a" href="/Yevamot.76a">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 76a</a>, the opinion that lesbian activity disqualifies a woman from marrying a priest is dismissed as not conforming to practice; lesbian activity is classified as “simply indecent”. Maimonides (<i>Issure</i> <i>Bi’ah</i> 21:8–9) duly notes that lesbian activity cannot qualify as sexual but then based on a aggadic statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot">Sifra (<i>Aḥare Parašah</i> 9(8</a>)), which defines “the acts of Egypt” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:3" href="/Leviticus.18.3"><i>Lev.</i> 18:3</a>) as same-sex marriages and polyandry, authorizes rabbinic punishment for lesbian activities, which is nowhere to be found in Talmudic sources. This is copied in <i>Šulḥan ‘Arukh Even Ha‘ezer</i> 20:2. These rules probably owe more to Islamic and Christian influences (<i>Romans</i> 1:26) than to strictly Rabbinic rules.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody divorced his wife but she stayed with him in a hostelry<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πανδοκεῖον.</i>, the House of Shammai say that she does not need a second bill of divorce from him, but the House of Hillel say that she needs a second bill of divorce from him<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The reasons are explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.9.2">Note 59</a>.</i>. When? If she was divorced after definitive marriage. But they agree that if she was divorced after preliminary marriage, she does not need a second bill of divorce from him since he was not intimate with her<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">They will not sleep with one another as a matter of course.</i>. If somebody married her based on a bald bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">A “bald” document is an irregular “knotted” document. In a usual, “simple” document, the witnesses sign on the document after the text. In a “knotted” document, the scribe will write a few lines, then these are folded, sewn together, fixed with a knot, and a witness signs on the verso. The document is valid if the number of ties equals the number of witnesses on the verso. A “bald” document has less witnesses than ties and is invalid. A woman who remarries on the basis of an invalid bill of divorce commits adultery. The rules of “knotted” documents are discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:10:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.10.2-6">Halakhah 12</a>.</i>, she shall be divorced from both of them and all the indicated consequences apply to her.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody divorced his wife but she stayed with him,” etc. Rebbi Mana said, the House of Shammai are consistent and the House of Hillel are consistent. Since the House of Shammai say that “a man may send away his wife with an old bill of divorce,” they say that she does not need a second bill of divorce from him. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the House of Shammai are consistent and the House of Hillel are consistent. Since the House of Shammai say that “a man shall not divorce his wife unless he found in her a matter of immorality<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah 9:11</a>.</i>,” thy say that she is dirty in his eyes and he is not suspected of having relations with her, therefore she does not need a second bill of divorce from him.. Since the House of Hillel say, “even if she spoiled his food<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah 9:11</a>.</i>”, they say that she is not dirty in his eyes, he is suspected of having relations with her, and she needs a second bill of divorce from him<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4">Mishnah 9:11</a>, the House of Shammai forbid divorce except for adultery (whether or not it is provable in court). Therefore, the House of Shammai can assume that the parties hate one another and will not sleep together. But the House of Hillel, who permit divorce for any (or no) reason, must be afraid that the wife could become pregnant after the bill was written and this would put her child in an untenable position. In any case, everybody agrees that the bill of divorce is valid in biblical law.</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 81b" href="/Gittin.81b">Babli 81b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 6:9" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).6.9">Tosephta 6:9</a>) hold that the House of Hillel require a second bill of divorce only if it was known that the divorced couple shared a bedroom in the hotel. This is not the position of the Yerushalmi.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in <i>Semaḥot</i> 2:14, a different formulation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 27b-28a" href="/Ketubot.27b-28a">Babli (<i>Ketubot</i> 27b/28a</a>).</i> If somebody divorces his wife she should not live in the same courtyard or the same place<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Or zarua‘ (<i>Yibbum waḥalȋṣah</i> #618) reads מבוי “dead-end-street” in place of מקום. This is the language of the Babli.</i>. If the courtyard was the wife’s property, the husband has to move out, if the husband’s, the wife has to move out. If it was common property, who has to move because of whom? The woman because of the man, unless they can manage to have separate exits<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of <i>Or zarua‘</i>, if they can build a wall dividing the property, each section having its own entrance door from the courtyard.</i>. When has this been said? If they were definitively married, not if they were not definitively married<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Preliminarily married couples are not supposed to have slept together; they are not suspected to do so after divorce.</i>. But the wife of a Cohen if she was not definitively married<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is biblically prohibited to her preliminary ex-husband.</i>, as well as the status of a preliminarily married woman in Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">She was unchaperoned together with her preliminary husband and presumed to have slept with him; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 1:5</a>.</i>, is that of one definitively married. Nobody has to move if a person gives <i>ḥalȋṣah</i> to his sister-in-law even if he had “bespoken<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The imitation of preliminary marriage before levirate marriage; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 2:1" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.2.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:1, Note 6</a>. This is a rabbinic formality; if the couple had relations, they would be biblically married and the marriage could only be terminated by a bill of divorce. The act of <i>ḥalȋṣah</i> guarantees that they never were intimate and the precautions enacted for divorced couples do not apply to them.</i>” her since “bespeaking” does not fully acquire.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Anybody can supplement a bald document<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., supply the additional signatures needed to make the document valid.</i> following the words of Ben Nanas<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the first signatures are those of valid witnesses, the remaining slots can be filled by the signatures of people who are inadmissible as witnesses either because they are relatives of one of the parties or because they have been declared untrustworthy by a court decree (Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:3–4).</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, one supplements the signatures only by relatives who would be able to testify under other circumstances<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees that relatives can sign but he holds that people barred because of disreputable or criminal behavior cannot.</i>. What is a bald document? One whose knots are more than its signatures.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Anybody can supplement a bald document,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">A closely related text, but from another editorial tradition, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.2-10"><i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a>.</i> From where that a document can be knotted<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The basic use for “knotted” documents was for real estate transactions, probably to hide the financial data from public knowledge. Such a document was certainly impossible in Egypt, where all real estate transactions had to be filed with the State registrar. Its validity in rabbinic tradition is given a biblical basis. It seems from the discussion in Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 10 that the use of “knotted” documents was a Palestinian peculiarity (such documents have been found in the Judean desert.)</i>? Rebbi Immi said, it is written: “I took the document,<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 32:11" href="/Jeremiah.32.11"><i>Jer</i>. 32:11</a>: “I took the document of acquisition, the sealed one, the orders and rules, and the public one.” The verse clearly states that a sealed document is the main object, accompanied by a public document.</i>” etc. “And [the sealed]”, that is the knotted [document]. “And the open,” that is the simple, part of the knotted<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the document, whose formulation is identical whether sealed or public.</i>. “And the orders and the rules”; orders and rules differ between these, for one is with two {witnesses], the other with three<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">A public document needs two witnesses, a sealed (knotted) one at least three.</i>; one in it, the other on its back<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A public document is signed by the witnesses on the recto of the sheet, starting exactly one line after the end of the text. A sealed document is signed on the verso, between the folds. {According to Rashbam (Commentary to <i>Baba batra</i> 10:1), the document was written with wide spaces between the lines, was signed by witnesses between the lines, and then was folded and sewn so that the document text was hidden but the signatures appeared on the outside. It seems impossible to accept this explanation for the Yerushalmi text. (Cf. H. Albeck’s commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.2-10"><i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a>).}</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah, on a simple document, the witnesses sign parallel<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">They simply sign on the lines after the body of the document, parallel to the lines used by the scribe.</i>, on a knotted one the witnesses sign lengthwise<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion, the witnesses sign in the back at a right angle to the scribe’s text, starting in the back of the last line of the document. Only the text covered by witnesses’ signatures in the back is certified.</i>. Rebbi Idi said, the witnesses sign between any two knots, but it must be on top<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the preceding description and requires that the witnesses sign in the back parallel to the text written by the scribe, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.9.1">Note 94</a>.</i>. But should one not be afraid that maybe he falsifies<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">How can the witnesses be sure that the writer of the document will not falsify the document after they affixed their signatures? For a public document there is no problem since anything following the signatures has to be disregarded. For a sealed document with signatures at a right angle there is no problem since any text not covered by the signatures at the back has to be disregarded. But if the signatures are affixed while the document is being written, where are the guarantees?</i>? Rav Naḥman<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i>: Rav Ḥuna. This seems to be the correct attribution, confirmed in the sequel.</i> wanted to argue that witnesses never sign below unless they first read before them: “I X son of Y accept everything written above.” But should one not be afraid that he erased and changed to falsify? Rav Huna is of the opinion that two times “I accept” makes it invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">A sealed document containing two different clauses of acceptation is considered fraudulent and not enforceable in court.</i>. But should one not be afraid that he erased and changed to falsify? Does Rav Huna not consider that he might write another text, erase and change to falsify? Rav Huna is of the opinion that an erasure makes invalid even if the correction is certified<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">An erasure in a sealed document is unacceptable even if confirmed as such by signatures. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 161b" href="/Bava_Batra.161b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 161b</a>, R. Joḥanan accepts an erasure in a sealed document if it carries a note: “This is a correction”.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said: There is no “she shall be divorced”. Rebbi Immi said, several boxes of cases came before Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish and they said, go and complete following Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">They disagree with the last statement in Mishnah 11 and permit the belated signing by relatives who otherwise are in good standing.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah applies if one witness is missing, but only if there are three or more<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">This disagreement between ben Nanas and R. Aqiba about the required qualifications of the witnesses refers to the case that only one additional signature is required in addition to the three statutory ones.</i>. Two or three<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Any number of irregular witnesses at the beginning is invalid for everybody.</i> at the beginning is invalid, one below the three is valid. Rebbi Mana asked: That is, if they are continuous. If they are with interruptions<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If the three statutory witnesses did not sign together but each one signed for a separate knot, with space in between.</i>? As one says, they are considered like testimony at a distance, they only serve to certify the testimony. And so it is here<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.4.3">Chapter 1, Note 117</a>.</i>. If there were nine, can he untie two and have two sign? Or are two witnesses on a knotted document nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The text is difficult to understand since a document with 9 knots needs an additional 6 signatures. One must assume that some additional signatures already are on the document and the question is directed against R. Joḥanan: Why should it not be possible to let two unqualified witnesses sign since the validation of a knotted document needs three witnesses in any case (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 10:3</a>).</i>? If there were five, it is obvious that he unties one and has it signed<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, there must be two knots untied and confirmed on the verso by two different signatures; only one of them was accepted by R. Joḥanan.</i>. It turns out that his untying permits it following Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel, for Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel says, the essential part is what validates<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.2">Chapter 3, Note 68</a>. If the text which contains the identities of the parties and their obligations is certified by signatures on the verso for knotted contracts or at the end for open contracts, the contracts are valid. If prepared forms are not certified for sealed contracts it does not impinge on the validity of the contract for R. Ḥananiah ben Gamliel.</i>, even if he ties and signs after that. That is, if he ties all knots and after that the first three [witnesses] did sign. But if he tied the first three<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Which contain the essential data and are certified by three witnesses as required for sealed contracts according to all sources.</i> and had them signed, even if he makes many ties and lets many<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Unqualified relatives.</i> sign it is valid. Practice is that a knotted document is first knotted and then signed. If he made the first knots and had them signed right away, it is obvious that the first two did not sign the knotted document<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since a “knotted” sealed document is validated only by three witnesses, the first two do not combine for validation.</i>.
Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is out of context here; it refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a> where R. Ḥananiah ben Gamliel states that a knotted document can be converted into a simple one if its witnesses sign on the document side.</i>? If he knotted and signed it inside, everybody agrees that it can be made simple<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">If the document was signed before it was sewn, it is a regular open document. The rules of sealed documents do not apply.</i>. If he knotted and signed it outside, everybody agrees that it cannot be made simple<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">If the signatures are on the back of the document, the text itself is left without witnesses; as an open document is would be worthless.</i>. But we must deal with the case that he knotted it from the inside and then signed it on the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">As the text stands, it is unintelligible. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a> implies that “knotted from the inside” means “had it signed on the inside and then sewed it and had it signed again on the outside”, where R. Ḥanania ben Gamliel holds that it can be used either way; but the opposing faction holds that once three validating signatures were on the back, the document can only be unsealed in court to be enforced.</i>; in that case Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel said that it can be made simple but the rabbis say, it cannot be made simple. <sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The text in braces is a first, incomplete version of the following, which the scribe forgot to delete.</i>{Rebbi objected to the statement of Ḥananiah ben Gamliel: “The body of the document.” If he knotted and signed it outside, everybody agrees that it cannot be made simple. But we must deal with the case that he knotted it from the inside and then signed it on the outside; in that case Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel said that it can be made simple, but the rabbis say, it cannot be made simple.) Rebbi objected to the statement of Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel: The body<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 164" href="/Bava_Batra.164">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 164a/b</a>, Rebbi is stating that the methods of computation of regnal years in open and sealed documents are different. Since sealed documents were not used in Babylonia, this argument seems irrelevant.</i> of the document shows whether it is simple or not simple. What is the body of the document? Rebbi Abba said, as Rav Huna said, witnesses never sign below unless they first read before them: “I X son of Y accept everything written above.<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a sealed document the witnesses start signing once there is space for three signatures, it follows that at the top of the document one has the identification of the person to whom the document refers together with any obligation incurred by that person. In an open document, the main point has to be repeated at the end of the text to make clear what the witnesses certify. In a sealed document, the place of the signatures on the verso of the main part is the certification.</i>” But did we not state: “That is the formula of a document”?<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Huna’s text is required for all documents, whether open or sealed.</i> Rebbi Mana said, that is the formula of a knotted document. Rebbi Abin said, even if you say it is the same for knotted and simple, for a knotted document it is a necessity, for a simple one it is not a necessity<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to write both sealed and open documents following the same formula. However, a sealed document whose three certifying signatures are not in the back of the text containing the name of the debtor and the exact nature of his obligation is invalid; an open document is valid if only it contains all the necessary information and two signatures. If a sealed document also contains two signatures inside, there is no reason not to follow R. Ḥananiah ben Gamliel.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, if one adds to practice<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A sealed document minimally valid cannot be turned into an open document. However, at places where the common practice requires more than the minimum, there may be reason to permit turning the sealed into an open document.</i>. A simple document [is witnessed] by two persons. If common usage is to have three, one makes it with three. A knotted document is signed on its back. If common usage is to sign it inside and out, one signs it inside and out.
The condition of validity is stated here and was stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:10:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.10.2-6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:12</a> and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.2-10"><i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a> one finds rules about sealed documents. These rules do not overlap. The rules about the bald document are not found in <i>Baba batra</i>, the requirement of three witnesses is not in <i>Giṭṭin</i>. One would have expected an argument why the rules of one tractate, dealing with divorce documents, also apply to the other, dealing with deeds of real estate and mortgages. For some reason, this seemed obvious to the editors of the Yerushalmi.</i>. It was necessary to state it there. If it had been stated here but not there, we would have said that here it is invalid because of the restrictions connected with adultery but there, since no restrictions connected with adultery are involved, it would be valid. Or if it had been stated there but not here, we would have said that there it is invalid since one comes to collect money based on that document but here, where one does not collect money, it would be valid. Therefore, it was necessary both to state it here and to state it there.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody in divorcing his wife says to her: Herewith you are permitted to any man except Mr. X, Rebbi Eliezer permits<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He permits the wife to remarry; the Sages prohibit since they reject the possibility of a divorce which permits control of the divorced wife by the former husband.</i> but the Sages prohibit. What should he do? He should take the document back from her and deliver it again while saying, herewith you are permitted to any man. If he had written the condition<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Which restricts the divorcee in the choice of her future husband.</i> in the document it would be invalid even if he had later erased it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody in divorcing his wife,” etc. Rebbi Ilai<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 82b" href="/Gittin.82b">Babli (82b</a>) this is ascribed to R. Yannai. There, the objection is that “she became another man’s” could be interpreted as “she became <i>any</i> other man’s”.</i> said, the reason of Rebbi Eliezer: “She left his house, went, and became another man’s<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:2" href="/Deuteronomy.24.2"><i>Deut</i>. 24:2</a>.</i>”, even if he did permit her only to one other man. This argument proves more than the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">By the mathematical principle<i>qui nimium probat nihil probat</i>, R. Ilai’s argument proves nothing.</i>! The Mishnah said if she was permitted to all but forbidden to one other man; the argument is that he forbade her to all but permitted her only to one other man. What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? Death permits and divorce permits. Since death permits only partially<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The pool of possible marriage partners available to a widow is smaller than the pool which was available to the same woman before her marriage since she is forbidden any close relatives of her deceased husband.</i>, so the bill of divorce permits only partially<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since the divorce does not remove the incest prohibitions, it seems possible that the husband can add prohibitions of his own.</i>. What is the reason of the rabbis? “She left his house, went, and became another man’s”; it brackets marriage and divorce. Since her divorce is without participation of a third party, so her marriage must be without participation of a third party<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If the former husband could impose marriage restrictions on his former wife, her remarriage would be subject to the former husband’s edict. This would create a kind of polyandry.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Different versions of the following discussions are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 83" href="/Gittin.83">Babli, 83a/b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:1-5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.1-5">Tosephta 7:1–5</a>, <i>Sifry</i> Deut. 269.</i> After Rebbi Eliezer’s death, four Elders assembled to object to Rebbi Eliezer’s words: Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, Rebbi Yose the Galilean, Rebbi Tarphon, and Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Joshua told them, one does not contradict the lion after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects all the following arguments (as does the Babli, except an argument attributed to R. Eleazar ben Azariah not mentioned in the Yerushalmi.)</i>. Rebbi Tarphon objected: If he said to her, you are permitted to any man except Mr. X; she went and married [the latter’s brother who]<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the text; to be added from the parallel attributed to R. Jeremiah.</i> died childless; how can she contract the levirate marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the prohibited man was the only brother of the deceased husband, the widow should have to marry him but she is prohibited by the former husband’s stipulation. In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.3">Note 10</a>) the conclusion is stated explicitly that “this is no cutting loose” (referring to the bill of divorce which in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a> is called “scroll of cutting loose” the wife from the husband’s power. This really refers to the rabbis’ argument, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.2">Note 9</a>.)</i>? Does it not turn out that he<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The first husband would in effect stipulate that his ex-wife cannot fulfill the commandment of the levirate marriage.</i> stipulated against what is written in the Torah, and anybody’s condition contradicting what is written in the Torah is invalid. Then nobody should be permitted to marry his brother’s daughter, lest he come to stipulate against what is written in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If the uncle married to the niece died childless, the widow clearly is forbidden to marry her own father in levirate. But the possibility of a marriage of the niece to the uncle is one of the doctrines of Pharisaic Judaism, in contrast to the Sadducees (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Yebamot</i>.)</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one understands the reason. There, the Torah forbade her to him<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, the greater part of Tractate <i>Yebamot</i> is devoted to cases where levirate marriage is impossible.</i>. But here, he forbids to him<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The first husband forbids levirate marriage to the brother of the second husband.</i>! Rebbi Ḥananiah objected in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Should one not state “sixteen women” following Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 1:1</a> enumerates 15 categories of women who are forbidden levirate and, in the opinion of the House of Hillel, automatically free their co-wives from levirate. According to R. Eliezer, should one not add the woman who married the brother of a man forbidden to her by divorce stipulation?</i>? Rebbi Mana said, the reason was already explained: there, the Torah forbade her to him. But here, he forbids to him<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The first husband forbids levirate marriage to the brother of the second husband.</i>! Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this has to read “R. Simeon ben Eleazar” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:5" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.5">Tosephta 7:5</a>.)</i> asked: If he said to her, you are permitted to any man except Mr. X; she went and married the latter’s brother who died childless, how can the latter permit what the first forbade<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is a <i>non sequitur</i>. It seems that one has to read with the Tosephta: “She went and married an unrelated person who then divorced her unconditionally”, i. e., the second husband by his divorce annulled the condition of the first divorce.</i>? His words imply that death and divorce permit what the first [husband] prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The condition “except Mr. X” according to R. Eliezer refers only to the next marriage of the divorcee. If the divorcee married according to the stipulation and then the second marriage is dissolved either by divorce or by the husband’s death, the widow or divorcee is free to marry anybody she wishes. The Babli agrees with this interpretation, 83a, which shows that R. Tarphon’s objection cannot be sustained.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, he said only death and divorce; therefore, not marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer, who declares the divorce valid, will insist that the first remarriage satisfy the first husband’s condition.</i>.
Rebbi Yose the Galilean said: Do we find in the Torah that what is forbidden to one is permitted to the other and what is permitted to one is forbidden for the other? Forbidden to one she is forbidden to everybody;permitted to one is permitted to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">His argument is the same in the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.3">Note 10</a>). It is difficult to accept the statement at face value.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba objected: If the one to whom she was prohibited was a Cohen and the divorcer died, would she not be considered a widow for him but a divorcee for all his clan of Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The situation would be paradoxical in that the divorcee would be forbidden to all Cohanim (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev.</i> 21:7</a>) except the Cohen to whom she was forbidden since for him she is a widow who is forbidden only to the High Priest. (One might ask why a man would forbid his divorcee to a Cohen since she is forbidden to him anyhow.)</i>? Where was the Torah more restrictive, for a divorcee or for a widow<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The lengthy amplification refers to the prior case that the person whom the divorcee could not marry was a Cohen, forbidden to her in any case.</i>? The Torah was more restrictive for a divorcee than for a widow. But since a divorcee, where one is more restrictive in all aspects, does not become forbidden with respect to what was permitted by her divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">As a married woman she was forbidden to everybody except her husband; as a divorcee she is permitted to most men.</i>, would it be logical that the widow should be forbidden from what was permitted her from her aspect of married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This is an involved argument repeating the paradoxical situation described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.4">Note 24</a>.</i>? Even him did the divorce touch<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The arguments of Notes 24, 27 are to be rejected. A divorcee who cannot be married is still a divorcee; the argument of R. Aqiba is invalid.</i>. Since both a widow and a divorcee were forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">To the High Priest.</i> even if her aspect of married woman was permitted, with restrictive divorce certainly she should be forbidden even in the aspect<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden to the rank-and-file Cohen. The sentence is an amplification of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.4">Note 28</a>.</i> that was permitted by her divorce.
Another argument<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 83a" href="/Gittin.83a">Babli, 83a</a>, this argument also is attributed to R. Aqiba.</i>: “Assume that he said to her, ‘you are permitted to every man except Mr. X;’ she went and married another and had children by him. Then her [second] husband died<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: “died or divorced her.” This is implied by the sequel here also.</i>; she went and married Mr. X. Will not her children by her second husband become bastards<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The third marriage violates the conditions of the first divorce. If the first divorce is invalidated, the second marriage retroactively becomes adulterous and the children bastards. The argument does not disprove R. Eliezer’s position; it shows that from a practical point of view, that position should not be tolerated.</i>?” His argument implies that divorce does not permit what the first husband had forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Contrary to what was asserted in the name of R. Eliezer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.1.3">Note 21</a>), a later divorce or widowhood does not eliminate the conditions of the first divorce.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said, do we find that a woman was not originally forbidden to a man because of adultery and then retroactively became forbidden as adulterous<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">He doubts that anybody can agree that the second husband, who contracted his marriage within the parameters of the first divorce, can become an adulterer without his knowledge by an action of the wife after his death or after a divorce.</i>? Rebbi Mana said to him, to what can you compare this? To one who told his wife, “this is your bill of divorce on condition that you sleep with Mr. X.” At the start, he is forbidden to have sexual relations with her. If he transgressed and slept with her, the divorce was retroactively validated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Even according to the Babli which holds that any expression “on condition that” means “from today, on condition that”, Mr. X is barred from marrying the divorcee as long as he had not slept with her since she is not yet divorced and, therefore, unable to contract any marriage. Therefore, when he goes to bed with the divorcee, it is an adulterous act. But at the moment of penetration, the condition of the divorce is satisfied and the aspect of adultery has disappeared. This is just the opposite of the situation which caused discomfort to R. Shammai. (Rashba, <i>Novellae</i> <i>ad</i> 84a.)</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the statement by the Mishnah, that the husband cannot simply annul the condition he had imposed but has to take the bill back and bodily deliver it while declaring his ex-wife free to marry any man she chooses.</i>, the practice follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar, since Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.2">Chapter 8, Note 27</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 78a" href="/Gittin.78a">Babli 78a</a>.</i> it never is a bill of divorce unless he declares at the moment of delivery: “This is your bill of divorce.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yannai: There is not even a hint of invalidity on her<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">An imperfect bill of divorce will nevertheless make the woman a divorcee according to the rules of the priesthood. Only a bill which clearly is null and void does not have this consequence (and if the woman became a widow before a corrected version could be delivered to her, she would be able to marry a Cohen.) In Yerushalmi language, the invalid bill implies no “hint of invalidity” <i>for the woman</i>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 86b" href="/Gittin.86b">Babli terminology (86b</a>), there is “no hint of a bill of divorce” <i>on the document</i> (רִיחַ הַגֵּט אֵין בּוֹ).</i>. Cahana said, this implies that they did not worry<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of the Mishnah accepts the statement of R. Yannai that the document delivered with the exclusion of a possible marriage partner is null and void. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84b" href="/Gittin.84b">Babli, 84b</a>, R. Joḥanan in the name of Cahana adopts the position here described as R. Aḥa’s.)</i>. If they did worry, might he not have said “you are permitted to any man” when it was still in her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If the delivery were valid to disqualify the woman from marrying a Cohen, it also should be valid for a full divorce upon a public disclaimer by the husband of the condition imposed.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa said, this implies that they did worry<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna of the Mishnah must reject the statement of R. Yannai. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84b" href="/Gittin.84b">Babli agrees, 84b</a>.</i>. If they did not worry, could he not divorce by any power he has to divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">He also rejects the determination of R. Joḥanan that the Mishnah follows R. Simeon ben Eleazar. The fact that the Mishnah requires the husband to retake possession of the bill after the wife already had it in her hand means that the wife already had acquired the bill, became forbidden to a Cohen, and, therefore, a simple statement by the husband would be invalid. (Rashi’s explanation, 84b).</i>?
Rebbi Ze‘ira said: Shila bar Binah stated: Anything which is invalid containing a condition if delivered on condition is also invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last statement in the Mishnah, that the condition written in the bill of divorce invalidates the bill. It is stated as a general principle that any condition, which written in the bill will invalidate it, also invalidates if not written but imposed orally at the time of the delivery. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84b" href="/Gittin.84b">Babli, 84b</a>, takes the opposite track: A written condition invalidates if and only if it invalidates if imposed orally. The Babli invalidates any condition formulated using the term “except” but validates every “on condition that”. This is foreign to the Yerushalmi.)</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “You are permitted to any man except to my father and your father, to my brother and your brother, to a slave, a Gentile,” or anybody she is unable to contract a preliminary marriage with<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">An unmarried woman can contract preliminary marriage with any Jewish man except those connections forbidden as incestuous relations which are deadly sins. Marriage is also impossible with Gentiles and slaves. A divorce stipulation which forbids no marriage possible under the law is empty.</i>, it is valid. “You are permitted to any man except as a widow to the High Priest, a divorcee or one having received <i>ḥalȋṣah</i> to a common priest, a bastard or a Gibeonite girl to an Israel, the daughter of an Israel to a a bastard or a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">All these are subsumed under “holiness prohibitions”, enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:4</a>.</i>,” or anybody she could contract a preliminary marriage with even if it is sinful<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Including common law “commandment prohibitions,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.4.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:4</a>.</i>, it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since for the rabbis who oppose R. Eliezer, any bill of divorce is invalid if it restricts the pool of legal marriage partners of the divorcee.</i>.<br>The essence<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The required text.</i> of the bill of divorce: You are herewith permitted to any man. Rebbi Jehudah says: This shall be for you from me a divorce scroll and a letter of abandonment, to enable you to marry any man you desire. The essence of the bill of manumission: You are a free person, you are on your own.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The essence of the bill of divorce: You are herewith permitted to any man,” etc. Rebbi Aḥa said: “Cutting off”, the Torah did not choose the term “cutting off” except to refer to a person with whom a marriage is possible<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This really refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> and explains why the prohibition of impossible relations does not invalidate the bill of divorce. The bill is called “a scroll of cutting off” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:3" href="/Deuteronomy.24.3">3</a>. If marriage was not possible, it cannot be dissolved.</i>.
Rebbi Mana said: He has to be careful not to say “וְדִין”.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">While the usual spelling of “this” is דֵין, in the divorce document the spelling must be defective so it cannot be read as וְדִין “by law” implying that the husband would not consider a divorce unless forced by some law. This would invalidate the divorce. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 85b" href="/Gittin.85b">Babli, 85b</a>, has a long list of similar spelling rules.</i> Rebbi Mana asked: “You are on your own”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Is that an acceptable formula for a divorce? The obvious negative answer is not given.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one must say: “You are a free person, you are on your own.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In a bill of manumission, both expressions are required. The Mishnah (with a very slight change in wording) supports this by stating both expressions in parallel, not connected by “or”.</i>” The Mishnah said so: “You are a free person, you are on your own.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Three kinds of bills of divorce are invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">They are invalid only because they violate customary rules, not because of biblical precepts. The woman is forbidden to remarry on the strength of such a bill, but if she did marry, the second marriage and its offspring are legitimate.</i>, but if she married, the child is legitimate: If he wrote in his own handwriting but it was not signed by witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses which validate the divorce are those present at the delivery. For the document itself, one can apply the principle valid for I.O.U’s: “The signature of the debtor is worth 100 witnesses.”</i>; it was signed by witnesses but is not dated<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">According to all opinions, the dating of a bill of divorce is a rabbinic requirement (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.2.2">Chapter 2, Note 32</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.3.3">Chapter 4, Note 64</a>).</i>; it is dated but only one witness signed it<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the document was in the husband’s handwriting, one witness alone is not sufficient to let the divorcee remarry without trouble.</i>. These are three invalid kinds of bills of divorce, but if she married, the child is legitimate.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “You are permitted to any man,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.9.2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.9.2">Yebamot 3:9, Notes 122-136 (י)</a>. It is inserted here at the wrong place since its topic is Mishnah 4, as explained in detail in Yebamot. The text here was copied negligently.</i>There really is no questionable divorce here. What are questionable <i>qiddušin</i>? As we have stated there: If he threw the betrothal gift to her and there is a doubt whether it fell closer to him or to her, those are questionable <i>qiddušin</i>. And here, if he threw the divorce document to her and there is a doubt whether it fell closer to him or to her, that is a questionable divorce.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ḥalaphta from Haifa: Any who were married on the basis of such a document should not leave<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Judicial confirmation of the genuineness of a document does not have to be written immediately below the signatures of the witnesses; it can be anywhere on the document. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 163b" href="/Bava_Batra.163b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 163b</a>, forbids <i>any</i> space between signatures and confirmation, not even one line.</i> in order not to give a bad reputation to her children. His daughter who was married outside on the basis of such a document should not leave in order to damage her co-wife to her father. Her co-wife who was married outside on the basis of such a document has to leave. His daughter who married his brother [on the basis of such a document has to leave. Her co-wife who was married to his brother]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Necessary text added from <i>Yebamot</i>, missing here.</i> on the basis of such a document, or even to her father, should not leave.
It was stated: [A claim based on] any of these three types of documents can be collected only from unincumbered property, not from mortgaged property. Rebbi Abba said, that means, if the document in the hand of the lender has not been confirmed. [But if the document in the hand of the lender has been confirmed, he may collect. Rebbi Yose asked: If the document in the hand of the lender has not been confirmed,]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Necessary text added from <i>Yebamot</i>, missing here.</i> he should not be able to collect even from unincumbered property! But one must deal with a document confirmed in the hand of the lender. And why can he not collect? Rebbi Bisna said, because of action in partnership. Rebbi Abina said, because it is invalid And here, because it is invalid. Rebbi Abin asked, so far, if the old man took the loan [and the old man mortgaged. But if the old man took the loan and the son mortgaged,]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Necessary text added from <i>Yebamot</i>, missing here.</i> can you say because of a conspiracy? No, because it is invalid. Rebbi Abun asked, was it not stated: The same holds for bills of divorce? Can you say there, because of action in partnership? No, because it is invalid. And here, because it is invalid.
“Three kinds of bills of divorce are invalid,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: All in handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The bill can only be used in an emergency if it was in the husband’s handwriting in all three cases.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar asked: There are witnesses; why do I need handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">He questions why the middle case of a signed but undated bill needs to be the husband’s autograph to be valid. He is reported in the same sense in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 86b" href="/Gittin.86b">Babli, 86b</a>.</i>? Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: All in handwriting except if its witnesses are with it<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Supporting R. Eleazar. A similar statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 86" href="/Gittin.86">Babli, 86a/b</a>. Samuel’s opinion, that the husband’s autograph is needed only in the first case, has no parallel in the Yerushalmi.</i>. Rav Hamnuna said, they testified to their own sin since they agreed to lie by signing on an undated bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">He supports R. Joḥanan. The witnesses who signed the undated document impeach themselves; their testimony can be accepted only in an emergency.</i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Hamnuna, Rav Ada bar Aḥa<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">These names are not known from any other source. It seems that one should read: Rebbi Aḥa, Rav Hamnuna, Rav Ada bar Aḥawa.</i> in the name of Rav: This is Rebbi Meїr’s<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 86a" href="/Gittin.86a">Babli agrees, 86a</a>.</i>. Why did you have to say this? Rebbi Mana said, since Rav Huna said in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 8:5, Note 71.</i>: “This entire Chapter is Rebbi Meїr’s except for ‘if he changed his or her name or the name of his or her city.’ ” You should not come to say that the first is Rebbi Meїr’s, the second the rabbis’<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">One should not think that only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.5.1">Chapter 8, Mishnah 5</a> ff. follows R. Meїr but all of Chapter 9 follows the rabbis opposing R. Meїr (who are represented by R. Eleazar in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.4.1">Mishnah 9:5</a>) who hold that all bills of divorce are validated by the witnesses to the delivery, not the signatures on the bill.</i>. Therefore it is necessary to say that this is Rebbi Meїr’s. Rebbi Yose said<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation of R. Mana is far-fetched.</i>, since Rav and Samuel both say that practice follows Rebbi Eleazar, that you should not be led to say the same here. Therefore it is necessary to say that this is Rebbi Meїr’s.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar says, it<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One of the irregular bills of divorce mentioned in the preceding Mishnah.</i> is valid even if there are no signatures of witnesses on it if only he delivered it in the presence of witnesses; and she can use it to collect from encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The testimony of the witnesses to the delivery will enable the divorcee to request the court to foreclose property if the husband is unwilling or unable to pay her <i>ketubah</i>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.3.1">Mishnah 4:3</a>.</i>. For the witnesses sign on the bill of divorce only for the public good<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The public good is that the divorcee is much less likely to become a burden on public charity if her claims on her ex-husband are documented rather than dependent on the testimony of witnesses who may or may not be available to testify.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eleazar says, it is valid even if there are no signatures of witnesses on it,” etc. Rav and Samuel both say that practice follows Rebbi Eleazar. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: The words of the Sages: It is invalid. There came a case before Rebbi Joḥanan about a Cohen’s wife and he wanted to act on Rebbi Yannai’s statement<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He wanted to declare the bill of divorce as nonexistent and permit the divorcee to return to her Cohen husband.</i> when it was heard that Rav and Samuel disagreed. There came before him [another case] involving an Israel, and he listened to that<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">He accepted the unsigned bill of divorce as valid and empowered the wife to collect her <i>ketubah</i>.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, in the opinion of everybody it is valid. But did not Rebbi Eleazar say, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If a name is attached to the statement it proves that it cannot be a unanimous opinion. The opposing, unnamed, rabbis must hold that the bill is invalid.</i>, and the rabbis say, it is invalid? What is their disagreement? Rebbi Eleazar said, it is valid and she can collect from encumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband is unable to pay her <i>ketubah</i> and if he sold real estate during the marriage, the wife’s lien on that real estate was not broken by the sale and she can foreclose from the buyer.</i>. The rabbis said, it is invalid and she can collect only from unencumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of divorce is not usable in money matters; the divorcee’s claim does not have the status of a prior mortgage, which can also be enforced against the innocent buyer, but that of an undocumented loan which on the testimony of witnesses can be enforced against the debtor (in this case, the ex-husband) but not against a buyer of real property (who could not have discovered the existence of the debt by a title search.)</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If two men sent two identical bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Two men, both called X ben Y, living in the same town, divorce their wives, both called Z bat U, living in the same town.</i> which became mixed up<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">They were written be the same scribe and entrusted to the same agent.</i>, one delivers both to both women. Therefore, if one of them was lost, the second became unusable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If two men sent two identical bills of divorce,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose asked before Rebbi Yose: Just as you say that a bill of divorce after a bill of divorce is invalid in the case of two wives<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.2-6">Halakhah 3:1</a>, where a man has two wives with identical names. If he writes two bills of divorce without specifying to whom it applies, he can divorce neither of his wives, not even if he delivers both bills to both of them.</i>, similarly two bills of divorce should be invalid for two women. He answered, that is correct. He said to him, but did we not state: “If two men sent two identical bills of divorce and they became mixed up, one delivers both to both women”?
