There<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">“There” is here, in the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.5.1">Mishnah 9:6</a>.</i>, each of the bills was written in the name of the respective wife; the mix-up caused the problem. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The question raised, which refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.1">Mishnah 3:1</a>.</i>, neither one was written as divorce in her name; when it was delivered, could it be considered as divorce in her name from the start?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If five wrote collectively a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">There is only one text; at the point where the names of the persons involed are to be declared, five different couples are mentioned.</i>, the man A divorces B and C D<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will point out that the verb “to divorce” is not repeated.</i> and the witnesses sign at the end, all are valid and [the bill] shall be delivered to each of them. If one wrote the text separately for each of them and the witnesses sign at the end, [only] the one with which the witnesses are read is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the witnesses sign at the end of the last text, that text is certified. The other texts are not certified and, therefore, invalid.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Five who wrote collectively a bill of divorce,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <i>S̄ebuot</i> 5:5; the reading there are noted <b>ש</b>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: “And I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed regarding everything<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If a witness to any contract, including a bill of divorce, signs and appends a greeting and formulates the greeting as a sentence standing alone, his signature cannot be counted as testimony. But if he writes: “<i>and</i> I am greeting”, he makes clear that his signature refers to the entire document. The same statement (R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan) is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 87a" href="/Gittin.87a">Babli, 87a</a>.</i>; “I am greeting X,” he signed only for the greeting. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if he said “I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed regarding everything<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli.</i>. What means “collectively” for Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to the Mishnah. How must a collective bill of divorce be formulated to be valid?</i>? X divorces Y and Z U. What means “collectively” for Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? We, X and Z, divorce our wives at place A<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is ambiguous since no further details are given about R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion. It is obvious from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.2-6">Halakhah 3:1</a> that a text which mentions men and women separately, “we X and Z divorce our wives Y and U” is biblically invalid since (a) one cannot divorce two women with one statement and (b) it is not clear which woman was married to which man. One has to assume that the text was something like: “we X and Z divorce our wives, X Y and Z U,” with mention of place and date.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that if he mentions divorce for each one separately he needs a text and witnesses for each one separately<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If the language is “X divorces Y and Z divorces U”, there can be no collective bill of divorce. It still can be written on one sheet but it must spell out in full that X divorces Y and frees her to marry any man she choses, Z divorces U and frees her to marry any man she choses, and each statement has to be separately validated by two witnesses.</i>. The strength of Rebbi Joḥanan is from the following: “ ‘That I shall not benefit, a <i>qorbān</i> for this one or that one’; each single one needs a separate opening<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 9:7. The quote is incorrect. The Mishnah states that if each vow separately is declared as <i>qorban</i>, it will have to be annulled separately. One has to read: “a <i>qorbān</i> for this one, a <i>qorbān</i> for that one”. This Mishnah supports R. Ze‘ira’s interpretation of R. Joḥanan’s position.</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah supports Rebbi Joḥanan: “If he wrote a separate text<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">And using a separate sentence containing the verb “to divorce” for each couple represents a separate text for each divorce.</i> for each of them and the witnesses signed at the end, [only] the one with which the witnesses are read is valid.” Samuel said, the detailed statement for Rebbi Meїr is the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah and the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is the general statement for Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in <i>Šebuot</i> 5:5 (36c 1. 4), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 38a" href="/Gittin.38a">Babli 38a</a>. It refers to Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 5:5: “Give me my wheat, barley, and spelt, (expressed in the plural) which you are holding!” “An oath that I am holding nothing of yours”, he is guilty only once (if he swore falsely). “An oath that I am not holding any wheat, or barley, or spelt (expressed in the plural) of yours”, he is guilty for every statement. Rebbi Meїr says, even if he said “wheat, or barley, or spelt (expressed in the collective singular)”, he is guilty for every statement. In a <i>baraita</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 38a" href="/Shevuot.38a">Babli <i>Šebuot</i> 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 25a" href="/Kiddushin.25a"><i>Qiddušin</i> 25a</a>; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.2-19">Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1</a>, 62b 1. 76) R. Meїr states that for a general statement in a false oath he is guilty only once, for a detailed statement he is guilty for every particular item. R. Jehudah notes that if a person is sued simultaneously by several people (Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 5:3) and he falsely swears that he owes “not to you, nor to you, nor to you”, he is guilty for every single statement. Samuel notes that what is a general statement for one may be a detailed statement for another.</i>. [Rebbi]<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">From the text in <i>Šebuot</i>. The Giṭṭin text cannot be correct since the first generation Samuel cannot quote the fourth generation R. Ze‘ira.</i> Samuel said in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira, the words of the rabbis show that the detailed statement for Rebbi Meїr is not the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah and the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is not the general statement for Rebbi Meїr, since Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: “and I am greeting X,” one can assume that he signed regarding everything; “I am greeting X,” he signed only for the greeting. If you would say that the detailed statement for Rebbi Meїr is the general statement for Rebbi Jehudah, even if he said “and I am greeting X,” can one assume that he signed regarding everything<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the <i>baraita</i>, R. Jehudah is mentioned as differing from R. Meїr, the question arises whether for R. Meїr there is a difference between a denial of a debt “to you, to you, to you” and “to you, and to you, and to you”. In the first case, there might be three denials, in the second case, there is only one. The implications for R. Jehudah would be the opposite.</i>? How is this<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">As the parallel shows, this interjection should be deleted.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah implies that the detailed statement for Rebbi Jehudah is not the general statement for Rebbi Meїr, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 5:5. The omission of תמן “there” in the parallel text is appropriate.</i>: “Rebbi Meїr says, even ‘wheat, and barley, and spelt’ makes him guilty for each one separately”, but nobody says “even” unless he refers to an earlier statement<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Which must have been “wheat, barley, spelt” without connectives.</i>. How is this<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">As the parallel shows, this interjection should be deleted.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina said, in Rebbi Meїr’s opinion, [whether]<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The necessary inserted text is from <i>Šebuot</i>.</i> he said “wheat, and barley, and spelt”, [or “wheat, barley, spelt] is a general statement and particulars<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are three particulars, he is obligated for three separate purificatioon offerings.</i>. [In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion,] if he said, “wheat grains, barley grains, and spelt grains”, it is a general statement without particulars<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">It is one connected statement which, if false, constitutes one sin.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he wrote two bills of divorce side by side and two Hebrew-writing witnesses signed from one column to the next, and two Greek-writing witnesses signed from one column to the next, the one whose witnesses are read with it is valid. If one Hebrew- and one Greek-writing witness are under each of them, reaching from one column to the other, all are invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote two bills of divorce in two parallel columns. When the witnesses came to sign, they used the space under both columns. Then only the bill whose witnesses sign directly under it is valid since for the other bill the first witness is at least two lines too far away to be counted. The additional complication is that the Hebrew text starts at the right but the Greek text on the left. For example:<br>               bill                                    bill<br>                                                יצחק בן אברהם<br>                                                משה בן עמרם<br>Ενως υἱὸς Σηθ<br>Σημ υἰὸς Νωε<br>In this case, if the first two signatures apply to the bill on the right hand side, that bill is valid. If they applied to the bill at the left, both bills would be invalid since the bill is read from the left but the signatures from the right. If each bill carries two signatures, one to be read from the right, the other from the left:<br>               bill                                    bill<br>                                                יצחק בן אברהם<br>Ενως υἱὸς Σηθ<br>                                                משה בן עמרם<br>Σημ υἰὸς Νωε<br>no bill can be read with its signatures and both are invalid. If both Hebrew signatures belong to one bill and both Greek to the other, both bills are still invalid since the second signature is only on the fourth line.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he wrote two bills of divorce side by side,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">There is a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.1.2-10"><i>Baba batra</i> 10:1</a>, noted <b>ב</b>. Even though this is evidently from a different edition, the text (except for the last two lines) is close enough to be considered identical. The text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta 7:11</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 162b" href="/Bava_Batra.162b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 162b</a>).</i>“It is invalid if the witnesses are separated from the text by the width of two lines; less than that it is valid”<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses cannot sign directly after the text since then they would not appear as witnesses. In commercial contracts, the distance cannot be two full lines since then the holder of the document could insert a line of text at the end after the signing. Since a commercial contract is invalid if the witnesses are too far from the text, the same standard is applied for bills of divorce and manumission.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">A corresponding rule holds for lacunae in the text: Chapter 3:2, Notes 68, 69.</i> Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: An empty space of two lines in a text about one subject<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Baba batra</i> reads: “Another subject”. The parallel in 3:2 shows that this is an editorial difference, not a scribal error.</i> makes invalid in any case. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said: The Mishnah says so: “One Hebrew- and one Greek-writing witness are under each of them, reaching from one column to the other, are all invalid.” Is not Hebrew combined with Greek one item<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i>: “Is Hebrew relative to Greek not another subject?” The text here is preferable since for a single bill on a sheet of paper it is perfectly acceptable to have one witness sign in Hebrew and the other in Greek or Roman letters. The bills are invalid only because the signatures would be separated by more than one line, not because they are in different alphabets (cf. <i>Tosephta ki F shutah Giṭṭin</i> p. 910).</i>? Rebbi Mana said, if he started at the end of the second line and ended at the fourth line, it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If the first witness does not start to sign at the start of the second line but towards its end, extending his signature to the third line and forcing the second witness to write his patronymic on the fourth line, the document is valid since it cannot be adulterated.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 7:11-12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).7.11-12">Tosephta 7:11–12</a>; an extended version is quoted in <i>Baba batra</i>.</i>“How far from the text should the witnesses sign? That they can be read with it, the words of Rebbi. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, one full line. Rebbi Dositheos ben Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i> erroneously: Dositheos ben Jehudah.</i> says, the width of the witnesses’ handwriting.” What kind of writing does one use to estimate? Rebbi Yasa ben Rebbi Sabbatai<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i> correctly: R. Yasa <i>in the name</i> of R. Sabbatai.</i> says, the width of the witnesses’ handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i>: The largest of the writing of the scribe or one of the witnesses. This seems to be from the editors of <i>Neziqin;</i> the text here implies that the two lines are judged by the larger of the signatures. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 163a" href="/Bava_Batra.163a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 163a</a>, explicitly states that the handwriting of the scribe is irrelevant.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqula: Corresponding to לך ולך<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The empty line must be large enough as to accomodate separately the height of ל and the bottom of ך. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 163a" href="/Bava_Batra.163a">Babli agrees, <i>Baba batra</i> 163a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Does the signature of Yose ben Yannai have equal weight with the signature of Shaul ben Barukh<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that it was acceptable if <sup class="footnote-marker">~</sup>ב was written for “son of”. In that case, “Yose ben Yannai” had no lengths above and below the line, but “Shaul ben Barukh” had both.</i>? He said to him: For that it is needed, that the signature of Yose ben Yannai has equal weight with the signature of Shaul ben Barukh<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">In judging a document, the actual signatures count, not theoretical distances. It might be that “Yose ben Yannai” uses up more space than “Shaul ben Barukh”.</i>.
A document which has an erasure or insertion in its main part<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Where the parties are identified and the sums of money specified.</i> is invalid; in the routine matter it is valid; but if it was mentioned later<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Reference is made to the erasure or the addition before the last line of text.</i> it is valid even in the main part. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, one has to mention it at least in the second line before the end of the text. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav: One has to mention the essence of the document with it<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">In justifying erasure or addition, the purpose has to be stated.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: If one did not mention the essence of the document with it<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is incomplete. The following discussion shows that one has to add: “it is invalid.”</i>. The argument of Rebbi Abba seems inverted. There, Rebbi Ba said in the name of Rav Jeremiah: Disqualified witnesses do not constitute remote testimony since they came only to support the document’s validity<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">If the first lines after the contract text were filled with the signatures of people invited for reasons other than testimony and the real witnesses sign only later, one validates the document even though the real signatures start more than one line after the text.</i>. And here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If the essence of the document is not stated at the acknowledgment of a correction, the document is invalid.</i>? There, in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah, here in the name of Rav.
If somebody contests the signature of the witnesses or the signature of the judges<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A debtor whose property is attached following a decree of a court in another jurisdiction claims that the document is counterfeit and the signatures of the witnesses and the judges are fake.</i>. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: If he wants to certify it from the handwriting of the witnesses it is certified, from the handwriting of the judges it is certified<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor can prove the document genuine either by proving the validity of the signatures of the witnesses or that of the judges.</i>; I am saying<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abba adds that only two signatures have to be verified; they can also be those of one witness and one judge.</i> that even from one witness and one judge it is certified. If he gave permission to the scribe to write and to the witnesses to sign, Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jeremiah: The witnesses have to write: “This loan is from the first of Nisan but we wrote at a later date.”<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor told the scribe and witnesses to execute a document of indebtedness. If it was not written on the same day, the fact has to be noted on the document even though a postdated document is valid (only a predated one is invalid). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 170b" href="/Bava_Batra.170b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 170b</a>, confirms the statement but notes that it is not enforced.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav Hamnuna: The confirmation of the judges is valid even if it is at some distance<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Judicial confirmation of the genuineness of a document does not have to be written immediately below the signatures of the witnesses; it can be anywhere on the document. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 163b" href="/Bava_Batra.163b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 163b</a>, forbids <i>any</i> space between signatures and confirmation, not even one line.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, is that correct? Could he then not certify from one handwriting<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana’s argument is that of the Babli: If there is an empty space, the creditor could insert text in between and create a fake document with genuine signatures.</i>? Rav said: The judges have to write: We certified this in the presence of X and Y. Why? So they can be convicted of perjury<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The root זמם, used to describe perjury, really means “to devise (a plot)”. It is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:16-21" href="/Deuteronomy.19.16-21"><i>Deut</i>. 19:16–21</a> to characterize the perjurer. In Talmudic theory, a wrong testimony is not usually perjured testimony; it is up to the court to judge the credibility of the witnesses. Perjury is impossible testimony, i. e., the witnesses pretend to have seen things they could not have seen since they were not present at the place at the time indicated. This leads to the notion that the validity of a document could be tested only if the exact time and place of the signing were indicated in the document.</i>. But then also on which date, at which time, at which place? A judgment was given before Rav and this was not written<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">It was executed by the signatures of the judges alone.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said: The judges have to know the parties. It happened that they falsified<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">A case of identity theft, where the signatures of the witnesses and the judges were all genuine but the presumed debtor gave a false identity, so that an innocent third party was hit with a decree of payment.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> It is valid if some of the text remained and was written in the second column<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe folded the sheet so that it formed two columns. He then used both columns for the text. The witnesses then signed at the end. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 88a" href="/Gittin.88a">Babli, 88a</a>, points out that this is valid only if the parchment or paper was folded, not if the two columns were glued together since they originally might have been parts of two different documents.)</i>, with the witnesses signing below. If the witnesses signed on top of the page, or at the sides<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The signatures are disregarded unless they are affixed at the end of the document, exactly one line from the end of the text.</i>, or on the other side for a simple document<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to a sealed document.</i>, it is invalid. If [two bills of divorce] start at the same place<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Two bills written on the same sheet, one from the middle to the bottom, the other from the middle to the top. The signatures do not belong to either text.</i> and the witnesses are in the middle, both are invalid. If the start of one bill is next to the end of the other and the witnesses are at the end, the bill whose witnesses are read with the end is valid. A bill of divorce written in Hebrew with its witnesses signing in Greek, written in Greek with its witnesses signing in Hebrew, or one witness signing in Hebrew and one in Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If there is only one document to be certified, it makes no difference if one signature is read from the right and the other from the left.</i>, or the scribe wrote with one witness, is valid. “X, witness” is valid. “The son of Y, witness” is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">If the signature was recognizable as that of a certain person, the exact wording of the signature is irrelevant.</i>. “X the son of Y”, but he did not write “witness”, is valid. If he wrote his or her surname<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The family name (in addition to the proper name but without the patronymic), in a society where family names were not common.</i>, it is valid; that is how the punctilious in Jerusalem used to write. 
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If some of the text remained,” etc. What does “remained” mean? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Baibon said, e. g., “true, valid, and clear<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “remained” implies that most of the text was in the first column. It is now asserted that the document is valid even if only the last line is written in the second column, in which the husband asserts that the contents of the bill conform to his wishes. (The Babylonian formulary has only the words “valid and true”.) Cf. M.A. Friedman, <i>Jewish Marriage in Palestine</i> (Tel Aviv 1980) Vol. 1, p. 478, Note 112.</i>.”
And what is invalid for a simple document is valid for a “knotted” one if it is on the back<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:10:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.10.2-6">Chapter 8:12</a> for the rules of simple and sealed (knotted) documents.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">In the ms., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:4-9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.4-9.3">Halakhah 10</a> starts only at 50d, line 12.</i> Rav said: Judges may sign even if they cannot read, but no witnesses may sign unless they know how to read<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">If judges sign the document, they only certify that it was presented to them and they attest to the genuineness of the signatures. Therefore, judges who do not know Greek can certify Aramaic signatures on a Greek document. But the witnesses testify to the genuineness of the document and they may be called to testify about its meaning; they are prohibited from signing unless they can read and understand it. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 109" href="/Ketubot.109">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 109a/b</a>, the rule is attributed to R. Ḥiyya, Rav’s uncle.</i>. He said, such should come over me<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">An oath; the speaker invokes all kinds of bad things which should happen to him if he ever did what he denies doing.</i> if I ever acted thus<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">He never acted on what he permitted in theory.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said before Rebbi Yose: Did no Greek document come before Rav that he could confirm it by its signatures<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav exercised jurisdiction only in Babylonia, it is quite possible than no Greek document ever came before him.</i>? The Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “A bill of divorce written in Hebrew with its witnesses signing in Greek, written in Greek with its witnesses signing in Hebrew, or one witness signing in Hebrew and one in Greek, is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Does this not mean that the witness signing in Greek did not know Hebrew and vice-versa?</i>.” He explains that they knew how to read but not how to sign; or if they knew both [languages], they could sign in either one of their choice.
Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah which lets the witnesses sign in any way they choose, if only their signatures are characteristic for them.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is Rebbi Jehudah’s<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> requires a lengthy text.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, you should not infer that the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan is that he differs, but he answered him following his argument. Lengthwise<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.8.1">Mishnah 9</a> speaks of the case that the sheet containing the bill was folded lengthwise, with the two columns then in parallel. This may be ascribed to R. Jehudah who in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> requires that the text be very specific in detailing the divorce.<br>The <i>editio princeps</i> reads instead of לאורכו two words לא רבו and takes it together with the next statement: לא רבו דו אמא: “Was it not his teacher who said”, viz., one supports R. Yose, who is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.2.2">Chapter 3, Note 66</a>, that his teacher R. Jeremiah quoted his own teacher R. Ze‘ira to the effect that one does not have to worry that two men, both called X ben Y, divorce their wives, both called A bat B, at the same place on the same day. Therefore, one does not have to worry either that a person whose characteristic signature consists only of one word be taken for another person.</i>. That is what he said in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Since pairing is infrequent, there was no pairing. So is the Mishnah: “If the scribe wrote with one witness, is it valid<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">J. N. Epstein (<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> מבוא לנוסח המשנה, p. 476–477) notes that in most documents from the Genizah, at the end the scribe appended his own name. The Mishnah admits this declaration as testimony to the genuineness of the document. This is rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 88a" href="/Gittin.88a">Babli, 88a</a>, in the name of R. Jeremiah. Cf. M.A. Friedman, <i>Jewish Marriage in Palestine</i> Vol. 1, p. 489.</i>. “X” is valid. “The son of Y” is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">If the signature was recognizable as that of a certain person, the exact wording of the signature is irrelevant.</i>. “X the son of Y” but he did not write “witness”, is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">In the revised version, no witness has to declare that he signs as a witness; his signature at the correct place automatically justifies it.</i>. If he wrote his or her surname, it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Evrybody, including J. N. Epstein, considers this as a scribal error for “valid”. But it might mean that the family name alone, without a proper name, is invalid.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even if he wrote “he is it<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Reading אִינּוֹ for הִנּוֹ. This is acceptable as a signature as long as it was written in a characteristic way that would be difficult to counterfeit, more or less in the tradition of Sephardic rabbinical signatures through the ages.</i>”, it is valid. Rebbi Abbahu wrote <b>א</b>. Rav Ḥisda wrote <b>ס</b>.<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Probably in a very convoluted manner. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 87b" href="/Gittin.87b">Babli, 87b</a>, a number of rabbis are mentioned who wrote their names in the form of a picture: R. Ḥanina a palm branch, Rav a beehive, Rabba bar Rav Huna a sail (the latter was imitated in the 18th. Cent. by R. H. Y. D. Azulay.)</i> Samuel wrote a palm branch.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A forced bill of divorce among Jews is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In a case where the marriage is valid but sinful.</i>, from Gentiles it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife’s family pays Gentiles to beat up the husband or the Gentile ruler to jail the husband until he agrees to a divorce (documented in many cases from Medieval Spain), the resulting divorce is invalid.</i>; but the Gentiles may whip him and tell him, do what the Jews tell you<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">If the rabbinic court asks the Gentile court to act on its behalf, the resulting divorce is valid.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “That is how the punctilious in Jerusalem used to write. A forced bill of divorce among Jews is valid, from Gentiles it is invalid”. <sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 9:4:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.9.4.2-9">Yebamot 9:5</a> (<b>י</b>) Notes 40–46.</i> Samuel said, it is invalid and disqualifies from the priesthood. Samuel said, one does not force, only disqualify. Samuel said, one does not force except for example a widow married to a High Priest, a divorcee or one freed by <i>ḥalȋṣah</i> in the case of a simple priest. But did we not state: “Secondarily forbidden”? Did he not say “for example”? But did we not state: “A person who by a vow forbids his wife to have any usufruct from him for up to 30 days shall appoint a caretaker; after 30 days he shall divorce her and pay <i>ketubah</i>”! We heard that he shall divorce; did we hear that one forces him?
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Jews who forced in the manner of Gentiles make invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Batra 48a:4:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Batra.48a.4.1">Tosaphot (88b <i>s.v.</i> ובגוים, <i>Baba batra</i> 48a <i>s.v.</i> גט</a>) if the forcing was executed under the supervision of a Gentile court or a Gentile ruler.</i>, even if he says, I shall not feed nor provide. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: Gentiles who forced in the manner of Jews are valid, even if he says, I shall not feed nor provide<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If the forcing is under the supervision of a rabbinic court, the divorce is valid even if the marriage is completely legitimate because the husband has no right to mistreat his wife in any way.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says this: “Gentiles may tell him<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This addition probably is a scribal error.</i>, whip him, and tell him, do what the Jews tell you to do.” Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli agrees in the name of Rav (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 77a" href="/Ketubot.77a"><i>Ketubot</i> 77a</a>). Samuel holds that the husband has to be forced to pay but Rav asserts that one cannot require the wife “to live in the same basket with a snake” and the court has to force a divorce.</i>: If somebody says: “I shall not feed nor provide”, one forces him to divorce. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Does one force? He said to him, do you still have doubts? If one forces because of a foul smell<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.8.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:10</a>.</i>, so much more because of sustenance! Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody says: “I shall not feed and I shall not provide”, one tells him: Either feed and provide, or divorce.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it became public knowledge in town that she was preliminarily married, she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If she had been unmarried, she cannot marry another man without receiving a divorce from the one to whom she is betrothed in the eyes of the public.</i>; divorced, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">And could not marry a Cohen even if previously unmarried.</i>, except if there was an excuse. What would be an excuse? Mr. X divorced his wife conditionally<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">And the condition was not satisfied.</i>, or he threw valuables for preliminary marriage to her and it was doubtful whether it was closer to her than to him<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">A doubt whether a doubtful marriage was concluded has to be dismissed as irrelevant.</i>; that is an excuse.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If it became public knowledge in town that she was preliminarily married, she is preliminarily married”. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If lights are burning and people talk<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">A person can be held married only if there was a marriage celebration and people talk about it. The same opinion is attributed to R. Joḥanan in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 89a" href="/Gittin.89a">Babli, 89a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira objected before Rebbi Yasa: But did we not state: “If it became public knowledge in town that she was preliminarily married, she is preliminarily married; divorced, she is divorced”? Can you say that lights are burning<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah treats divorce as parallel to marriage and usually people do not make divorce parties, no celebration can be required.</i>? No, people talk. So here also, people talk<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Likewise for marriage, no testimony about festivities is required.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If the title was checked and found to be from the mouth of women or minors, the title is abolished<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">The court cannot act (to require a divorce or to prohibit marriage to a Cohen) unless it is informed by people able to appear as formal witnesses in court.</i>. Rav said, they permitted only in case of a hearsay witness<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Rav disagrees with R. Joḥanan. If a formal proof were required, the Mishnah would be unnecessary. Therefore, only a determination of facts is needed, and women can be asked about facts. He agrees only that if the information was by hearsay and its source cannot be located, it has to be disregarded. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 89a" href="/Gittin.89a">Babli, 89a</a>, Rav holds that if the source was located but cannot be asked because the informant left, one accepts the rumor as true.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai say, a person should not divorce his wife unless he found her immoral, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>.</i>: “For he found with her a matter of nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband had proof of her infidelity, he would by law be obligated to divorce her (cf. <i>Soṭah</i> 1:1, Notes 13,14). In the absence of proof, the House of Shammai counsel him to divorce her. This probably is also the interpretation to be given to <i>Matth</i>. 19:9 and is the basis of the disapproval of marrying a divorcee expressed in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 270.</i>.” But the House of Hillel say, even if she spoiled his dish, as it was said: “For he found with her a bad thing<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The Houses of Shammai and Hillel explain the same verse. The House of Shammai read עֶרְוַת דָּבָר as if it were (in rabbinic Hebrew) דְּבַר עֶרְוָה “a matter of nakedness (immorality)”. The House of Hillel read the construct state עֶרְוַת as a modifier of דָּבָר “thing”: an undesirable thing; e.g., being a bad cook.</i>.” Rebbi Aqiba said, even if he found another more beautiful than her, as it was said: “It will be if she does not appear pleasing in his eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">171,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Even if she is beautiful in an objective way, but not beautiful <i>in his eyes</i>, he may divorce her. In his opinion (adopted in practice), a divorcee may be completely blameless.</i>, etc.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:1, Notes 13,14.</i> Was it not stated in the name of the House of Shammai: Not only that the woman must leave because of incest; from where that she must leave if her head’s [hair] is loose, if the side seams of her dress are open, or her arms stripped bare? The verse says, “for he found in her a matter of nakedness.” How can the House of Shammai confirm this<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, the House of Shammai admit only adultery as cause of divorce. In the <i>baraita</i>, they admit all kinds of lewd behavior.</i>? Lest you say that one divorced because of immorality is forbidden, for anothercause she would be permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Could be remarried by her first husband after having had a second husband.</i>. Rebbi Shila from Kefar-Tamarta said: The verse is difficult for the House of Shammai: “Her first husband, who had sent her away, cannot afterwards retake her.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:4" href="/Deuteronomy.24.4"><i>Deut</i>. 24:4</a>.</i>” Where do we hold? If to forbid her to him, is she not already forbidden to him<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">If she committed adultery, she is automatically forbidden to her husband, even if she does not remarry. The prohibition to remarry the first husband after a remarriage seems to be unnecessary for the House of Shammai.</i>? But we must hold, to burden him with a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">In remarrying her, the first husband would commit two sins in one act (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Gittin 90a:7:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Gittin.90a.7.1">Tosaphot 90a, <i>s.v</i>. מה</a>).</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:33" href="/Leviticus.15.33"><i>Lev</i>. 15:33</a>. The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 64b" href="/Shabbat.64b">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 64b</a>, <i>Sifra Meṣoraʻ Pereq</i> 9(12).</i>: “And the unwell in her menstruation and the sufferer from gonorrhea in his flow.” The earlier Elders used to say, “she shall be in her menstruation<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:19" href="/Leviticus.15.19"><i>Lev</i>. 15:19</a>.</i>”, she shall not use <i>kohl</i> nor any make-up until she comes into water. Rebbi Aqiba said to them: Is that a reason? If you say so, she makes herself ugly and he starts thinking to divorce her. It turns out that the Elders follow the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Since she cannot be divorced, it does not matter how she looks.</i> and Rebbi Aqiba the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, R. Aqiba does not disagree with the House of Hillel; he simply takes their argument to its logical conclusion.</i>.
